# Graphical perception & colour — FULL research corpus (recovered from transcripts)

> **What this is.** The complete per-agent deep-research behind the `graphical-perception-and-color`
> skill, extracted **verbatim** from the `StructuredOutput` of every research agent across the three
> research passes (`wf_66c7999f`, `wf_a2d5d541`, `wf_a19dfafc`). This is the RAW corpus the 26-finding
> synthesis in [graphical-perception-and-color-research.md](graphical-perception-and-color-research.md)
> was distilled from. **De-noised by construction** — only StructuredOutput research content; no prompts,
> tool logs, rate-limit / retry / token-accounting text. Extracted deterministically (jq/Python), not
> re-summarized.

> **Scale:** 78 source extractions · 225 adversarial verifications · 22 synthesis records, from 325 agents over 3 passes.

---

## Part A — Source extractions (78)

Each agent read one source and extracted claims with a verbatim supporting quote and importance rating.

### Pass 1 — encoding / CVD / colormap science (2026-06-21)

#### Source `a0159d24` — quality: **primary** · published: 2014
- **[central]** Empirical testing of Tufte-style 'chartjunk' minimalism is mixed rather than settled: visual embellishment does not reliably destroy comprehension, so the blanket prescription to eliminate non-data ink is opinion/convention, not a uniformly supported empirical finding. This study extends prior embellishment work (Bateman et al.) to larger datasets (10+ data points) and time-limited viewing.
  > "Although many well-cited theories or guidelines for visualization design advocate 'minimalism', designers tend to include a wide variety of visual embellishments in their charts... Various studies have shown mixed results depending on the details of the task and context."
- **[central]** Chart type (embellished Holmes-style vs. plain) significantly affected short-term recall of chart content, and the availability of a time limit affected both comprehension and short-term recall performance — i.e., embellishment effects are moderated by viewing-time constraints rather than being universally good or bad.
  > "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall."
- **[supporting]** Embellished (Holmes) charts scored higher on subjective attractiveness and memorability than plain charts, providing experimental support for the engagement/aesthetic-value benefit of embellishment that purely data-ink-ratio guidance ignores.
  > "Participants found Holmes charts more attractive and memorable. Additionally, these visual embellishments can increase ratings of engagement and aesthetic value."
- **[supporting]** Chart type also affected the processing/review time needed to answer questions about the chart, indicating embellishment changes reading time (an efficiency cost/benefit) measurably rather than leaving interpretation effort unchanged.
  > "The type of chart affected the time needed to review the chart while answering the questions."

#### Source `a05b8438` — quality: **primary** · published: 2023-05 (May/June 2023; IEEE Computer Graphics and Applications, vol. 43, no. 3, pp. 88-93)
- **[central]** The blanket avoidance of rainbow colormaps has become unquestioned dogma in the visualization community, yet this paper (by senior visualization researchers Ware, Stone, Szafir, Rhyne) argues that position is overstated and that rainbow colormaps have underappreciated useful properties. This is a direct, citable counter to the common 'rainbow/jet is always harmful' claim in the research question.
  > "avoiding rainbow colormaps, along with their derivatives, has become dogma in the visualization community... We argue that rainbow colormaps have properties that are underappreciated by existing design conventions."
- **[central]** Rainbow colormaps can be the right choice for some specific applications; the choice of colormap is application-dependent rather than a universal good/bad rule. This supports nuanced, evidence-based guidance for encoding continuous magnitude rather than a flat 'never use rainbow' rule.
  > "Choosing a colormap is a complex task, and rainbow colormaps can be useful for selected applications."
- **[supporting]** The paper is an explicit rebuttal/update to Borland & Taylor's influential 'Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful' (2007), whose stated case was that rainbows confuse viewers, obscure data, and mislead interpretation. This documents the canonical anti-rainbow argument the broader literature relies on.
  > "The paper argued that the "rainbow colormap's characteristics of confusing the viewer, obscuring the data and actively misleading interpretation make it a poor choice for visualization.""
- **[supporting]** Despite a sustained, well-publicized recommendation against them, practicing scientists keep using rainbow colormaps, which the authors treat as evidence that rainbows may offer real perceptual value (e.g., for resolving fine features) not captured by perceptual-distance critiques. Relevant to weighing convention/opinion vs empirical benefit.
  > "Despite this loud and persistent recommendation, scientists continue to use rainbow colormaps. Have we failed to communicate our message, or do rainbow colormaps offer advantages that have not been fully appreciated?"

#### Source `a2e435a5` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010
- **[central]** Heer & Bostock (2010) replicated Cleveland & McGill's classic graphical perception studies of spatial (position/length) encoding and luminance contrast using Amazon Mechanical Turk crowdsourcing, and found crowdsourced results reproduced the prior lab findings — validating crowdsourcing as a viable method for graphical perception experiments.
  > "We replicate previous studies of spatial encoding and luminance contrast and compare our results... Our results demonstrate that crowdsourced perception experiments are viable and contribute new insights for visualization design."
- **[central]** The study conducted controlled experiments specifically on rectangular area perception, the encoding used in treemaps and cartograms, providing empirical data on how accurately humans judge area-based magnitude encodings (directly relevant to bubble/dot-area encoding).
  > "We also conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms) and on chart size and gridline spacing."
- **[supporting]** The paper is peer-reviewed primary research published at ACM CHI 2010 (received an Honorable Mention Award), authored by Jeffrey Heer and Michael Bostock, establishing it as an authoritative replication of Cleveland & McGill's perceptual-task framework.
  > "Authors: Jeffrey Heer, Michael Bostock; Venue: Proc. ACM Human Factors in Computing Systems (CHI); Year: 2010; Award: Honorable Mention Award"
- **[supporting]** Understanding low-level human perception is treated as foundational to (not optional for) effective visualization design — the empirical premise motivating perceptual-effectiveness rankings of visual encodings.
  > "Understanding perception is critical to effective visualization design."
- **[tangential]** The study also empirically examined chart size and gridline spacing as design variables affecting perceptual accuracy, extending beyond encoding-type comparisons to chart formatting choices.
  > "We also conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms) and on chart size and gridline spacing."

#### Source `a3373239` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2016 (updated 2026-01-09)
- **[central]** Preattentive visual properties are processed by the eye and brain in under 500 milliseconds, without conscious action, occurring in sensory memory before focused attention is applied.
  > "It takes less than 500 milliseconds for the eye and the brain to process a preattentive property"
- **[central]** Following Colin Ware's framework, preattentive visual properties fall into four categories: Color (hue and intensity), Form (size, shape, orientation, curvature, numerosity, etc.), Movement (flicker and motion), and Spatial Positioning (2D position, stereoscopic depth, concave/convex).
  > "Hues and intensities are preattentively processed and can be used to separate visual elements"
- **[supporting]** Assigning data attributes to preattentive visual channels (visual mapping) has no universally agreed-upon optimal combination; effectiveness depends on the user group and context rather than a fixed rule.
  > "There is no universal agreement on which attributes in which combination will be most effective for any given user group."
- **[supporting]** For analyzing and assigning color in visualization, hue should be separated from intensity, with hue being the named-color dimension (e.g., red, violet) and saturation/lightness measuring intensity (the HSL model).
  > "Hue is a measure of the color that we normally give a name to such as 'Red' or 'Violet'. Saturation and lightness are a measure of the intensity"

#### Source `a35ee3e0` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015-09-12
- **[central]** Many vendor-supplied colormaps have highly uneven perceptual contrast across their range, including 'perceptual flat spots' that can hide a data feature as large as one tenth (10%) of the total data range.
  > "Many colour maps provided by vendors have highly uneven perceptual contrast over their range. It is not uncommon for colour maps to have perceptual flat spots that can hide a feature as large as one tenth of the total data range."
- **[central]** The single most important factor in designing a good colormap is ensuring that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness across the map is uniform.
  > "The most important factor in designing a colour map is to ensure that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness of the colours is uniform."
- **[central]** Prior perceptually-uniform colormap design work erred by relying on CIELAB, because CIELAB is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies — so it does not guarantee uniformity for the higher-frequency detail in real data images.
  > "Previous work in the design of perceptually uniform colour maps has mostly failed to recognise that CIELAB space is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies."
- **[supporting]** Colormaps can contain perceptual discontinuities that induce the appearance of false features (artifacts) not present in the underlying data — a known failure mode of rainbow/jet-style maps.
  > "Colour maps may also have perceptual discontinuities that induce the appearance of false features."
- **[supporting]** The design requirements differ by colormap type — linear (sequential), diverging, rainbow, and cyclic maps each have distinct specific requirements, and the paper provides two test images for objectively evaluating colormaps.
  > "The specific requirements for linear, diverging, rainbow and cyclic colour maps are developed in detail. To support this work two test images for evaluating colour maps are presented."

#### Source `a396272d` — quality: **primary** · published: 2018-08-01
- **[central]** Color vision deficiency affects up to 8% of men and 0.5% of women (over 600 million people globally), with deuteranomaly (red-green deficiency) being the most common form.
  > "CVD reduces the ability to distinguish between certain colors and affects up to 8% of men and 0.5% of women."
- **[central]** Viridis, though the 'gold standard' colormap, is optimized for normal vision and is not optimal for color vision deficiency; cividis is designed to enable nearly-identical data interpretation for both normal-vision and CVD viewers.
  > "it is optimal for those with normal vision and not CVD"
- **[central]** Cividis was created by mathematically optimizing colormaps using the CIECAM02-UCS (Uniform Color Space) model via a Python module (cmaputil), ensuring perceptual uniformity in the a'-b' plane and a linearized, maximized J' (lightness) range.
  > "Equal Euclidean distances between values in this space leads to an equal color difference perception"
- **[central]** A colormap should use a linear (monotonic) increase in lightness to avoid the perception of gradients that are not actually present in the data — the core flaw that makes rainbow/jet colormaps misleading.
  > "A linear increase in lightness should be used to avoid the perception of gradients that are not present."
- **[supporting]** CVD-safe colormap optimization was performed using the Machado et al. simulation model at severity 100 (complete dichromacy) so that anyone with partial or complete red-green deficiency benefits from the same colormap.
  > "ensure that anyone with either partial or complete red-green color deficiency could benefit from the same optimized colormap"

#### Source `a3f6ab55` — quality: **primary** · published: 2022-12-12
- **[supporting]** Human circadian photoreception (melatonin suppression and circadian phase resetting) is most sensitive to short-wavelength blue light, with peak sensitivity (lambda-max) near 481 nm for melatonin suppression and 483 nm for phase resetting over a 6.5-hour exposure, consistent with predominant melanopsin/ipRGC mediation.
  > "Melatonin suppression: "lambdamax of 481 nm" ... Circadian resetting: "lambdamax of 483 nm""
- **[supporting]** The spectral sensitivity of the circadian/melatonin response is duration-dependent: during the first quarter of a 6.5-h exposure, peak melatonin-suppression sensitivity shifted to ~441 nm with a second peak at 550 nm (indicating strong initial cone contribution), then decayed so that lambda-max returned to ~485 nm (melanopsin-dominated) by the final quarter.
  > "For melatonin suppression, lambdamax was 441 nm in the first quarter of the 6.5-h exposure with a second peak at 550 nm, suggesting strong initial S and L+M cone contribution. This contribution decayed over time; lambdamax was 485 nm in the final quarter"
- **[tangential]** Both cones and melanopsin contribute to nonvisual light responses, with cones driving the initial response and melanopsin dominating over longer exposures; therefore long-exposure lighting recommendations should be based on melanopsin stimulation rather than extrapolated from short-duration data.
  > "melanopsin contributions are likely to predominate, and quantification of melanopsin stimulation should underpin lighting recommendations for real-world environments"
- **[tangential]** Extrapolating circadian light-effect findings from short-duration exposures to longer photoperiods may be inaccurate because the relative photoreceptor contributions change with exposure duration.
  > "translation of findings from short-duration exposures to longer photoperiods...may be premature, or at least less accurate"

#### Source `a414ee06` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2025-07-18
- **[central]** A visual task completed in under approximately 200 ms is considered pre-attentive, and the primary pre-attentive attributes are color (hue and intensity), shape, size, orientation, and spatial position.
  > "A widely accepted heuristic is that any visual task completed in less than approximately 200 ms is considered pre-attentive."
- **[central]** Cleveland & McGill's hierarchy ranks position and aligned length as the most accurate perceptual encodings, while area, angle, and color are more error-prone for quantitative data.
  > "Their hierarchy indicates the following: Position and aligned length are the most accurate and cognitively efficient; Area, angle, and colour (for quantitative data) are more error-prone."
- **[central]** Rainbow (jet) color scales are ineffective for quantitative interpretation because they introduce non-linear perceptual gradients and create false discontinuities; perceptually-uniform palettes like viridis and cividis are recommended instead.
  > "Rainbow colour scale...is widely recognised as ineffective for quantitative interpretation. Rainbow scales introduce non-linear perceptual gradients, create false discontinuities."
- **[central]** Perceptually-uniform palettes such as viridis and cividis maintain legibility for users with color vision deficiencies, whereas rainbow scales are inaccessible to those individuals.
  > "Select perceptually uniform palettes (e.g., viridis, cividis) that maintain legibility across different visual conditions and for users with colour vision deficiencies."
- **[supporting]** While Miller's classic finding put working memory capacity at approximately seven plus or minus two items, more recent research suggests the true capacity is closer to four items.
  > "working memory capacity is limited to approximately seven ± two items ... more recent research suggests that the true capacity may be closer to four items."

#### Source `a5e5895c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2014-08-19
- **[central]** In a controlled proofreading experiment, positive polarity displays (dark text on light background) produced both smaller pupil sizes and better proofreading performance than negative polarity displays (light text on dark background) — providing empirical, mechanism-level support for the 'positive polarity advantage' (i.e., light mode outperforming dark mode for reading detail).
  > "Indeed, pupil sizes were smaller and proofreading performance was better with positive than with negative polarity displays."
- **[central]** The positive polarity advantage is attributable to display luminance, not color or polarity per se: the greater brightness of light-background displays constricts the pupil, yielding a sharper retinal image and better perception of fine detail. This means the relevant variable for screen reading comfort/legibility is luminance, which constrains claims that dark backgrounds are inherently 'easier on the eyes.'
  > "The results are compatible with the hypothesis that the positive polarity advantage is an effect of display luminance."
- **[supporting]** The established baseline finding the study builds on is that reading performance is better for dark text on light background (positive polarity) than for light text on dark background (negative polarity).
  > "Reading performance is better for dark text on light background (positive polarity) than for light text on dark background (negative polarity)."

#### Source `a7d224c3` — quality: **primary** · published: 2007-07
- **[central]** Positive polarity displays (dark text on a light background) produced consistently better proofreading performance than negative polarity displays (light text on a dark background) across a series of experiments.
  > "In a series of experiments, proofreading performance was consistently better with positive polarity (dark text on light background) than with negative polarity displays (light text on dark background)."
- **[central]** The positive-polarity performance advantage held regardless of ambient lighting level (darkness vs. typical office illumination), meaning the effect is not an artifact of room lighting.
  > "independent of ambient lighting (darkness vs. typical office illumination) and of chromaticity (black and white vs. blue and yellow)"
- **[supporting]** The positive-polarity advantage was also independent of chromaticity, persisting whether the display used black/white or blue/yellow color pairs.
  > "independent of ambient lighting (darkness vs. typical office illumination) and of chromaticity (black and white vs. blue and yellow)"
- **[central]** Color (chrominance) contrast cannot substitute for luminance contrast: red text on a green background failed to compensate for a lack of luminance contrast, implying data marks and text must be distinguished by luminance, not hue alone.
  > "A final experiment showed that colour contrast (red text on green background) could not compensate for a lack of luminance contrast."

#### Source `a8b79b7d` — quality: **primary** · published: 1984
- **[central]** Cleveland & McGill (1984) is the foundational paper that proposed putting graphical methods for data analysis and presentation on a scientific footing via the study of 'graphical perception' (how people decode quantitative information from graphs).
  > "The subject of graphical methods for data analysis and for data presentation needs a scientific foundation. In this article we take a few steps in the direction of establishing such a foundation."
- **[central]** The paper identifies a set of elementary perceptual tasks people use to extract quantitative values from charts and ranks them by accuracy, then uses that ranking to recommend chart forms — implying position-based encodings are more accurate than area-, angle-, or color/shading-based ones (relevant to encoding magnitude via dot area).
  > "The authors identify elementary perceptual tasks people perform when extracting quantitative data from visualizations, then rank these tasks by accuracy."
- **[supporting]** On the basis of its accuracy ranking, the paper recommends replacing common chart types (bar, pie, divided-bar) with alternatives such as dot charts and framed-rectangle charts, establishing accuracy of perceptual decoding as a design criterion.
  > "They apply this principle to common chart types (bar, pie, divided bar charts) and propose alternatives like dot charts and framed-rectangle charts as improvements over traditional graphical forms."
- **[tangential]** The work is a highly influential, peer-reviewed primary source in the data-visualization literature (Journal of the American Statistical Association, 1984), with on the order of ~2,800+ citations.
  > "Citation Count: 2,865 cited by"

#### Source `a98fa454` — quality: **primary** · published: 2007-03
- **[central]** The rainbow (jet) color map has no perceptual ordering: viewers cannot infer the order of data values from the colors without consulting a legend, because the spectral/wavelength ordering of hues is not perceived as ordered (e.g., yellow is not seen as 'between' green and red in magnitude).
  > "The rainbow color map confuses viewers through its lack of perceptual ordering, obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation, and actively misleads interpretation through the introduction of non-data-dependent gradients."
- **[central]** The rainbow colormap's luminance varies non-monotonically and uncontrolled across its range, which obscures/hides fine detail in scalar data (low-luminance and high-luminance bands suppress perceived contrast unevenly).
  > "obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation"
- **[central]** The rainbow colormap introduces visual artifacts not present in the data: it creates sharp/false boundaries where hue transitions are abrupt and flattens regions where hue changes slowly, biasing perceived structure and causing misinterpretation.
  > "creates sharp boundaries that are not in the data"
- **[supporting]** The paper demonstrates these failures empirically using 2D synthetic datasets with known ground truth, showing the rainbow map distorts the apparent structure that perceptually ordered alternatives (e.g., grayscale and isoluminant/perceptually ordered maps) preserve.
  > "Borland and Taylor list three aspects of how a rainbow colormap can be misleading using 2D synthetic data examples: has no perceptual order, hides details and creates sharp boundaries that are not in the data."
- **[supporting]** Published in IEEE Computer Graphics and Applications, vol. 27, no. 2 (March/April 2007), pp. 14-17, by David Borland and Russell M. Taylor II (UNC Chapel Hill); DOI 10.1109/MCG.2007.323435 — a peer-reviewed venue establishing this as a foundational citeable source against rainbow/jet colormaps.
  > "IEEE Computer Graphics and Applications in March-April 2007 (volume 27, issue 2, pages 14-17) by David Borland and M Russell Taylor from the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill"

#### Source `a9e068c2` — quality: **primary** · published: 2014
- **[central]** Munzner's framework devotes a dedicated chapter, Ch 5 'Marks and Channels,' to visual encoding — i.e., she organizes encoding effectiveness around the concepts of marks (geometric primitives) and channels (the visual attributes that control their appearance), which is the structural basis for ranking encodings like position, length, area, and color.
  > "Ch 5. Marks and Channels"
- **[supporting]** Munzner treats color not as a standalone topic but as one channel among several, grouping it with other channels in Ch 10 'Map Color and Other Channels,' consistent with her marks-and-channels effectiveness framework where color (hue/luminance/saturation) is compared against spatial channels.
  > "Ch 10. Map Color and Other Channels"
- **[central]** The empirical basis for Munzner's Chapter 5 channel-effectiveness treatment is the graphical-perception literature, explicitly Cleveland & McGill's 1984 experiments, Bertin's Semiology of Graphics, and Stevens's measurement-scales work — establishing that the position/length/angle/area/color rankings derive from controlled perceptual studies rather than opinion.
  > "Graphical Perception: Theory, Experimentation and the Application to the Development of Graphical Models by William S. Cleveland and Robert McGill (1984) - appears to be the primary empirical work on channel effectiveness"
- **[supporting]** Munzner's Chapter 5 cites Colin Ware's 'Information Visualization: Perception for Design' (3rd edition, 2013) as the contemporary perception reference, linking the design rules-of-thumb to perceptual-science evidence.
  > "Information Visualization: Perception for Design (3rd edition) by Colin Ware (2013) - contemporary perception reference"
- **[tangential]** The source is a citeable, fixed-edition scholarly book: Tamara Munzner, Visualization Analysis and Design, A K Peters Visualization Series, CRC Press, 2014.
  > "A K Peters Visualization Series, CRC Press, 2014"

#### Source `aa8a235a` — quality: **primary** · published: 1996
- **[central]** For categorical/segmentation color coding where data marks must be found rapidly and accurately (preattentive visual search), only about 5 to 7 distinct colors can be reliably identified with a low error rate; Healey tested displays with 3, 5, 7, and 9 colors and found rapid low-error identification breaks down as the count rises toward 7-9. This directly bounds how many categorical colors to use for a small number of data series (~3-6 is safely within the reliable range).
  > "Healey (1996) found that only 5 to 7 colours can be found rapidly and with a low error rate... studies using 3, 5, 7, and 9 colors found rapid identification for 3 and 5 colors but mixed results for 7 and 9 colors"
- **[central]** Effective categorical color selection is not a matter of taste but is governed by three measurable perceptual effects that must be jointly controlled: color distance (separation in a perceptually-balanced color space), linear separation (how easily a target color is linearly separable from the background colors in color space), and color category (named-hue membership). Choosing categorical palettes by maximizing perceptual distance alone is insufficient.
  > "three separate effects need to be considered during colour selection: colour distance, linear separation, and colour category. The paper describes a simple method for measuring and controlling all of these effects."
- **[supporting]** The recommendations are empirically validated, not opinion: the color-selection method was tested in controlled target-identification experiments with 38 human observers searching for a color target among differently colored background elements, and the selected color set gave good differentiation between data elements.
  > "tested by performing a set of target identification studies analyzing the ability of thirty-eight observers to find a colour target in displays that contained differently coloured background elements"
- **[supporting]** When the seven chosen colors were spaced at constant perceptual neighbor distance and linear separation (reported as 59.4 and 24.6 delta-E units respectively, with green given a larger separation), search performance was strong: mean response error was only 5.2% and response-time curves were nearly flat across all seven colors, demonstrating that controlling distance and linear separation yields a usable ~7-color categorical set.
  > "constant neighbour distance d and linear separation 1 (59.4 ... and 24.6 ... respectively) ... Mean response error was 5.2%. Response time graphs for all seven colours are much flatter"

#### Source `aab822a6` — quality: **primary** · published: 2022
- **[supporting]** Automated accessibility checkers detect only about half of accessibility errors in data systems, so accessible visualization evaluation requires manual heuristic review; Chartability is a set of synthesized heuristics (from WCAG, research, and communities of practice) for auditing data visualizations across visual, motor, vestibular, neurological, and cognitive accessibility.
  > "Chartability, a set of heuristics synthesized from these various sources which enables designers, developers, researchers, and auditors to evaluate data-driven visualizations and interfaces for visual, motor, vestibular, neurological, and cognitive accessibility."
- **[central]** Color vision deficiency has been the single most prominent accessibility topic in the visualization research literature since the 1990s, while non-vision disabilities (motor, cognitive, vestibular) have been comparatively neglected — meaning CVD-safe color design is well-studied but not the whole of accessibility.
  > "Since the 1990s, the most prominent and active accessibility topic in visualization has been color vision deficiency."
- **[central]** Color/text contrast is the most commonly failed accessibility criterion: in the Chartability user study, 7 of 8 tests (87.5%) involving the contrast heuristic failed, consistent with the web-wide finding that ~83.9% of the top one million websites fail contrast testing.
  > "87.5% of tests (7 out of 8) from our user study involving this heuristic failed"
- **[supporting]** There is no shared, cross-platform standard governing data-visualization accessibility; general web standards like WCAG do not fully cover charts, which is why Chartability extends WCAG's four POUR principles (Perceivable, Operable, Understandable, Robust) with three visualization-specific principles — Compromising, Assistive, Flexible (POUR+CAF).
  > "there are no common shared guidelines across environments, platforms, and contexts in which data visualizations are authored"
- **[tangential]** Applying a structured heuristic checklist measurably improves auditing: novice practitioners reported higher confidence and found auditing easier after using Chartability, and expert accessibility practitioners chose to adopt it into their own workflows.
  > "were more confident and found auditing to be easier after using Chartability"

#### Source `aabc3202` — quality: **primary** · published: Updated 01 June 2026
- **[central]** WCAG 2.1 Success Criterion 1.4.11 requires non-text content (UI components and graphical objects) to have a contrast ratio of at least 3:1 against adjacent colors.
  > "The visual presentation of the following have a contrast ratio of at least 3:1 against adjacent color(s):"
- **[central]** The 'graphical object' requirement applies to the important parts of a complex diagram, explicitly including each line in a graph.
  > "The term 'graphical object' applies to stand-alone icons such as a print icon (with no text), and the important parts of a more complex diagram such as each line in a graph."
- **[central]** Individual data marks/lines in a chart must meet 3:1 contrast against their background, but do not need to contrast with each other.
  > "Chart lines "should have 3:1 contrast against their background" though they "do not need to contrast with each other.""
- **[supporting]** Reference/gridlines that convey information (e.g., graduated value increments) must be discernible, falling under the same contrast requirement needed to understand the graph.
  > "To perceive the values of each line along the chart you need to discern the grey lines marking the graduated 100 value increments."
- **[supporting]** When comparing computed contrast to the 3:1 threshold, values must not be rounded — e.g., 2.999:1 does not pass.
  > "When comparing the computed contrast ratio to the success criterion ratio, the computed values should not be rounded (e.g. 2.999:1 would not meet the 3:1 threshold)."

#### Source `ab993281` — quality: **secondary** · published: N/A (Wikipedia article, continuously updated; Tufte's major books published 1982, 1990, 1997, 2006)
- **[supporting]** Edward Tufte is professor emeritus of political science, statistics, and computer science at Yale University, establishing him as an authoritative figure in data visualization rather than the source of peer-reviewed experimental evidence.
  > "Professor emeritus of political science, statistics, and computer science at Yale University"
- **[central]** Tufte coined the term 'chartjunk' to denote decorative elements that add no information or obscure the data, a key concept for evaluating whether chart elements (gridlines, 3D effects, ornamentation) should be removed from analytical charts.
  > "coined the word chartjunk to refer to useless, non-informative, or information-obscuring elements of quantitative information displays"
- **[central]** Tufte advocates the 'small multiple' — many series shown across several separate small charts sharing axes — as easier to read than overplotting all series on one pair of axes, directly relevant to displaying 3-6 data series.
  > "the small multiple, a chart with many series shown on a single pair of axes that can often be easier to read when displayed as several separate pairs of axes placed next to each other"
- **[central]** Tufte's framework centers on three named concepts — the lie factor, the data-ink ratio, and data density — which constitute his quantitative-sounding but design-philosophy criteria for graphical integrity and efficiency.
  > "key concepts include what he calls the lie factor, the data-ink ratio, and the data density of a graphic"
- **[central]** Tufte's data-ink principle holds that distorting the data marks (the ink encoding the numbers) for decorative or editorial effect is wrong, the basis for maximizing the proportion of ink devoted to actual data.
  > "it is wrong to distort the data measures—the ink locating values of numbers—in order to make an editorial comment or fit a decorative scheme"

#### Source `abd6e8e5` — quality: **blog** · published: ?
- **[central]** WCAG 3 is developing a new text-contrast model called APCA (from Myndex Research) that incorporates text size, but APCA addresses only text contrast and provides no model for non-text/graphical contrast.
  > "The current new standard in progress (WCAG 3) is developing a new model for evaluating text contrast called APCA by Myndex Research."
- **[central]** As of the writing, there is no good accepted model for evaluating non-text contrast for graphical elements such as buttons and data visualizations.
  > "So what about _non-text contrast_? What about buttons, data visualizations, and other meaningful, graphical elements? We don't have any good models for this yet."
- **[central]** The size (in pixels) of colored marks significantly affects how discriminable two colors are, so a single fixed contrast ratio cannot correctly evaluate marks of different sizes (e.g., 1px lines, 2px stems, 8px circles, 25px bars).
  > "One of the biggest issues for comparing color difference is that the size of the colors significantly affects discriminability."
- **[supporting]** Under current standards, data-visualization chart elements with a contrast ratio below 3:1 fail the non-text contrast requirement, but the author argues this fixed threshold may be inappropriately strict for large, bright marks.
  > "Note that anything below 3:1 fails contrast requirements for chart elements. Wouldn't you argue that some of these thick, bright bars are actually above a minimum level of discriminability?"
- **[supporting]** A future accessibility contrast model should be both easy to use and empirically grounded in the perception of the people whose access it aims to evaluate (rather than a single luminance-ratio formula).
  > "What WCAG 3 and future accessibility standards need is a model that is both easy to use and based off of the people it intends to evaluate access for."

#### Source `abd760c3` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2018-08-22 (page last modified)
- **[central]** Tufte (1983) defines data-ink as the non-erasable core of a graphic and prescribes maximizing the data-ink ratio toward 1.0 by removing non-data-ink — establishing data-ink maximization as a normative design principle (not an empirical finding).
  > "Data-ink is the non-erasable core of a graphic, the non-redundant ink arranged in response to variation in the numbers represented. (Tufte, 1983) ... The goal is to maximize the data-ink ratio toward 1.0 while removing non-data-ink that doesn't convey necessary information."
- **[central]** An empirical study by Inbar, Tractinsky & Meyer (2007) tested Tufte's minimalist principle by asking 87 students to rate preference between a standard bar graph and a minimalist (high data-ink) version of the same data.
  > "They asked 87 students to rate their preference for two different graphs displaying identical information—a standard bar-graph and a minimalist version. (Inbar et al., 2007)"
- **[central]** In that study, most participants did NOT prefer Tufte's minimalist bar-graph design and instead preferred the version with 'chartjunk' — contradicting the claim that maximizing data-ink improves the user experience.
  > "The results showed that the majority students did not like Tufte's minimalist design of bar-graphs—instead they seem to prefer 'chartjunk.' (Inbar et al., 2007)"
- **[supporting]** Aggressively erasing non-data-ink can remove information-bearing elements: stripping the chart's top border deleted an implied reference line and obscured the chart's actual data range — a concrete downside of literal data-ink maximization.
  > "removing the top border of the chart removed an implied 20% line"

#### Source `ac455b09` — quality: **secondary** · published: ?
- **[central]** The Okabe-Ito Color Universal Design palette was created by Masataka Okabe (Jikei Medical School) and Kei Ito (University of Tokyo) and is color-blind friendly (CVD-safe).
  > "Masataka Okabe (Jikei Medical School) and Kei Ito (University of Tokyo) have devised a very nice palette that is color-blind friendly."
- **[central]** The CVD-safe Okabe-Ito categorical palette consists of 8 specific colors, given as hex codes #000000, #E69F00, #56B4E9, #009E73, #F0E442, #0072B2, #D55E00, #CC79A7 (i.e., black plus 7 chromatic colors).
  > "#E69F00 #56B4E9 #009E73 #F0E442 #0072B2 #D55E00 #CC79A7"
- **[supporting]** Each palette color is specified across multiple color models (RGB on 0-255 scale, RGB on 0-1 scale, hexadecimal, and CMYK percentages), making the palette directly usable for both screen and print reproduction.
  > "for quick reference, are the colors as RGB, Hex and CMYK values"
- **[tangential]** Validation of the palette's color-blind-friendly properties is sourced externally to the original Okabe-Ito Color Universal Design resource rather than to this page itself.
  > "color-blind friendly"

#### Source `ac6df645` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010-04-10
- **[central]** In a controlled within-subjects experiment (20 participants), there was no measurable difference in interpretation accuracy between heavily embellished charts (Nigel Holmes 'chartjunk' style, low data-ink) and plain minimalist charts for describing the chart's subject, its categories, or its trend - directly challenging the Tufte data-ink/chartjunk premise that embellishment harms comprehension.
  > "First, we did not find any difference in people's in terpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts, despite the fact that the embellished charts contained a much larger amount of non-data-ink."
- **[supporting]** Embellished (Holmes-style) charts were significantly better recalled than plain charts after a delay of more than ~12 days, indicating that strong visual imagery aids long-term memory of both a chart's topic and its details.
  > "Second, participants were able to remember significantly more about the embellished charts after a gap longer than 12 days, suggesting that the addition of strong visual images does help people to remember both the topic and details of the chart."
- **[supporting]** The specific t-test statistics show interpretation/description accuracy was statistically indistinguishable between embellished and plain charts for subject (t19=0.84, p=.412), categories (t19=1.38, p=.185), and trend (t19=0.23, p=.818); the only description difference favored Holmes charts on a 'value message' (t19=3.37, p=.003).
  > "There were no differences in the scores of chart descriptions for the s ubject of the chart (t 19 =0.84, p=.412), the categories in the charts (t 19 =1.38, p=.185), or the trend of the charts (t 19 =0.23, p=.818), depending on whether or not visual embellishments were present"
- **[tangential]** Participants overwhelmingly preferred Holmes-style embellished charts over plain charts in subjective ratings for both reading and remembering - a preference (opinion-level) finding distinct from the objective accuracy results.
  > "in their subjective ratings of which type of charts they preferred for reading and remembering participants overwhelmingly chose Holmes-style charts over plain charts."
- **[supporting]** Despite the positive memorability result, the authors explicitly decline to recommend embellishment as a general design principle, warning that strong images can intentionally or unintentionally bias the viewer's interpretation of the data - an author opinion, not an empirical finding.
  > "However, we do not advocate this strategy as a general principle, because the use of strong images in charts is contentious  for example, images convey messages that may intentionally or unintentionally bias the viewers interpretation of the data."

#### Source `ad4e5686` — quality: **primary** · published: 2003-06 (June 2003); creationDate D:20030619
- **[central]** ColorBrewer divides color schemes into three types matched to data nature: sequential (for ordered low-to-high data, dominated by lightness steps), diverging (for data with a critical midpoint/break, varying both hue and lightness around an emphasized middle), and qualitative (for nominal/categorical data, relying primarily on hue with near-constant lightness and saturation). This typology is the foundational, widely-adopted framework for matching palette type to data type.
  > "Sequential colour schemes (Figure 3a) imply order and are suited to representing data that ranges from low-to-high values... Lightness steps dominate the look of these schemes, usually with light colours for low data values and dark colours for high values. 'Dark equals more' is a standard cartographic convention. ... Diverging colour schemes (Figure 3b) should be used when a critical data class or break point needs to be emphasized. The break or class in the middle of the sequence is emphasized by a hue and lightness change... Qualitative colour schemes (Figure 3c) rely primarily on differences in hue to create a colour scheme that does not imply order, merely difference in kind... Qualitative schemes work best when hue is varied and saturation and lightness are kept or nearly constant."
- **[central]** There is a practical upper limit on the number of categories/classes that remain reliably distinguishable: ColorBrewer caps schemes at 12 classes, but many sequential schemes stop at 9 because further divisions become perceptually unreliable, and as a rule of thumb cartographers seldom use more than seven classes. This directly bounds how many categorical colors humans can reliably tell apart (relevant to choosing ~3-6 data series).
  > "These sets have been designed to produce attractive colour schemes of similar appearance for maps ranging from 3 to 12 classes. Not all colour schemes can be expanded to 12 classes, and many of the sequential scheme sets, for example, stop at 9 classes because further divisions within the scheme are perceptually unreliable... The maximum number of data classes ColorBrewer supports is 12, although only a few of the colour schemes can be divided into this many steps and remain differentiable. As a general rule of thumb, cartographers seldom use more than seven classes on a choropleth map."
- **[central]** Color-vision-deficiency (CVD) safety varies systematically by scheme type: sequential schemes are all usable by red-green colorblind readers because they encode lightness differences; most diverging hue pairs were chosen to be CVD-safe (except red-yellow-green and full-spectral); but almost all qualitative (categorical) schemes are difficult for colorblind readers. This gives a concrete, evidence-informed rule for designing CVD-safe palettes.
  > "Generally, the hue pairs for diverging schemes were selected to accommodate colour-blind readers, with the exception of red-yellow-green and full spectral schemes. Almost all of the qualitative schemes are difcult for colour-blind readers and all of the sequential schemes are useful to them, because they include visible lightness differences."
- **[supporting]** Simultaneous contrast is a real, documented perceptual error in reading data marks against a colored surround: a medium patch surrounded by dark areas appears lighter than it is, and physically-separated similar light colors surrounded by dark can become indistinguishable. This supports the warning that color marks must be tested in context (and against the actual background), not just in an ordered legend.
  > "A well-known problem with choropleth maps is simultaneous contrast (Brewer, 1997). For example, a single enumeration unit (e.g. county) of medium lightness that is surrounded by dark enumeration units will appear lighter than it actually is... two different light yellows side-by-side would be easily differentiated. When physically separated and surrounded by dark colours, they will likely appear to be the same colour. As a general rule, the more complex the spatial patterns of the maps, the harder it will be to distinguish slightly different colours."
- **[supporting]** ColorBrewer schemes were NOT generated from a perceptually-uniform color space; they were built in CMYK by experience and trial-and-error, informed by (not specified in) perceptual spaces like Munsell, and Lab specs were merely converted afterward. This is an important caveat: ColorBrewer is expert-tuned and convention-grounded rather than algorithmically perceptually-uniform (contrast with later colormaps like Viridis/Cividis that are designed directly in perceptually-uniform space).
  > "The scheme was not designed using perceptual colour specications, but knowledge of the relationships between CMYK colour mixture (cyan, magenta, yellow and black) and perceptually ordered colour spaces were used, such as Munsell (Brewer, 1989), to design the schemes. ... The sets of colour schemes in ColorBrewer were designed using both experience and trial and error."

#### Source `addfea8c` — quality: **primary** · published: 2000 (first edition; Academic Press / Morgan Kaufmann). Archive.org metadata lists 1999.
- **[central]** Preattentive (pop-out) processing operates at roughly <10 ms per item, versus ~40 ms per item or more for non-preattentive features; this defines whether a visual feature can be detected 'at a glance' independent of the number of distractors.
  > "As a rule of thumb, anxhhing that is processed at a rate that is faster than 10 msec per item is considered to be pre-attentive Typical processing rates for non-pre-attentive features are 40 msec per item and more (Triesman and Gormican. 1988)."
- **[central]** The preattentively-processed visual channels fall into four categories — form (orientation, length, width, size, curvature, numerosity, etc.), color (hue, intensity), motion (flicker, direction), and spatial position — while line junctions and parallelism are NOT preattentive.
  > "The features that are pre-attentively processed can be organized into a number of categories based on form, color, motion, and spatial position... Orientation, size, basic shape, convexity, concavity, and an added box around an object are all pre-attentively processed. However, the junction of two lines is not pre-attentively processed; nor is the paial- lelism of pairs of lines"
- **[central]** Only a small number of distinct colors can be used reliably as categorical (nominal) codes — Ware states 'between six and twelve,' and cites Healey (1996) for an estimate of about five to ten.
  > "The color coding of graphical objects belongs in the first category. We can only use between six and twelve color codes reliably. This results partly from contrast effects that can alter the appearance of colors."
- **[central]** The spectrum-approximation (rainbow) pseudocolor sequence is not a perceptual sequence and fails to reveal surface shape — ridges and valleys visible in grayscale appear only as stripes under the spectrum colormap.
  > "where clearly identifiable ridges and valleys are perceived in the gray-scale version, only a set of stripes can be seen in the spectmm-approximation version. Thus, the spectrum pseudocolor sequence is not always a good way to reveal the form of a surface."
- **[supporting]** The chromatic (red-green and yellow-blue) channels have much lower spatial resolution than the luminance channel and are inferior to it in almost every respect; therefore purely chromatic differences should never be used to display shape, motion, or detailed information such as text — adequate luminance contrast is required.
  > "the red-green and yellow-blue chan- nels aie inferior to the luminance channel in almost every respect. The general implications for data display are clear. Purely chromatic differences should never be used for displaying object shape, object motion, or detailed information such as text."

#### Source `af18a580` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010-04-10
- **[central]** Heer & Bostock's Mechanical Turk replication reproduced Cleveland & McGill's accuracy ranking of visual encodings for proportional judgment, and position encoding still significantly outperformed length encoding — confirming the elementary-perceptual-task hierarchy on a crowdsourced sample.
  > "The ranking of types by accuracy is consistent between the two experiments... position encoding still significantly outperformed length encoding."
- **[central]** For encoding quantitative magnitude, area is among the least accurate channels: the study's new angle/area judgment types confirmed the psychophysical prediction that area performs worse than angle, and both perform significantly worse than position. This directly bears on using dot AREA (bubble/scatter) to encode magnitude.
  > "the new results match expectations: psychophysical theory predicts area to perform worse than angle, and both to be significantly worse than position."
- **[supporting]** In rectangular-area (treemap/cartogram) judgments, aspect ratio significantly affects accuracy and square (1:1) rectangles produced the WORST performance — viewers appear to use 1D side-length as a proxy for area, which is maximally misleading for squares.
  > "comparisons of rectangles with aspect ratio 1 exhibited the worst performance... consistent with the hypothesis that viewers use 1D length comparisons to help estimate area."
- **[tangential]** There was no significant difference between plain-rectangle and treemap area judgments, indicating surrounding treemap layout elements do not by themselves degrade area-comparison accuracy.
  > "We found no significant difference between the rectangle (T8) and treemap (T9) conditions, suggesting that other elements in a treemap display do not interfere with judgment accuracy."
- **[supporting]** The paper validates crowdsourced graphical-perception experiments as a viable, low-cost replication method: results matched prior lab studies (Cleveland & McGill spatial encoding; Stone & Bartram gridline luminance) and theoretical predictions.
  > "Our new results match previous work, are consistent with theoretical predictions, and suggest that crowdsourcing is viable for testing graphical perception."

#### Source `af3f71fe` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2020-07-19
- **[central]** In a controlled study (Piepenbrock et al., 2013), light mode (positive contrast polarity) outperformed dark mode on both visual-acuity and proofreading tasks across age groups, and the advantage grew as font size shrank.
  > "light mode won across all dimensions... the smaller the font, the better it is for users to see the text in light mode"
- **[central]** The performance advantage of light mode for normal-vision users is explained by pupil contraction: more ambient light constricts the pupil, reducing spherical aberration and increasing depth of field for sharper focus.
  > "with positive contrast polarity, there is more overall light and so the pupil contracts more. As a result, there are fewer spherical aberrations, greater depth of field, and overall better ability to focus on details"
- **[supporting]** For users with cloudy ocular media (e.g., cataracts), the polarity effect reverses — dark mode produced better reading rates — while users with impaired central vision showed no polarity effect.
  > "participants with cloudy ocular media had better reading rates with dark modes"
- **[supporting]** Dark mode may carry a long-term cost: a study (Aleman et al., 2018) found significant retinal/choroidal membrane thinning when reading in light mode and thickening in dark mode, a pattern associated with myopia risk.
  > "significant thinning of this membrane when participants read text presented in light mode and significant thickening when reading text presented in dark mode"
- **[supporting]** The light-mode advantage is environment-dependent: in glanceable-reading tests (Dobres et al., 2017) light mode beat dark mode at nighttime, but there was no significant polarity effect during daytime/bright ambient light.
  > "during daytime, there was no significant effect of contrast polarity, but during nighttime, light mode led to better performance than dark mode"

#### Source `afe93546` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2026-06-07 (last updated)
- **[central]** The term "chartjunk" was coined by Edward Tufte in his 1983 book The Visual Display of Quantitative Information, defined as all visual elements in charts that are not necessary to comprehend the information or that distract the viewer from it. This is the foundational concept underlying the data-ink ratio framework.
  > "Chartjunk consists of all visual elements in charts and graphs that are not necessary to comprehend the information represented on the graph, or that distract the viewer from this information."
- **[central]** Empirical research (Bateman et al. 2010, "Useful junk?", and Borkin et al. 2013) found that chartjunk/embellishment can INCREASE long-term memorability of a chart, directly contradicting Tufte's opinion-based prescription to minimize non-data ink. This is a key example of evidence-vs-opinion in the design literature.
  > "chartjunk increases long-term memorability of the chart"
- **[supporting]** Chartjunk is not monolithic: Robert Kosara distinguishes harmful embellishments (e.g., a busy background) from harmless ones (e.g., borders or pictures) and helpful ones (e.g., annotations), meaning blanket "remove all chartjunk" rules are not empirically supported.
  > "not all chartjunk are the same, some are harmful (e.g. a busy background), others harmless (e.g. nice borders or pictures), and some even helpful (e.g. annotations)"
- **[supporting]** Chartjunk in the form of semantically meaningful icons increased the accessibility of charts for people with Intellectual and Developmental Disabilities (Wu et al. 2021), showing decorative elements can aid specific audiences rather than uniformly degrade comprehension.
  > "chartjunk, in the form of semantically meaningful icons, increased accessibility of charts for people with Intellectual and Developmental Disabilities (IDD)"
- **[tangential]** Tufte's original definition of chartjunk has been criticized as imprecise/over-broad by practitioners, undermining its use as a rigorous design rule.
  > "Tufte's original definition of chartjunk was 'too loose'"

### Pass 2 — colour harmony / preference / emotion (2026-06-26)

#### Source `a171ec18` — quality: **primary** · published: 2018
- **[central]** The models were built on psychophysical data from 12 world regions (Argentina, China, France, Germany, Hungary, Iran, Japan, Spain, Sweden, Taiwan, Thailand, UK), making this a genuinely cross-cultural color-emotion/harmony dataset rather than a single-population study.
  > "A new set of quantitative models of colour emotion and colour harmony were developed using psychophysical data collected from 12 regions in the world, including Argentina, China, France, Germany, Hungary, Iran, Japan, Spain, Sweden, Taiwan, Thailand, and the UK."
- **[central]** Three color-emotion dimensions can be predicted from color coordinates with strong cross-regional correlation: warm/cool r=0.78, heavy/light r=0.80, active/passive r=0.81 — these are the dimensions with robust support, not arbitrary emotion labels.
  > "For colour emotion, three new models were derived, showing satisfactory predictive performance in terms of an average correlation coefficient of 0.78 for "warm/cool", 0.80 for "heavy/light" and 0.81 for "active/passive"."
- **[central]** A quantitative two-color harmony model achieves only moderate predictive accuracy (average r=0.72), lower than the color-emotion models — harmony is partially computable but less so than warm/cool or heavy/light judgments.
  > "The new colour harmony model had satisfactory predictive performance, with an average correlation coefficient of 0.72."
- **[supporting]** Classic harmony principles (hue similarity, chroma similarity, lightness difference, high lightness) are only partially endorsed and that agreement holds within a region rather than universally — directly qualifying the paper's own 'universal' framing and cautioning against treating harmony rules as culture-free laws.
  > "Principal component analysis shows that the common colour harmony principles, including hue similarity, chroma similarity, lightness difference and high lightness principles, were partly agreed by observers of the same region."

#### Source `a20c78ec` — quality: **primary** · published: 2005
- **[central]** What guides bottom-up (stimulus-driven) attention is feature CONTRAST against the local surround, not the absolute strength of a feature. (For UI/dataviz: an element pops because it differs from its neighbors, so a saturated/intense color is not inherently attention-grabbing if its surround matches it.)
  > "Third and most importantly, what appears to matter in guiding bottom-up attention is feature contrast, not local absolute feature strength. Indeed, not only are most early visual neurons tuned to some type of local spatial contrast (such as center-surround or oriented edges), but neuronal responses are also strongly modulated by context"
- **[central]** Single-feature (pop-out) search is fast and effectively parallel — reaction time is roughly flat as the number of distractors grows — whereas conjunction search (target defined by TWO features at once) is slow and scales with set size. The saliency model reproduces these human search slopes. (Implies: make a critical element distinguishable by ONE channel for preattentive pop-out; requiring a conjunction defeats it.)
  > "search is easy and slopes are at in the pop-out cases (when the target diers from the distractors in one feature dimension, e.g., color), while search is more dicult and search time increases with the number of items in the display when the target diers from the distractors by a conjunction of two features"
- **[central]** Attention is deployed to scene locations in order of decreasing saliency: the most salient location is selected first, then a winner-take-all plus inhibition-of-return moves the focus to the next most salient. (This is the mechanistic basis for 'guiding the eye' by saliency in an interface.)
  > "The saliency map receives inputs from early visual processing, and provides an ecient control strategy by which the focus of attention simply scans the saliency map in order of decreasing saliency."
- **[supporting]** The bottom-up saliency model derives salience from three early feature channels — intensity, color, and orientation — computed by center-surround differences across multiple spatial scales (Itti, Koch & Niebur 1998: 8 spatial scales, 6 center-surround scale pairs, colors R/G/B/Y and orientations 0/45/90/135 degrees, totaling 42 feature maps), which are then combined into per-feature 'conspicuity' maps and one saliency map.
  > "Visual features are computed using linear ltering at eight spatial scales, followed by center-surround dierences among six pairs of scales, which compute local spatial contrast in each feature dimension, for a total of 42 maps. ... After competition, feature maps are combined into a single "conspicuity map" for each feature type. The seven conspicuity maps then are summed into the unique topographic saliency map."
- **[supporting]** At a given location there is little evidence for strong interaction between different visual feature modalities (e.g., color and orientation); they contribute largely independently to salience, and humans cannot efficiently search for conjunctive targets. (Supports treating separate visual channels as roughly additive when designing distinguishable alert/encoding levels.)
  > "at a given visual location, there is little evidence for strong interactions across dierent visual modalities, such as color and orientation (Nothdurft, 2000). This is not too surprising from a computational standpoint, as one would otherwise expect these interactions to also be trainable and modulable top-down, resulting in the ability to learn to eciently detect conjunctive targets, which we lack"

#### Source `a23381d6` — quality: **primary** · published: 2020 (Collabra: Psychology, vol. 6, issue 1, article 3; Semantic Scholar lists 2019 as online-first year). DOI 10.1525/collabra.277
- **[central]** Four pre-registered experiments attempting to replicate the 'semantic red effect' (the claim that merely processing the word 'red' impairs intellectual/cognitive test performance) failed to find the effect, providing strong disconfirming evidence against it.
  > "Together, these results do not corroborate the assumption that processing the word red impairs intellectual performance."
- **[central]** The original effect tested was Lichtenfeld, Maier, Elliot & Pekrun (2009), and Experiments 1 and 2 were direct replications of it; both produced near-zero effect sizes on verbal reasoning (Cohen's d = 0.04 and -0.23, the latter in the wrong direction), with samples of N=69 and N=104.
  > "Experiments 1 and 2 (Ns = 69 and 104) are direct replications of Lichtenfeld, Maier, Elliot, and Pekrun (2009). Both experiments failed to uncover a red color effect on verbal reasoning scores among high school students and undergraduates (Cohen’s d = 0.04 and –0.23)."
- **[supporting]** Experiments 3 and 4 also found no effect of processing 'red' on general-knowledge scores (Cohen's d = 0.19 and 0.01); Experiment 4 was well-powered with N=1,149 and still returned a near-zero effect (d=0.01), making a true large effect implausible.
  > "Experiments 3 and 4 (N = 103 and 1,149) failed to identify an effect of processing red on general knowledge test scores (Cohen’sd = 0.19) and 0.01) among undergraduates and adults."
- **[supporting]** The paper frames the broader (now-contested) color-psychology premise it is testing: that colors, and even color words, can impair intellectual performance in achievement situations.
  > "Colors convey meaning and can impair intellectual performance in achievement situations. Even the processing of color words can exert similar detrimental effects."

#### Source `a293768b` — quality: **primary** · published: 2012
- **[supporting]** Healey & Enns (2012) is a peer-reviewed survey in IEEE TVCG that consolidates the attention/visual-perception literature specifically for visualization — making it a foundational secondary reference for preattentive pop-out and attention-guidance design rules (Section 7 of the research question).
  > "This paper surveys research on attention and visual perception, with a specific focus on results that have direct relevance to visualization and visual analytics."
- **[supporting]** The paper frames a layered model in which low-level visual perception (the level at which preattentive features operate) is the empirical foundation for higher-level visual memory and attention — i.e., feature-level encoding underlies attentional guidance.
  > "We discuss theories of low-level visual perception, then show how these findings form a foundation for more recent work on visual memory and visual attention."
- **[central]** Perceptual encoding choices in an image measurably change task performance, providing the evidentiary basis for treating color/attention design as a performance (not aesthetic) variable.
  > "How we "see" details in an image can directly impact a viewer's efficiency and effectiveness."

#### Source `a3dabb1a` — quality: **primary** · published: 2017 (Social Psychology, Vol. 48, No. 3, pp. 174-183; OSF project j3fyq, registered replication)
- **[central]** Two pre-registered replications of the romantic-red effect (Elliot & Niesta 2008; Elliot et al. 2010) failed to replicate: red had little-to-no effect on perceived attractiveness or desired sexual behavior in either direction.
  > "In both cases, we are sad to report that there was little-to-no effect of red on perceived attractiveness or desired sexual behavior."
- **[central]** For men rating women, the red effect was effectively null and far smaller than the original claims: d = 0.09 with a 95% CI spanning zero (N = 242), in the predicted direction but not significant.
  > "d = 0.09, 95% CI [−0.17, 0.34], N = 242"
- **[central]** For women rating men, the red effect ran in the OPPOSITE direction to the original hypothesis and was null: d = −0.09, 95% CI [−0.30, 0.12], N = 360.
  > "d = −0.09, 95% CI [−0.30, 0.12], N = 360"
- **[central]** Despite using original materials, pre-registration, a positive control, and adequately powered samples, the authors concluded there is no strong evidence that incidental red color increases perceived attractiveness.
  > "We found no strong evidence that incidental red influences perceived attractiveness."
- **[supporting]** The failed replication fits a wider accumulating pattern of null results, indicating the original romantic-red literature does not hold up under rigorous replication.
  > "there is a growing number of studies reporting little-to-no effect of red manipulations on attraction"

#### Source `a3e4ca3a` — quality: **primary** · published: 2018-10
- **[central]** Meta-analyzed across 45 contrasts and 2,961 male participants, the effect of incidental red on men's ratings of women's attractiveness is small (d = 0.26 [0.12, 0.40]), dropping to d = 0.19 [0.09, 0.29] when an outlier study is removed.
  > "d = 0.26 [0.12, 0.40], p = .0004, N = 2,961 ... Without the outlier study: d = 0.19 [0.09, 0.29], p = .0004"
- **[central]** The red-attractiveness effect for women rating men is even smaller (d = 0.13 [0.01, 0.25], N = 2,739), and preregistered studies found essentially no effect or a slight reversal (d = -0.10 [-0.24, 0.04]) versus d = 0.21 in unregistered studies.
  > "Preregistered studies obtained "d = −0.10 [−0.24, 0.04]" versus unregistered "d = 0.21 [0.08, 0.34]""
- **[central]** There is statistically significant evidence of publication bias: effect size correlated with standard error (smaller studies reported larger effects), r = .52 [.23, .72], p = .001 for women rating men.
  > "Bias (women): "statistically significant relationship" between effect size and standard error: "r = .52 [.23, .72], p = .001""
- **[central]** Two of the three authors (first and third) concluded the true effect of incidental red on attraction is very small or potentially nonexistent, while the second author argued it is real but context-moderated, signaling the effect is not robustly established.
  > "the true effect of incidental red on attraction is very small, potentially nonexistent"
- **[supporting]** Heterogeneity across the red-attractiveness studies was very high for men rating women (I-squared = 89% [82, 94], or 66% with the outlier removed) and moderate for women (I-squared = 53%), indicating the effect is inconsistent across studies.
  > "Heterogeneity (men): "I2 = 89% [82, 94]" and with outlier removed: "I2 = 66%""

#### Source `a408e3f6` — quality: **primary** · published: 2025-11-06
- **[central]** Color-pairing preferences are highly hue-dependent, which empirically refutes the 'hue-independence' assumption underlying classical harmony rules (the idea that preference depends only on the angular distance between two hues, not their absolute positions).
  > "Our results reveal that preferences are highly hue dependent, challenging the assumption of universal harmony rules proposed in the literature... Our findings challenge this hue-independence assumption to a large extent... the assumption of hue independence cannot be reasonably upheld."
- **[central]** When preferences are averaged across all reference hues, there is a robust preference for the contrast region (hue separations of 160-220 degrees, i.e. near-complementary ~180 degrees), but the 'similarity' region near the reference hue that Moon & Spencer (1944) proposed is NOT supported -- so contrast is favored while the classical similarity rule fails.
  > "Higher preferences are observed in the contrast region (between 160○and 220○), flanked by regions of lower preference at smaller angular distances. Such global preference for contrast aligns rather well with the universal harmony model proposed by Moon and Spencer[22]... However, the other preference regions they proposed—coined similarity and located near the reference color—are not consistently supported by our hue-averaged data."
- **[central]** Preferred color pairings statistically match hue distributions in natural landscape images (peaks at blue and orange-yellow, minima at greens and purples), and dominant hues in natural scenes are most often separated by ~180 degrees -- evidence that preference tracks ecological exposure rather than an abstract harmony geometry. Replicated across two image datasets (12,000 and 4,319 images).
  > "the peaks and valleys of the distribution match remarkably well that of the Combinability index and absolute preferences (maximum for blue and orange-yellow tints, minimum for greens and purples)... dominant hues in natural scenes are most often separated by approximately 180°... we also measured the hue distribution using an independent dataset of 4,319 landscape images; the results were found to be virtually identical."
- **[supporting]** A color's 'combinability' (how harmoniously it pairs with all other hues, summed across pairs) correlates closely with that color's standalone aesthetic preference as measured independently by the Berkeley Color Project (Palmer & Schloss 2010); blue and yellow rank highest, red/green/purple lowest. Based on a 346-respondent controlled survey using fixed-S/L (S=0.8, L=0.5) HSL checkerboard pairs of 13 hues.
  > "individual hue preferences closely mirror the Combinability index, suggesting that a color’s ability to form harmonious combinations aligns with its overall aesthetic appeal... We collected 346 responses from colleagues at École Polytechnique and CFM (France), OIST (Japan), as well as volunteers across Europe."
- **[supporting]** The authors acknowledge HSL is perceptually non-uniform and device-dependent, limiting fidelity to human perception, and recommend perceptually uniform spaces (CIELAB, OKLab) for future work -- supporting the broader claim that perceptual spaces are preferable to HSL/RGB for color comparison.
  > "our use of the HSL color space... deserves further discussion. HSL suffers from perceptual non-uniformity and device dependence, limiting its fidelity in capturing human color perception. Future research could explore alternative, perceptually uniform color spaces such as CIELAB and OKLAB."

#### Source `a58320d7` — quality: **primary** · published: 1995
- **[central]** Chromostereopsis (the color-depth illusion where saturated red and blue at the same physical distance appear at different depths) is fully explained by the eye's transverse chromatic aberration (TCA) interacting with pupil position, with no additional neural mechanism required.
  > "These seemingly paradoxical results could nevertheless be explained by a simple model of optical TCA without the need to postulate additional factors or mechanisms."
- **[supporting]** The direction of the perceived color-depth effect reverses (red can appear nearer OR farther than blue) depending on the background: targets on a black background versus a background matching the targets' spectral composition produce opposite-signed depth.
  > "In all cases, chromatic diplopia and chromostereopsis were found experimentally to reverse in sign with this change in background."
- **[supporting]** The same colored target placed on different background colors within one display is perceived at different depth planes, so chromostereopsis is context-dependent rather than a fixed property of the color.
  > "A given coloured target could be located in different depth planes within the same display when located on different background colours."
- **[tangential]** The magnitude and sign of chromostereopsis depend on pupil aperture position relative to the optical axis, manipulated experimentally with 1.78 mm artificial pupils placed nasally versus temporally.
  > "red, blue, and green stimuli viewed monocularly and binocularly through 1.78 mm artificial pupils positioned nasally and temporally to induce variable TCA"

#### Source `a5a233d7` — quality: **primary** · published: 2011
- **[central]** Both preference for a color pair and its perceived harmony increase as the hue similarity between the figure and ground colors increases (peaking when hues match and declining monotonically with hue difference); there is no support for art-theory 'harmony rules' that strong hue contrasts (e.g. complementary) are preferred or harmonious.
  > "pair preference and harmony both increase as hue similarity increases ... No evidence favoring art theoretic claims that color combinations with strong hue contrasts are either preferred or harmonious"
- **[central]** Perceived harmony and aesthetic preference for color pairs are strongly correlated but distinct constructs: harmony ratings correlate with preference at r = +.79 and alone explain 62.3% of preference variance, while a combined model (harmony + component color preferences + lightness contrast) explains 80.8%.
  > "Correlation: r = +.79 between preference and harmony ratings ... Harmony alone explained 62.3% of variance in preference ratings ... Combined model (harmony + component preferences + lightness): 80.8% variance explained"
- **[supporting]** Preference depends on the components/lightness in a way harmony does not: component (single) color preferences explained 21.7% of pair-preference variance but only 1.4% of harmony variance, and preferred pairs contained more dark/saturated colors whereas harmonious pairs were generally lighter.
  > "Component color preferences explained 21.7% of pair preference variance but only 1.4% of harmony variance ... Preferred pairs contained more dark/saturated colors; harmonious pairs were generally lighter"
- **[supporting]** Harmony and similarity are highly correlated (r = +.83) but diverge on lightness contrast: harmony increases with lightness contrast while perceived similarity decreases with it.
  > "Correlation: r = +.83 between similarity and harmony ... Key difference: Harmony increased with lightness contrast; similarity decreased with it"
- **[supporting]** Figure-ground assignment produces small but reliable preference asymmetries: warmer figures on cooler grounds are preferred (r = +.13, p < .001) and lighter figures on darker grounds are preferred (r = +.14, p < .001), effects small relative to overall color differences.
  > "Warmer figures on cooler grounds preferred (r = +.13, p < .001) ... Lighter figures on darker grounds preferred (r = +.14, p < .001) ... Effects were small compared to color differences"

#### Source `a5b9c02f` — quality: **primary** · published: 2006 (ACM SIGGRAPH 2006; ACM Transactions on Graphics 25(3):624-630)
- **[central]** The paper defines color harmony by RELATIVE hue relationships (rotatable templates on the hue wheel), not by specific colors — directly supporting the research question's premise that perceptual/relational color spaces, not absolute RGB values, govern harmony. Their 8 templates (i, V, L, I, T, Y, X, N) are angular hue sectors that 'may be rotated by an arbitrary angle.'
  > "Harmony among colors is not determined by specific colors, but rather by their relative position in color space. ... We refer to these distributions as templates, since they define the radial relationships on the color wheel, rather than specific colors (meaning that any template may be rotated by an arbitrary angle)."
- **[central]** The paper EXPLICITLY concedes there is no formal or empirically validated definition of a harmonic color set; the harmony rules rest on artist consensus, not a proven preference formula. This is the key 'math vs taste' admission: the templates the algorithm optimizes are aesthetic convention, not a controlled-study result.
  > "Generating harmonic colors has been an open problem among artists and scientists [Holtzschue 2002]. ... Although currently there is no formulation that defines a harmonic set, there is a consensus among artists that defines when a set is harmonic, and there are some forms, schemes and relations in color space that describe a harmony of colors [Matsuda 1995; Tokumaru et al. 2002]. Professional artists usually rely on experience and intuition to choose their favorite harmonic colors."
- **[supporting]** The harmonic templates are Matsuda's (1995) 80 color schemes, which descend from Itten's color-wheel harmony theory; the only claimed empirical grounding is offloaded to Matsuda's 'extensive psychophysical research' — a secondhand, Japanese-language citation the paper does not independently validate or quantify. No effect sizes or preference experiments appear in this work.
  > "Based on Itten's schemes and extensive psychophysical research, Matsuda [1995] introduced a set of 80 color schemes, defined by combining several types of hue and tone distributions. ...
