The notion of color harmony in this work is based on the schemes developed by Matsuda [Matsuda 1995; Tokumaru et al. 2002], which descend from Itten's notions of harmony [Itten 1960], widely accepted in applicable fields involving colors."
- **[supporting]** The harmonization algorithm operates in HSV on the hue channel only, scoring harmony F as the saturation-weighted sum of arc-length (radian) hue distances from each pixel to the nearest template sector; the best scheme minimizes F over all templates and rotations. This is a concrete 'harmony by calculation' procedure operating purely on hue geometry.
  > "Figure 2 illustrates the eight harmonic types defined over the hue channel of the HSV color wheel. ... we define a function F(X,(m,a)) which measures the harmony of an image X with respect to the scheme (m,a): F(X,(m,a)) = sum over p in X of ||H(p) - E_Tm(a)(p)|| * S(p) ... where H and S denote the hue and the saturation channels ... the hue distance ||.|| refers to the arc-length distance on the hue wheel (measured in radians). ... Color harmony is mainly affected by the hue channel."
- **[tangential]** The method only alters hue and cannot change low-saturation colors; the authors acknowledge that shifting hues can itself alter apparent color contrast because 'human perception may react differently to varied color combinations' — an explicit admission that hue-template harmony does not fully control perceptual outcome.
  > "It should be noted that our technique cannot change colors with low saturation since it only alters the hue channel. ... when changing hues, the apparent color contrast may change since human perception may react differently to varied color combinations."

#### Source `a6139dea` — quality: **primary** · published: 2010-05-11
- **[central]** The Ecological Valence Theory holds that color preferences arise from people's average affective responses to the objects characteristically associated with each color (learned, ontogenetic mechanism), not from innate hue preference per se.
  > "color preferences arise from people's average affective responses to color-associated objects"
- **[central]** A Weighted Affective Valence Estimate (WAVE), computed from color-object associations, object valence ratings, and color-object matching strength with no fitted free parameters, predicts adult color preferences at r = +0.893, accounting for ~80% of the variance.
  > "The WAVE achieved a correlation of r = +0.893 with color preferences, "accounting for 80% of the variance with a single predictor." Notably, "no free parameters were estimated in calculating the WAVE.""
- **[central]** Preference peaks at blue/cyan (associated with clear skies and clean water) and troughs at chartreuse and dark-yellow/dark-orange (brown) hues (associated with feces and rotting food) — i.e. preference tracks the valence of associated objects rather than a harmony rule.
  > "A "narrow trough at chartreuse" and pronounced aversion to "dark yellow" and "dark orange (brown)," linked to objects like "feces and rotting food.""
- **[supporting]** More saturated colors are reliably preferred over less saturated colors of matched hue, a statistically significant main effect.
  > "The s colors were preferred to the l and m colors [F(1,47) = 9.20, P < 0.01]"
- **[supporting]** The ecological/object-association (WAVE) model substantially outperforms alternative predictors of color preference: cone-contrast explained 37%, color-appearance 60%, and color-emotion 55% of variance, versus 80% for the WAVE.
  > "Cone-contrast model: 37% variance explained; Color-appearance model: 60% variance; Color-emotion model: 55% variance"

#### Source `a8663b2d` — quality: **blog** · published: 2022-11-14
- **[central]** Color-emotion associations are not universal: in China, red is linked to happiness and fortune rather than the romance/passion associations common in Western branding — supporting cross-cultural contingency of color meaning.
  > "in China, red is associated with happiness and fortune—not romance or passion"
- **[central]** A 2018 meta-analysis found red increases heterosexual men's attraction to women only to a small degree, and had no effect on women's attraction — an example of a small, sex-specific red effect rather than a universal color-emotion law.
  > "Red increases heterosexual attraction "to a small degree" for men, but had "no effect on women""
- **[central]** Color preference/effect in branding depends on perceived appropriateness for the product category: blue was judged more appropriate than red for functional products (e.g. a bank), while red was judged more appropriate than blue for sensory-social products.
  > "blue was deemed more appropriate than red for functional products like a bank, while red was considered more appropriate than blue for sensory-social products"
- **[supporting]** There is a kernel of real science behind color-emotion claims; the problem is oversimplified 1:1 universal mappings (red=passion, blue=calm), not total fabrication.
  > "There are _some_ connections between color and emotion"
- **[tangential]** A controlled study found exercising in green rooms produced lower heart rates than exercising in red rooms, an example of a measurable physiological color effect.
  > "Exercise in green rooms produces lower heart rates than in red rooms"

#### Source `a8c018a9` — quality: **primary** · published: 2022-02-17
- **[central]** Material Design's HCT color space stands for Hue, Chroma, Tone; its Tone (lightness) axis is identical to CIELAB's L*, while its hue and chroma axes are taken from CAM16 (not Lab, because Lab's hue/chroma were judged too perceptually inconsistent for design use).
  > "Our brand new, perceptually accurate, color system is called HCT, which stands for hue, chroma, tone... HCT's lightness measure, tone, is the same as L*a*b*'s lightness... HCT's hue and colorfulness measures, hue and chroma, are the same as CAM16's hue and chroma... when we tried using it in design, L*a*b* was too inconsistent perceptually."
- **[central]** HSL is not perceptually accurate: at a fixed HSL 'lightness' value of 50, the actual measured (perceptual) lightness of colors ranges from 33 to 96. HSL was designed for fast computation on 1970s hardware, not perceptual accuracy.
  > "At HSL “lightness” 50, accurately measured lightness ranges from 33 to 96!... HSL isn't remotely accurate, and doesn't try to be: it was built to make computing colors fast on 1970s computers."
- **[central]** WCAG contrast requirements can be re-expressed as a minimum HCT tone difference: small elements (<40dp / under 1/4 inch) need a tone difference of 50 from their background, and larger elements need a tone difference of 40, claimed to hold consistently for any color pair.
  > "to meet WCAG contrast requirements, smaller elements (less than ¼” or 40 dp) require a tone difference of 50 with their background, larger elements require a tone difference of 40. This principle works consistently for any pair of colors."
- **[supporting]** A Material 'tonal palette' is defined by a single fixed hue and chroma, with its member colors generated by varying only tone; the 'core palette' that drives a theme is a set of exactly 5 tonal palettes derived from one source color.
  > "Using the source color, we create the core palette, which is a set of 5 tonal palettes. A tonal palette is defined by a hue and chroma; the colors in the palette come from varying tones."
- **[supporting]** Material You's dynamic color is generated by quantizing the wallpaper's colors (via Google's implementation of Celebi's algorithm), then scoring remaining colors by colorfulness and by how much of the image they cover, and filtering out near-monochrome colors, to select a source color whose hue and chroma drive the scheme.
  > "First, the wallpaper is quantized, reducing the thousands of colors in it to a smaller number by merging them in color space... Android 12 gives colors points for colorfulness and how much of the image they represent, and it filters out colors close to monochrome."

#### Source `a8fc7fa8` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2011 (Machine Graphics and Vision, vol. 20, pp. 383-412; preprint dated 2012, uploaded to ResearchGate 2017)
- **[central]** Perceived color difference can be quantified on a concrete, experimentally-grounded Delta-E scale: 0<dE<1 is imperceptible, 1<dE<2 only an experienced observer notices, 2<dE<3.5 even an untrained observer notices, 3.5<dE<5 is a clear difference, and dE>5 reads as two different colors. This gives numeric thresholds for making alert/role colors mutually distinguishable.
  > "When: 0 < ∆E < 1 - observer does not notice the difference, 1 < ∆E < 2 - only experienced observer can notice the difference, 2 < ∆E < 3.5 - unexperienced observer also notices the difference, 3.5 < ∆E < 5 - clear difference in color is noticed, 5 < ∆E - observer notices two different colors. These data represent experimentally veriﬁed statistics."
- **[central]** CIELAB is NOT perceptually uniform; equal Delta-E distances are not seen as equally different, which is why ISO recommends CIEDE2000 over the plain CIELAB Euclidean formula for color-difference assessment. This is the core justification for preferring corrected/perceptual metrics when choosing or comparing colors.
  > "In fact, the CIEL∗a∗b∗space is nonuniform. Therefore, the ISO recommends in place of the formula for ∆ELab use of another formula: ∆E2000 (mathematically expanded) especially for determining the color difference in its assessment of industrial."
- **[central]** Naive RGB Euclidean distance (sqrt(dR^2+dG^2+dB^2)) is a poor predictor of perceived color difference because the eye has unequal sensitivity to R, G and B; even the weighted/brightness-corrected RGB formulas remain 'rough' and unsuitable for colorimetry, printing or paint. This supports the rule that RGB/HSL are wrong spaces for comparing colors and building gradients.
  > "the human eye has a different sensitivity to changes in the light intensity of the R, G, B components, and changes in the intensity of the individual components produce different sensations."
- **[supporting]** A unit step of the CIELAB Euclidean formula corresponds to roughly 2.3 just-noticeable-difference (JND) units per Nickerson, and CIELAB's perceptual uniformity is markedly better than XYZ, CIE 1960 UCS and OSA spaces — yet still 'not optimal'. This both quantifies the dE unit and bounds CIELAB's accuracy.
  > "∆E∗ ab calculated using the formula equals 2.3 JND (Just Noticeable Difference) units coming from D. Nickerson... Uniformity (conformity of the calculated and perceived color difference) achieved by its use is signiﬁcantly better than for the XYZspace, and much better than its predecessors: CIE 1960 UCS and OSA. However, it is not optimal."
- **[supporting]** CIE94 (introduced by the CIE committee in 1995 for industrial small-difference applications) is a weighted Euclidean distance in CIELAB using lightness/chroma/hue weighting terms (SL=1, SC=1+0.045*C, SH=1+0.015*C) and is intended only for small differences with dE<3 — showing the dependence of color-difference metrics on the magnitude regime.
  > "In 1995, the CIE committee introduced a formula for industrial applications with small differences in the ∆ECMC color difference, called CIE94... The above parameters should be used for values of ∆E < 3."

#### Source `a940749f` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2014
- **[central]** Borland & Taylor (2007, IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications) identify three specific, distinct failure modes of the rainbow/jet colormap: it lacks perceptual ordering (confuses viewers), has uncontrolled luminance variation (obscures data), and introduces non-data-dependent gradients (actively misleads). This is the canonical anti-pattern statement for sequential/interval gradients.
  > "The rainbow color map confuses viewers through its lack of perceptual ordering, obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation, and actively misleads interpretation through the introduction of non-data-dependent gradients."
- **[central]** Empirical evidence (Borkin et al. 2011, IEEE TVCG) shows user PREFERENCE diverges from performance: participants preferred the rainbow map (familiarity, saturation, pleasing appearance) yet made FEWER diagnostic errors with a perceptually appropriate colormap on real coronary-artery medical data. Preference is not a valid proxy for effectiveness.
  > "Participants initially preferred the rainbow color map, citing familiarity, more saturated colors, and more pleasing appearance. Remarkably, and in spite of the expressed preference of study participants, the researchers observed that using a "perceptually appropriate color map" resulted in fewer diagnostic mistakes."
- **[central]** A perceptually uniform colormap is defined as one where equal data intervals map to equal perceived color differences across the whole scale, and this uniformity is achieved using a perceptual color space (CIELAB / CIELCh), not RGB — directly supporting the claim that perceptual spaces beat RGB/HSL for building smooth gradients.
  > "A perceptual color map has the property that equal data intervals correspond to the same perceived color difference in all parts of the scale... Perceptual uniformity is usually achieved with the aid of a color space such as L*a*b* or L*ch."
- **[supporting]** Construction rule for good gradients: use monotonic lightness (L*) for perceptual ordering and colorblind accessibility, but do NOT vary lightness alone because of simultaneous contrast — add hue or saturation variation alongside lightness.
  > "Use monotonic lightness (L*) to achieve perceptual ordering and assist color-impaired viewers... Do not use lightness variation alone because of the problem of simultaneous contrast. Use hue or saturation variation in addition to lightness variation."
- **[supporting]** Controlled experiment (Spence et al. 1999, Journal of Experimental Psychology: Applied 5(4):393-412) found perceptually linear colormaps varying in brightness, or brightness+hue+saturation, yielded faster and more accurate value judgments than a hue-only map — empirical support that luminance, not hue, carries quantitative reading and form perception.
  > "Colormaps that were perceptually linear and varied in "only brightness" or "brightness + hue + saturation" resulted in faster, more accurate judgments than a hue-only color map. Hue does confer an advantage if the task involves segregation or classification."

#### Source `a9533274` — quality: **primary** · published: 2021-02-05
- **[central]** Visual search is guided by five combined sources — bottom-up salience, top-down feature guidance, prior-attention history/priming, value/reward, and scene structure/meaning — which sum into a single attentional priority map; attention is deployed to the most active location on that map. For UI/dataviz this means a critical element pops out only if it wins this combined competition, not by color alone.
  > "five types of guidance that combine to create an attentional 'Priority Map.' Bottom-up (salience) and top-down (user/template-driven) guidance by preattentive visual features are retained from all previous versions of GS. Newer data support guidance by the history of prior attention (e.g. priming), value (e.g. rewarded features), and, very importantly, guidance from the structure and meaning of scenes."
- **[central]** Only a limited set of basic features (e.g., color and orientation) are processed preattentively in parallel across the whole visual field and can guide attention; arbitrary real-world objects do not guide search. This bounds which encodings can produce true preattentive pop-out in an interface to the basic visual primitives.
  > "Search for a distinctive real-world object in a varied set of other objects seems to be essentially unguided"
- **[central]** The strength of bottom-up pop-out follows two quantitative rules: guidance is stronger the larger the feature difference between target and distractors (target-distractor difference), and stronger the more similar the distractors are to each other (distractor-distractor similarity). Actionable for UI: make a critical alert maximally different from a homogeneous background to make it pop.
  > "guidance to a target will be stronger when the featural differences between target and distractor are larger (TD differences) and guidance to a target will be stronger when the featural differences amongst distractors are smaller (DD similarity)"
- **[supporting]** Feature guidance (including color) operates on a coarse, categorical representation of the feature rather than a fine one, so attention can be steered by broad feature categories rather than precise values. This implies fine color distinctions guide attention poorly compared to coarse, well-separated color categories.
  > "Guidance is based on a coarse representation of a feature. That coarse representation is not simply the fine representation divided by some constant"
- **[supporting]** Preattentive guidance is not uniform across the visual field — its effectiveness varies with retinal eccentricity as the eyes move, so the same salient feature guides more strongly near fixation than in the periphery.
  > "preattentive processing will occur across large swaths of the visual field at the same time, the results of that processing will vary with eccentricity"

#### Source `a975ca26` — quality: **secondary** · published: Format introduced December 2020 (Ottosson); CSS adoption since December 2021. Wikipedia article continuously revised (no single publish date).
- **[central]** Oklab was introduced by Björn Ottosson in December 2020 as a perceptual color space designed to improve perceptual uniformity, hue/lightness prediction, and color blending.
  > "Introduced by Björn Ottosson in December 2020 ... uniform color space for device-independent color designed to improve perceptual uniformity, hue and lightness prediction, color blending"
- **[central]** Oklab was created specifically to fix unexpected hue and lightness shifts in blue colors that occur in CIELAB, making it a more perceptually faithful space for choosing/comparing colors.
  > "unexpected hue and lightness changes in blue colors present in the CIELAB color space"
- **[central]** Generating a gradient at constant lightness in OKLCh produces perceptually even lightness, whereas constant-value HSV gradients show irregular lightness (light yellows, dark blues) — direct evidence that perceptual-space interpolation yields smoother gradients than RGB/HSV.
  > "Irregular perceptual lightness in a gradient generated with constant value in HSV space, with light yellows and dark blues, compared to one generated with constant lightness in Oklch"
- **[supporting]** Oklab is standardized for the web: it is included in CSS Color Level 4 and Level 5 drafts (since December 2021) and supported by recent major browsers, marking adoption of perceptual-space color interpolation in production CSS.
  > "included in the CSS Color Level 4 and Level 5 drafts for device-independent web colors since December 2021"
- **[supporting]** Oklab's model is fitted to color-appearance datasets — CAM16 data for lightness and chroma, and IPT data for hue — rather than being a naive RGB transform, which underlies its claimed simplification of color schemes and smoother gradients.
  > "Oklab's model is fitted with improved color appearance data: CAM16 data for lightness and chroma, and IPT data for hue."

#### Source `aa333887` — quality: **primary** · published: 2009 (ISVC proceedings; PDF created 2009-11-24)
- **[central]** Piecewise-linear interpolation through white in CIELAB creates an artificial Mach band at the midpoint because luminance sharply transitions from increasing to decreasing; interpolating instead in the Msh color space (a polar form of CIELAB) softens this and yields a smooth luminance change across the whole map. This is the core mechanism for building a perceptually smooth diverging gradient without false midpoint banding.
  > "A piecewise linear interpolation is mostly effective, but can create an artificial Mach band at white where the luminance sharply transitions from increasing to decreasing as demonstrated in Figure 1. ... unlike the piecewise linear interpolation in CIELAB space, this piecewise linear interpolation in Msh space results in a smooth change in luminance throughout the entire color map."
- **[central]** If saturation s is changed linearly while magnitude M and hue h are held constant in Msh space, the rate of perceptual change (the derivative norm of the color path) resolves to the constant Msm, which mathematically satisfies the criterion for a perceptually uniform/linear color map. This is the proof that a good gradient comes from linear motion in a perceptual polar space, not naive RGB interpolation.
  > "we can show that if s changes linearly while M and h are held constant, Equation 4 is constant, which is our criterion for a uniform color map. ... Clearly Equation 7 resolves to a constant and therefore meets our criterion for a "uniform" color space."
- **[central]** The recommended cool-to-warm diverging colormap is defined by three Msh control points - Blue (M=80, s=1.08, h=-1.1), White (M=88, s=0, h=1.061/-1.661), Red (M=80, s=1.08, h=0.5) - is perceptually linear, distinguishable under dichromatic (colorblind) vision, and because its endpoints match the rainbow map it serves as a drop-in replacement.
  > "Applying the design described in Section 4, we can build the cool to warm color map shown in Figure 3. The control points, to be interpolated in Msh space, are given in Table 1. ... the rate of change is perceptually linear, and the colors are still easily distinguished by those with dichromatic vision. ... Because its endpoints match those of the rainbow color map most often currently used, it can be used as a drop-in replacement."
- **[supporting]** Color comparison and interpolation should use a perceptual space (CIELAB) rather than RGB/display space, because Euclidean distance in CIELAB (delta-E) approximates perceived color difference, whereas display nonlinearities interfere with RGB blending and interpolation.
  > "the display may have nonlinearities that interfere with the blending and interpolation of colors ... The Euclidean distance between two points is the approximate perceived difference between the two colors. This Euclidean distance in CIELAB space is known as ∆E and makes a reasonable metric for comparing color differences"
- **[supporting]** The rainbow/spectral colormap is perceptually nonuniform - color change appears faster in the cyan and yellow regions than in blue, green, and red - and this nonuniformity simultaneously introduces visual artifacts and obscures real data; it also has no natural perceived ordering.
  > "perceptual changes in the colors are not uniform. The colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions than in the blue, green, and red regions. These nonuniform perceptual changes simultaneously introduce artifacts and obfuscate real data"

#### Source `ab1d3597` — quality: **primary** · published: 2025-01-13 (online); August 2025 issue, Psychonomic Bulletin & Review
- **[central]** A large, century-spanning evidence base supports systematic, cross-culturally consistent colour-emotion associations: 132 empirical studies (1895-2022), 42,266 participants, 64 countries. This is the strongest meta-level case that colour-emotion links are real rather than noise.
  > "Jonauskaite and Mohr collected 132 peer-reviewed reports of empirical studies on color and emotion. These studies spanned 128 years—from 1895 to 2022. Altogether, the studies had a total of 42,266 participants across 64 countries."
- **[central]** Specific direction-of-effect findings match the dimensional model: red maps to arousing/high-power emotions (love, anger); yellow/orange to positive high-arousal; green/blue/blue-green to positive LOW-arousal (comfort, relaxation) — empirically supporting the 'blue/green = calm' and 'red = arousal' associations.
  > "RED links to positive and negative, arousing, and high-power emotions (e.g., love, anger, passion)... YELLOW/ORANGE link to positive and high arousal emotions... GREEN/BLUE/BLUE-GREEN link to positive and low arousal emotions (e.g., comfort, relaxation, happiness)"
- **[central]** Associations are driven by physical colour properties (lightness, saturation, colour temperature) and are many-to-many, not one-to-one: a single colour conveys multiple emotions. This contradicts pop 'one colour = one emotion' color-psychology claims; lighter/more-saturated colours skew positive/high-arousal/high-power.
  > "While each color can convey multiple emotions – like red signalling both love and anger – these associations follow systematic patterns based on lightness, saturation, and 'color temperature.' ... Saturated colors were linked to positive, high arousal, and high power emotions, while desaturated colors were linked to negative, low arousal, and low power emotions."
- **[supporting]** Key skeptical caveat: the reviewed evidence largely measures abstract colour-emotion ASSOCIATIONS, not felt/lived emotional experience; causal effects of colour on actual emotion remain under-evidenced and need more research. This bounds how strongly applied 'color psychology' claims can lean on this literature.
  > "The study notes these were largely tested at an abstract level as associations rather than direct emotional experiences... more research is needed to understand the causal effects of colours on lived emotional experience"

#### Source `ab35fb8d` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2020-10-05
- **[central]** IBM Carbon's 14-color categorical data-visualization palette is fully WCAG-2.1 1.4.11 (3:1) contrast-accessible against the chart background and maintains an average >2:1 contrast between neighboring colors, achieved by manual curation rather than algorithmic harmony rules.
  > "The categorical palette is fully 3:1 contrast-accessible against background, and has an average of >2:1 contrast between neighboring colors."
- **[supporting]** Algorithmically generated sequential palettes fail accessibility: nearly half the colors do not meet a 3:1 contrast ratio against the background, so contrast must be checked rather than assumed.
  > "nearly half of the colors do not satisfy 3:1 contrast ratio against the background"
- **[supporting]** Carbon restricts chart backgrounds to only the lightest (White) and darkest (Gray 100) theme backgrounds to maximize available foreground contrast.
  > "We restricted chart backgrounds to only the brightest (White) and darkest (Gray 100) of the theme backgrounds"
- **[central]** Color should not be the sole encoding channel; Carbon mandates redundant non-color cues — texture (8 textures), shape (two sets of 8), 3:1-contrast axes and outlines, tooltips, and data tables — for colorblind/accessibility robustness.
  > "Texture is a well-established alternative to color coding areas"
- **[supporting]** Color differentiation should be evaluated with perceptual just-noticeable-difference (JND) tooling (Viz Palette), not contrast ratios alone.
  > "The team used "Viz Palette" to measure color differentiation via "just-noticeable difference (JND)" rather than simple contrast ratios alone."

#### Source `acfe8f5b` — quality: **secondary** · published: ?
- **[central]** Radix Colors bases its text-contrast targets on the APCA algorithm rather than WCAG 2.x, making it a notable real-world design system that adopts APCA for accessibility. This is directly relevant to the research question's item-5 coverage of WCAG contrast and 'accessible text' roles, showing the modern-convention drift away from the WCAG 2.x contrast ratio toward APCA.
  > "Contrast targets are based on the modern APCA contrast algorithm, which accurately predicts how human vision perceives text."
- **[central]** Radix guarantees that its designated text-color steps pass target contrast ratios against the corresponding background steps in the same scale, an explicit functional-palette contract pairing 'accessible text' colors with 'background' colors.
  > "Text colors are guaranteed to pass target contrast ratios against the corresponding background colors."
- **[central]** Radix structures every color as a 12-step scale whose steps are assigned to specific functional UI roles — backgrounds, interactive components, borders/separators, solid colors, and accessible text — a concrete example of role-based semantic palette structure (research item 5).
  > "Backgrounds ... Interactive components ... Borders and separators ... Solid colors ... Accessible text"
- **[tangential]** Radix Colors ships wide-gamut P3 color support and explicitly acknowledges that color blending behaves differently in wide-gamut spaces, relevant to gamut-limit and gradient-interpolation concerns (research items 3 and 4).
  > "P3 color gamut support"
- **[supporting]** Radix implements light/dark theming by applying a CSS class to a container, an example of a modern dark-mode token system (research item 8) rather than recomputing colors at the component level.
  > "Switching to dark theme is as simple as applying a class to a container. Dark mode Just Works™."

#### Source `ad12f082` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015-09-12
- **[central]** The single most important factor in designing a good colour map is to ensure the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness across the map is uniform; this is what makes a gradient perceptually smooth.
  > "The most important factor in designing a colour map is to ensure that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness of the colours is uniform."
- **[central]** Many vendor-supplied colour maps have highly uneven perceptual contrast, including 'flat spots' where the perceptual change is so small that a feature as large as one tenth of the data range can be hidden.
  > "Many colour maps provided by vendors have highly uneven perceptual contrast over their range. It is not uncommon for colour maps to have perceptual flat spots that can hide a feature as large as one tenth of the total data range."
- **[central]** Colour maps can contain perceptual discontinuities that make the eye see false features (banding/false edges) in data that is actually smooth.
  > "Colour maps may also have perceptual discontinuities that induce the appearance of false features."
- **[supporting]** CIELAB is not a complete solution for colour-map design because it is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies, a limitation prior perceptually-uniform colormap work largely ignored.
  > "Previous work in the design of perceptually uniform colour maps has mostly failed to recognise that CIELAB space is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies."
- **[supporting]** Linear, diverging, rainbow, and cyclic colour maps each have distinct construction requirements, and the paper supplies two purpose-built test images to evaluate whether a colour map meets them.
  > "The specific requirements for linear, diverging, rainbow and cyclic colour maps are developed in detail. To support this work two test images for evaluating colour maps are presented."

#### Source `ad99161f` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2026-03-27
- **[central]** Chromostereopsis is a real perceptual depth illusion produced by red–blue (and red–green, red–grey, blue–grey) color pairs in flat 2D images, driven by chromatic aberration — making saturated red-on-blue a mechanistically grounded UI anti-pattern.
  > "a visual illusion whereby the impression of depth is conveyed in two-dimensional color images ... usually of red–blue or red–green colors, but can also be perceived with red–grey or blue–grey images"
- **[central]** The underlying mechanism is chromatic aberration: light of different wavelengths refracts and focuses at different depths, so red and blue elements at the same physical plane are perceived at different apparent depths.
  > "Chromatic aberration results from the differential refraction of light depending on its wavelength, causing some light rays to converge before others"
- **[supporting]** The typical (positive) effect makes red appear in front of blue, but the effect is not universal — some viewers see the reverse and others perceive no depth at all, so a red/blue UI cannot be relied on to read consistently across users.
  > "Positive chromostereopsis is exhibited when the red bars are perceived in front of the blue ... this is not true for everyone, as some people see the opposite and others no effect at all"
- **[supporting]** Background luminance modulates and can reverse the effect: on a white background red can appear farther and blue protruding, the opposite of the black-background case — so the same color pair behaves differently depending on the surrounding lightness.
  > "red bars were perceived as farther away and the blue bars as protruding when the bars are paired on a white background instead of a black background"
- **[tangential]** Lower illumination decreases positive chromostereopsis because pupil dilation changes which pupil region admits light (Stiles–Crawford effect), and uncorrected/eyeglass-corrected myopia strongly changes the effect's magnitude.
  > "positive chromostereopsis is decreased when illumination is lowered. At lower illumination, the dilation of pupil increases the pupillary peripheral region"

#### Source `adf462aa` — quality: **primary** · published: 2026-02-21 (latest release noted: @adobe/leonardo-mcp@0.1.0; repository is older, exact original publish date not stated in README)
- **[central]** Leonardo generates color palettes by treating the target WCAG contrast ratio as the generative input (it computes colors that meet a desired contrast against a background), rather than picking colors first and auditing contrast afterward.
  > "conform to WCAG minimum contrast standards by using contrast ratio as the starting point"
- **[supporting]** Leonardo is built on D3 color and leverages perceptual color appearance models such as CIE CAM02 for its color computations.
  > "state-of-the-art color appearance models, such as CIE CAM02"
- **[supporting]** Because output colors are quantized to the RGB display space, the generated color usually lands at a contrast ratio slightly above the requested value; this is acceptable because WCAG specifies a minimum, not an exact, contrast.
  > "most often outputs a contrast ratio slightly higher ... the WCAG requirement is defined as a _minimum contrast requirement._"
- **[supporting]** Leonardo supports adaptive/dynamic theming by regenerating an entire UI color palette in response to parameter adjustments while preserving the specified contrast relationships.
  > "dynamically regenerate[ing] the entire UI color palette"

#### Source `ae1be4a4` — quality: **primary** · published: 2020-09-08
- **[central]** A large cross-cultural study (4,598 participants, 30 nations, 22 native languages) found robust UNIVERSAL color-emotion associations, with very high cross-national pattern consistency (average similarity coefficient r = .88) when participants matched 20 emotion concepts to 12 color terms. This supports treating core color-emotion associations as empirically real and broadly shared rather than arbitrary.
  > "Pattern-similarity analyses revealed universal color-emotion associations (average similarity coefficient r = .88)."
- **[central]** Color-emotion associations are NOT purely universal: a machine-learning analysis showed nation predicts associations beyond the universal pattern, and similarity is higher for linguistically or geographically proximate nations. This is the skeptical caveat — color-emotion effects are partly culturally contingent, so single-culture findings should not be assumed universal.
  > "A machine-learning algorithm revealed that nation predicted color-emotion associations above and beyond those observed universally. Similarity was greater when nations were linguistically or geographically close."
- **[supporting]** The methodology and sample size meet the 'large controlled study' bar requested: 4,598 participants across 30 nations and 22 languages associating 20 emotion concepts with 12 color terms — a well-powered, pre-specified cross-cultural design rather than a small-N or single-population study.
  > "we tested emotional associations of colors in 4,598 participants from 30 nations speaking 22 native languages. Participants associated 20 emotion concepts with 12 color terms."
- **[tangential]** The authors explicitly frame the work as bearing on applied design practice, supporting its use as evidence for an evidence-based color reference — while keeping the universal-plus-cultural nuance.
  > "These results pose further theoretical and empirical questions about the affective properties of color and may inform practice in applied domains, such as well-being and design."

#### Source `af0f2e23` — quality: **primary** · published: 1997
- **[supporting]** PROVENANCE CORRECTION: This paper (PII S0042698997000126, PMID 9327055, 'Isoluminance and chromatic motion perception throughout the visual field,' Vision Research 37(15):2073-2081) is authored by L. Bilodeau & J. Faubert, NOT by Gegenfurtner et al. as the task labeled it, and the volume is 37(15), not 37(8). Any reference list using this source must cite Bilodeau & Faubert (1997).
  > "Authors: L Bilodeau, J Faubert | Journal: Vision Research | Year: 1997 | Volume/Pages: 37(15):2073-81 | PMID: 9327055"
- **[supporting]** Isoluminance is not a fixed, location-independent property of a color pair: the red/green luminance ratio that produces an isoluminant (equiluminant) match shifts systematically with retinal eccentricity, with observers needing to raise red luminance contrast (for fixed green) as the stimulus moves into the periphery. A pair tuned to be equiluminant at fixation is therefore not equiluminant off-center.
  > "Isoluminance and chromatic motion perception for red/green gratings were measured throughout an 80 deg visual field. Generally, the red/green isoluminance values changed with increasing eccentricity, i.e., observers increased the red luminance contrast for a fixed green luminance contrast."
- **[tangential]** The eccentricity dependence of isoluminance has a spatial threshold: within the central ~20 degrees the isoluminance values are stable against changes in target size and spatial frequency, whereas beyond 20 degrees the isoluminance ratios decline, indicating the shift is driven by post-receptoral (not purely optical/receptoral) scaling.
  > "Enlarging target size and decreasing spatial frequency did not alter isoluminance values within the central 20 degrees, though isoluminance ratios declined beyond that point. The findings indicated a need for post-receptoral scaling in chromatic motion perception."
- **[tangential]** Chromatic (red/green) gratings near isoluminance still support motion perception across a wide field, which is why the authors could measure chromatic motion throughout an 80-degree field — evidence that the motion system receives chromatic input rather than going fully blind at equiluminance.
  > "Isoluminance and chromatic motion perception for red/green gratings were measured throughout an 80 deg visual field."

#### Source `af3a8079` — quality: **primary** · published: 2015 (SciPy2015 talk; viridis became Matplotlib default in v2.0)
- **[central]** A 'perceptually uniform' colormap is defined as one whose 'perceptual deltas' plot is a flat horizontal line (equal perceptual steps per equal data steps), and the viridis-family maps were engineered to meet this criterion.
  > "A "perceptually uniform" colormap is one for which the "perceptual deltas" plot makes a simple horizontal line."
- **[central]** The new colormaps were designed to be perceptually uniform not only in color but also when converted to grayscale/black-and-white, ensuring monotonic lightness.
  > "analytically be perfectly perceptually-uniform, both in regular form and also when converted to black-and-white"
- **[central]** Matplotlib's old default colormap, jet, is not perceptually uniform and was the problematic default these alternatives replace.
  > "You can see that jet is not perceptually uniform."
- **[supporting]** The colormaps follow a bluish-to-reddish-to-yellowish hue sequence specifically chosen to remain legible under the most common forms of colorblindness (e.g., moderate deuteranomaly affecting ~5% of white men).
  > "a bluish to reddish to yellowish sequence, because this is relatively friendly to common forms of colorblindness"
- **[supporting]** Viridis is a greenish sequential option based on Eric Firing's design, modified with more yellow added at the top end; it became the Matplotlib default in version 2.0.
  > "a newly added greenish alternative option, based on a design by Eric Firing...but with more yellow added at the top"

#### Source `aff696b5` — quality: **primary** · published: ?
- **[central]** Material Design 3 uses a perceptual color space called HCT (hue, chroma, tone) that, unlike RGB or HSL, lets you change hue and chroma without changing tone (lightness) — the property that makes tone-based contrast control possible.
  > "The system uses a color space called HCT, which defines all colors using three dimensions: hue, chroma, and tone... Unlike other color spaces (like HSL or RGB), HCT allows the manipulation of a color's hue and chroma without affecting its tone."
- **[central]** M3 derives WCAG contrast directly from tone difference: a tone gap of ~48 (tones 50 vs 98) yields ~3:1 contrast and a larger gap (~68, tones 30 vs 98) yields ~7:1, so accessible pairings are computed, not hand-judged.
  > "Using tones 50 and 98 for a button and its label creates an accessible 3:1 contrast... For even more contrast, the algorithms assign tones even farther apart, achieving a 7:1 contrast. ... Using colors of tones 30 and 98 for a button and its label create a 7:1 contrast"
- **[central]** M3 assigns specific palette tones to 26 semantic color roles (e.g. primary, on primary, primary container) with fixed tone mappings, automatically pairing contrasting tones for foreground/background roles in both light and dark themes.
  > "the algorithm assigns the color tone primary40 to the primary role and the tone primary100 to the on primary role... primary roles are picked from the primary tonal palette, while surface roles are picked from the neutral tonal palette."
- **[supporting]** Each key color is expanded into a tonal palette numbered 0 (black) to 100 (white) in increments of 10, plus extra steps 95, 98 and 99, from five key colors: primary, secondary, tertiary, neutral, and neutral variant.
  > "Colors in these palettes are given a number from 0 to 100 in increments of 10, as well as 95, 98, and 99. Some palettes include more values."
- **[supporting]** M3 offers three discrete contrast levels (standard, medium, high) with stated minimum ratios — medium guarantees 3:1 and high guarantees 7:1 — applied uniformly across light and dark themes for vision-needs accessibility.
  > "Medium contrast Provides a minimum contrast ratio of 3:1 ... High contrast Further emphasizes essential elements with a 7:1 contrast ratio"

### Pass 3 — screens / contrast / gradients / design systems (2026-06-26)

#### Source `a032586f` — quality: **primary** · published: 2025-09-16
- **[central]** WCAG 1.4.1 (Level A) normatively requires that color not be the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element — i.e. color-coding alone is non-conformant and must be paired with text, shape, icons, or patterns.
  > "Color is not used as the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element."
- **[central]** A lightness difference between two hues used to convey content counts as an additional (non-color) visual distinction only when the relative-luminance contrast ratio between the colors is 3:1 or greater.
  > "If content is conveyed through the use of colors that differ not only in their hue, but that also have a significant difference in lightness, then this counts as an additional visual distinction, as long as the difference in relative luminance between the colors leads to a contrast ratio of 3:1 or greater."
- **[supporting]** Meeting the 3:1 luminance-contrast threshold is not always sufficient: when content relies on the user accurately perceiving or differentiating a specific color, a separate visual indicator is still required regardless of the contrast ratio.
  > "if content relies on the user's ability to accurately perceive or differentiate a particular color an additional visual indicator will be required regardless of the contrast ratio."
- **[supporting]** Users with color vision deficiency, monochrome/limited-color displays, partial sight, or age-related vision loss can be excluded by color-only encoding and benefit when the same information is provided through other visual means.
  > "Users with color vision deficiency who cannot distinguish between certain colors benefit when information conveyed by color is available in other visual ways."

#### Source `a08fea70` — quality: **primary** · published: ?
- **[central]** Radix Colors structures every color into a fixed 12-step semantic scale where each step range is assigned a single UI role: steps 1-2 = app/subtle backgrounds, steps 3-5 = UI component backgrounds (normal/hover/active), steps 6-8 = borders, steps 9-10 = solid backgrounds, steps 11-12 = text. This is a concrete semantic-token convention for assigning color to roles.
  > "Steps `1` and `2` are designed for app backgrounds and subtle component backgrounds. ... Steps `3`, `4`, and `5` are designed for UI component backgrounds. ... Steps `6`, `7`, and `8` are designed for borders. ... Steps `9` and `10` are designed for solid backgrounds. ... Steps `11` and `12` are designed for text."
- **[central]** Radix specifies text-step contrast using APCA (Lc) rather than WCAG 2 ratios, guaranteeing the two text steps reach Lc 60 and Lc 90 against the step-2 background of the same scale. This is a modern design system adopting APCA as its contrast metric, bearing directly on the APCA-vs-WCAG2 debate.
  > "Steps `11` and `12`—which are designed for text—are guaranteed to Lc 60 and Lc 90 APCA contrast ratio on top of a step `2` background from the same scale."
- **[supporting]** A single step (step 9) is reserved as the highest-chroma / most saturated color in the whole scale, designated for solid accent backgrounds, graphics, and overlays — i.e. the emphasis/accent color is one reserved slot, not spread across the palette.
  > "Step `9` has the highest chroma of all steps in the scale. In other words, it's the purest step, the step mixed with the least amount of white or black."
- **[supporting]** Border roles are further subdivided by interactivity: step 6 for subtle borders on non-interactive components (sidebars, cards, separators), step 7 for subtle borders on interactive components and focus rings, step 8 for stronger borders on interactive components and focus rings.
  > "Subtle borders on components which are not interactive ... Subtle borders on interactive components ... Stronger borders on interactive components and focus rings"

#### Source `a0e66c38` — quality: **primary** · published: ?
- **[central]** Material Design 3's color system is built on the HCT color space (hue, chroma, tone), whose defining property is that tone (= luminance) can be held fixed while hue and chroma change — something RGB and HSL cannot do. This is the mechanism that lets a token system guarantee accessible contrast independent of hue. [CONVENTION / best-practice, with a perceptual-science basis]
  > "Unlike other color spaces (like HSL or RGB), HCT allows the manipulation of a color's hue and chroma without affecting its tone."
- **[central]** M3 maps tonal DISTANCE directly to a contrast ratio: tones 50 and 98 give an accessible 3:1, and tones 30 and 98 give 7:1. This is a concrete, falsifiable token rule — contrast is driven by lightness/tone difference, and the system reserves wider tonal gaps for higher-contrast modes. [CONVENTION / best-practice]
  > "Using tones 50 and 98 for a button and its label creates an accessible 3:1 contrast"
- **[central]** The functional palette structure is fixed: a single source color generates exactly five key colors (primary, secondary, tertiary, neutral, neutral variant), each expanded into a 13-step tonal palette (tones 0–100 in increments of 10, plus 95, 98, 99), and tones are then assigned to 26 semantic color roles based on emphasis, container type, and relationship — supporting the role-based 'ground / ink / reserved accent' model in topic 5. [CONVENTION / best-practice]
  > "Material's color algorithms manipulate the source color's hue … and chroma … to generate five complimentary key colors."
- **[supporting]** M3 offers three user-selectable contrast levels (Standard, Medium = minimum 3:1, High = 7:1) and explicitly bounds 'more contrast is better': it warns that some users find higher contrast uncomfortable due to halation — counter-evidence to a naive maximize-contrast rule, relevant to dark-mode legibility costs. [CONVENTION, with bounding/contrary note]
  > "Provides a minimum contrast ratio of 3:1 for those who need more contrast, but may experience visual discomfort with higher contrasts from effects like halation."
- **[supporting]** M3 documents that screen-gamut and biological limits make highly saturated light blues and light reds unattainable, forcing chroma to vary across a tonal palette — directly relevant to topic 3's gamut-limit and saturated-pure-blue/red-on-screen concerns. [CONVENTION, consistent with sRGB gamut physics]
  > "Color limitations are the reason colors such as bright light blue or bright light red are not quite possible. This is why the chroma value may increase or decrease for some tones in a tonal palette."

#### Source `a1430415` — quality: **primary** · published: 2014-11
- **[central]** On LCD (emissive) displays, the most legible text/background pairings are high-contrast combinations with POSITIVE polarity (dark text on a light background), whereas on CRTs the most legible were NEGATIVE-polarity combinations (light text on dark) — so optimal polarity is display-technology-dependent, and this is direct empirical pushback on any universal 'dark mode reads better' claim for self-lit screens.
  > "A new legibility table is proposed for LCDs where the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with positive polarity whereas for CRT displays, the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with negative polarity."
- **[central]** Legibility rankings do not transfer across display technologies: neither the printed (Le Courier) legibility table nor the CRT legibility table correctly predicts text legibility on LCDs, so screen-color guidance derived from print or older CRT studies is not valid for modern emissive sRGB panels.
  > "The results show that neither the Le Courier legibility table nor the CRT legibility table is appropriate for LCDs."
- **[supporting]** The conclusions rest on a controlled study with both objective and subjective measures: 56 foreground/background color combinations, 308 participants, with an objective Snellen-style character-identification task (count of correctly identified uppercase letters) plus a 10-point Likert legibility rating — qualifying it as [STRONG] empirical evidence rather than convention.
  > "The legibility of 56 elementary color combinations was tested by 308 participants. The first task required the participants to identify 21 uppercase alphabetic characters selected and presented in conformance with a Snellen chart... In the second task, participants subjectively rated the legibility of color combinations on a ten-point Likert scale."

#### Source `a14febfa` — quality: **blog** · published: 2026-04-11
- **[central]** APCA (the contrast method proposed for WCAG 3) was removed from the WCAG 3 specification by the W3C Accessibility Guidelines Working Group by July 2023, citing lack of working-group support; it had entered as an exploratory/experimental method with automatic-removal provisions if it failed to gain support within six months.
  > "By July 2023, the W3C Accessibility Guidelines Working Group had removed APCA from the specification entirely, citing lack of working group support."
- **[supporting]** Courts and US enforcement agencies (e.g. DOJ) recognize WCAG 2.1 Level AA as the binding technical standard and do not treat APCA / experimental future-spec methods as equivalent, so adopting APCA in place of WCAG 2.1 creates legal/compliance risk.
  > "Courts and enforcement agencies don't typically recognize experimental approaches as equivalent to established requirements."
- **[supporting]** The recommended practice is to meet WCAG 2.1 contrast ratios first and apply APCA only as an enhancement layered on top, never as a replacement for the established WCAG 2 criteria.
  > "Ensure all color choices meet WCAG 2.1 contrast ratios, then potentially apply additional experimental criteria as enhancement rather than replacement."
- **[tangential]** The author frames premature APCA adoption as harmful in practice, claiming it creates false confidence in compliance and can add complexity while introducing new accessibility barriers rather than removing them.
  > "adds complexity while potentially creating new barriers"

#### Source `a15e9d85` — quality: **primary** · published: 2004
- **[central]** Luminance contrast, not colour contrast, drives legibility: visual search time, number of eye fixations, and mean fixation durations all increase strongly as luminance contrast decreases, even when strong colour contrast is present. This is direct empirical [STRONG] evidence that low-luminance-contrast text is an anti-pattern regardless of hue difference.
  > "Visual search times, number of eye fixations, and mean fixation durations increased strongly with decreasing luminance contrast despite the presence of colour contrast."
- **[central]** High colour contrast does NOT guarantee fast/legible perception when luminance contrast is small (the equiluminant case), because human visual acuity is far lower for pure-chromatic information than for luminance information. This [STRONG] finding refutes the common [MYTH] that strong hue contrast alone is enough for readable UI text.
  > "Moderate or even high colour contrast does not guarantee quick visual perception, if the luminance contrast between characters and background is small. This is probably due to the fact that visual acuity (the ability to see small details) is considerably lower for pure colour information than for luminance information."
- **[supporting]** The luminance-contrast penalty is strongest at small character sizes (0.17 deg) but persists at medium (0.37 deg) and even, in some cases, large (1.26 deg) characters — so the effect is not limited to fine text and applies across typical UI type sizes.
  > "The effects were largest for small characters (0.17 deg), but occurred also for medium (0.37 deg), and in some cases for large (1.26 deg) characters."
- **[supporting]** Study design is a controlled visual-search experiment with eye tracking: ~108 participants, character/background pairs of moderate colour contrast (green/grey) versus high colour contrast but low luminance contrast (green/red, yellow/blue), across three character sizes (0.17, 0.37, 1.26 deg) — establishing it as primary controlled evidence, not opinion.
  > "The study involved 108 participants and examined stimuli with moderate (green/grey) or high colour contrast (green/red or yellow/blue), and three character sizes (0.17, 0.37, and 1.26 deg)."

#### Source `a31d1f50` — quality: **blog** · published: 2026-04-10 (updated 2026-04-13)
- **[central]** As of the W3C's April 8, 2026 WCAG 3 draft, no contrast algorithm has been selected — the choice between WCAG 2's contrast-ratio method and APCA (or another method) remains formally open.
  > "The W3C's April 8, 2026 draft states: "The contrast algorithm used in WCAG 3 is yet to be determined.""
- **[central]** APCA was marked exploratory and removed from active WCAG 3 consideration, and even its creator acknowledged the submitted versions were obsolete — meaning APCA is not (as of April 2026) an adopted standard.
  > "APCA was marked exploratory and removed from consideration... The APCA creator acknowledged submitted versions were "early versions that were very obsolete.""
- **[supporting]** Roselli's practical guidance for the APCA-vs-WCAG2 debate is to use APCA only for colors that ALSO pass WCAG 2 contrast requirements, to limit legal risk — treating WCAG 2 as the still-authoritative floor.
  > "Roselli recommends choosing colors that "also conform to WCAG2 contrast requirements" if using APCA to limit legal risk."
- **[supporting]** Per APCA author Andrew Somers, colors passing APCA at a given font size and weight typically exceed WCAG 2's minimum contrast thresholds in the vast majority of cases — i.e. the two methods diverge, with APCA generally stricter for body text.
  > "Andrew Somers' comment (April 15, 2026) notes: "colors that pass APCA for a given font size and weight greatly exceed WCAG 2's minimums in the vast majority of cases.""

#### Source `a426f72f` — quality: **primary** · published: 2018 (Proc. ACM CHI)
- **[central]** In relative-distance color discrimination tasks, viridis maintained consistently low error across all comparison spans, while the single-hue Blues colormap matched viridis accuracy at larger spans but degraded notably at the smallest span — showing perceptual-uniformity benefits are span/task-dependent, not uniform.
  > "Viridis exhibits consistently low error across the board ... [Blues] drops notably for the smallest span."
- **[central]** Diverging colormaps incur measurable error specifically when value comparisons straddle the central hue boundary: the blue-orange diverging scheme produced errors when comparisons were made across the central blue-orange transition — a concrete failure mode of diverging maps.
  > "blueorange diverging scheme exhibits errors when comparison is made across the central blue-orange hue boundary"
- **[central]** Perceptual color-space metrics alone do not fully predict colormap effectiveness; combining perceptual color space with color-naming measures predicts user performance better than either alone, yet overall predictive accuracy remains poor — qualifying the 'perceptually uniform is sufficient' assumption.
  > "a combination of perceptual color space and color naming measures more accurately predict user performance than either alone, though the overall accuracy is poor"
- **[supporting]** The study empirically evaluated quantitative (sequential/diverging) colormaps via controlled relative-distance judgments among systematically sampled color triplets, measuring speed and accuracy, across four single-hue and five multi-hue colormaps.
  > "Subjects performed relative distance judgments among color triplets systematically drawn from each colormap."
- **[supporting]** The authors frame the goal of quantitative color encoding as accurately mapping perceptual color dimensions to data structure, and derive design recommendations for more effective scalar color encodings from the empirical results.
  > "An essential goal of quantitative color encoding is the accurate mapping of perceptual dimensions of color to the logical structure of data."

#### Source `a4d89eaa` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019 (IEEE VIS short paper; pre-registered at osf.io/ng9qw; latest cited references 2019)
- **[central]** For judging which of two scalar fields has steeper average gradient, the perceptually-uniform monotonic-luminance colormap viridis performed WORST: its just-noticeable-difference was 3.61% gradient (CI 3.31-3.91%), significantly larger (worse sensitivity) than diverging cool-warm at 2.86% (CI 2.62-3.09%) and rainbow at 3.05% (CI 2.82-3.28%); main effect of colormap F(2,520)=14.771, p<0.001, both cool-warm-vs-viridis and rainbow-vs-viridis p<0.001. This directly contradicts the 'perceptually-uniform colormaps are always better' convention for this task class.
  > "It appears that cool-warm (JND gradient: 2.86%, CI: 2.62-3.09%) and rainbow (JND: 3.05%, CI: 2.82-3.28%) afford higher sensitivity to gradients as compared to viridis (JND: 3.61%, CI: 3.31-3.91%)."
- **[central]** The mechanism is that discrete color bands / discontinuities in rainbow and cool-warm ramps -- which perceptual-uniformity advocates treat as undesirable artifacts -- actually AID gradient perception, because viewers use the apparent size and frequency of colored patches as proxies for gradient magnitude. So 'hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing.'
  > "although discrete color bands are essentially artifacts [5], viewers may rely on such features as proxies for gradient. For instance, a viewer could estimate the apparent size of the blue and red patches in Figure 1, or how frequently they seem to occur in the image... the experiment suggests that hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing. Color bands may aid some tasks where the goal is to compare structural differences, including net gradients."
- **[supporting]** The contrarian advantage is real but modest: the effect size of cool-warm and rainbow over viridis was small-to-moderate (Cohen's d = 0.376 for cool-warm, 0.264 for rainbow). This bounds the finding -- it does not show rainbow/diverging are dramatically superior, only measurably so for this gradient-comparison task.
  > "The estimated effect size (Cohen's d) is 0.376 for cool-warm over viridis and 0.264 for rainbow over viridis... The observed effect size (0.264-0.376) is small to moderate."
- **[supporting]** The authors explicitly caution this does NOT rehabilitate rainbow as a general default: rainbow colormaps remain problematic for other tasks and exclude colorblind viewers, so should not be used as standalone visualizations. The finding is task-dependent (gradient/structure comparison), not a blanket endorsement.
  > "It is important to note, however, that rainbow colormaps can be problematic in other tasks [4,5], and can exclude people with color vision deficiency. Therefore, they should still not be recommended as standalone visualizations."
- **[supporting]** Discriminating gradients got harder at high spatial frequency (JNDs 29.7% greater, F(1,520)=49.272, p<0.001, consistent with Weber's law), but there was NO colormap x spatial-frequency interaction -- cool-warm and rainbow kept outperforming viridis even at high spatial frequency, so the second hypothesis (that the banding heuristic would break down at fine scale) failed.
  > "The JNDs at high spatial frequency were 29.7% greater compared with the low the frequency condition. We found no interaction between colormap and spatial frequency; the latter reduces sensitivity equally in all tested colormaps... we observe that cool-warm and rainbow continue to outperform viridis even at high spatial frequency."

#### Source `a5a5c2f8` — quality: **primary** · published: 2012
- **[central]** Limits on human visual memory produce change blindness: viewers often fail to detect even large differences between two images when the images cannot be directly/simultaneously compared. This bounds how much an interface can rely on a viewer noticing a change across views or over time.
  > "A change blindness example, it is often difficult to immediately see the difference between the left and the right images...Limits on visual memory make it difficult to compare the images."
- **[supporting]** Human visual perception is a primary determinant of visualization effectiveness; how a viewer perceptually 'sees' details in an image directly affects task efficiency and effectiveness, so visual-attention findings should drive design decisions.
  > "How we 'see' details in an image can directly impact a viewer's efficiency and effectiveness."
- **[supporting]** This is an authoritative IEEE TVCG survey (Healey & Enns 2012, vol. 18(7), pp. 1170-1188) synthesizing theories of low-level visual perception as the foundation for visual memory and attention research applied to visualization and graphics — making it a primary citable source for preattentive/attention mechanisms (Treisman, Wolfe, Itti & Koch territory).
  > "The authors survey research on attention and visual perception relevant to visualization design, discussing "theories of low-level visual perception" and how findings form foundations for work on visual memory and attention."

#### Source `a679b040` — quality: **primary** · published: 2017
- **[central]** Perceptual uniformity built from uniform color spaces (e.g., CIELAB) does NOT necessarily produce uniformity in terms of feature detection — the standard CIELAB-distance basis for 'perceptually uniform' colormaps may fail to equalize discriminability. This is direct contrary/bounding evidence against the 'uniform color-space => uniform perception' assumption underlying viridis-style construction.
  > "there are reasons for thinking that uniformity in terms of feature detection may not be achieved by current methods which are based on the use of uniform color spaces"
- **[central]** The paper proposes an empirical alternative to color-space-distance uniformity: a directly measured feature detection threshold function across the colormap, validated by crowdsourced (Amazon Mechanical Turk) experiments on nine colormaps at three feature sizes — i.e., discriminative power should be measured behaviorally, not assumed from CIELAB spacing.
  > "presents a new method for evaluating the feature detection threshold function across a colormap, and uses crowdsourced studies for the direct evaluation of nine colormaps for three feature sizes"
- **[supporting]** Empirically, two newly designed double-ended (diverging) color sequences had the highest discriminative power while also retaining good uniformity, showing that high feature-discrimination and uniformity can coexist but are distinct properties that must be checked separately rather than one guaranteeing the other.
  > "the results show that two new double ended sequences have the highest discriminative power and good uniformity"
- **[supporting]** The 'good colormap = perceptually uniform' rule is explicitly defined as the requirement that differences between adjacent colors be equally distinct along the sequence — the paper frames this as the prevailing convention it then tests and partially challenges for feature detection.
  > "the common requirement that a good colormap be perceptually uniform, meaning that differences between adjacent colors in the sequence be equally distinct"

#### Source `a6a9be67` — quality: **primary** · published: 2026-06-23
- **[central]** Carbon mandates that color alone is insufficient to convey status, explicitly citing color-vision-deficient users, and requires color to be paired with text or other visual cues (shape/symbol). This is a design-system [CONVENTION] aligned with WCAG 1.4.1 Use of Color, directly supporting colorblind-safe multi-level alert encoding.
  > "Relying solely on color to convey status is insufficient, especially for users with color vision deficiencies (refer to Use of Color). While color can be useful, it must be paired with text or other visual cues."
- **[central]** Carbon requires at least 3:1 contrast between status-indicator colors and between the indicator and the page background (WCAG non-text contrast), and uses a grayscale-differentiability test: if statuses remain distinguishable in grayscale, color is not load-bearing. Indicators must rely on at least two of color/shape/symbol.
  > "ensure that there's at least a 3:1 contrast between colors used for status indicators, as well as between the indicator and the page background. If the contrast is sufficient, even in grayscale, users should still be able to differentiate statuses without relying solely on color."
- **[central]** Carbon assigns fixed semantic roles to hues with named tokens ($status-red, $status-orange, $status-yellow, $status-green, $status-blue, $status-gray, $status-purple) and specific hexes (red60 da1e28, orange40 ff832b, yellow30 f1c21b, green50 24a148, blue70 0043ce): red=danger/error, orange=serious warning, yellow=regular warning, green=normal/success, blue=passive/info, gray=drafts/not-started, purple=outliers. This is a concrete semantic-token convention.
  > "Typically, red represents danger or error, orange represents a serious warning, yellow represents a regular warning, green represents normal or success, and blue represents passive notifications, usually involving additional information and workflow progress."
- **[supporting]** Carbon structures multiple critical/severity levels on a 1-10 scale mapped to distinct colors AND shapes (Critical/High=red, Medium=orange 4-6, Low=yellow 0-3), keeping levels mutually distinguishable by pairing color with shape and label rather than color alone.
  > "High $status-red Indicates high severity threat... based on a 1-10 scale Used for: high threat (7-9)... Medium $status-orange... Used for: medium threat (4-6)... Low $status-yellow... Used for: low threat (0-3)"
- **[supporting]** Carbon maintains an extended yellow/orange palette 'only for added contrast accessibility' because the standard light yellows and oranges lack sufficient contrast, and requires outlines on light status colors (orange, yellow) in light themes — concrete evidence that certain saturated hues fail luminance-contrast thresholds on screen.
  > "This palette is only for added contrast accessibility when using yellows and oranges... using outlines and pairing text with shape indicators is essential to enhance their accessibility, especially with lighter status colors such as orange and yellow in Carbon light themes."

#### Source `a7073a2c` — quality: **primary** · published: 1995-10
- **[central]** Chromostereopsis (the colour-depth illusion, e.g. saturated red appearing to float at a different depth than blue) is produced by a purely optical mechanism — the interocular difference in monocular chromatic diplopia caused by the eye's transverse chromatic aberration (TCA) — and the data could be fully explained by this optical model with no need for additional neural factors or mechanisms.
  > "Although many colour-depth phenomena are predictable from the interocular difference in monocular chromatic diplopia caused by the eye's transverse chromatic aberration (TCA)... These seemingly paradoxical results could nevertheless be explained by a simple model of optical TCA without the need to postulate additional factors or mechanisms."
- **[central]** The direction of chromostereopsis is NOT fixed per colour — it reverses in sign with background colour: red and blue targets on a black background show the opposite depth ordering from the same targets on a magenta (red+blue) background. This disconfirms the simple convention that 'red always advances and blue always recedes.'
  > "In all cases, chromatic diplopia and chromostereopsis were found experimentally to reverse in sign with this change in background."
- **[supporting]** A single coloured element can be perceived at different depth planes within one display depending on the local background it sits on, so the chromostereoptic depth of a UI colour is context-dependent rather than an inherent property of the hue.
  > "we found that a given coloured target could be located in different depth planes within the same display when located on different background colours"
- **[supporting]** The magnitude and sign of TCA-driven chromostereopsis depend on pupil position relative to the visual axis: displacing the (1.78 mm artificial) pupil nasally versus temporally induces a variable, controllable degree of TCA and thus of perceived depth.
  > "These apertures were displaced nasally and temporally from the visual axis under controlled conditions to induce a variable degree of TCA."
- **[supporting]** The effect was demonstrated on an emissive colour computer display (a Macintosh colour monitor) using red, blue and green stimuli, confirming chromostereopsis is reproducible on self-lit screens with saturated primaries — the relevant case for UI design.
  > "A Macintosh colour monitor was used to present red, blue, and green test stimuli which were viewed monocularly or binocularly (haploscopically) through 1.78 mm artificial pupils."

#### Source `a750cb7d` — quality: **primary** · published: 2024-09-17
- **[central]** In a controlled study of 104 adults, neither dark mode (negative polarity) nor light mode (positive polarity) consistently outperformed the other on visualization analysis tasks (cluster-counting and distribution-characterization across bar/line/scatter); each polarity benefited comparable proportions of participants.
  > "each polarity benefiting comparable proportions of participants...the polarity that led to better performance did not always match their preferred polarity"
- **[central]** Contrast polarity's effect on visual-analysis performance did not change meaningfully with age; older adults (>=60) and younger adults (<60) showed no significant divergence, with accuracy percent-differences averaging mu=0.26 (older) vs mu=0.22 (younger).
  > "the effects of contrast polarity on visual analysis performance do not noticeably change with age"
- **[supporting]** Participants' stated polarity preference frequently failed to match the polarity that actually produced better performance, meaning self-reported dark/light preference is an unreliable predictor of measured task outcome.
  > "disagreement between the two, indicating that what participants prefer may not actually be what they are best-suited to use"
- **[supporting]** Chart/visualization type had a noticeably larger effect on accuracy than contrast polarity did for both age groups, bounding the practical importance of the dark-vs-light choice for analysis tasks.
  > "Visualization type had a noticeably greater impact on accuracy for both age groups"
- **[supporting]** These visualization-task results diverge from prior reading-task research (Piepenbrock et al.) that found positive (dark-on-light) polarity gave better reading performance, suggesting the dark-mode legibility advantage for text does not generalize to data-visualization analysis.
  > "differ from those reported by Piepenbrock et al., which observed better reading performance using positive contrast polarity"

#### Source `a93a46f9` — quality: **primary** · published: 2009 (ISVC; this expanded PDF dated 2010-10-19 per metadata)
- **[central]** Naive piecewise-linear interpolation in CIELAB space produces a visible Mach-band artifact at the white midpoint of a diverging colormap (where luminance abruptly switches from rising to falling); interpolating instead in Moreland's polar Msh color space smooths this by letting luminance level off while chromaticity changes faster. This is the core mechanism behind 'CIELAB-linear is not enough' for diverging maps.
  > "Using piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space causes Mach bands in the white part of diverging color maps (left image). The transition can be softened by interpolating in Msh space (right image)."
- **[central]** The rainbow/jet colormap's perceptual non-uniformity actively creates false banding AND hides real data: changes appear fast in cyan/yellow (causing Mach bands) and slow in blue/green/red (creating wide color bands that mask underlying variation). This gives a concrete perceptual mechanism for why rainbow is an anti-pattern for continuous data.
  > "The colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions, which can cause Mach bands in those regions. The colors appear to change more slowly in the blue, green, and red regions, which creates larger bands of color. These bands can hide important changes in the underlying data."
- **[supporting]** Despite being well-established as harmful, the rainbow colormap dominated by convention/default, not by evidence of effectiveness: it was the default in 8 of 9 surveyed toolkits and was used in 51% of IEEE Visualization papers from 2001-2005 (citing Borland & Taylor 2007). Supports the [MYTH]/inertia framing for rainbow.
  > "Borland and Taylor also find that in IEEE Visualization papers from 2001 to 2005 the rainbow color map is used 51 percent of the time."
- **[supporting]** Simultaneous contrast measurably distorts perceived lightness: when comparing the luminance of two objects separated by distance and background, human subjects err by up to 20% (citing Ware 1988). A concrete number for the simultaneous-contrast anti-pattern and a stated weakness of pure-grayscale luminance encodings.
  > "when asked to compare the luminance of two objects separated by distance and background, human subjects err up to 20%"
- **[supporting]** Disconfirming/limiting caveat: the canonical cool-to-warm diverging colormap was NOT validated by any user study; its design rests on theory of color perception alone. This bounds claims that 'perceptually-uniform = empirically better' for this specific widely-adopted map.
  > "Although we have not been able to do user studies, the design of this color map is based on well established theories on color perception."

#### Source `a9c31334` — quality: **primary** · published: Last active July 30, 2025 (GitHub Gist; no fixed publish date)
- **[central]** WCAG 2's contrast formula loses validity for dark colors: once the brightest color in a pair is darker than #a0a0a0, the WCAG 2 contrast ratio no longer predicts readability usefully. This bears directly on topic 6/8's low-luminance-contrast anti-patterns and the APCA debate.
  > "When the brightest color is darker than `#a0a0a0`, WCAG 2 contrast prediction degrades until it is no longer providing useful results."
- **[central]** In a test using thousands of random colors, the WCAG 2 method incorrectly PASSED 49% of pairs that were actually too low in contrast to read — a concrete, falsifiable false-pass rate against the popular 4.5:1 best practice.
  > "Using thousands of random colors, WCAG 2 _incorrectly passed_ 49% — colors that were too low in contrast to read and should have failed."
- **[central]** A specific dark color pair (#797979 text on #110402 background) passes WCAG 2 at 4.5:1 and is permitted for body text, yet APCA scores it Lc31 (total fail) — a worked example of WCAG2 passing an unreadable pair.
  > "WCAG 2: Passes as 4.5:1, permits body text ... APCA: Reports total fail (Lc31), prohibits readable content text ... "Do you agree? I don't, and neither does APCA""
- **[central]** APCA's threshold scales with element spatial frequency (text size and weight): high-spatial-frequency body text requires the highest contrast, while a large button reads well at much lower contrast (Lc45, even Lc30). This is the perceptual mechanism APCA adds that WCAG2's single 4.5:1 cutoff lacks.
  > "APCA guidelines are much more aligned to use cases and element spatial frequency (i.e. size and weight). ... a large element like a button works well with a much lower contrast, such as Lc45, even as low as Lc30."
- **[supporting]** WCAG 2's contrast values are deliberately inflated over part of the range as a 'brute force' workaround for the formula's shortcomings, whereas APCA claims perceptual uniformity so a single Lc value is valid across the whole range.
