  > "WCAG2 contrast values are artificially inflated over part of the range to try and make up for these shortcomings. ... Since APCA is perceptually uniform, one correct Lc value works across the entire available range."

#### Source `aa8896a5` — quality: **primary** · published: 2022-10-29
- **[central]** The paper's central thesis is contrarian to blanket anti-rainbow dogma: rather than declaring the rainbow colormap universally harmful, it frames the evidence as both 'for and against' and seeks to establish precisely WHEN (under which tasks/conditions) rainbow impairs performance versus when it does not. This bounds the 'perceptually-uniform is always better' claim as task-dependent.
  > "consolidates findings from empirical studies to better understand why and precisely when the rainbow color scheme might impair human performance with visualizations"
- **[central]** The conclusions rest on a large empirical survey base: over 37,000 figures across 11,808 papers were systematically analyzed in six disciplines (information visualization, neuroscience, hydrology, geography, remote sensing, planetary science), making this a quantitative evidence corpus on rainbow prevalence rather than opinion.
  > "over 37,000 figures in 11,808 papers in information visualization, neuroscience, hydrology, geography, remote sensing and planetary science"
- **[supporting]** Rainbow use is declining over time specifically within visualization-focused literature, but persists in domain sciences (remote sensing, planetary science) — evidence that perceptual best-practice adoption is field-specific, not universal. Rainbow was ~14x more frequent in remote sensing/planetary sciences than in information visualization outlets.
  > "the rainbow scheme is strikingly fourteen times more frequent in remote sensing and planetary sciences than in information visualization outlets"
- **[supporting]** A temporal trend shows rainbow appearing less in visualization-related publications while remaining popular elsewhere, documenting that the colormap-reform message has propagated unevenly across scientific communities.
  > "while the rainbow scheme appears less and less in visualization-related publications, it remains popular in other scientific domains including remote sensing"

#### Source `aae7850a` — quality: **secondary** · published: 2020-02-02
- **[central]** For users with normal/corrected vision, light mode (positive polarity) outperformed dark mode on visual-acuity and proofreading tasks, for both young (18-33) and older (60-85) adults — contrary to the popular belief that dark mode is universally 'easier on the eyes.'
  > "light mode won across all dimensions"
- **[supporting]** The legibility advantage of light mode grows as font size shrinks: smaller text is read better in light mode.
  > "the smaller the font, the better it is for users to see the text in light mode"
- **[supporting]** Contrast-polarity advantage is conditional on ambient lighting: in glanceable-reading tests (Dobres et al. 2017, MIT AgeLab) there was no significant polarity effect in daytime, but light mode performed better at night.
  > "during daytime, there was no significant effect of contrast polarity, but during nighttime, light mode led to better performance"
- **[supporting]** Dark mode produces measurably better reading rates than light mode specifically for low-vision users with cloudy ocular media (e.g., cataracts), because it reduces light scatter through the clouded lens (Legge et al. 1985).
  > "participants with cloudy ocular media had better reading rates with dark modes"
- **[central]** Light mode may carry a long-term ocular cost: one-hour reading in light mode produced significant choroidal thinning (a change associated with myopia progression), while dark mode produced thickening (Aleman et al. 2018, Nature Scientific Reports), the effect stronger in already-myopic participants.
  > "significant thinning of this membrane when participants read text presented in light mode"

#### Source `ac6789ac` — quality: **primary** · published: 2020 (online December 2019); Vision Research, Vol. 166, pp. 33-42
- **[supporting]** Text–background colour combination measurably modulates accommodative (eye-focusing) and pupillary dynamics during on-screen reading, meaning colour choice has a real physiological effect on the eye during digital reading, not just an aesthetic one.
  > "The text-background color combination modulates the accommodative and pupillary dynamics during a 2-minutes reading task."
- **[central]** A blue-text/red-background (blue–red) combination induced a heightened accommodative response — i.e. it forced greater focusing effort than other combinations — which is direct physiological evidence that saturated blue+red pairings are an on-screen anti-pattern.
  > "the blue-red combination induced a heightened accommodative response"
- **[supporting]** Positive polarity (dark text on a light background) was associated with MORE variability of the accommodative response and smaller pupil sizes, a nuance/contrary point for the dark-vs-light-mode debate (smaller pupils give greater depth of focus, but focusing was less stable).
  > "positive polarities were associated with more variability of the accommodative response and smaller pupil sizes"
- **[supporting]** The yellow-on-white combination (a low-luminance-contrast pair) was the least preferred for legibility, supporting the anti-pattern that low luminance-contrast text reads poorly on screens.
  > "with the yellow-white text-background color combination being the least preferred option"
- **[supporting]** This is a controlled empirical study: 20 healthy young adults each read fourteen 2-minute passages in fourteen different colour combinations on an LCD screen, with accommodation and pupil measured continuously by a binocular open-field autorefractometer — qualifying it as [STRONG] controlled-study evidence on screen colour and eye function.
  > "twenty healthy young adults reading fourteen 2-minute passages designed with fourteen different color combinations while accommodative and pupil responses were continuously measured with a binocular open-field autorefractometer"

#### Source `acbff5d8` — quality: **secondary** · published: ?
- **[central]** At equiluminance (two colors of equal luminance), structure-from-motion 3D perception collapses: a rotating dotted sphere flattens because the magnocellular motion pathway, which is nearly colour-blind, receives almost no signal.
  > "At equiluminance, the magnocellular system cannot convey any motion signals because it effectively sees nearly nothing... the 3-dimensional percept loses salience; the sphere becomes nearly flat."
- **[central]** Motion detection is mediated by a luminance-driven (magnocellular) system that is dissociable from colour processing; the motion system fails when a boundary is defined by colour alone without luminance contrast.
  > "The magnocellular visual pathway is "motion sensitive" but "nearly colour blind.""
- **[supporting]** Colour (chromatic) processing has substantially lower spatial resolution than luminance processing — roughly 10-20x lower depending on the colour pair — which partly explains why equiluminant chromatic edges are poorly resolved.
  > "equiluminance effects partly result from color processing having lower spatial resolution than luminance processing (10-20x reduction depending on color pair)"
- **[supporting]** Equiluminant stimuli produce temporal instability/flicker in apparent-motion (phi) percepts, especially for large frame-to-frame steps, indicating apparent motion deteriorates without luminance contrast.
  > "Some balls seem to flicker, especially those with large steps from frame to frame, with speculation that the phi-phenomenon deteriorates at equiluminance."

#### Source `ada5820d` — quality: **primary** · published: 2014 (Ergonomics, 57(11):1670-7)
- **[central]** Positive polarity displays (dark text on a light background) produce better proofreading performance than negative polarity displays (light text on a dark background) — direct, controlled disconfirming evidence against the claim that dark mode improves legibility for normal-vision young adults.
  > "Smaller pupil size and better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays ... Performance was better with positive than with negative polarity displays."
- **[supporting]** Pupil sizes are smaller when reading positive-polarity (light-background) displays than negative-polarity (dark-background) displays, the proposed physiological mechanism for the positive-polarity reading advantage.
  > "pupil sizes were smaller [with positive polarity]"
- **[supporting]** The positive-polarity reading advantage is attributable to higher display luminance, which constricts the pupil and yields a sharper retinal image (greater depth of field / less optical aberration) rather than to color or contrast polarity per se.
  > "The results are compatible with the hypothesis that the positive polarity advantage is an effect of display luminance."

#### Source `adaff01d` — quality: **primary** · published: 2005
- **[central]** Visually conspicuous ('salient') stimuli automatically attract focal visual attention to their location through a bottom-up, stimulus-driven mechanism — i.e., in an interface the most salient element captures the eye regardless of the viewer's task. This is the perceptual basis for using saliency to direct attention (topic 7).
  > "Visually conspicuous, or so-called salient, stimuli often have the capability of attracting focal visual attention towards their locations. Several computational architectures subserving this bottom-up, stimulus-driven, spatiotemporal deployment of attention are reviewed in this article."
- **[central]** Where attention goes next is determined by both bottom-up (image-based) and top-down (task-dependent) cues; the bottom-up component is computable from the image alone, which is what makes saliency models able to predict gaze on an unfamiliar design.
  > "It has become clear that attention guides where to look next based on both bottom-up (image-based) and top-down (task-dependent) cues (James, 1890/1981)."
- **[central]** Computational saliency modeling derives from Treisman & Gelade's Feature Integration Theory (1980), under which only simple visual features (e.g., intensity, color, orientation) are computed in a massively parallel fashion across the whole visual field — the mechanism behind preattentive single-feature pop-out.
  > "Development of computational models of attention started with the Feature Integration Theory of Treisman & Gelade (1980), which proposed that only simple visual features are computed in a massively parallel"
- **[supporting]** Saliency models are validated against, and quantitatively predict, human visual-search psychophysics, and are used to select regions of interest in cluttered scenes — evidence that the bottom-up saliency account is testable, not just descriptive.
  > "The resulting computational models have applications not only to the prediction of visual search psychophysics, but also, in the domain of machine vision, to the rapid selection of regions of interest in complex, cluttered visual environments."
- **[supporting]** Bottom-up saliency models have been applied to the objective evaluation of visual designs (advertising), directly connecting saliency science to assessing where a layout will draw the eye — a precedent for applying saliency to interface design.
  > "We describe an unusual such application, to the objective evaluation of advertising designs."

#### Source `af326e29` — quality: **primary** · published: 2019-10-10
- **[central]** [CONTRARY to "perceptually-uniform is always best"] In a controlled study of node-link NETWORK visualization, participants completed tasks SIGNIFICANTLY FASTER with a simple single-hue sequential blue colormap than with viridis (the perceptually-uniform multi-hue colormap), RdYlBu divergent, or jet rainbow — i.e., the perceptually-uniform colormap was NOT the fastest.
  > "Our results show that participants complete their tasks significantly faster with blue (single-hue, sequential) as compared to viridis (multi-hue, sequential), RdYlBu (divergent, red-yellow-blue), and jet (rainbow) colormaps."
- **[central]** Perceptual uniformity helped ACCURACY but not speed: viridis was the least error-prone colormap of the four, and overall correctness differed significantly between colormaps — establishing a speed/accuracy tradeoff (fast simple blue vs. most-accurate viridis) rather than a single 'best' colormap.
  > "Additionally, the overall correctness rate shows significant differences between colormaps, with viridis being the least error-prone among the colormaps studied."
- **[supporting]** The study is a controlled empirical comparison of four single- and multi-hue colormaps used to encode node attributes in node-link diagrams, measuring task completion time and correctness rate — i.e., a quantitative user study, not opinion.
  > "we compare four different single- and multi-hue colormaps for node attributes, and evaluate their effectiveness in terms of task completion time and correctness rate."
- **[supporting]** Prior colormap research had not examined network visualization specifically; colormap recommendations validated on other chart types (e.g., scalar fields, heatmaps) did not have empirical support transferred to node-link graphs before this study.
  > "While several studies have investigated the effects of colormaps in various types of information visualization, there have been no studies on their effects on network visualization."

#### Source `afa4310f` — quality: **secondary** · published: ?
- **[central]** Saturated pure-red-on-blue (and blue-on-red) text is illegible on screens because widely separated red and blue wavelengths cannot be focused by the eye simultaneously (chromatic aberration), making the text appear fuzzy and uncomfortable. This is an established legibility anti-pattern. [CONVENTION/mechanism]
  > "don't use red-on-blue text. It looks fuzzy and hurts to read ... Different wavelengths focus differently...Highly separated wavelengths (red & blue) can't be focused simultaneously"
- **[central]** Pure/saturated blue should not be used for text or small details against dark backgrounds because the fovea has very few S (blue) cones and cannot resolve small blue features unless contrast is high; aging further filters blue. This bears directly on legibility of saturated blue text on emissive screens. [CONVENTION, perceptual mechanism]
  > "Fovea has few S cones. Can't resolve small blue features (unless they have high contrast) ... don't use blue against dark backgrounds where small details matter (text!)"
- **[supporting]** Highly saturated colors cause visual fatigue and prolonged color afterimages; the recommended practice is desaturated/pastel colors. Cited evidence: air traffic controllers viewing strongly saturated green text for hours had trouble seeing pink/red for up to 15 minutes after their shift. [CONVENTION with empirical anecdote]
  > "Avoid saturated colors...Saturated colors can cause visual fatigue because the eye must keep refocusing on different wavelengths ... air traffic controllers...viewed strongly saturated green text...for many hours had trouble seeing pink or red...for up to 15 minutes after their shift"
- **[central]** A functional UI palette should use few hues — ideally one weakly-saturated hue plus black/white/grays — with a single reserved accent (e.g. red) that pops out preattentively; more colors increase perceived clutter and complexity. This is the dominance/single-accent convention. [CONVENTION]
  > "colors should be used sparingly...An interface with many colors appears more complex, more cluttered, and more distracting ... A simple and very effective color scheme uses just one hue...combined with black, white, and shades of gray ... On top of a scheme like that, a bit of red in an icon will pop out wonderfully"
- **[central]** Color must not be the sole carrier of meaning because red-green color blindness affects 8% of males and 0.4% of females; designs should use redundant encodings (brightness, location, shape). Microsoft Office's red (spelling) vs green (grammar) wavy underlines are cited as a failure of this rule. [CONVENTION with prevalence numbers]
  > "Red-green color blindness...8% of males...0.4% of females ... don't depend solely on color distinctions...use redundant signals: brightness, location, shape ... Microsoft Office applications fail in this respect: red wavy underlines indicate spelling errors, while identical green wavy underlines indicate grammar errors"

---

## Part B — Adversarial verifications (225)

Each claim was put to skeptical voters told to try to REFUTE it; ≥2/3 refutations kill it.

### Pass 1 — encoding / CVD / colormap science (2026-06-21)

#### V1 `a0701632` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Empirical testing of Tufte-style 'chartjunk' minimalism is mixed rather than settled: visual embellishment does not reliably destroy comprehension, so the blanket prescription to eliminate non-data ink is opinion/convention, not a uniformly supported empirical finding. This study extends prior embellishment work (Bateman et al.) to larger datasets (10+ data points) and time-limited viewing."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Although many well-cited theories or guidelines for visualization design advocate 'minimalism', designers tend to include a wide variety of visual embellishments in their charts... Various studies have shown mixed results depending on the details of the task and context."

*Evidence:* Claim VERIFIED. Source is peer-reviewed: Li & Moacdieh (2014), "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment," Proc. Human Factors and Ergonomics Society Annual Meeting 58(1):1516-1520 (HFES, a legitimate scientific society). Every component of the claim checks out: (1) The study DOES explicitly extend Bateman et al. (2010) to "larger data sets (10 or more data points)" with "different time limits for viewing" — verbatim match to the claim. (2) "Mixed rather than settled" is corroborated by convergent independent sources: Bateman 2010 (embellished-chart accuracy "no worse" than plain; significantly BETTER recall at 2-3 wks); Inbar et al. 2007 (users prefer non-minimalist bar graphs); and the data-ink literature directly stating "effects of ink are highly conditional on the features of the graph and task" so "simple rules like Tufte's will not suffice," and that Tufte's data-ink ratio "lacks empirical validation" as a universal rule. (3) "Does not reliably destroy comprehension" is accurately hedged and matches Bateman's null accuracy result. The supporting quote ("Various studies have shown mixed results depending on the details of the task and context") directly underwrites the core assertion. Refutation attempts failed: the one pro-minimalism finding (Gillan & Richman 1994, low data-ink => lower accuracy) confirms rather than overturns "mixed," and the literature's nuance that RANDOM/unrelated embellishments DO hurt topic recall (while meaningful data-related ones don't) is an omission the claim survives because its wording is appropriately weak ("does not reliably," "opinion/convention," not "chartjunk is good"). Claim strength is well-calibrated to source strength; not extraordinary, not outdated, not marketing. The only caveat for the knowledge base: the benefit-of-embellishment finding applies to MEANINGFUL, data-related embellishment, NOT a license for arbitrary decoration on analytical dashboards.
*Counter-source:* https://sites.stat.columbia.edu/gelman/communication/Bateman2010.pdf (Bateman et al. 2010, the study being extended); https://www.researchgate.net/publication/307953810_Data-ink_Ratio_and_Task_Complexity_in_Graph_Comprehension (data-ink ratio is task-conditional, "simple rules like Tufte's will not suffice"); https://www.researchgate.net/publication/220956267_Minimalism_in_information_visualization_Attitudes_towards_maximizing_the_data-ink_ratio (Inbar et al. 2007, users prefer non-minimalist) — all CORROBORATE rather than contradict the claim

#### V2 `a081c046` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The single most important factor in designing a good colormap is ensuring that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness across the map is uniform."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The most important factor in designing a colour map is to ensure that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness of the colours is uniform."

*Evidence:* Quote verified verbatim in primary source. Peter Kovesi, "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them" (arXiv:1509.03700, Sep 2015), abstract states exactly: "The most important factor in designing a colour map is to ensure that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness of the colours is uniform." This is a foundational, heavily-cited reference underpinning perceptually-uniform colormaps (matplotlib viridis-family, ColorCET). The claim faithfully represents it. Independent literature CORROBORATES rather than contradicts: "perceptual contrast between colours is dominated by the difference in lightness... any difference in hue or chroma/saturation is relatively unimportant" (Kovesi, echoed in Survey of Colormaps PMC4959790). TWO minor qualifications, not refutations: (a) the claim adds the word "single" ("single most important factor") which the source omits — but "the most important factor" already denotes rank #1, so this is a faithful paraphrase, not a distortion; (b) the claim drops Kovesi's own scoping — lightness primacy is strongest at HIGH spatial frequencies / for resolving fine structure (CIELAB is only perceptually uniform at low spatial frequencies), and multiple-criteria sources (Color Maker arXiv:2401.15032; Survey of Colormaps) stress design should not rely on a single metric since hue gives a complementary value-estimation channel. No credible source disputes lightness-uniformity as THE dominant/most-important factor for colormap perceptual contrast. Source quality (primary, domain-authoritative, 2015 but still current standard) matches claim strength. Not marketing, not outdated, not cherry-picked.
*Counter-source:* No source refutes it. Qualifying (not contradicting) sources: A Survey of Colormaps in Visualization (PMC4959790) and Color Maker (arXiv:2401.15032) note colormap design needs multiple criteria (monotonic lightness, hue progression, neutral zero) and hue provides a complementary value channel — but both still treat lightness uniformity as the dominant contrast factor, agreeing with the claim. Kovesi himself scopes the claim to high-spatial-frequency / fine-structure resolution.

#### V3 `a09355b1` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow (jet) color map has no perceptual ordering: viewers cannot infer the order of data values from the colors without consulting a legend, because the spectral/wavelength ordering of hues is not perceived as ordered (e.g., yellow is not seen as 'between' green and red in magnitude)."

**Source:** https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The rainbow color map confuses viewers through its lack of perceptual ordering, obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation, and actively misleads interpretation through the introduction of non-data-dependent gradients."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported and survives skeptical review. (1) Quote fidelity: The source (IEEE 4118486 = Borland & Taylor 2007, "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful"; the 403 paywall confirms the doc exists at that ID) states rainbow "confuses viewers through its lack of perceptual ordering." The claim is a faithful, conservative restatement, not an overreach. (2) Independent corroboration: The Zhou & Hansen survey (PMC4959790) states rainbow has "no perceptual order"; Ware's metric-vs-form framework holds rainbow fails order/metric reading because monotonic LUMINANCE, not hue, drives perceived magnitude; multiple syntheses note "the spectral order of visible light carries no inherent magnitude message" and that a legend is therefore essential. (3) Strongest counter-source does NOT refute this point: Ware, Stone et al. (2023) "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (ieeexplore 10128890 / scholars.unh.edu) defends rainbow only for a DIFFERENT property — detecting fine detail/high-spatial-frequency structure and familiarity — while conceding it is poor for reading ordered magnitude. So the leading pro-rainbow paper concurs on perceptual ordering. (4) Source quality matches claim strength: foundational peer-reviewed primary source + survey + Ware's experiments; not marketing/forum. (5) Not outdated: the specific ordering point is reaffirmed by both critics and defenders through 2023. Honest qualification (does not refute): Ware notes humans CAN sort spectral hues when the range is small/few colors — this limits the claim only for a handful of categorical hues, not for the continuous-magnitude reading the claim addresses.
*Counter-source:* Ware, Stone, Szafir et al. (2023), "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE CG&A 43(3):88-93 (ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/10128890) — the leading pro-rainbow paper; defends rainbow for fine-detail/form detection and familiarity but does NOT dispute the lack of perceptual ordering, so it does not contradict this specific claim. Minor qualifier: Ware also notes viewers can sort a small number of spectral hues correctly, limiting (not refuting) the claim at the categorical margin.

#### V4 `a0bf09f5` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Heer & Bostock's Mechanical Turk replication reproduced Cleveland & McGill's accuracy ranking of visual encodings for proportional judgment, and position encoding still significantly outperformed length encoding — confirming the elementary-perceptual-task hierarchy on a crowdsourced sample."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1753326.1753357 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The ranking of types by accuracy is consistent between the two experiments... position encoding still significantly outperformed length encoding."

*Evidence:* Both halves of the claim are verbatim-supported by the primary source (Heer & Bostock, CHI 2010), read directly from the authors' own PDF (vis.stanford.edu/files/2010-MTurk-CHI.pdf; mirror idl.cs.washington.edu/files/2010-MTurk-CHI.pdf). Results of Exp. 1A (proportional judgment replicating Cleveland & McGill) state verbatim: (1) RANKING CONSISTENT — "The ranking of types by accuracy is consistent between the two experiments (Figure 4)" and "the rough shape and ranking of judgment types by accuracy (T1-5) are preserved, supporting the validity of the crowdsourced study." (2) POSITION > LENGTH — "Types 4 and 5 are more accurate than in the original study, but position encoding still significantly outperformed length encoding." These map exactly onto the claim's two assertions (ranking reproduced; position significantly beats length). No contradicting or heavily-qualifying source found across multiple independent secondaries (Univ. of Iowa STAT4580 course notes, ResearchGate, Semantic Scholar, RStudio-pubs writeups, and later arXiv papers citing it), all of which describe the ranking as replicated/preserved. Source quality is maximal: peer-reviewed primary source read in full. Minor nuance (not refuting): the authors hedge that new results were "similar (though not identical)" and note Types 1 and 2 were closer in the crowdsourced run — but the claim's wording ("confirming... hierarchy") matches the paper's own conclusion and does not overstate.

#### V5 `a0de107a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Munzner's framework devotes a dedicated chapter, Ch 5 'Marks and Channels,' to visual encoding — i.e., she organizes encoding effectiveness around the concepts of marks (geometric primitives) and channels (the visual attributes that control their appearance), which is the structural basis for ranking encodings like position, length, area, and color."

**Source:** https://www.cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/vadbook/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Ch 5. Marks and Channels"

*Evidence:* VERIFIED by primary source plus multiple independent corroborations. (1) Munzner's own UBC textbook page (cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/vadbook/) lists the full TOC; Chapter 5 is titled exactly "Marks and Channels." Author is Tamara Munzner (female — claim's "she" is correct), book is "Visualization Analysis and Design" (CRC Press, 2014). (2) Independent confirmations: O'Reilly (oreilly.com/library/view/visualization-analysis-and/9781466508910/K14708_C005.xhtml), Taylor & Francis (taylorfrancis.com chapter DOI 10.1201/b17511-5), and Munzner's own course slides (cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/courses/547-17/slides/marks-4x4.pdf) all title Ch 5 "Marks and Channels." (3) The substantive claim is accurate to the chapter's content: "Marks are basic geometric elements that depict items or links" (= geometric primitives) and "channels control their appearance" / "the properties of marks that we modify ... are called channels" (= visual attributes). (4) The chapter is indeed the structural basis for ranking encodings — it states "Channels are not equally effective" and uses the effectiveness principle to rank channels by salience, matching magnitude-conveying channels (position, length) to ordered data and identity channels to categorical data, which is exactly the position>length>area>color-type ranking framing the claim describes. The supporting quote "Ch 5. Marks and Channels" matches the primary source verbatim. No contradicting evidence found. Minor non-issue: vadbook page is the 2014 1st ed.; a 2021 ed. exists but Ch 5 title/content are unchanged.

#### V6 `a194999a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Many vendor-supplied colormaps have highly uneven perceptual contrast across their range, including 'perceptual flat spots' that can hide a data feature as large as one tenth (10%) of the total data range."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Many colour maps provided by vendors have highly uneven perceptual contrast over their range. It is not uncommon for colour maps to have perceptual flat spots that can hide a feature as large as one tenth of the total data range."

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim from the abstract of Peter Kovesi, "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them" (arXiv:1509.03700, 2015), and is substantiated in the body, not merely asserted. The "10%" figure is the author's deliberately constructed test: a "sineramp" image (sine wave on a linear ramp, ~5 cycles/degree to match peak human contrast sensitivity) whose "amplitude is set so that the range from peak to trough represents a series of features that are 10% of the total data range." Named vendor colormaps (MathWorks, Geosoft, ESRI, Wolfram, plus jet/rainbow) demonstrably lose this 10% feature in perceptual flat spots when rendered on the test image. Perceptual contrast is measured via lightness difference (delta-L) between successive entries. Source quality matches claim strength: Kovesi (Centre for Exploration Targeting, UWA) is the recognized authority; though arXiv is a preprint, the work is independently corroborated by wide adoption — his CET/colorcet maps and the sineramp test are integrated into matplotlib, Bokeh, HoloViews, Datashader, and R's 'pals' package, making this the de facto standard for colormap perceptual-uniformity evaluation. No contradicting source found. Claim is appropriately hedged ("not uncommon," "can hide"). The only nuance: 10% is the test's chosen feature scale, so the precise meaning is "flat spots can hide features at the ~10%-of-range scale" — which is exactly what is claimed, not a refutation.

#### V7 `a1967e25` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "ColorBrewer divides color schemes into three types matched to data nature: sequential (for ordered low-to-high data, dominated by lightness steps), diverging (for data with a critical midpoint/break, varying both hue and lightness around an emphasized middle), and qualitative (for nominal/categorical data, relying primarily on hue with near-constant lightness and saturation). This typology is the foundational, widely-adopted framework for matching palette type to data type."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Sequential colour schemes (Figure 3a) imply order and are suited to representing data that ranges from low-to-high values... Lightness steps dominate the look of these schemes, usually with light colours for low data values and dark colours for high values. 'Dark equals more' is a standard cartographic convention. ... Diverging colour schemes (Figure 3b) should be used when a critical data class or break point needs to be emphasized. The break or class in the middle of the sequence is emphasized by a hue and lightness change... Qualitative colour schemes (Figure 3c) rely primarily on differences in hue to create a colour scheme that does not imply order, merely difference in kind... Qualitative schemes work best when hue is varied and saturation and lightness are kept or nearly constant."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported and survives refutation attempts. (1) Quote-match: the supplied passage maps clause-for-clause onto the claim — sequential = "imply order... low-to-high... Lightness steps dominate... light for low, dark for high... 'Dark equals more'"; diverging = "critical data class or break point... emphasized by a hue and lightness change"; qualitative = "rely primarily on differences in hue... does not imply order... saturation and lightness kept or nearly constant." No overreach. (2) Independent corroboration, no contradiction: the OFFICIAL colorbrewer2.org "Color scheme types" page verbatim confirms each ("Lightness steps dominate the look of these schemes, with light colors for low... dark for high"; qualitative "Hues are used to create the primary visual differences between classes"); Penn State GEOG 486 cartography course and WUSTL/Pratt sources reproduce the identical three-type typology. The only caveats found (diverging usually capped at 2 hues; ~12-swatch limit) are constraints WITHIN the typology, not disputes of it. (3) Source quality matches strength: primary peer-reviewed paper by the framework's own creators — Harrower, M. & Brewer, C.A. (2003), "ColorBrewer.org: An Online Tool for Selecting Colour Schemes for Maps," The Cartographic Journal 40(1):27-37 (labeled "REFEREED PAPER"; confirmed via Tandfonline DOI 10.1179/000870403235002042 and ADS 2003CartJ..40...27H). Brewer designed the schemes. (4) Not outdated: typology is still the live standard categorization in colorbrewer2.org, ggplot2 scale_*_brewer, RColorBrewer, matplotlib, d3; perceptually-uniform colormaps (Viridis) extend rather than overturn the data-type→scheme-type matching. (5) Not marketing/forum — peer-reviewed journal + authoritative tool docs. Minor note: "foundational/widely-adopted" is interpretive (not a verbatim quote) but is substantiated by near-universal toolchain adoption. Caveat on the PDF itself: WebFetch could not parse it (binary) and its text streams are FlateDecode-compressed so byte-scan found 0 keyword hits — verification therefore rests on the official ColorBrewer site, the two independent cartography sources, and the journal citation metadata rather than on re-reading the PDF body, but those independently confirm every clause.

#### V8 `a1bc2fa4` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Chart type (embellished Holmes-style vs. plain) significantly affected short-term recall of chart content, and the availability of a time limit affected both comprehension and short-term recall performance — i.e., embellishment effects are moderated by viewing-time constraints rather than being universally good or bad."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall."

*Evidence:* The two factual sub-claims ARE supported by the source's abstract (Li & Moacdieh 2014, "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment," Proceedings of the Human Factors and Ergonomics Society Annual Meeting 58(1):1516-1520). Multiple independent snippets reproduce the abstract: "the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall." That confirms (a) chart type → short-term recall, and (b) time limit → comprehension + short-term recall.

BUT the claim's load-bearing interpretive clause overreaches: "embellishment effects are MODERATED by viewing-time constraints rather than being universally good or bad." Moderation = a statistical INTERACTION (time limit changes the size/direction of the chart-type effect). The quoted abstract describes two SEPARATE MAIN EFFECTS, not an interaction: time limit affected comprehension+recall; chart type independently affected recall. No chart-type × time-limit interaction is stated in the supporting quote, so the "i.e., moderated" rephrasing is not established by the evidence offered. The original SAGE page returned HTTP 403 (paywalled), so I could not confirm/deny an interaction in the full results body — but the only quote provided does not support moderation.

Source-strength concern: this is a ~4-5 page lightly-reviewed HFES conference proceedings piece, not a full peer-reviewed journal article — adequate for the narrow descriptive finding, thin for the stronger "effects are moderated" generalization. Corroborating literature explicitly characterizes Li & Moacdieh (2014) as showing "mixed results depending on the details of the task and context," consistent with the modest reading, not a clean moderation narrative. Per the checklist (unsupported-by-quote on the distinctive clause; default-to-refuted when uncertain), the claim as worded is refuted; a corrected version stating two independent main effects (without "moderated") would be accurate.
*Counter-source:* https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/1541931214581316 (abstract, as reproduced verbatim in WebSearch snippets; full text inaccessible — HTTP 403)

#### V9 `a1d2244a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "ColorBrewer divides color schemes into three types matched to data nature: sequential (for ordered low-to-high data, dominated by lightness steps), diverging (for data with a critical midpoint/break, varying both hue and lightness around an emphasized middle), and qualitative (for nominal/categorical data, relying primarily on hue with near-constant lightness and saturation). This typology is the foundational, widely-adopted framework for matching palette type to data type."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Sequential colour schemes (Figure 3a) imply order and are suited to representing data that ranges from low-to-high values... Lightness steps dominate the look of these schemes, usually with light colours for low data values and dark colours for high values. 'Dark equals more' is a standard cartographic convention. ... Diverging colour schemes (Figure 3b) should be used when a critical data class or break point needs to be emphasized. The break or class in the middle of the sequence is emphasized by a hue and lightness change... Qualitative colour schemes (Figure 3c) rely primarily on differences in hue to create a colour scheme that does not imply order, merely difference in kind... Qualitative schemes work best when hue is varied and saturation and lightness are kept or nearly constant."

*Evidence:* CLAIM CONFIRMED by primary source + canonical tool + independent corroboration; three adversarial searches for criticism found NO dispute of the typology itself.

1) SUPPORTING QUOTE IS FAITHFUL. Canonical colorbrewer2.org/learnmore/schemes_full.html independently confirms all three definitions nearly verbatim: Sequential = "Lightness steps dominate the look of these schemes, with light colors for low data values to dark colors for high data values" (ordered low-to-high). Diverging = "the break or class emphasized by a hue and lightness change to represent a critical value in the data such as the mean, median, or zero." Qualitative = "hues are used to create the primary visual differences between classes... best suited to representing nominal or categorical data." Direct PDF text extraction (zlib decompression of the cs.rpi.edu PDF) recovered the diverging/qualitative recipe passage confirming "middle critical class rather than a critical break" and shared 15-color diverging sets — consistent with the quoted text.

2) SOURCE QUALITY MATCHES CLAIM STRENGTH. Primary = refereed paper (extracted text retains "REFEREED PAPER" marker; Brewer, Harrower et al., Cartographic Perspectives 2003). Cynthia Brewer is the originating cartographer. Plus canonical colorbrewer2.org. Both are authoritative, not marketing.

3) "FOUNDATIONAL, WIDELY-ADOPTED" IS TRUE, NOT OVERREACH. The sequential/diverging/qualitative typology is embedded as of 2024-2026 in matplotlib, seaborn (choose_colorbrewer_palette), R (RColorBrewer), ggplot2 (scale_colour_brewer / scale_fill_brewer), and GIS software. Launched 2001-2002, still the active standard.

4) NO CONTRADICTING EVIDENCE. Targeted adversarial searches for "criticism/flawed/oversimplified" surfaced ONLY application caveats — don't misapply diverging palettes, set the midpoint to a meaningful center (zero/mean), diverging schemes needn't be symmetric. These refine HOW to use the types and do not dispute the three-category framework. No scholarly source challenges the typology's validity.

5) MINOR NUANCE (not a refutation): claim says qualitative keeps "near-constant lightness and saturation." The original paper states exactly this ("saturation and lightness are kept or nearly constant"), but colorbrewer2.org's deployed palettes use "subtle lightness differences" for print/CVD robustness — a practice refinement, not a contradiction. The claim's hedges ("primarily" hue, "near-constant") accurately track the paper's own wording.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. Adversarial searches (criticism/flawed/oversimplified ColorBrewer typology; diverging-scheme criticism) returned only application caveats (PolicyViz, course/GIS pages on setting a meaningful midpoint and non-symmetric diverging schemes), which refine usage rather than dispute the sequential/diverging/qualitative framework.

#### V10 `a1de9ef2` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Cleveland & McGill (1984) is the foundational paper that proposed putting graphical methods for data analysis and presentation on a scientific footing via the study of 'graphical perception' (how people decode quantitative information from graphs)."

**Source:** https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01621459.1984.10478080 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The subject of graphical methods for data analysis and for data presentation needs a scientific foundation. In this article we take a few steps in the direction of establishing such a foundation."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported and tightly matches the quote. The paper's abstract (confirmed verbatim across 3 independent search-engine extractions plus secondary sources) opens: "The subject of graphical methods for data analysis and for data presentation needs a scientific foundation. In this article we take a few steps in the direction of establishing such a foundation. Our approach is based on graphical perception—the visual decoding of information encoded on graphs..." This exactly states the claim's two assertions: (a) proposing a scientific footing for graphical methods, (b) doing so via the study of graphical perception (how people decode quantitative info from graphs). Source quality matches claim strength: primary is JASA 1984 (top peer-reviewed statistics journal), pp. 531-554, cited thousands of times, universally described as "seminal"/"a touchstone." Adversarial checks: (1) No overreach — the claim paraphrases the abstract almost word-for-word. (2) Disputes I found (Kosara & Skau on pie-chart angle decoding; Heer & Bostock 2010 replication; "Risks of Ranking" arXiv 2212.10533; Talbot et al.) all contest SPECIFIC empirical rankings of perceptual tasks, NOT the foundational/scientific-footing framing — critics affirm the paper IS foundational while quibbling with details, which reinforces rather than refutes this claim. (3) "Foundational 1984 paper" is a historical claim, cannot be outdated by later refinements. (4) Not marketing. Only caveat: primary PDF was inaccessible (tandfonline 403 paywall; York/PKU mirrors ECONNREFUSED; no local PDF tooling), so verbatim confirmation rests on convergent secondary extractions rather than my own read of the primary — but convergence is strong and the abstract is reproduced identically everywhere.
*Counter-source:* No source disputes the foundational claim. Located critiques (Kosara & Skau on pie-chart angle perception; Heer & Bostock 2010; arXiv 2212.10533 "The Risks of Ranking") challenge only the specific perceptual-task accuracy rankings, not that the paper proposed a scientific foundation for graphics via graphical perception. These critiques explicitly call the paper "seminal"/"a touchstone," corroborating its foundational status.

#### V11 `a1e9cde3` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Viridis, though the 'gold standard' colormap, is optimized for normal vision and is not optimal for color vision deficiency; cividis is designed to enable nearly-identical data interpretation for both normal-vision and CVD viewers."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "it is optimal for those with normal vision and not CVD"

*Evidence:* The primary source (Nuñez, Anderton & Renslow 2018, PLOS ONE 13(7):e0199239) directly supports both halves of the claim. Part 1 (Viridis): the paper states verbatim "Right now, viridis is seen as the gold standard since it follows each of the design principles discussed above; however, it is optimal for those with normal vision and not CVD." Part 2 (Cividis): the abstract states cividis "enables nearly-identical visual-data interpretation to both groups [normal vision and CVD], is perceptually uniform in hue and brightness, and increases in brightness linearly." Independent secondary sources (marcosci/cividis R package "Color Vision Deficiency Optimized Version Of Viridis", COMSOL, CRAN viridis vignette) restate the same characterization with no contradiction. Source quality is maximal for this claim: it is the peer-reviewed primary paper that defined Cividis, exactly matching the claim's strength; 2018 and uncontradicted, not outdated; a methods paper, not marketing. The one qualifying finding I located — a 455-participant XAI heatmap study reporting that accessibility colormaps like cividis did not always improve confidence/effort for visually-impaired users — addresses downstream real-world efficacy, NOT the paper's design intent, so it does not refute a claim worded as "designed to enable." Confidence is high rather than absolute only because the phrase "not optimal for CVD" could be misread as "bad for CVD" — Viridis is in fact already reasonably CVD-friendly by design and Cividis merely improves on it — but the claim's own hedged wording ("gold standard," "not optimal") preserves this nuance and does not overstate.
*Counter-source:* No credible source refutes the claim. Nearest qualifier: a 455-participant XAI heatmap user study finding accessibility colormaps (incl. cividis) did not mitigate, and sometimes worsened, confidence/effort gaps for visually-impaired users — but this concerns real-world efficacy, not the paper's stated design intent, so it does not contradict the claim as worded.

#### V12 `a1ea6e34` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color-vision-deficiency (CVD) safety varies systematically by scheme type: sequential schemes are all usable by red-green colorblind readers because they encode lightness differences; most diverging hue pairs were chosen to be CVD-safe (except red-yellow-green and full-spectral); but almost all qualitative (categorical) schemes are difficult for colorblind readers. This gives a concrete, evidence-informed rule for designing CVD-safe palettes."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Generally, the hue pairs for diverging schemes were selected to accommodate colour-blind readers, with the exception of red-yellow-green and full spectral schemes. Almost all of the qualitative schemes are difcult for colour-blind readers and all of the sequential schemes are useful to them, because they include visible lightness differences."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully paraphrases Harrower & Brewer (2003), "ColorBrewer.org: An Online Tool for Selecting Colour Schemes for Maps," The Cartographic Journal 40(1):27-37 (DOI 10.1179/000870403235002042) — a peer-reviewed primary source by the actual ColorBrewer authors. The supporting quote matches the paper's known content, and all three sub-claims are independently corroborated by the tool's own operationalization at colorbrewer2.org and multiple secondary sources: (1) Diverging schemes are CVD-safe except specific exceptions — colorbrewer2.org flags exactly RdYlGn (red-yellow-green), Spectral (full-spectral), and RdGy as NOT colorblind-safe while the other six diverging schemes pass. (2) Almost all qualitative schemes fail for CVD readers — confirmed: only Dark2, Paired, Set2 are safe at 3 categories, only Paired at 4. (3) All sequential schemes are usable because of visible lightness differences — confirmed by every independent source ("All sequential palettes from ColorBrewer are colorblind-safe, including one-hue, two-hue, and three-hue palettes like Blues, OrRed, YlOrRd") and by ColorBrewer's own colorblind-safe filter. The mechanism is sound: red-green CVD leaves the luminance/lightness channel intact (opponent-process theory), so lightness-encoded sequences survive. The only legitimate caveat is a discriminability ceiling — adjacent steps get hard to tell apart for ALL viewers beyond ~7-9 classes — but that is a step-count limit, not hue-confusion failure, and does not contradict the claim. No credible source disputes any of the three sub-claims; sources that add nuance (perceptual-uniformity advantages of Viridis) concern a different property, not CVD safety. The claim is correctly attributed, well-supported, source quality (peer-reviewed primary) matches claim strength, and rests on a stable perceptual fact rather than a fast-moving field, so the 2003 date is not a liability. Refutation attempts on quote-misread, contradiction, and source-adequacy all failed.
*Counter-source:* https://colorbrewer2.org/ (authoritative operationalization corroborating the claim, not contradicting it); secondary corroboration: https://datavizpyr.com/introduction-to-color-palettes-in-r-with-rcolorbrewer/ . No credible contradicting source found; the only qualifier (discriminability ceiling at high class counts) does not negate the lightness-based CVD-safety mechanism.

*Claim under review:* "For encoding quantitative magnitude, area is among the least accurate channels: the study's new angle/area judgment types confirmed the psychophysical prediction that area performs worse than angle, and both perform significantly worse than position. This directly bears on using dot AREA (bubble/scatter) to encode magnitude."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1753326.1753357 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the new results match expectations: psychophysical theory predicts area to perform worse than angle, and both to be significantly worse than position."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source. I downloaded the actual Heer & Bostock CHI 2010 PDF (vis.stanford.edu/files/2010-MTurk-CHI.pdf, 6pp) and extracted the verbatim passage: "Indeed, the new results match expectations: psychophysical theory [7,34] predicts area to perform worse than angle, and both to be significantly worse than position. Theory also suggests that angle should perform worse than length, but the results do not support this." The supporting quote is exact. Crucially, "the new results MATCH expectations" means their EMPIRICAL data (new judgment types T6=angle, T7=circular area) confirmed the area<angle<position ordering — the claim's wording "confirmed the psychophysical prediction" is accurate, not an overreach.

(1) Quote support: full. The claim correctly isolates ONLY the supported portion. The single prediction their results did NOT support (angle worse than length) is explicitly excluded from the claim.

(2) Contradicting evidence: none credible. The lone apparent contradiction — a WebFetch summary of a UIowa course page asserting Cleveland & McGill found "area more accurate than angle" — is an AI-paraphrase artifact, refuted by (a) Cleveland & McGill's (1984) own published task ordering: position(1,2) > length(3) > direction(4) > angle(5) > slope(6) > area(7) > volume(8), where area ranks BELOW angle; and (b) the H&B primary text. Independent convergence: Stevens' power law gives area an exponent ≈0.7 (compressive), causing systematic underestimation of area-encoded magnitude (Wikipedia/Stevens psychophysics; multiple viz sources confirm a circle must be ~2.7x area to look 2x). This independently supports area being a poor magnitude channel.

(3) Source quality: gold standard — peer-reviewed CHI 2010 replication, verbatim quote from canonical PDF, grounded in Cleveland & McGill (1984, JASA) and Stevens psychophysics.

(4) Not outdated: static-encoding graphical perception is settled; these remain the canonical 2026 citations.

(5) Not marketing: peer-reviewed replication study.

Bubble/scatter inference is sound: T7 "circular area" IS the bubble-size case. The claim is accurate and arguably understated (it confirms empirically, not just theoretically).
*Counter-source:* Only apparent counter was an AI-generated WebFetch summary of homepage.divms.uiowa.edu/~luke/classes/STAT4580-2025/percep.html claiming area>angle in Cleveland-McGill; this is a paraphrase hallucination, contradicted by the actual primary texts and by Cleveland & McGill's own published ordering (area ranks below angle).

#### V14 `a2cceed9` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A colormap should use a linear (monotonic) increase in lightness to avoid the perception of gradients that are not actually present in the data — the core flaw that makes rainbow/jet colormaps misleading."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A linear increase in lightness should be used to avoid the perception of gradients that are not present."

*Evidence:* The claim is well-supported and survives refutation attempts.

PRIMARY SOURCE (verified): Nuñez, Anderton & Renslow (2018), PLOS ONE, "Optimizing colormaps with consideration for color vision deficiency to enable accurate interpretation of scientific data" — directly states "First, a linear increase in lightness should be used to avoid the perception of gradients that are not present." It also explains the false-gradient mechanism: colormaps that "quickly change in brightness and hue, causing small changes in the underlying data to seem significant or, in regions where the values change too slowly, large changes to seem negligible." The supporting quote is accurate and the claim is a faithful paraphrase, not an overreach.

CORROBORATION (independent peer-reviewed): Borland & Taylor (2007), IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications / TVCG, "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful" — lists as the FIRST of three arguments against rainbow/jet maps that they "are not monotonic in luminance, which can distort the shapes people infer from the data." This is exactly the false-gradient/false-feature mechanism the claim attributes to non-monotonic lightness. Kindlmann et al. (2002) and the TVCG colormap survey (PMC4959790) both state that lightness is the PRIMARY driver of perceived color difference (hue secondary), making non-monotonic lightness the dominant cause of perceived-but-absent gradients. Perceptually-uniform maps (viridis, inferno, cividis, CET) are explicitly built on monotonic luminance for this reason.

REFUTATION CHECK — minor nuances, none disqualifying:
1. "Core flaw" framing: Borland & Taylor name THREE flaws (non-monotonic luminance; non-perceptual-uniformity; banding). So lightness is not the ONLY flaw. But the claim ties it specifically to "the perception of gradients not actually present," and non-monotonic luminance is precisely the flaw the literature links to that effect (distorting inferred shapes / false edges). The claim does not assert it is the sole flaw. Defensible.
2. "Linear" vs "monotonic": the general principle is monotonic lightness; strictly LINEAR is the specific recommendation of the 2018 paper (and what perceptually-uniform maps approximate). The claim hedges this with "linear (monotonic)" and quotes a source that does say "linear." Not an overreach relative to the cited source.
3. Scope: diverging colormaps are not monotonic across the full range (lightness peaks at the midpoint), only monotonic per-half. But the claim is in the standard sequential/continuous-magnitude context; this is a scope nuance, not a contradiction.

No credible source disputes the underlying principle; the dissenting paper "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE 2023) argues rainbows can be acceptable for some tasks but does NOT dispute that non-monotonic luminance creates false gradients. Source quality (PLOS ONE primary + IEEE TVCG corroboration) matches the claim strength; sources are current (2002-2018, principle reaffirmed through 2023-2025 literature).
*Counter-source:* Borland & Taylor (2007) "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful" lists non-monotonic luminance as only ONE of three flaws (not unambiguously "the core" one); and diverging colormaps intentionally use non-monotonic (midpoint-peaked) lightness — both are scope/emphasis nuances the claim already hedges, not contradictions.

#### V15 `a3332791` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled within-subjects experiment (20 participants), there was no measurable difference in interpretation accuracy between heavily embellished charts (Nigel Holmes 'chartjunk' style, low data-ink) and plain minimalist charts for describing the chart's subject, its categories, or its trend - directly challenging the Tufte data-ink/chartjunk premise that embellishment harms comprehension."

**Source:** https://scottbateman.github.io/publication/2010-01-01-Useful-junk-The-effects-of-visual-embellishment-on-comprehension-and-memorability-of-charts (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "First, we did not find any difference in people's in terpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts, despite the fact that the embellished charts contained a much larger amount of non-data-ink."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source (peer-reviewed ACM CHI 2010 full paper, PDF extracted directly from vis.csail.mit.edu/Bateman-UsefulJunk.pdf). The supporting quote is VERBATIM accurate: "First, we did not find any difference in people's interpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts, despite the fact that the embellished charts contained a much larger amount of non-data-ink." Every specific in the claim checks out: (1) n=20 confirmed ("20 participants... 7 graduate, 13 undergraduate"). (2) Within-subjects confirmed in substance — "Each participant saw... fourteen charts, alternating between Holmes and plain versions. Each participant saw only one version of each chart... Presentation order... counterbalanced" — so each participant experienced both the embellished (Nigel Holmes-style) and plain conditions. (3) The description task explicitly measured "subject, values, trend, and message"; Figure 4 plots subject/categories/trend scores for Holmes vs Plain, and the abstract states "people's accuracy in describing the embellished charts was no worse than for plain charts." (4) "Directly challenging Tufte" is the authors' own framing — their results "questioned some of the premises of the minimalist approach." SKEPTICAL CHECKS: Stephen Few's well-known critique (perceptualedge.com chartjunk_debate) attacks GENERALIZABILITY — that the "plain" charts were poorly designed and the Holmes charts were skilled (arguably not true chartjunk), plus the small n=20 — but does NOT dispute the within-study null on interpretation accuracy. Later work (Skau/Harrison/Kosara 2015 CGF; Li et al. replication) refines the debate but does not overturn this specific finding. Claim is correctly scoped to what the experiment found, uses "challenging" not "refuting," and cites a top-tier peer-reviewed venue. One minor nuance: design is more precisely "within-subjects on the embellishment factor with each individual chart shown in only one version per participant," but "within-subjects experiment (20 participants)" is a fair, non-misleading characterization.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (perceptualedge.com) — critiques generalizability (poorly-designed plain charts, skilled Holmes charts not meeting a strict chartjunk definition, n=20), but does NOT dispute the within-study interpretation-accuracy null; later studies (Skau/Harrison/Kosara 2015; Li et al. replication) qualify rather than refute it.

#### V16 `a3458f73` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow colormap's luminance varies non-monotonically and uncontrolled across its range, which obscures/hides fine detail in scalar data (low-luminance and high-luminance bands suppress perceived contrast unevenly)."

**Source:** https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation"

*Evidence:* CLAIM SURVIVES skeptical review — well-supported, current, primary-sourced, and corroborated even by its main academic dissent.

(1) QUOTE SUPPORT — exact, no overreach. Primary source is Borland & Taylor 2007, "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications (doc 4118486). The paper's own wording confirmed via multiple mirrors: the rainbow map "obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation." This directly supports both "uncontrolled luminance variation" and "obscures/hides detail." The IEEE page is paywalled but the verbatim quote was independently confirmed (ADS, Semantic Scholar, MathWorks annotated bibliography).

(2) "NON-MONOTONIC" qualifier independently verified. Jet's L* in CIELAB is demonstrably non-monotonic ("Rainbow (JET) colormap in L*a*b* space is obviously not a monotonic function in L*"). Kovesi 2015 ("Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them," arXiv:1509.03700) documents the mechanism precisely: rainbow maps have "perceptual flat spots that can hide a feature as large as one tenth of the total data range," with an "extended section of near constant lightness between cyan and yellow" and "reversal of lightness gradients at yellow and red." His sine-wave-on-a-ramp test image operationalizes exactly the claim's mechanism: where perceptual (luminance) contrast is low, the superimposed detail vanishes. This is direct empirical/methodological support for "suppress perceived contrast unevenly."

(3) ATTEMPTED REFUTATION via the strongest dissent FAILS. Ware, Stone & Szafir 2023 "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE doc 10128890) is the leading paper pushing back on rainbow-hate — but it does NOT dispute the claim's mechanism. It CONFIRMS it: "feature resolving power correlates strongly with luminance variation," and "because luminance changes back and forth across the scale, it is impossible to read shape from a visualization that uses a rainbow colormap." It only argues this limitation is often practically irrelevant (e.g., reading a value off a key on a weather map). That qualifies the IMPORTANCE of the effect for certain tasks, not the TRUTH of the luminance-obscures-detail mechanism. The dissent reinforces, not refutes.

(4) SOURCE QUALITY exceeds claim strength: primary peer-reviewed (IEEE CG&A 2007) + independent corroboration from Kovesi 2015 + Stevens/HESS 2021 hydrology guidance ("non-linear and erratic change of hue and luminance ... causes misrepresentation of data values"). Multiple converging primary sources; not marketing or forum speculation.

(5) NOT OUTDATED: 2007 primary, corroborated 2015/2021, mechanism still affirmed by 2023 dissent; rests on stable opponent-process perceptual science.

MINOR CAVEAT (does not refute): the parenthetical "low-luminance and high-luminance bands suppress perceived contrast unevenly" is the claimant's accurate gloss of the documented "flat spots / non-uniform perceptual contrast" mechanism, not a verbatim quote. It is a faithful restatement.
*Counter-source:* Ware, Stone & Szafir (2023) "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE TVCG/CG&A doc 10128890 — the leading academic pushback. But it CONFIRMS luminance drives feature detection ("feature resolving power correlates strongly with luminance variation"; "impossible to read shape from a rainbow colormap"); it only argues the effect is often practically irrelevant for key-lookup tasks. It qualifies practical importance, not the mechanism's validity — so it does not refute the claim.

The "up to" hedge makes it defensible.

This qualifies — but does not refute — the claim, since the claim uses "up to 8%" (an upper bound for Northern European populations), which is the correct framing.

*Claim under review:* "The rainbow colormap's luminance varies non-monotonically and uncontrolled across its range, which obscures/hides fine detail in scalar data (low-luminance and high-luminance bands suppress perceived contrast unevenly)."

**Supporting quote:** "obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation"

*Evidence:* Claim survives adversarial review on all checks. (1) SUPPORTED BY QUOTE: The supporting quote "obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation" is verbatim from the primary source, Borland & Taylor (2007), "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful" (IEEE CG&A, doc 4118486). The paper explicitly states rainbow maps "are not monotonic in luminance" and that "detail can be seen at much lower contrast in luminance-varying grayscale images than with rainbow color maps." No overreach. (2) NO CREDIBLE CONTRADICTION: The strongest potential refutation — Ware, Stone & Szafir (2023) "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE doc 10128890), written specifically to rebut the harmful-rainbow dogma — CONCEDES the luminance point, acknowledging classic rainbows' "nonmonotonic luminance," "erratic luminance profiles," and "uneven hue distribution," arguing only that *well-designed* rainbows can mitigate these. Defenders confirm the physical fact; they dispute only the universality of the "harmful" conclusion, not this claim. (3) SOURCE QUALITY: Three independent peer-reviewed sources converge — Borland & Taylor 2007 (primary, IEEE), Kovesi 2015 "Good Colour Maps" (arXiv 1509.03700: "flat spots of low perceptual contrast that prevent you from seeing features," can "hide a feature as large as one tenth of the total data range"; jet "lightest at cyan and yellow... not monotonic"), and Ware/Stone/Szafir 2023. Sufficient for the claim's strength. (4) NOT OUTDATED: 2023 literature still affirms the characterization; the physics of jet (lightest at cyan/yellow, non-monotonic) is settled. (5) NOT MARKETING/SPECULATION: peer-reviewed visualization science. MINOR PRECISION CAVEAT (does not refute): the parenthetical phrasing "low-luminance and high-luminance bands suppress perceived contrast unevenly" is a loose paraphrase — the mechanism in the literature is that high-luminance bands (cyan/yellow) draw the eye and fabricate false features while low-gradient flat spots hide real detail; net effect (uneven contrast that both hides and invents detail) is exactly correct.
*Counter-source:* Ware, Stone & Szafir (2023), "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE CG&A, doc 10128890) — the principal dissent against rainbow-harmful dogma — yet it explicitly concedes classic rainbows have nonmonotonic/erratic luminance, so it does NOT contradict this specific claim; it only qualifies the broader conclusion.

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 (primary)

Human Factors and Ergonomics Society Annual Meeting, DOI 10.1177/1541931214581316 (peer-reviewed proceedings). The FACTUAL CORE of the claim is supported — the abstract states verbatim: "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall." So "time limit affected comprehension+recall" and "chart type affected short-term recall" are accurate.

The quote reports two SEPARATE MAIN EFFECTS, not an interaction — two main effects on the same outcome is not moderation. The abstract as quoted contains no interaction term. Two WebSearch glosses claimed "this indicates an interaction effect," but that is the search model's inference (and logically incorrect), not the paper's wording. Full text could not be retrieved to confirm whether an interaction was actually tested (SAGE and ResearchGate both returned HTTP 403).

But the specific causal word "moderated" exceeds what the cited quote establishes. Refuted on overreach grounds (claim strength > evidence in the quote), not on factual falsity. Note also the claim's source is distinct from the more-famous Bateman et al. 2010 "Useful Junk?" (CHI), which it extends; the two were not conflated here.
