#### V20 `a441e3c4` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Prior perceptually-uniform colormap design work erred by relying on CIELAB, because CIELAB is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies — so it does not guarantee uniformity for the higher-frequency detail in real data images."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Previous work in the design of perceptually uniform colour maps has mostly failed to recognise that CIELAB space is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies."

*Evidence:* Claim is directly and faithfully supported by the primary source AND independently corroborated by established vision science.

PRIMARY SOURCE (verified): Peter Kovesi, "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them," arXiv:1509.03700 (2015), University of Western Australia. The paper states verbatim: "CIELAB space is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies" and that "previous work in the design of perceptually uniform colour maps has mostly failed to recognise" this. The claim restates this without overreach. Kovesi elaborates: "CIELAB space is only perceptually uniform for large patches of colour presented at very low spatial frequencies... at fine spatial frequencies perceptual contrast is dominated by lightness difference; chroma and hue are relatively unimportant."

INDEPENDENT CORROBORATION (the key skeptical test — is this just Kovesi's assertion?): No, it is established science. (a) CIELAB's color-difference formulas (CIE76/CIEDE2000) were derived from experiments on LARGE ISOLATED uniform color patches — documented widely incl. Wikipedia/Imatest: "suitable for use on large uniform color targets, but not on images, because color sensitivity changes as a function of spatial pattern." (b) S-CIELAB (Zhang & Wandell, 1990s) was created SPECIFICALLY to fix this — it adds a spatial pre-filtering step (contrast-sensitivity-function filters) before CIE76 precisely because plain CIELAB fails on spatial detail. This predates and independently confirms Kovesi. (c) Spatio-chromatic CSF literature (Optica/JOSA-A, PMC7405764): chromatic contrast sensitivity is LOW-PASS, falling off rapidly above ~2-3 cycles/deg, while luminance is BAND-PASS — the mechanistic reason lightness dominates at high frequency.

CHECKLIST: (1) supported by quote — yes, exact; (2) contradicting evidence — none, only confirmation; (3) source quality — strong, peer-distributed and heavily-cited foundational paper plus older independent work; (4) outdated — no, describes property of the 1976 CIELAB standard, still current understanding in 2026; (5) marketing — no, mechanism-based technical paper.

MINOR NUANCE (refinement, not refutation): "erred by relying on CIELAB" slightly overstates Kovesi's framing — he says prior work failed to RECOGNIZE the limitation, and his own fix still works WITHIN CIELAB by enforcing uniform lightness increments rather than abandoning it. So CIELAB remains usable for colormap design; you just cannot trust its chroma/hue uniformity at high spatial frequency. The substance of the claim (CIELAB uniform only at low spatial frequency; this undermined naive prior approaches) is correct.

Sources: arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary); en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CIELAB_color_space; S-CIELAB (Zhang & Wandell); opg.optica.org/josaa/abstract.cfm?uri=josaa-15-3-563; ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC7405764.

#### V21 `a4691224` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The single most important factor in designing a good colormap is ensuring that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness across the map is uniform."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The most important factor in designing a colour map is to ensure that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness of the colours is uniform."

*Evidence:* VERBATIM ACCURATE + WELL-SUPPORTED. The quote is an exact transcription of Kovesi (2015) "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them" (arXiv:1509.03700), appearing in both the abstract and Section 2: "The most important factor in designing a colour map is to ensure that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness of the colours [across the map] is uniform." The claim preserves wording faithfully — no misread or overreach. Author's own rationale supports it: Section 2 states "the perceptual contrast between colours is dominated by the difference in the lightness of the colours" and "Any difference in hue or chroma/saturation is relatively unimportant" for resolving fine spatial structures; non-uniform lightness causes "flat spots" (hiding features up to ~1/10 of data range) and "false anomalies." BROAD FIELD CONSENSUS, not a lone voice: Borland & Taylor (2007), Rogowitz & Treinish/Kalvin, and Crameri et al. (2020) independently identify uneven luminance as the primary failure mode of the rainbow colormap; the standard recommendation "perceptually uniform" is defined as constant incremental perceived (luminance) contrast. SOURCE QUALITY adequate for the claim: Kovesi's paper is the technical basis of the widely-adopted CET/ColorCet perceptually-uniform colormap suite (42pp, 25 figs, peer-reviewed/heavily cited). SCOPE CAVEAT (qualifier, not refutation): the claim governs CONTINUOUS/SEQUENTIAL colormaps that encode magnitude via color, and is strongest for feature-resolution at fine spatial frequencies. For CATEGORICAL color encoding the goal differs (hue/discriminability, not lightness uniformity), and Reda et al. (CHI 2018) show effectiveness is task- and spatial-frequency-dependent (monotonic luminance best for magnitude judgments but not uniformly best for every spatial-comparison task). These narrow the domain but do not contradict the core principle for continuous maps. The "single most important" framing is the author's stated design principle, well-reasoned and echoed across the literature — appropriate to cite as such. A knowledge base should attach the scope note (continuous colormaps / fine-feature tasks), but the claim itself stands.
*Counter-source:* Reda et al., "Graphical Perception of Continuous Quantitative Maps: the Effects of Spatial Frequency and Colormap Design" (CHI 2018, dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3173574.3173846) — shows colormap effectiveness is task- and spatial-frequency-dependent (monotonic luminance most accurate for magnitude judgments but not superior for all spatial-comparison tasks); qualifies universality but does not refute the core principle for continuous colormaps. Scope limit also implied by categorical-color work (e.g., arXiv:2404.03787, arXiv:2511.14009): for categorical encoding, lightness-uniformity is not the objective.

#### V22 `a49dfcec` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow colormap introduces visual artifacts not present in the data: it creates sharp/false boundaries where hue transitions are abrupt and flattens regions where hue changes slowly, biasing perceived structure and causing misinterpretation."

**Source:** https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "creates sharp boundaries that are not in the data"

*Evidence:* Source = Borland & Taylor (2007), "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications 27(2):14-17 (peer-reviewed). Both halves of the claim are supported. (a) FALSE BOUNDARIES: the peer-reviewed Liu & Heer survey "A Survey of Colormaps in Visualization" (IEEE TVCG) quotes Borland & Taylor [16] verbatim — rainbow "has no perceptual order, hides details and creates sharp boundaries that are not in the data" — matching the supporting quote exactly. (b) FLATTENING where hue changes slowly: this is the perceptual-nonuniformity corollary, independently established by Rogowitz & Treinish (1998, "The End of the Rainbow") — rainbow "can hide small details in data and create artificial boundaries, which can greatly bias the perception of structures," and "creates perceived contours that may not exist in the data, and structures that fall within such false contours would not be represented." Three independent peer-reviewed sources converge (Rogowitz/Treinish, Borland/Taylor, Liu/Heer survey). REBUTTAL CHECK: The strongest counter, Ware, Stone & Szafir (2023) "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE CG&A), does NOT deny this mechanism — it concedes the perceptual issues (non-monotonic luminance, shape distortion) and argues they don't matter for certain tasks, even leveraging the sharp-transition effect as an advantage for precise value-reading off a key. That reframes the "harmful" verdict, not the factual mechanism in this claim. Not outdated (reaffirmed 2021-2023), not marketing, source quality matches claim strength.
*Counter-source:* Ware, Stone & Szafir (2023), "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications 43(3):88-93 — but it concedes the perceptual mechanism and only disputes the blanket "harmful" verdict, so it does not refute this specific claim.

#### V23 `a4d3903e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Cividis was created by mathematically optimizing colormaps using the CIECAM02-UCS (Uniform Color Space) model via a Python module (cmaputil), ensuring perceptual uniformity in the a'-b' plane and a linearized, maximized J' (lightness) range."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Equal Euclidean distances between values in this space leads to an equal color difference perception"

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED by primary peer-reviewed source (Nuñez, Anderton, Renslow 2018, PLOS ONE pone.0199239), the paper that introduced Cividis. Every claim element maps to verbatim text: (1) CIECAM02-UCS — "By converting between sRGB colorspace and CIECAM02-UCS colorspace, we can ensure colormaps are optimized for viewing via modern monitors." (2) cmaputil — "We developed a Python module, cmaputil, to create CVD-optimized colormaps... perceptually uniform in CVD-safe colorspace while linearizing and maximizing the brightness range." (3) a'-b' plane uniformity — achieved by "interpolating a' vs. b' to generate points equidistance from each other." (4) Linearized/maximized J' (lightness) — "linearizing and maximizing the J' range"; Cividis specifically "generated by optimizing the viridis colormap and selecting the J' linearization that maximizes the range of J'." Supporting quote is accurate and correctly attributed to CIECAM02-UCS (appears in Introduction): "Equal Euclidean distances between values in this space leads to an equal color difference perception." Source is the primary paper introducing Cividis (highest possible quality for this claim); independently corroborated by R (marcosci/cividis) and MATLAB (AlexHenderson/cividis) ports. Not outdated (color-space methods stable), not marketing, no contradicting evidence found. Claim survives all five refutation checks.

#### V24 `a4dc8e9c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For categorical/segmentation color coding where data marks must be found rapidly and accurately (preattentive visual search), only about 5 to 7 distinct colors can be reliably identified with a low error rate; Healey tested displays with 3, 5, 7, and 9 colors and found rapid low-error identification breaks down as the count rises toward 7-9. This directly bounds how many categorical colors to use for a small number of data series (~3-6 is safely within the reliable range)."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/2639665_Choosing_Effective_Colours_for_Data_Visualization (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Healey (1996) found that only 5 to 7 colours can be found rapidly and with a low error rate... studies using 3, 5, 7, and 9 colors found rapid identification for 3 and 5 colors but mixed results for 7 and 9 colors"

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the primary source and independently corroborated. The primary source (Healey 1996, "Choosing Effective Colours for Data Visualization," peer-reviewed IEEE Visualization '96, by a domain expert) states verbatim: "Healey (1996) found that only 5 to 7 colours can be found rapidly and with a low error rate" — this is the author's OWN finding, not a misattribution. Healey ran target-identification experiments with exactly the color counts the claim names (3, 5, 7, 9; 38 observers). Reported results: 3-color and 5-color = low error; 7-color mean error 3.4% (still accurate); 9-color mean error 8.1% overall, up to ~14% for some target hues. So error rises with color count, bounding categorical use to a small range. Independent corroboration: a 2021/2022 peer-reviewed study (Lalanne et al., arXiv 2103.06084 / Visual Informatics, "Impacts of the Numbers of Colors and Shapes on Outlier Detection") found "the capacity limit (7 for color, 5 for shape)," with low error up to that point and a sharp jump (~29%) at the breaking color count. Broader literature converges (ColorBrewer qualitative palettes cap ~8-12; multiple sources cite ~6-8 readily distinguishable). No credible source contradicts the core conclusion that ~3-6 categorical colors is safely within the reliable range. ONE caveat (qualifies, not refutes): the claim's secondary paraphrase ("mixed results for 7 and 9" / "breaks down toward 7-9") is slightly loose — in Healey's actual data 7 colors was still low-error (3.4%); the real breakdown was at 9. But the headline figure it bounds on ("5 to 7 colors") is exactly what the source states. Source quality matches claim strength; finding is foundational and reconfirmed by 2022 work, not outdated.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. The only nuance is a minor self-imprecision: at 7 colors Healey measured 3.4% error (low), so "mixed results for 7" overstates degradation — but this does not undercut the 5-7 bound or the 3-6 safe-range conclusion. The only genuinely "disputed" area in the literature is the UPPER limit (8 vs 12 vs 24 via the Polychrome package, arXiv/RColorBrewer), which is irrelevant to this claim's conservative lower bound.

#### V25 `a4e4ed40` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "ColorBrewer divides color schemes into three types matched to data nature: sequential (for ordered low-to-high data, dominated by lightness steps), diverging (for data with a critical midpoint/break, varying both hue and lightness around an emphasized middle), and qualitative (for nominal/categorical data, relying primarily on hue with near-constant lightness and saturation). This typology is the foundational, widely-adopted framework for matching palette type to data type."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Sequential colour schemes (Figure 3a) imply order and are suited to representing data that ranges from low-to-high values... Lightness steps dominate the look of these schemes, usually with light colours for low data values and dark colours for high values. 'Dark equals more' is a standard cartographic convention. ... Diverging colour schemes (Figure 3b) should be used when a critical data class or break point needs to be emphasized. The break or class in the middle of the sequence is emphasized by a hue and lightness change... Qualitative colour schemes (Figure 3c) rely primarily on differences in hue to create a colour scheme that does not imply order, merely difference in kind... Qualitative schemes work best when hue is varied and saturation and lightness are kept or nearly constant."

*Evidence:* The claim is accurately supported by the supplied quote and corroborated by multiple independent authoritative sources; I could not refute it.

(1) Quote match: The three definitions in the claim map near-verbatim onto the supporting quote from Harrower & Brewer (2003), "ColorBrewer.org: An Online Tool for Selecting Colour Schemes for Maps," The Cartographic Journal 40(1):27-37 (the cited RPI-hosted PDF). Sequential = ordered low-to-high, lightness-dominated ("Lightness steps dominate... light colours for low... dark for high"); diverging = critical break/midpoint emphasized via hue+lightness change; qualitative = hue-driven with saturation/lightness held nearly constant. No misread or overreach in the substantive typology.

(2) Independent corroboration of the SAME three-type typology (sequential/diverging/qualitative matched to data nature): Penn State GEOG 486 "Types of Color Schemes," WUSTL "Digital Cartography," ggplot2/RColorBrewer documentation, and MacEachren (1994) "Some Truth with Maps" — all reproduce it. The framework traces to Brewer (1994), "Color Use Guidelines for Mapping and Visualization," Ch. 7 in MacEachren & Taylor (eds.), Visualization in Modern Cartography (Elsevier).

(3) The "foundational/widely-adopted" meta-claim (claimant's framing, not a direct quote) is substantiated by peer-reviewed origin (Cartographic Journal) plus broad tool/textbook adoption (ggplot2 scale_brewer, RColorBrewer, cartography curricula). Source quality matches claim strength.

(4) The one sub-claim with nuance — "dark equals more" — is actually STRENGTHENED, not weakened: Schloss lab studies (e.g., PMC7683863) provide independent empirical evidence of a robust "dark-is-more bias." The only qualifications found (bias can invert on black backgrounds; hotspot-is-more effects) concern the perceptual basis of that convention, NOT the validity of the three-type typology.

(5) Not outdated (stable foundational knowledge, 1994-2003, still standard in 2026 tooling) and not marketing (peer-reviewed cartographic literature + free public tool). No contradicting source found across five targeted searches.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. The nearest nuance — Schloss et al. (PMC7683863) and "Dark-is-More Bias Also in Dark Mode?" (KN J. Cartography, 2024, doi:10.1007/s42489-024-00171-z) — qualifies only the perceptual basis of the "dark equals more" sub-convention (it can invert on dark backgrounds), and on balance supports rather than refutes the typology.

#### V26 `a52cafe7` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Prior perceptually-uniform colormap design work erred by relying on CIELAB, because CIELAB is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies — so it does not guarantee uniformity for the higher-frequency detail in real data images."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Previous work in the design of perceptually uniform colour maps has mostly failed to recognise that CIELAB space is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies."

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-supported by the primary source AND independently corroborated by peer-reviewed work. Kovesi (arXiv:1509.03700, "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them") abstract states exactly: "Previous work in the design of perceptually uniform colour maps has mostly failed to recognise that CIELAB space is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies." Both halves of the claim map directly to this — no overreach. The underlying mechanism is not Kovesi's idiosyncrasy: it traces to the peer-reviewed Zhang & Wandell S-CIELAB paper (J. Society for Information Display, 1997), which built a spatial pre-filtering extension PRECISELY because standard CIELAB was derived from large uniform color patches and fails on fine spatial patterns; over uniform regions S-CIELAB equals CIELAB, over patterned regions it corrects it. CIELAB's origin in low-spatial-frequency, large-uniform-patch psychophysics (~2°/10° fields) is textbook color science (Wikipedia, ScienceDirect). Physiological basis is established: chromatic contrast sensitivity is low-pass with a much lower cutoff than luminance, so fine detail is carried by lightness — which is why Kovesi pivots to lightness uniformity. I actively searched for disputes and found NONE; sources only corroborate. Not outdated (mature, slow-moving vision science; 1997 + 2015 findings current in 2026). Not marketing/forum — academic methods paper with no commercial angle. Caveats (do not refute): (a) "mostly failed to recognise" is unquantified editorializing about field awareness, not a measured fact; (b) the arXiv paper is technical-report form not formally journal-peer-reviewed, but this is acceptable because the claim is NOT extraordinary — it restates established vision science whose load-bearing premise rests on the peer-reviewed Zhang & Wandell line, and Kovesi's CET maps are widely adopted (R cetcolor, Julia, HoloViz Colorcet, cited in Matplotlib docs). Note CIELAB also has separate well-documented hue/chroma non-uniformity (blue region), but the specific spatial-frequency claim is sound.

#### V27 `a53dbc46` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow (jet) color map has no perceptual ordering: viewers cannot infer the order of data values from the colors without consulting a legend, because the spectral/wavelength ordering of hues is not perceived as ordered (e.g., yellow is not seen as 'between' green and red in magnitude)."

**Source:** https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The rainbow color map confuses viewers through its lack of perceptual ordering, obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation, and actively misleads interpretation through the introduction of non-data-dependent gradients."

*Evidence:* The claim's core mechanism is well-supported by the primary peer-reviewed source and corroborated across the literature. Borland & Taylor 2007 ("Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," IEEE CG&A, doc 4118486) states verbatim that "the rainbow color map confuses viewers through its lack of perceptual ordering" — the supplied quote is accurate. The literature consensus is explicit that the rainbow's wavelength ordering (shorter→longer) is NOT a perceptual ordering: e.g., MathWorks' "Rainbow Color Map Critiques" bibliography and tutorials state "there is no natural perceptual ordering of the spectral colors," so "viewers have to refer to the color key more often to determine data values." The specific illustration in the claim (yellow not perceived as "between" green and red in magnitude) is the standard, accurate textbook example of non-perceptual hue ordering and matches the documented finding that high-lightness yellow/cyan segments break consistent value ordering. The interdisciplinary review (Pieper et al. 2022, ISPRS J., S0924271622002659) confirms "rainbow color schemes 'fail' on intuitiveness of order and perceptual distance between hues in multiple studies."

One soft spot, not a refutation: the absolute word "cannot" is slightly stronger than the most careful sources. Brewer (1997) and Bujack et al. ("Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE CG&A 2023, doc 10128890) note that LEARNED ROYGBIV exposure lets some viewers apply a memorized order — so order is recoverable via convention + legend, not metaphysically impossible. But this nuance reinforces rather than contradicts the claim's mechanism: people lack an INTUITIVE/perceptual order and therefore must consult the legend. Critically, the leading rebuttal paper (Bujack 2023) does NOT dispute the lack of perceptual ordering; it argues harms are mitigable and rainbow aids OTHER tasks (hue-based segmentation), while conceding rainbow impairs magnitude-ordering tasks. Source quality is sufficient (primary peer-reviewed IEEE article); claim is current (consensus reaffirmed in HESS 2021, Pieper 2022, even by the 2023 contrarian paper). Recommend softening "cannot" to "is impaired / must rely on memorized convention and frequent legend reference," but the substantive claim stands.
*Counter-source:* Bujack et al., "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE CG&A 2023 (ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/10128890); Brewer (1997, cited in Pieper et al. 2022, sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0924271622002659) — both argue learned exposure can confer some hue ordering, but neither disputes the absence of intuitive perceptual ordering; the 2023 paper concedes rainbow impairs magnitude-ordering tasks.

#### V28 `a558cb41` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For encoding quantitative magnitude, area is among the least accurate channels: the study's new angle/area judgment types confirmed the psychophysical prediction that area performs worse than angle, and both perform significantly worse than position. This directly bears on using dot AREA (bubble/scatter) to encode magnitude."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1753326.1753357 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the new results match expectations: psychophysical theory predicts area to perform worse than angle, and both to be significantly worse than position."

*Evidence:* The claim is well-supported by primary and corroborating sources, with only a minor framing imprecision that does not undermine its substance.

SUPPORT FOR THE CORE ORDERING (area < angle < position):
- Cleveland & McGill (1984), the foundational JASA paper, ranks elementary perceptual tasks (multiple independent copies confirm verbatim): "Position along a common scale > Positions along nonaligned scales > Length, direction, angle > Area > Volume, curvature > Shading/color saturation." Area sits below angle and well below position.
- Heer & Bostock (2010, CHI, Best Paper nominee) replicated this ordering; summaries of the paper state results "adhere to theoretical predictions" and reproduce "the same order found in Cleveland and McGill's original study." The supporting quote ("psychophysical theory predicts area to perform worse than angle, and both to be significantly worse than position") is a faithful basis for the claim.
- Independent corroboration: human-subjects experiments show "significant accuracy advantage for position judgments over both length and angle judgments, which in turn proved to be better than area judgments."
- Mechanistic basis: Stevens' power law gives area an exponent of ~0.7-0.8, producing systematic underestimation of area magnitudes — the psychophysical reason area is less accurate. This directly justifies the bubble/scatter-area caution in the claim.

NO CONTRADICTING EVIDENCE found across multiple searches; the finding is foundational, stable (1984 + 2010 replication, re-confirmed since), peer-reviewed, not marketing, not outdated.

MINOR IMPRECISION (does not refute): The claim says "the study's NEW angle/area judgment types." Angle was part of Cleveland & McGill's ORIGINAL 1984 design and was a REPLICATION (Experiment 1) in Heer & Bostock, not new. The genuinely new experiment Heer & Bostock added was RECTANGULAR area perception (treemaps/cartograms); circular area was part of the replicated proportional-judgment task. This is a framing slip about what was "new," not an error in the substantive area < angle < position finding or its bubble-chart implication.

CAVEAT: I could not retrieve the exact verbatim sentence from the primary PDF directly (ACM 403; vis.stanford.edu TLS/cert error; Semantic Scholar body empty), so verbatim confirmation of the supporting quote relies on search-engine extracts of the paper plus the foundational 1984 source and multiple secondary corroborations — all consistent.

#### V29 `a5790f19` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The paper identifies a set of elementary perceptual tasks people use to extract quantitative values from charts and ranks them by accuracy, then uses that ranking to recommend chart forms — implying position-based encodings are more accurate than area-, angle-, or color/shading-based ones (relevant to encoding magnitude via dot area)."

**Source:** https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01621459.1984.10478080 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The authors identify elementary perceptual tasks people perform when extracting quantitative data from visualizations, then rank these tasks by accuracy."

*Evidence:* Claim is accurate and well-supported by the primary source (Cleveland & McGill, "Graphical Perception: Theory, Experimentation, and Application to the Development of Graphical Methods," JASA 1984, vol. 79, no. 387 — a top-tier peer-reviewed statistics journal and the foundational citation in the field). The paper (a) identifies elementary perceptual tasks for extracting quantitative values from graphs, (b) ranks them by accuracy, and (c) uses that ranking to recommend chart forms. Multiple independent sources confirm the exact ordering, most→least accurate: 1) position along a common scale, 2) position along nonaligned scales, 3) length/direction/angle, 4) area, 5) volume/curvature, 6) shading/color saturation. This directly supports the claim's implication that position-based encodings are more accurate than area-, angle-, or color/shading-based ones — and that AREA ranks 4th (below all position-based encodings), the point relevant to encoding magnitude via dot area. The paper's chart recommendations follow from the ranking: dot/bar charts to replace pie charts (judging position+length beats angle/area), and framed-rectangle maps to replace shaded maps (shading ranks lowest). The claim is descriptive/historical, so age is not a defect; moreover the core ranking was replicated and extended by Heer & Bostock (2010, CHI) via Amazon Mechanical Turk crowdsourcing. The only contrary scholarship found ('The Risks of Ranking,' arXiv 2212.10533, 2022) challenges the UNIVERSALITY of a single fixed ranking across individuals — it does not dispute that the paper established the ranking or that position outperforms area on aggregate; this qualifies generalizability, not the factual claim under review. No marketing, no cherry-picking, source quality exceeds the bar for the claim's strength.
*Counter-source:* arXiv 2212.10533 'The Risks of Ranking: Revisiting Graphical Perception to Model Individual Differences in Visualization Performance' (2022) — but this only qualifies the universality of a fixed ranking due to individual differences; it does NOT refute that Cleveland & McGill established the ranking or that position beats area on aggregate, so it does not defeat the claim as stated.

#### V30 `a6153881` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "There is a practical upper limit on the number of categories/classes that remain reliably distinguishable: ColorBrewer caps schemes at 12 classes, but many sequential schemes stop at 9 because further divisions become perceptually unreliable, and as a rule of thumb cartographers seldom use more than seven classes. This directly bounds how many categorical colors humans can reliably tell apart (relevant to choosing ~3-6 data series)."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "These sets have been designed to produce attractive colour schemes of similar appearance for maps ranging from 3 to 12 classes. Not all colour schemes can be expanded to 12 classes, and many of the sequential scheme sets, for example, stop at 9 classes because further divisions within the scheme are perceptually unreliable... The maximum number of data classes ColorBrewer supports is 12, although only a few of the colour schemes can be divided into this many steps and remain differentiable. As a general rule of thumb, cartographers seldom use more than seven classes on a choropleth map."

*Evidence:* The supporting quote is authentic (verified verbatim against the primary cs.rpi.edu PDF via WebSearch and corroborated by Brewer's own PSU ColorBrewer "Learn More" page and ColorBrewer's scheme structure). The REFUTATION is that the claim misreads/overreaches the quote by conflating choropleth *data classes* with *categorical colors*. (1) ColorBrewer's "12-class max" and "sequential schemes stop at 9" are about SEQUENTIAL/ORDERED schemes, not categorical hues; ColorBrewer's QUALITATIVE (categorical) schemes — the actual analog to "data series" — themselves go to 12 (Set3, Paired), which CONTRADICTS the claim that these numbers bound categorical colors to a low count. (2) The "cartographers seldom use more than seven classes" figure is the cartographic "Rule of Seven," which the literature explicitly defines as the limit for distinguishing LIGHTNESS steps within a SINGLE HUE (a sequential ramp): "most map readers will only be able to distinguish seven different value levels based on lightness-darkness of the color... HOWEVER, if differences in hue and/or saturation are incorporated, that limit increases significantly to as many as 10-12 classes" (gis-university / PSU GEOG486 / clfuture cartography sources). Since categorical data series use distinct HUES, the relevant ceiling is ~10-12, not 7 — the claim inverts its own evidence. (3) The claim treats three different statement types as interchangeable: a tool ceiling ("supports 12"), a data-binning convention ("seldom >7", which the source itself caveats — isoline/regular-pattern maps "can safely use more than seven"), and a human perceptual limit. The claim's conclusion (low practical limit for ~3-6 series) lands in a region the broader literature loosely supports (~6-8 for categorical), but it is reached via invalid reasoning from misapplied numbers; the phrase "this DIRECTLY bounds how many categorical colors humans can reliably tell apart" is unsupported by and partly contradicted by the cited source and surrounding cartographic literature.
*Counter-source:* https://gis-university.com/choropleth-map-data-classification/ (Rule of Seven applies to single-hue lightness; hue/saturation raises limit to 10-12); ColorBrewer qualitative schemes (Set3/Paired) support 12 categorical colors per https://colorbrewer2.org/ and https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf; PSU GEOG486 https://courses.ems.psu.edu/geog486/node/881

*Claim under review:* "Many vendor-supplied colormaps have highly uneven perceptual contrast across their range, including 'perceptual flat spots' that can hide a data feature as large as one tenth (10%) of the total data range."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Many colour maps provided by vendors have highly uneven perceptual contrast over their range. It is not uncommon for colour maps to have perceptual flat spots that can hide a feature as large as one tenth of the total data range."

*Evidence:* The claim is a near-verbatim, faithful paraphrase of the primary source's abstract. Peter Kovesi, "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them" (arXiv:1509.03700, 2015), states verbatim: "Many colour maps provided by vendors have highly uneven perceptual contrast over their range. It is not uncommon for colour maps to have perceptual flat spots that can hide a feature as large as one tenth of the total data range." I confirmed this exact text via WebFetch of both arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 and the ar5iv full-text rendering. The claim preserves the source's hedging ("as large as," "one tenth/10%") without overreach. SOURCE QUALITY: This IS the primary source — Kovesi originated the perceptual-flat-spot concept; the paper produced the CET/colorcet perceptually-uniform colormaps now standard in matplotlib/Python/Julia, and is also hosted in the UWA research repository. Foundational, heavily cited, current (color-perception science is slow-moving; 2015 remains canonical in 2026). NO CONTRADICTION FOUND: searches surfaced only corroborating/derivative peer-reviewed work (e.g., PLOS One 2018 / arXiv:1712.01662 on CVD-aware colormaps) that builds on, not disputes, Kovesi. Not marketing, not a benchmark, not forum speculation. ONE NON-FATAL NUANCE: the specific "one tenth" magnitude is linked to the paper's evaluation test image, whose sine-wave peak-to-trough amplitude is deliberately set to 10% of the data range (Appendix A) as a demonstration threshold rather than a first-principles-derived constant; the paper gives no closed-form derivation of "10%" in the abstract. But because the claim mirrors the source's own qualified language ("as large as," "not uncommon"), this nuance qualifies but does not refute it.

#### V32 `a64a6de9` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The empirical basis for Munzner's Chapter 5 channel-effectiveness treatment is the graphical-perception literature, explicitly Cleveland & McGill's 1984 experiments, Bertin's Semiology of Graphics, and Stevens's measurement-scales work — establishing that the position/length/angle/area/color rankings derive from controlled perceptual studies rather than opinion."

**Source:** https://www.cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/vadbook/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Graphical Perception: Theory, Experimentation and the Application to the Development of Graphical Models by William S. Cleveland and Robert McGill (1984) - appears to be the primary empirical work on channel effectiveness"

*Evidence:* CLAIM HOLDS (mainstream scholarly consensus; not refuted). The claim asserts Munzner's Ch.5 channel-effectiveness rankings derive empirically from Cleveland & McGill 1984, Bertin's Semiology of Graphics, and Stevens's measurement-scales work — controlled studies, not opinion. All three attributions are independently corroborated.

(1) Cleveland & McGill 1984: Iowa STAT4580 course page (homepage.divms.uiowa.edu/~luke/classes/STAT4580/percep.html) states the ranking came from "formal experiments with about 50 subjects" focused on "accuracy of decoding," and that Heer & Bostock (2010) replicated it ("consistent with Cleveland-McGill results"). Wikipedia "Graphical perception" + the C&M paper note the work is cited 1585+ times and "replicated many times." This is the empirical core and is uncontested.

(2) Bertin Semiology of Graphics (1967): universally credited as the origin of the visual-variables/marks-and-channels vocabulary Munzner adopts; C&M and Mackinlay (APT, 1986) built on Bertin. Confirmed across multiple academic sources.

(3) Stevens: Iowa page explicitly lists "Stevens (1957) for psychophysics/power law" as a foundation alongside Bertin and C&M. The claim cites Stevens's MEASUREMENT-SCALES work specifically — the correct lever for Munzner's expressiveness principle (matching channel type to nominal/ordinal/quantitative data). Accurate.

(4) "Controlled studies, not opinion": directly supported — the position>length>angle>area>color magnitude ranking is the experimental C&M result; Bertin/Mackinlay/Stevens-measurement are the theoretical scaffolding. This is exactly how the literature characterizes it.

WEAKNESS (not disqualifying): the SUPPLIED quote names only Cleveland & McGill as "primary empirical work"; it does not itself mention Bertin or Stevens. So the quote under-supports the full three-source claim. But the underlying factual claim is correct and corroborated by independent high-quality sources, so the gap is in the quote's completeness, not the claim's truth. Minor nuance: Stevens's POWER LAW (distinct from his measurement scales) is sometimes framed as a competing/complicating account of magnitude estimation rather than a clean foundation — but the claim invokes measurement scales, which is uncontested. Source quality is high (foundational textbook as primary + Munzner's own minicourse + multiple university lectures + peer-reviewed surveys).
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the lineage. Closest qualification: Stevens's power law (vs his measurement-scales taxonomy) is treated by some as a competing account of magnitude perception rather than a foundation — but the claim cites measurement scales, which is uncontested. Separately, the supplied quote names only Cleveland & McGill, so it alone does not evidence the Bertin/Stevens parts; those are confirmed via external sources (homepage.divms.uiowa.edu/~luke/classes/STAT4580/percep.html).

#### V33 `a66cfbb2` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Cividis was created by mathematically optimizing colormaps using the CIECAM02-UCS (Uniform Color Space) model via a Python module (cmaputil), ensuring perceptual uniformity in the a'-b' plane and a linearized, maximized J' (lightness) range."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Equal Euclidean distances between values in this space leads to an equal color difference perception"

*Evidence:* Every element of the claim is confirmed verbatim in the primary source (Nuñez, Anderton & Renslow 2018, PLOS ONE 13(7):e0199239, full text at PMC6070163). (1) Color space: the paper writes the exact full form "CIECAM02-UCS (Uniform Color Space)" — the claim matches the paper's own terminology, not even the looser "CAM02-UCS" shorthand. (2) Python module: "We developed a Python module, cmaputil, to create CVD-optimized colormaps." (3) a'-b' plane uniformity: a' and b' arrays are interpolated (10,000 points) to select equidistant points preserving "the total arc length through a'b' space," yielding perceptual uniformity; "three values are used to describe a color: lightness (J'), a red-green correlate (a'), and a yellow-blue correlate (b')." (4) Linearized/maximized J': verbatim — cividis was "generated by optimizing the viridis colormap and selecting the J' linearization that maximizes the range of J'." (5) Supporting quote confirmed verbatim: "Equal Euclidean distances between values in this space leads to an equal color difference perception, enabling the straightforward creation of perceptually uniform colormaps." The source is the definitive peer-reviewed paper that created Cividis itself — source quality fully matches claim strength. No contradicting evidence exists; this is the uncontested origin account. Minor: "mathematically optimizing colormaps" is a faithful paraphrase (specifically optimizing viridis→cividis), not an overreach.

#### V34 `a6a15d67` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Munzner's framework devotes a dedicated chapter, Ch 5 'Marks and Channels,' to visual encoding — i.e., she organizes encoding effectiveness around the concepts of marks (geometric primitives) and channels (the visual attributes that control their appearance), which is the structural basis for ranking encodings like position, length, area, and color."

**Source:** https://www.cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/vadbook/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Ch 5. Marks and Channels"

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED on every load-bearing point. Primary source — Munzner's own official book page (https://www.cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/vadbook/) — confirms the full table of contents with "Chapter 5: Marks and Channels" exactly as quoted (author: Tamara Munzner; "Visualization Analysis and Design," CRC Press / A K Peters, 2014). Multiple independent authoritative sources corroborate both the chapter title AND its substantive content: the publisher Taylor & Francis/CRC (taylorfrancis.com/chapters/mono/10.1201/b17511-5) and O'Reilly (oreilly.com/library/view/visualization-analysis-and/9781466508910/K14708_C005.xhtml) host the actual chapter; university course materials (UBC cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/courses, Wisconsin pages.graphics.cs.wisc.edu, Michigan Tech, HKUST) independently confirm the definitions and ranking. Search results verify the chapter (1) defines marks as geometric primitives (point, line, area), (2) defines channels as visual attributes controlling mark appearance independent of geometry (position, color, shape, tilt/angle, size), and (3) presents an effectiveness ranking — magnitude channels ordered position-on-common-scale > position-on-unaligned-scale > length (1D size) > area (2D size) > luminance > saturation > tilt/angle, and identity channels = hue, shape, motion — governed by the expressiveness and effectiveness principles. This directly substantiates the claim's assertion that Ch 5 "organizes encoding effectiveness around marks and channels" as "the structural basis for ranking encodings like position, length, area, and color." No contradicting source found. Source quality (author's primary site + publisher + neutral university courses) exceeds the bar for this non-extraordinary, foundational claim. Note: the encoding-rank lineage traces to Cleveland & McGill / Mackinlay, but the claim only credits Munzner's framework with *organizing/structuring* the ranking around marks/channels, which is accurate and not an overreach. Munzner correctly identified as female ("she").

#### V35 `a717588e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The study conducted controlled experiments specifically on rectangular area perception, the encoding used in treemaps and cartograms, providing empirical data on how accurately humans judge area-based magnitude encodings (directly relevant to bubble/dot-area encoding)."

**Supporting quote:** "We also conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms) and on chart size and gridline spacing."

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED. The core claim is verbatim from the paper's own abstract (Heer & Bostock, "Crowdsourcing Graphical Perception," CHI 2010), confirmed across the primary source (idl.uw.edu / vis.stanford.edu), ACM DL (10.1145/1753326.1753357), and Semantic Scholar: "We also conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms) and on chart size and gridline spacing." (1) "Controlled experiments specifically on rectangular area perception" — TRUE; the paper ran new MTurk area-perception experiments varying aspect ratio, finding 1:1 squares performed WORST (viewers approximate area via 1D length). (2) "The encoding used in treemaps and cartograms" — TRUE, verbatim. (3) "Empirical data on how accurately humans judge area-based magnitude encodings" — TRUE; it measured proportion-estimation accuracy/error. Source quality is strong: primary author lab page + peer-reviewed CHI Best Paper Nominee. No contradicting source found. ONE minor soft spot: the new H&B rectangular experiment used rectangles/squares, NOT circles, so "directly relevant to bubble/dot-area encoding" is an inference, not a tested result — but the claim says "relevant to," not "tested," and the relevance is well-grounded (Cleveland & McGill 1984 found rectangular and circular area judgments have similar accuracy; both suffer Stevens'-law area underestimation). This parenthetical does not rise to an overreach that would refute the substantive claim. The 2010 date is irrelevant — it is a historical fact about what experiments the paper ran, which does not decay.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Nuance only: the paper's NEW rectangular-area experiment tested rectangles/squares, not circles (per paper methodology summaries and the abstract); the circle/area equivalence comes from Cleveland & McGill (1984), not the H&B rectangular experiment itself. This qualifies but does not refute the "relevant to bubble/dot-area" parenthetical.

#### V36 `a720c085` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The paper identifies a set of elementary perceptual tasks people use to extract quantitative values from charts and ranks them by accuracy, then uses that ranking to recommend chart forms — implying position-based encodings are more accurate than area-, angle-, or color/shading-based ones (relevant to encoding magnitude via dot area)."

**Source:** https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01621459.1984.10478080 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The authors identify elementary perceptual tasks people perform when extracting quantitative data from visualizations, then rank these tasks by accuracy."

*Evidence:* The claim accurately describes Cleveland & McGill (1984), "Graphical Perception: Theory, Experimentation, and Application to the Development of Graphical Methods," JASA 79(387):531-554. The paper's abstract (confirmed via Semantic Scholar and multiple secondary summaries) states the theory "identifies a set of elementary perceptual tasks that are carried out when people extract quantitative information from graphs, and orders these tasks on the basis of how accurately people perform them" — directly matching claim parts (1) identify tasks and (2) rank by accuracy. The published ordering, most-to-least accurate: position along a common scale > position along nonaligned scales > length/direction/angle > area > volume/curvature > shading/color saturation. This places position above area, angle, and color/shading exactly as the claim states (relevant to encoding magnitude via dot area, which falls in the lower-accuracy 'area' tier). Claim part (3) is also correct: the paper used the ranking to propose replacements for bar/pie charts that 'employ higher-order perceptual tasks.' The supporting quote maps onto the abstract's actual wording with no overreach.

Skeptical checks: (a) Critiques exist — McColeman et al. found the encoding rankings do not hold under a different task and that cardinality matters more; Kosara & Skau found pie charts are not read by angle as Cleveland & McGill assumed. But these dispute the GENERALITY/VALIDITY of the ranking's prescriptions, not WHETHER the paper made these claims. The claim under review is purely descriptive of the paper's content and method, which is undisputed. (b) Heer & Bostock (2010) crowdsourced replication broadly reproduced the original ordering. (c) Source is the foundational peer-reviewed JASA paper itself — quality exceeds the claim's strength. (d) Not outdated: claim describes a fixed historical document, not a fast-moving empirical assertion. (e) Not marketing. The only minor nuance is that the authors framed the ordering as 'a framework within which to work,' not a 'precise prescription' — but the claim's hedged wording ('ranks them... then uses that ranking to recommend chart forms — implying...') is consistent with that framing.
*Counter-source:* Critiques of the ranking's validity (not its existence): McColeman et al. (CHI/IEEE VIS work on rankings not holding under different tasks; cardinality dominating); Kosara & Skau (pie charts not read by angle). Neither refutes the descriptive claim. Replication support: Heer & Bostock (2010), "Crowdsourcing Graphical Perception," CHI.

#### V37 `a766fa03` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A colormap should use a linear (monotonic) increase in lightness to avoid the perception of gradients that are not actually present in the data — the core flaw that makes rainbow/jet colormaps misleading."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A linear increase in lightness should be used to avoid the perception of gradients that are not present."

*Evidence:* The claim is partly supported by its quote but materially overreaches by conflating "linear" with "monotonic" and by mislabeling the mechanism of rainbow/jet failure.

1) QUOTE vs CLAIM: The PLOS source (Nuñez, Anderton & Renslow 2018, "Optimizing colormaps...", doi:10.1371/journal.pone.0199239) does literally say "a linear increase in lightness should be used to avoid the perception of gradients that are not present." So the narrow phrase is verbatim. BUT the claim's parenthetical "linear (monotonic)" treats the two as synonyms. The literature treats them as distinct: linear = constant rate of lightness change; monotonic = never reverses direction (no peaks/valleys). They are not interchangeable.

2) LINEAR IS NOT REQUIRED — MONOTONIC IS THE LOAD-BEARING PROPERTY: Authoritative sources state lightness may legitimately rise non-linearly. Matplotlib's colormap docs and Kovesi ("Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them", arXiv:1509.03700) note luminance control points "can increase linearly, OR according to a power law that accounts for the non-linearity of brightness perception." Moreland's color-advice and the Kindlmann/Inferno/Plasma maps are described as "monotonically increasing luminance," not "linear." So linear lightness is one acceptable design choice within perceptual uniformity, not a necessary condition to avoid false gradients.

3) THE CORE-FLAW ATTRIBUTION IS WRONG: The claim says non-linearity is "the core flaw that makes rainbow/jet colormaps misleading." The seminal source on this exact point — Borland & Taylor II (2007), "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful" — attributes the false-gradient flaw to NON-MONOTONIC / uncontrolled luminance: jet "obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation, and actively misleads interpretation through the introduction of non-data-dependent gradients." Jet is lightest at yellow/cyan in the MIDDLE (a luminance peak, i.e. it rises then falls), creating false edges/bands. A colormap with strictly monotonic-but-non-linear lightness would NOT exhibit jet's false-gradient artifact. Crameri et al. 2020 (Nat. Commun.) and the hydrology review (HESS 2021; Stoter/Crameri) likewise pin the flaw on non-monotonic luminance plus non-perceptual-uniformity (red-green collapse under CVD), not on non-linearity.

VERDICT: refuted because the claim (a) equates linear with monotonic (a category error the cited and surrounding literature explicitly separate), and (b) names the wrong mechanism — the core flaw of rainbow/jet is non-monotonic / uncontrolled luminance and lack of perceptual uniformity, not non-linear lightness. The accurate, citeable statement is: "A sequential colormap's lightness should increase MONOTONICALLY (and ideally perceptually-uniformly, e.g. linear in CAM02-UCS L*); rainbow/jet mislead chiefly because their luminance is NON-MONOTONIC (peaks at yellow/cyan), creating false gradients/bands."
*Counter-source:* Borland & Taylor II (2007) "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful" (IEEE CG&A) — attributes flaw to "uncontrolled luminance variation" / non-data-dependent gradients (non-monotonic), not non-linearity; Kovesi (2015) "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them" arXiv:1509.03700 — lightness may increase by power law, not only linearly; Matplotlib colormap docs — monotonic lightness as the criterion; Crameri et al. (2020) Nat. Commun. "The misuse of colour in science communication" — non-monotonic luminance + CVD failure as core problems.

#### V38 `a7a237ef` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Empirical testing of Tufte-style 'chartjunk' minimalism is mixed rather than settled: visual embellishment does not reliably destroy comprehension, so the blanket prescription to eliminate non-data ink is opinion/convention, not a uniformly supported empirical finding. This study extends prior embellishment work (Bateman et al.) to larger datasets (10+ data points) and time-limited viewing."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Although many well-cited theories or guidelines for visualization design advocate 'minimalism', designers tend to include a wide variety of visual embellishments in their charts... Various studies have shown mixed results depending on the details of the task and context."

*Evidence:* The claim survives adversarial review. Its descriptive facts are all confirmed, and its interpretive conclusion is carefully hedged in a way the literature supports.

SOURCE IDENTIFIED & DETAILS VERIFIED: DOI 10.1177/1541931214581316 = Li & Moacdieh, "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment," Proc. Human Factors and Ergonomics Society Annual Meeting (2014). It does extend Bateman et al. to charts with 10+ data points under time-limited viewing (confirmed). Caveat: this is a CONFERENCE PROCEEDINGS paper (HFES), not a strict peer-reviewed journal article, and it is paywalled (full text not readable). But the claim does not rest on it alone.

CORE ASSERTION CORROBORATED BY MULTIPLE PEER-REVIEWED PRIMARY SOURCES: (1) Bateman et al. "Useful Junk?" (CHI 2010) — embellished charts had equal comprehension accuracy and superior long-term recall. (2) Haroz, Kosara & Franconeri "ISOTYPE Visualization" (CHI 2015) — pictographs showed no accuracy cost and some working-memory/engagement benefits. (3) Parsons & Shukla, "Data Visualization Practitioners' Perspectives on Chartjunk" (CHI 2020) — documents pluralistic, context-dependent understanding and inconsistent results across ~20+ studies. CHI is a top peer-reviewed venue.

THE "NOT UNIFORMLY SUPPORTED" FRAMING IS DEFENSIBLE: Even Gillan & Richman (1994), the study that DID find empirical support for data-ink maximization, concluded Tufte's principle is "too simplistic" — background pictures hurt, axes help, redundant coding effects depend on task/graph. So no source uniformly supports a blanket "eliminate all non-data ink" rule. The claim says embellishment "does not RELIABLY destroy comprehension" and the rule is "not a UNIFORMLY supported empirical finding" — both hedges are accurate. The claim does NOT assert embellishment is good, only that the blanket prohibition is unproven.

STRONGEST REFUTATION ATTEMPT FAILS: Stephen Few ("The Chartjunk Debate," Perceptual Edge 2011) attacks the Bateman-line studies as flawed (N=20 undergrads, Holmes charts aren't true chartjunk, deliberately ugly controls). But Few argues the PRO-embellishment evidence is weak — he does not claim minimalism is empirically settled. His critique makes "mixed/not settled" MORE true, not less. This counter-evidence is therefore consistent with the claim's core point.

VERDICT: Well-supported, current (debate ongoing through 2020-2024), and the modest strength of the claim matches the evidence body despite the primary source's proceedings-level status.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate: A Close Examination of Recent Findings" (Perceptual Edge, 2011) — argues Bateman et al.'s pro-embellishment study is methodologically flawed (N=20, non-representative Holmes charts as "embellished," deliberately poor controls). But this critique does NOT support the blanket minimalism rule either; it argues the question is unresolved, which is consistent with the claim. Source quality caveat: the primary cited DOI is a HFES conference-proceedings paper, not a strict peer-reviewed journal, but the core assertion is independently backed by CHI papers (Bateman 2010, Haroz 2015, Parsons & Shukla 2020).

#### V39 `a7bddbd8` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Color-vision-deficiency (CVD) safety varies systematically by scheme type: sequential schemes are all usable by red-green colorblind readers because they encode lightness differences; most diverging hue pairs were chosen to be CVD-safe (except red-yellow-green and full-spectral); but almost all qualitative (categorical) schemes are difficult for colorblind readers. This gives a concrete, evidence-informed rule for designing CVD-safe palettes."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Generally, the hue pairs for diverging schemes were selected to accommodate colour-blind readers, with the exception of red-yellow-green and full spectral schemes. Almost all of the qualitative schemes are difcult for colour-blind readers and all of the sequential schemes are useful to them, because they include visible lightness differences."

*Evidence:* The claim is mostly faithful to its quote but overreaches on two independent grounds.

(1) INCOMPLETE/CONTRADICTED diverging exceptions. The quote and claim name only TWO non-CVD-safe diverging schemes ("red-yellow-green and full spectral"). But ColorBrewer's own tool and the consensus secondary literature flag THREE: RdYlGn, Spectral, AND RdGy (red-gray). Per colorbrewer2.org and multiple sources (e.g., SAS "The DO Loop" blogs.sas.com/content/iml; datavizpyr.com), the colorblind-safe diverging set is exactly {BrBG, PiYG, PRGn, PuOr, RdBu, RdYlBu} and the NOT-safe set is {RdGy, RdYlGn, Spectral}. The claim's parenthetical "(except red-yellow-green and full-spectral)" therefore states an inaccurate rule — RdGy is also not CVD-safe. The 2003 prose summary (the quote) understates the exceptions relative to ColorBrewer's own per-scheme flags, and the claim inherited that error.

(2) OVERCLAIMED EVIDENTIARY STRENGTH. The source (Harrower & Brewer 2003, The Cartographic Journal — legitimate peer-reviewed primary source) describes these as DESIGN SELECTIONS ("the hue pairs for diverging schemes were SELECTED to accommodate colour-blind readers"), i.e. expert design intent rooted in Olson & Brewer 1997 + trial-and-error, not a comprehensive per-scheme empirical CVD validation. Later colorimetric work (R Journal "Coloring in R's Blind Spot," RJ-2023-071; HCL-palette literature) explicitly notes ColorBrewer "relied on expert opinion and trial and error" and that adequate CVD lightness separation requires thresholds (~30 CIELAB units per pair) not guaranteed by every multi-hue sequential scheme at all class counts. The blanket "ALL sequential schemes are usable by red-green colorblind readers" is a sound heuristic but is design judgment, not a validated empirical law. The claim explicitly markets this as "a concrete, evidence-informed rule," upgrading design-intent into proven fact — a strength mismatch.

The core directional pattern (sequential generally CVD-OK via lightness; most qualitative bad; most diverging engineered to be OK) is REAL and the quote does support the three sub-claims nearly verbatim. But as worded the claim states an inaccurate exception list and overstates the evidentiary basis, so it does not survive skeptical review.
*Counter-source:* colorbrewer2.org (per-scheme colorblind-safe flags); SAS "The DO Loop" (blogs.sas.com/content/iml/?p=42475) and datavizpyr.com both list NOT-safe diverging = {RdGy, RdYlGn, Spectral}, contradicting the quote's two-exception list; R Journal "Coloring in R's Blind Spot" (journal.r-project.org/articles/RJ-2023-071/) on ColorBrewer relying on expert opinion/trial-and-error rather than comprehensive CVD validation.

#### V40 `a7daa2b8` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow (jet) color map has no perceptual ordering: viewers cannot infer the order of data values from the colors without consulting a legend, because the spectral/wavelength ordering of hues is not perceived as ordered (e.g., yellow is not seen as 'between' green and red in magnitude)."

**Source:** https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The rainbow color map confuses viewers through its lack of perceptual ordering, obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation, and actively misleads interpretation through the introduction of non-data-dependent gradients."

*Evidence:* Claim is accurately supported by the cited source AND independently corroborated by peer-reviewed empirical work; the strongest counter-source does NOT dispute this specific point.

1) SOURCE VERIFIED: IEEE doc 4118486 = Borland & Taylor, "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications 27(2):14-17, 2007. The supporting quote ("confuses viewers through its lack of perceptual ordering, obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation, and actively misleads interpretation") is genuine and directly states the lack-of-perceptual-ordering claim. The quote supports the claim — no misread/overreach.

2) EMPIRICAL CORROBORATION (not just opinion): Gołębiowska & Çöltekin 2022, "What's wrong with the rainbow?" (ISPRS J. Photogramm. Remote Sens., S0924271622002659) — large empirical study "clearly demonstrated" the rainbow scheme has no intuitive global order; "little to no agreement in ordering of rainbow colors while sequential colors are indeed intuitively ordered by participants." Multiple sources confirm "if a task requires ordering magnitudes, the rainbow colormap impairs performance." The yellow example in the claim is also grounded: yellow perceived as brightest but red assigned highest value.

3) ADVERSARIAL TEST PASSED: The obvious refutation candidate, Bujack et al. 2023 "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE CG&A, 10.1109/MCG.2023.3246111), disputes only the BLANKET "all bad" verdict — it argues rainbow helps certain NON-ordering tasks (fine local detail/edges). It does not refute the perceptual-ordering deficiency for magnitude/ranking. Literature consensus: conflicting results "most likely explained by task type"; ordering tasks consistently degraded. Ware's pro-rainbow findings concern other task types, not ordering.

4) MINOR WORDING NOTE (does not flip verdict): claim says "cannot infer order without consulting a legend" — literature phrases it as ordering being non-intuitive / not reliably agreed-upon rather than literally impossible, but the legend qualifier matches the source's intent (a legend supplies the mapping perception fails to provide). Fair restatement.

Source quality high (primary IEEE paper + peer-reviewed empirical replication through 2022-2023), current, not marketing.
*Counter-source:* Bujack et al. 2023, "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE CG&A (10.1109/MCG.2023.3246111) — but it only rebuts the blanket "all bad" framing for non-ordering tasks; it does NOT dispute the lack of perceptual ordering for magnitude/ranking tasks, so it does not refute this specific claim.

#### V41 `a815fffe` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Supporting quote:** "creates sharp boundaries that are not in the data"

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the cited primary source and corroborated across the literature; even its strongest critic concedes the mechanism. Both mechanisms in the claim are documented: false/sharp boundaries from abrupt hue+luminance transitions, AND flattening where the map is perceptually indistinct (the green/cyan flat-luminance bands appear uniform). (2) Independent corroboration: Stoelzle & Stein 2021 HESS hydrology review (hess.copernicus.org/articles/25/4549/2021) and Crameri, Shephard & Heron 2022 interdisciplinary empirical review (ScienceDirect S0924271622002659) reaffirm the same effect; MathWorks engineering analyses replicate it on real data. (3) Contradiction test FAILED to refute: The leading contrarian paper, Ware, Stone & Szafir 2023 'Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad' (IEEE doc 10128890), explicitly CONCEDES luminance non-uniformity and impaired shape perception; it only argues these are less relevant for value-reading tasks. It rebuts 'rainbow is always bad,' NOT the artifact mechanism — so the claim survives its main critic. (4) Currency: 2007 origin but mechanism reaffirmed in 2021-2023. Minor precision nuance (not a refutation): literature attributes the artifacts chiefly to non-monotonic LUMINANCE and perceptual non-uniformity; the claim's emphasis on 'hue transitions' is secondary, but the false-boundary and flattening outcomes are both established. WebFetch on the IEEE/ScienceDirect PDFs was paywalled (403/empty), so verification relied on indexed abstracts, PubMed records, and the eagereyes summary of the contrarian paper rather than full text — but the convergence across five independent sources is strong.

#### V42 `a81c9f34` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The study conducted controlled experiments specifically on rectangular area perception, the encoding used in treemaps and cartograms, providing empirical data on how accurately humans judge area-based magnitude encodings (directly relevant to bubble/dot-area encoding)."

**Source:** https://idl.uw.edu/papers/crowdsourcing-graphical-perception (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We also conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms) and on chart size and gridline spacing."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source PDF (Heer & Bostock, CHI 2010), text extracted directly. The claim is fully supported on every point:

1. CONTROLLED EXPERIMENT ON RECTANGULAR AREA — confirmed. The paper defines "Experiment 1B" / "rectangular area judgments," stating: "We studied rectangular area judgments (Exp. 1B), following the methodology of Cleveland & McGill to enable comparison." It is a controlled experiment with manipulated independent variables and inferential statistics (p < 0.05 effects, 95% CIs, "Figure 5: Rectangular area judgments by aspect ratios (1B)").

2. ENCODING USED IN TREEMAPS/CARTOGRAMS — confirmed verbatim. Abstract: "We also conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms)." Body: "We sought to compare our circular area judgment (T7) results with rectangular area judgments arising in visualizations such as cartograms [9] and treemaps [26]." They tested two display conditions — bare rectangles (T8) and actual "600 × 400 pixel treemaps depicting 24 values" (T9).

3. EMPIRICAL DATA ON ACCURACY OF AREA-BASED MAGNITUDE ENCODING — confirmed. Design: "Aspect ratios were determined by the cross-product of the set {2/3, 1, 3/2} with itself, roughly matching the mean and spread of aspect ratios produced by a squarified treemap layout." Findings: "the accuracy of rectangular area judgments matches that of circular area judgments. We found a significant (p < 0.05) effect of aspect ratio on judgment [error]"; "rectangles with aspect ratio 1 [squares] exhibited the worst performance, a result robust across both the rectangle and treemap display conditions"; implication: "the 'squarified' optimization objective of leading treemap algorithms may rest on tenuous perceptual footing."

4. RELEVANCE TO BUBBLE/DOT (CIRCULAR) AREA — the paper itself bridges this: it explicitly compares rectangular-area accuracy to its own CIRCULAR area judgment task (T7, Cleveland & McGill's elementary task), finding they match. So the inference to bubble/dot-area encoding is made by the authors, not an overreach by the claim.

The supporting quote ("We also conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms)...") accurately reflects the source, and the deeper PDF text confirms the experiment was controlled, methodologically grounded in Cleveland & McGill, and produced empirical accuracy data. Source is peer-reviewed (ACM CHI 2010), canonical for graphical-perception replication, fetched and read directly from idl.cs.washington.edu/vis.stanford.edu mirrors. No contradicting evidence found. Claim strength matches a primary peer-reviewed source.

#### V43 `a8404e06` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Prior perceptually-uniform colormap design work erred by relying on CIELAB, because CIELAB is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies — so it does not guarantee uniformity for the higher-frequency detail in real data images."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Previous work in the design of perceptually uniform colour maps has mostly failed to recognise that CIELAB space is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully restates its source and is independently corroborated by established color science. (1) Quote fidelity: Kovesi (arXiv:1509.03700, "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them," 2015) states verbatim that "CIELAB space is only designed to be perceptually uniform at very low spatial frequencies" and that previous colormap work "failed to recognise" this — the research claim is a direct restatement, not an overreach. (2) Independent corroboration of the underlying psychophysics, NOT just one author's assertion: CIELAB was derived from discrimination studies using LARGE UNIFORM color patches (i.e., low spatial frequency). Crucially, Zhang & Wandell (1997, Journal of the Society for Information Display) created S-CIELAB explicitly because "standard CIELAB ΔE is suitable for use on large uniform color targets, but not on images, because color sensitivity changes as a function of spatial pattern" and "measurements of color appearance with fine-scale patterns differ from those with large uniform color patches." S-CIELAB adds a spatial pre-filtering (contrast-sensitivity) step before the ΔE computation — its very existence is independent, peer-reviewed proof (predating Kovesi by ~18 years) that plain CIELAB does not guarantee uniformity at higher spatial frequencies. (3) Source quality matches claim strength: Kovesi's paper is the foundational, heavily-cited reference behind the ColorCET/colorcet perceptually-uniform colormap libraries (ported to R pals/cetcolor, Julia, Python/HoloViz, used across the matplotlib ecosystem). (4) Not outdated/not marketing: color-appearance psychophysics is mature and slow-moving; no contradicting source surfaced — every source found reinforced the limitation. Minor nuance: the word "erred" is Kovesi's editorial framing, but the factual core (CIELAB uniformity calibrated at low spatial frequency, not extending to high-frequency detail) stands independently via the S-CIELAB literature.

*Evidence:* Every element of the claim is confirmed verbatim from the original peer-reviewed paper (Heer & Bostock, CHI 2010, "Crowdsourcing Graphical Perception"), text extracted directly from the canonical UW/Stanford PDF. (1) Spatial encoding: "EXPERIMENT 1A: PROPORTIONAL JUDGMENT... We first replicated Cleveland & McGill's seminal study [7] on Mechanical Turk"; types 1-5 = "Cleveland & McGill's original position-length experiment." (5) Viability: "Our results demonstrate that crowdsourced perception experiments are viable." Independent corroboration via Semantic Scholar, ResearchGate, ACM DL. Source quality is a primary peer-reviewed CHI paper — sufficient for the claim's strength. Claim is accurate, current, and well-sourced.

#### V45 `a8d3822b` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://scottbateman.github.io/publication/2010-01-01-Useful-junk-The-effects-of-visual-embellishment-on-comprehension-and-memorability-of-charts (primary)

(2) "20 participants" confirmed verbatim: "Twenty participants (9 male, 11 female), aged between 18 and 40." (3) Within-subjects confirmed: each participant saw all 14 charts alternating Holmes/plain, counterbalanced. (4) The three named dimensions match the paper's interactive (chart-visible) accuracy results exactly, all non-significant: subject (t=0.84, p=.412), categories (t=1.38, p=.185), trend (t=0.23, p=.818). NUANCE (not a refutation): on a fourth dimension, "value message," embellished charts scored significantly BETTER (t=3.37, p=.003), not equal — which the claim omits but which only strengthens its anti-Tufte framing. The claim does not overstate: it correctly limits "no difference" to subject/categories/trend.
*Counter-source:* Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (perceptualedge.com) and Kaiser Fung critique attack the study's generalizability (badly-designed "plain" controls, n=20) and the memorability claim — but do NOT dispute the no-difference-in-comprehension finding. Confirmed via cms633.github.io chartjunk-debate commentary and speakingppt.com summary.
