#### V48 `a9db4d4a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color-vision-deficiency (CVD) safety varies systematically by scheme type: sequential schemes are all usable by red-green colorblind readers because they encode lightness differences; most diverging hue pairs were chosen to be CVD-safe (except red-yellow-green and full-spectral); but almost all qualitative (categorical) schemes are difficult for colorblind readers. This gives a concrete, evidence-informed rule for designing CVD-safe palettes."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Generally, the hue pairs for diverging schemes were selected to accommodate colour-blind readers, with the exception of red-yellow-green and full spectral schemes. Almost all of the qualitative schemes are difcult for colour-blind readers and all of the sequential schemes are useful to them, because they include visible lightness differences."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully transcribes the primary source. Direct PDF extraction of Harrower & Brewer (2003), The Cartographic Journal, confirms verbatim: "Generally, the hue pairs for diverging schemes were selected to accommodate colour-blind readers, with the exception of red-yellow-green and full spectral schemes. Almost all of the qualitative schemes are difcult for colour-blind readers and all of the sequential schemes are useful to them, because they include visible lightness differences." All three factual sub-claims map onto the quote exactly; no misread.

Refutation attempt 1 (contradiction): FAILS. An independent evaluation using the Color Oracle dichromacy simulator (Shah Taj, GeoMundus 2019) corroborates: "all ColorBrewer sequential color schemes were color blind safe, this was further confirmed when Color Oracle simulations were run," and shows diverging schemes remaining "distinguishable" under deuteranope/protanope/tritanope simulation. Mechanism is sound color science (monotonic lightness ramp is preserved under all CVD types). Wikipedia/RColorBrewer docs also state all sequential palettes are colorblind-safe and only a subset of diverging are.

Refutation attempt 2 (outdated): FAILS. 2003 paper, but the classifications still ship in the live colorbrewer2.org tool, RColorBrewer, matplotlib, d3; principle reinforced (not overturned) by later perceptually-uniform colormaps (viridis/cividis). Nothing in the literature reverses "sequential = CVD-robust, qualitative = the problem case."

Refutation attempt 3 (overreach): PARTIAL only — does not kill. The source itself hedges: its CVD ratings were "approximate," based on theory (Olson & Brewer 1997) plus "an individual with red-green colour impairment" (n=1), with the authors asking for "more thorough testing." And "usable/useful" is not "every class pair discriminable": the same independent eval notes sequential steps are "easy to see in the legend but difficult to distinguish when they appear on the map," and colorbrewer2.org's safe flag is class-count dependent (a ramp can lose the safe flag at 8-9 classes). BUT the claim self-labels as "evidence-informed" (a heuristic), not "empirically proven," which correctly matches the source's hedged strength, and it inherits the "all usable" phrasing directly from the source rather than inventing it.

Net: only ~1 of 3 attacks lands, and only as a minor qualification, below the >=2/3 kill threshold. Caveat to carry forward when turning this into a skill: treat "all sequential = CVD-safe" as scheme-family-level and class-count-conditional, and verify the specific class count in the colorbrewer2.org safe filter rather than assuming a 9-class ramp is fully discriminable.
*Counter-source:* Independent corroboration: Shah Taj, "Evaluation of ColorBrewer & Color Oracle for Colorblind-Safe Cartography," GeoMundus 2019 (https://erasmus-mundus-geotech-master.github.io/Geomundus2019/docs/papers/ShahTaj.pdf) — simulator-tested, confirms all sequential schemes CVD-safe and diverging schemes distinguishable. Primary source verified by direct extraction: Harrower & Brewer (2003), The Cartographic Journal 40(1) (https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf). Caveat source: colorbrewer2.org live tool, whose "colorblind safe" filter is class-count dependent. No source contradicts the claim.

#### V49 `a9e6f4c5` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The paper identifies a set of elementary perceptual tasks people use to extract quantitative values from charts and ranks them by accuracy, then uses that ranking to recommend chart forms — implying position-based encodings are more accurate than area-, angle-, or color/shading-based ones (relevant to encoding magnitude via dot area)."

**Source:** https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01621459.1984.10478080 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The authors identify elementary perceptual tasks people perform when extracting quantitative data from visualizations, then rank these tasks by accuracy."

*Evidence:* CONFIRMED, not refuted. The primary source — Cleveland & McGill (1984), "Graphical Perception: Theory, Experimentation, and Application to the Development of Graphical Methods," JASA 79(387):531-554 — supports every part of the claim. (1) The paper identifies "elementary perceptual tasks" people perform to extract quantitative information from graphs. (2) It hypothesizes an ordering of these tasks by accuracy of human judgment. (3) Its title and abstract explicitly cover "Application to the Development of Graphical Methods," and multiple independent summaries confirm it uses the ranking to recommend chart forms (e.g., dot charts / bar charts as replacements for pie charts and divided/stacked bars; framed-rectangle charts instead of shaded statistical maps). (4) The published ordering, most-to-least accurate, is: (1) position along a common scale, (2) position along nonaligned scales, (3) length/direction/angle, (4) area, (5) volume/curvature, (6) shading/color saturation. Area is rank 4 and color/shading is rank 6 — both below all position-based encodings — directly confirming the implication relevant to encoding magnitude via dot AREA: position is more accurate than area, angle, or color/shading. Cross-checks: Heer & Bostock (2010, ACM CHI, "Crowdsourcing Graphical Perception") crowdsourced-replicated the Cleveland-McGill position-length and position-angle experiments "with similar findings," so the claim is NOT outdated or overturned — it remains foundational and replicated. The only nuance (not a refutation): the authors themselves caveat that the list is neither exhaustive nor a set of fully distinct tasks, and they GROUP some tasks (length/direction/angle share rank 3) where evidence didn't permit a finer split — which is exactly why angle is tied with length rather than strictly ranked. This is a precision point, not a contradiction. Source quality is high (peer-reviewed JASA primary + replicated at CHI), matching the claim's strength. No credible contradicting source found.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. The nearest qualifier is the authors' OWN caveat (in the 1984 paper) that the task list is not exhaustive and that certain tasks — length, direction, angle — are grouped at one rank rather than strictly separated; this refines but does not refute the position>area>color ordering. Heer & Bostock (2010, ACM CHI) reinforce rather than dispute it.

#### V50 `aa38d4b6` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The empirical basis for Munzner's Chapter 5 channel-effectiveness treatment is the graphical-perception literature, explicitly Cleveland & McGill's 1984 experiments, Bertin's Semiology of Graphics, and Stevens's measurement-scales work — establishing that the position/length/angle/area/color rankings derive from controlled perceptual studies rather than opinion."

**Source:** https://www.cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/vadbook/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Graphical Perception: Theory, Experimentation and the Application to the Development of Graphical Models by William S. Cleveland and Robert McGill (1984) - appears to be the primary empirical work on channel effectiveness"

*Evidence:* The three citations are real: Munzner's Ch. 5 "Marks and Channels" does build on Bertin's Semiology of Graphics (1967), Cleveland & McGill (1984), and Stevens's psychophysics/scales — confirmed across UBC course slides, the Annual Review of Vision Science (annualreviews.org/10.1146/annurev-vision-110323-110009), and multiple university lecture notes. Cleveland & McGill is genuinely the empirical backbone. BUT the claim's load-bearing assertion — that the rankings "derive from controlled perceptual studies rather than opinion" — is contradicted by the same literature: (1) "Bertin's rankings were reasoned phenomenologically, while Cleveland and McGill derived their rankings from experimental studies" — Bertin's contribution is theoretical/cartographic analysis, NOT controlled experiments. (2) Mackinlay's extension to nominal/ordinal channels (which Munzner's full ranking incorporates) was "reasoned from existing psychophysics studies, NOT empirically validated in the context of graph comprehension" (arxiv.org/pdf/2109.01271; homepages.cwi.nl/~steven/chi97/doc/ltn2_txt.htm). So Munzner's composite ranking is a blend of empirical (Cleveland-McGill quantitative core) + phenomenological reasoning (Bertin) + un-validated psychophysical inference (Mackinlay/Stevens). The "rather than opinion" framing overstates the empirical basis on exactly the point the claim emphasizes; the established literature draws this distinction explicitly. Additionally, the supporting quote alone only substantiates Cleveland & McGill, not the Bertin/Stevens citations or the "derives from controlled studies" conclusion (which it does not contain).
*Counter-source:* https://arxiv.org/pdf/2109.01271 (Review and Collation of Graphical Perception Knowledge) and https://homepages.cwi.nl/~steven/chi97/doc/ltn2_txt.htm (Mackinlay/Senay-Ignatius graphical encoding) — both document that Bertin's rankings were phenomenological and Mackinlay's psychophysics-based ordinal/nominal extension was not empirically validated; also https://www.annualreviews.org/content/journals/10.1146/annurev-vision-110323-110009

#### V51 `aa7e0faf` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The study conducted controlled experiments specifically on rectangular area perception, the encoding used in treemaps and cartograms, providing empirical data on how accurately humans judge area-based magnitude encodings (directly relevant to bubble/dot-area encoding)."

**Source:** https://idl.uw.edu/papers/crowdsourcing-graphical-perception (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "We also conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms) and on chart size and gridline spacing."

*Evidence:* VERDICT: NOT REFUTED (with one minor imprecision noted). The core claim is verbatim-supported by the primary source and confirmed in the full PDF.

WHAT IS SUPPORTED (strong):
- Heer & Bostock (2010), "Crowdsourcing Graphical Perception" (ACM CHI 2010, peer-reviewed primary source) DID run a controlled experiment specifically on rectangular area perception. The full PDF (idl.cs.washington.edu/files/2010-MTurk-CHI.pdf, mirror of vis.stanford.edu) confirms "Experiment 1B: Rectangular Area Judgments": rectangles with aspect ratios from 1:1 to 5:1, measuring judgment accuracy, finding systematic underestimation following a power law with exponent ~0.8 (consistent with Stevens' power law). This is a genuine controlled experiment, not a passing mention.
- The "treemaps or cartograms" framing is the paper's OWN words, verbatim from the abstract: "we conduct new experiments on rectangular area perception (as in treemaps or cartograms)..." So the claim's "encoding used in treemaps and cartograms" is directly quoted, not an overreach.
- "providing empirical data on how accurately humans judge area-based magnitude encodings" — accurate for rectangular area.

THE ONE WEAKNESS (minor, not refutation-worthy):
- The parenthetical "(directly relevant to bubble/dot-area encoding)" conflates RECTANGULAR area (what the paper tested) with CIRCULAR area (bubbles/dots). The PDF explicitly confirms only rectangular stimuli were tested; circular area was NOT in this study. So "directly relevant" is slightly strong — "relevant by analogy" would be more precise. HOWEVER: (a) this parenthetical is the claim author's framing of relevance, not a statement falsely attributed to the paper; (b) it is defensibly true — independent literature (WebSearch, Stevens' power law) confirms circle-area judgments ALSO follow a power law with underestimation, with a reported circle-area exponent of ~0.79, very close to the rectangular ~0.8. The transferable lesson (area encodings are judged inaccurately with underestimation) does apply to bubbles.

REFUTATION CHECKLIST: (1) supported by quote — yes, core claim is verbatim; (2) contradicting sources — none; sources corroborate; (3) source quality — peer-reviewed CHI 2010 primary source, matches claim strength; (4) outdated — no, foundational and still cited; (5) marketing/cherry-pick — no. Zero of five refutation grounds met. The substantive claim is accurate and primary-sourced; only the bubble parenthetical is imprecise (rectangular vs circular), which is a qualification, not grounds to kill the claim.
*Counter-source:* Full paper PDF (http://idl.cs.washington.edu/files/2010-MTurk-CHI.pdf): confirms Experiment 1B tested RECTANGULAR stimuli only (aspect ratios 1:1 to 5:1), and explicitly did NOT test circular areas — so the claim's "directly relevant to bubble/dot-area" parenthetical conflates rectangular with circular area. Mitigating source (WebSearch / Stevens' power law literature, e.g. en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stevens's_power_law and circle-size studies): circle-area perception follows the same power-law underestimation with exponent ~0.79, so the analogy to bubbles is defensible despite the imprecision.

#### V52 `aa9caf5a` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "There is a practical upper limit on the number of categories/classes that remain reliably distinguishable: ColorBrewer caps schemes at 12 classes, but many sequential schemes stop at 9 because further divisions become perceptually unreliable, and as a rule of thumb cartographers seldom use more than seven classes. This directly bounds how many categorical colors humans can reliably tell apart (relevant to choosing ~3-6 data series)."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "These sets have been designed to produce attractive colour schemes of similar appearance for maps ranging from 3 to 12 classes. Not all colour schemes can be expanded to 12 classes, and many of the sequential scheme sets, for example, stop at 9 classes because further divisions within the scheme are perceptually unreliable... The maximum number of data classes ColorBrewer supports is 12, although only a few of the colour schemes can be divided into this many steps and remain differentiable. As a general rule of thumb, cartographers seldom use more than seven classes on a choropleth map."

*Evidence:* The supporting quote is authentic (Harrower & Brewer, "ColorBrewer.org," The Cartographic Journal 40(1), 2003 — verbatim phrasing "maximum number of data classes ColorBrewer supports is 12... remain differentiable" and "cartographers seldom use more than seven classes on a choropleth map" was independently reproduced by WebSearch against the same paper). But the claim COMMITS A CATEGORY ERROR in interpretation. (1) The "many sequential schemes stop at 9 because further divisions are perceptually unreliable" caveat is EXPLICITLY about SEQUENTIAL schemes — ordered data encoded by lightness steps (light→dark). Slicing one luminance ramp finer is a fundamentally different perceptual task than distinguishing UNORDERED categorical hues. ColorBrewer's own QUALITATIVE (categorical) schemes — Set3 and Paired — go up to 12 colors, and the paper does NOT say categorical hues become unreliable at 9. The claim lifts a sequential-scheme limit and mis-applies it to categorical distinguishability. (2) The "seldom more than seven classes" rule is about DATA CLASSIFICATION (how many bins to cut a continuous variable into on a choropleth, driven by adjacent-color legibility + Miller's 7±2), not how many distinct series colors a viewer can identify in a legend — a harder, different task. (3) Therefore the claim's stated chain ("This directly bounds how many categorical colors humans can reliably tell apart") is a misread: it conflates choropleth classification limits for ordered data with categorical color discrimination. The DIRECTIONAL conclusion (a practical upper bound on categorical colors exists, ~6-8 optimal / ~8-10 practical) is independently supported by the broader dataviz literature, but it is NOT what this specific quote establishes. Refuted on overreach/misattribution of the cited evidence, not on the broad conclusion.
*Counter-source:* https://colorbrewer2.org/learnmore/schemes_full.html (ColorBrewer qualitative schemes Set3/Paired reach 12 colors; qualitative vs sequential vs diverging are distinct types — qualitative "useful if colors have no special order but should be distinguishable," sequential dominated by lightness steps); ColorBrewer learnmore confirms all three scheme types (incl. qualitative) are available at 9 classes

#### V53 `aaa320eb` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For encoding quantitative magnitude, area is among the least accurate channels: the study's new angle/area judgment types confirmed the psychophysical prediction that area performs worse than angle, and both perform significantly worse than position. This directly bears on using dot AREA (bubble/scatter) to encode magnitude."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1753326.1753357 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the new results match expectations: psychophysical theory predicts area to perform worse than angle, and both to be significantly worse than position."

*Evidence:* Claim VERIFIED against primary source and faithful to its supporting quote. Heer & Bostock 2010 (CHI, ACM peer-reviewed) directly replicated and extended Cleveland & McGill 1984 (JASA, foundational). Heer's own slide deck (homes.cs.washington.edu/~jheer/files/jheer-crowdflower.pdf, extracted via PyMuPDF) states Experiment 1 used "original types: position (3) + length (2)" and added "new types: angle + circular area" — confirming AREA was genuinely tested (not just angle), grounding the quote's reference to area. The same source's "Relative Magnitude Comparison" ranking lists, most-to-least accurate: Position (common) > Position (non-aligned) > Length > Slope > Angle > Area > Volume. Cleveland & McGill's hierarchy (cross-confirmed by Iowa STAT4580 course, Wikipedia, multiple sources): position-common > position-nonaligned > length/direction/angle > area > volume/curvature > shading/saturation — angle (rank 3) above area (rank 4), both below position (rank 1); UW CSE412 slides (extracted from saved PDF) show this ordering is based on Stevens' psychophysical power law (area exponent ~0.7 < 1.0). The supporting quote is a faithful, non-overreaching restatement: the claim copies it almost verbatim and the area<angle, both<<position ordering is exactly what the experiments and the cited psychophysical theory predict. Source quality (primary peer-reviewed, ~2000+ citations) matches the claim's strength. The bubble/scatter inference is textbook-correct: dot area IS the area channel these studies measured as least accurate among spatial encodings; Cleveland & McGill themselves recommended replacing area/angle encodings with position/length. ADVERSARIAL CHECK — the strongest potential contradiction, McColeman/Yang/Brady/Franconeri 2021 "Rethinking the Ranks of Visual Channels" (IEEE TVCG), found the classic ranking does NOT hold for a DIFFERENT task (reproducing 2-8 previously-seen values) and that other factors (mark count, value magnitude) dominate channel choice — but it does NOT claim area beats angle/position in the two-value ratio-judgment paradigm the claim is scoped to, so it qualifies generalizability to other tasks rather than refuting this specific finding. Claim is appropriately hedged ("among the least accurate," not "the least accurate" — correct, since color/saturation rank even lower). Survived all refutation attempts.
*Counter-source:* McColeman, Yang, Brady & Franconeri (2021), "Rethinking the Ranks of Visual Channels," IEEE TVCG (arxiv.org/abs/2107.11367) — qualifies but does NOT refute: it shows the ranking fails for a multi-value reproduction task and that mark-count/value-magnitude dominate, yet does not overturn area<angle<position for the two-value ratio-judgment paradigm the claim addresses.

#### V54 `aae7dd52` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Empirical testing of Tufte-style 'chartjunk' minimalism is mixed rather than settled: visual embellishment does not reliably destroy comprehension, so the blanket prescription to eliminate non-data ink is opinion/convention, not a uniformly supported empirical finding. This study extends prior embellishment work (Bateman et al.) to larger datasets (10+ data points) and time-limited viewing."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Although many well-cited theories or guidelines for visualization design advocate 'minimalism', designers tend to include a wide variety of visual embellishments in their charts... Various studies have shown mixed results depending on the details of the task and context."

*Evidence:* Claim survives skeptical scrutiny on all three components. (1) SOURCE IS REAL AND CORRECTLY DESCRIBED: Li & Moacdieh (2014), "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment," Proc. Human Factors and Ergonomics Society Annual Meeting 58(1):1516-1520 — a peer-reviewed venue. It does extend Bateman et al. (2010) to charts with 10+ data points and time-limited viewing, exactly as claimed. (2) QUOTE SUPPORTS CLAIM: "Various studies have shown mixed results depending on the details of the task and context" directly backs "mixed rather than settled." (3) INDEPENDENTLY CORROBORATED by multiple peer-reviewed sources, not just the cited paper: Bateman et al. (2010, CHI, dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/1753326.1753716) found embellished Holmes-style charts were no worse in comprehension accuracy and significantly better in long-term recall; Borgo et al. (2012, IEEE TVCG 18(12):2759-2768) found via dual-task paradigm that embellishments did NOT raise cognitive load and aided memory retention. This is a genuinely contested area in vis research. (4) APPROPRIATELY HEDGED: the claim says embellishment "does not RELIABLY destroy comprehension" and minimalism is not "UNIFORMLY supported" — it does NOT overclaim that minimalism is wrong or that chartjunk is good. This weak framing is well-supported. The counter-evidence (Borgo: embellishment slows visual-search/analysis tasks; Kosara/eagereyes caveat: studies measured message-recall not numerical accuracy, and Holmes charts were expertly executed) does not refute the claim because the claim only asserts the evidence is "mixed," which is precisely what these costs-plus-benefits findings show. The "opinion/convention not uniformly empirically supported" framing is the actual mainstream position in the visualization literature.
*Counter-source:* Borgo et al. (2012, IEEE TVCG) and Kosara/eagereyes.org both note embellishment carries real costs (slower visual search for analysis tasks; memorability studies measured message-recall not numerical accuracy; Holmes charts were expertly executed) — i.e., minimalism is not pure opinion. But these QUALIFY rather than refute, since the claim only asserts the evidence is "mixed," not that minimalism is wrong.

#### V55 `ab04db34` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow colormap's luminance varies non-monotonically and uncontrolled across its range, which obscures/hides fine detail in scalar data (low-luminance and high-luminance bands suppress perceived contrast unevenly)."

**Source:** https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation"

*Evidence:* The supporting quote "obscures data through its uncontrolled luminance variation" is a verified verbatim quote from Borland & Taylor (2007), "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," IEEE CG&A 27(2):14-17 (the cited IEEE doc 4118486), confirmed by multiple independent sources (MathWorks annotated bibliography, ROOT/CERN, Towards Data Science). The "non-monotonic luminance" portion is also directly documented as one of the three classic arguments: rainbow maps "are not monotonic in luminance, which can distort the shapes people infer from the data." The "suppresses fine detail / perceived contrast unevenly" elaboration matches the documented finding that "equal steps in data values do not map to equal steps in perceived colour, which means fine details are obscured." Same point traces back further to Rogowitz & Treinish (1998), "Data Visualization: The End of the Rainbow." Checked the obvious refutation candidate — Ware et al. (2023) "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE) — but it does NOT dispute the luminance property; it accepts the perceptual characteristics and argues rainbows remain useful for selected tasks. That is a scope qualification on usefulness, not a contradiction of the luminance fact. Minor nuance: "uncontrolled" is the authors' framing (the ramp has a luminance profile, just not a monotonic/designed one), and the parenthetical gloss is a reasonable elaboration rather than a misread. Source quality (primary peer-reviewed IEEE article + corroborating literature) matches the claim strength; the property is a stable perceptual fact, not an outdated or fast-moving claim.
*Counter-source:* Ware, Turton, Bujack et al. (2023), "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE CG&A (https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/10128890) — but this qualifies usefulness for selected tasks; it does not refute the non-monotonic/uncontrolled luminance property itself.

#### V56 `ab37f1a2` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In a controlled within-subjects experiment (20 participants), there was no measurable difference in interpretation accuracy between heavily embellished charts (Nigel Holmes 'chartjunk' style, low data-ink) and plain minimalist charts for describing the chart's subject, its categories, or its trend - directly challenging the Tufte data-ink/chartjunk premise that embellishment harms comprehension."

**Source:** https://scottbateman.github.io/publication/2010-01-01-Useful-junk-The-effects-of-visual-embellishment-on-comprehension-and-memorability-of-charts (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "First, we did not find any difference in people's in terpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts, despite the fact that the embellished charts contained a much larger amount of non-data-ink."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source (Bateman et al., CHI 2010 PDF text extracted directly). Every factual element of the claim checks out:

(1) 20 PARTICIPANTS: "Twenty participants (9 male, 11 female), aged between 18 and 40 were recruited from a local university (7 graduate, 13 undergraduate). Of the 20 participants..." Confirmed.

(2) WITHIN-SUBJECTS for the interpretation comparison: "Each participant saw only one version of each chart, either Holmes or plain. Presentation order for the versions was counterbalanced." Each participant viewed 7 Holmes + 7 plain charts and was tested on both conditions; accuracy was compared via PAIRED t-tests with df=19 (t19), i.e. n=20 within-subject. Confirmed. (The separate recall task used two between-subjects groups of 10, but the claim concerns interpretation accuracy, which was within-subjects.)

(3) NO DIFFERENCE IN INTERPRETATION ACCURACY for subject/categories/trend — verbatim: "There were no differences in the scores of chart descriptions for the subject of the chart (t19=0.84, p=.412), the categories in the charts (t19=1.38, p=.185), or the trend of the charts (t19=0.23, p=.818)." This maps EXACTLY onto the claim's "describing the chart's subject, its categories, or its trend." Confirmed.

(4) Abstract verbatim: "First, we did not find any difference in people's interpretation accuracy between the embellished and minimalist charts, despite the fact that the embellished charts contained a much larger amount of non-data-ink." Matches the supporting quote.

(5) DIRECTLY CHALLENGES TUFTE: the authors themselves frame it this way ("our results question some of the premises of the minimalist approach"); they explicitly cite Tufte/Cleveland data-ink minimalism as the target.

The claim is precisely SCOPED and does not overreach. It limits itself to the three measures where no difference was found. Note: the paper DID find one difference — in describing the "value message" (t19=3.37, p=.003) — but that difference FAVORED the embellished charts (better, not worse), so it does not undercut the claim; the claim correctly omits it from the "no difference" list.

ON CONTRADICTING EVIDENCE: Credible critics exist (Andrew Gelman: "Is chartjunk really 'more useful' than plain graphs? I don't think so"; Stephen Few: the "plain" charts were "plain ugly"; Robert Kosara). BUT — and this is decisive for an adversarial review — none of them dispute the within-study no-difference-in-accuracy FINDING itself. They dispute GENERALIZABILITY: they argue the "plain" baseline was a strawman (axis lines removed, etc.), so the result doesn't license the broader inference that chartjunk beats WELL-designed minimalist charts. That criticism attacks the conclusion "chartjunk is better," not the narrow empirical claim under review (no measured accuracy difference vs these particular plain charts, challenging the data-ink premise). The claim as worded does not assert chartjunk is superior in accuracy — only that no accuracy penalty was measured. That is exactly what the data show and what even critics concede.

SOURCE QUALITY: Primary peer-reviewed paper, ACM CHI 2010 (a top-tier venue), heavily cited foundational study. Not outdated for what it claims (it is itself the cited landmark). Not marketing/forum speculation.
*Counter-source:* Critiques exist but target generalization, not the finding: Andrew Gelman, "Is chartjunk really 'more useful' than plain graphs? I don't think so" (statmodeling.stat.columbia.edu, 2010); Stephen Few, "The Chartjunk Debate" (perceptualedge.com) — argues the Bateman "plain" charts were "plain ugly" and not Tufte-standard minimalist; Robert Kosara, "Chart Junk Considered Useful After All" (eagereyes.org). All three accept the no-accuracy-difference result while disputing the inference that embellishment is therefore preferable to well-designed minimal charts. Extended follow-up: Li & Moacdieh (2014), "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment" — examines larger datasets where embellishment may impose accuracy costs, i.e., qualifies scope but does not refute Bateman's small-chart finding.

#### V57 `ab653a0f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Many vendor-supplied colormaps have highly uneven perceptual contrast across their range, including 'perceptual flat spots' that can hide a data feature as large as one tenth (10%) of the total data range."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Many colour maps provided by vendors have highly uneven perceptual contrast over their range. It is not uncommon for colour maps to have perceptual flat spots that can hide a feature as large as one tenth of the total data range."

*Evidence:* Quote is verbatim in the abstract of Kovesi (2015) "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them" (arXiv:1509.03700), confirmed via WebFetch, ar5iv HTML, and two WebSearches. The claim under review is a faithful near-verbatim paraphrase that preserves the original hedge ("not uncommon"), so no overreach. The "one tenth" figure is substantiated in the body, not asserted blind: the paper's 'sineramp' test image is deliberately built so peak-to-trough features equal 10% of the data range (Sec 1.1: "the range from peak to trough represents a series of features that are 10% of the total data range"), and vendor maps from MathWorks, Geosoft, ESRI, and Wolfram are shown failing it (Fig 3), with rainbow green/cyan flat spots demonstrated (Fig 1). The underlying mechanism (CIELAB is only perceptually uniform at low spatial frequency; lightness difference dominates fine-detail contrast) is independently corroborated by van der Walt & Smith (viridis), Petroff 2019, HESS 2021, and PLOS One 2017. Source is an arXiv preprint (not formally peer-reviewed), but it is foundational and widely cited, with the companion CET colormaps (colorcet.com) adopted across matplotlib/Julia/R. Claim is current (perceptual facts stable through 2021+ literature) and non-commercial. Only mild caveat: "one tenth" is the author's chosen test-amplitude demonstrating that flat spots CAN hide a 10%-amplitude feature — which is exactly the hedged wording of the claim ("can hide ... as large as one tenth"), not a measured universal worst case. No contradicting source found.

#### V58 `abcef927` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The single most important factor in designing a good colormap is ensuring that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness across the map is uniform."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The most important factor in designing a colour map is to ensure that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness of the colours is uniform."

*Evidence:* The quote is verbatim-accurate from the primary source (Kovesi 2015, "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them," arXiv:1509.03700): "The most important factor in designing a colour map is to ensure that the magnitude of the incremental change in perceptual lightness of the colours is uniform." So the claim is NOT a misread of its source. However, the claim's absolute strength ("THE single most important factor") reflects ONE author's emphatic framing, not the scholarly consensus, and is contradicted/heavily-qualified by the peer-reviewed literature: (1) Bujack, Turton & Ware et al., "The Good, the Bad, and the Ugly: A Theoretical Framework for the Assessment of Continuous Colormaps" (IEEE TVCG / VIS) define THREE distinct co-equal properties — order, uniformity, AND discriminative power — with none singularly dominant; perceptual uniformity is described merely as "one of the most commonly cited requirements," not THE most important. (2) Liu & Heer, "Somewhere Over the Rainbow: An Empirical Assessment of Quantitative Colormaps" (CHI 2018) found the best colormap is task-dependent and that discriminability/discriminative power is critical (e.g., Inferno's steeper lightness slope gives more JND discriminative power; grayscale's uniform lightness can suffer LOW discriminability in low-contrast regions — a direct counterexample to lightness-uniformity-as-paramount). (3) Kenneth Moreland's authoritative "Color Map Advice" presents context-dependent priorities (3D shading vs 2D images) rather than one supreme principle. (4) Post-2015 work (Nuñez/cividis 2018; Crameri 2020/2024) elevated CVD-safety and hue/order considerations alongside lightness. Source-quality note: the claim rests on a single non-peer-reviewed arXiv technical report from 2015; the "single most important" superlative is an unsupported universalization of that one expert's wording. Uniform lightness change is strongly evidence-backed as A central/necessary property, but the absolute "THE single most important factor" is an overstatement the broader literature does not endorse.
*Counter-source:* Bujack, Turton, Ware et al., "The Good, the Bad, and the Ugly: A Theoretical Framework for the Assessment of Continuous Colormaps" (IEEE TVCG, https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/37f5c97a235057763d41186fca500c001c2a3b21) — defines order, uniformity, and discriminative power as three distinct co-equal properties, none dominant; also Liu & Heer, "Somewhere Over the Rainbow" CHI 2018 (https://idl.cs.washington.edu/files/2018-QuantitativeColor-CHI.pdf) — colormap effectiveness is task-dependent and discriminability is critical.

#### V59 `abd23701` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Chart type (embellished Holmes-style vs. plain) significantly affected short-term recall of chart content, and the availability of a time limit affected both comprehension and short-term recall performance — i.e., embellishment effects are moderated by viewing-time constraints rather than being universally good or bad."

**Source:** https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/1541931214581316 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Results showed that the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall."

*Evidence:* The claim's two factual halves are verbatim-supported by the source abstract, reproduced identically across SAGE, ResearchGate, and Semantic Scholar: "the presence of a time limit affected comprehension and short-term recall performance, while the type of chart significantly affected short-term recall." The paper is genuine and peer-reviewed: Li, H. & Moacdieh, N. (2014), "Is 'chart junk' useful? An extended examination of visual embellishment," Proc. Human Factors and Ergonomics Society Annual Meeting 58(1):1516-1520, DOI 10.1177/1541931214581316. Conditions: embellished Nigel-Holmes charts vs. plain grayscale charts, larger datasets (>=10 data points), viewed under different time-limit conditions. No credible source contradicts it; it sits consistently within the mixed/context-dependent chartjunk literature (Bateman et al. 2010, which used NO time limit and found a long-term recall benefit; Gillan & Richman 1994). Two caveats, neither rising to refutation: (1) The interpretive gloss "moderated by viewing-time constraints" slightly overstates the quote — the abstract reports chart type and time limit as separate MAIN effects, not explicitly an interaction; "moderated" implies a statistical interaction the quote does not assert. (2) Source is a short HFES conference proceedings paper, and the exact F/p statistics and sample size could not be retrieved (publisher restricts the abstract field; full text paywalled), so the precise statistics remain unverified though the direction is consistently reported. The core claim — chart-type significantly affected recall, time limit affected both comprehension and recall, embellishment is context-dependent not universally good/bad — is accurate and well-sourced.

#### V60 `abdddf96` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For categorical/segmentation color coding where data marks must be found rapidly and accurately (preattentive visual search), only about 5 to 7 distinct colors can be reliably identified with a low error rate; Healey tested displays with 3, 5, 7, and 9 colors and found rapid low-error identification breaks down as the count rises toward 7-9. This directly bounds how many categorical colors to use for a small number of data series (~3-6 is safely within the reliable range)."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/2639665_Choosing_Effective_Colours_for_Data_Visualization (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Healey (1996) found that only 5 to 7 colours can be found rapidly and with a low error rate... studies using 3, 5, 7, and 9 colors found rapid identification for 3 and 5 colors but mixed results for 7 and 9 colors"

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source (Healey 1996, "Choosing Effective Colours for Data Visualization," IEEE Visualization '96), extracted verbatim from the PDF, plus independent peer-reviewed corroboration.

(1) FOUR STUDIES CONFIRMED VERBATIM: "three-colour study... R, GY, and PB; five-colour study... R, Y, GY, B, and P; seven-colour study... R, Y, GY, G, BG, P, and RP; nine-colour study... R, YR, Y, GY, G, BG, PB, P, and RP." Exact match to the claim's "3, 5, 7, and 9 colors."

(2) PAPER'S OWN CONCLUSION (verbatim): "We found that an isoluminant slice through a monitor's gamut yielded up to seven different colours, any one of which could be rapidly and accurately detected, even in the presence of all the others." The paper also cites the prior guideline "no more than five to seven colours at a time" (rendered "vetoseven" in extraction — the 'fi' ligature dropped). This directly supports "about 5 to 7."

(3) BREAKDOWN AT 7-9 CONFIRMED VERBATIM: Seven-colour study — "Mean response error... was still low, at approximately 3.3%... The G and GY targets, however, gave response times typical of serial search" (partial degradation at 7). Nine-colour study — "Overall mean response error increased to 8.1%... highest for the G, GY, and RP targets (approximately 14%)... The remaining targets showed some form of serial search" (clear degradation at 9). This matches the claim's "rapid identification for 3 and 5 colors but mixed results for 7 and 9."

(4) INDEPENDENT PEER-REVIEWED CORROBORATION: Stojanovic et al. 2021, HESS (Copernicus, peer-reviewed): "Healey (1996) showed that only around seven different hues can be found accurately and rapidly on a map." Multiple academic citations independently render the finding as "only 5 to 7 colours can be found rapidly and with a low error rate."

The claim's applied conclusion ("~3-6 is safely within the reliable range") is conservative and correct, since Healey's clean ceiling was up to seven well-chosen colors. The only qualification (not a refutation): Healey's seven required controlling colour distance, linear separation, and colour category — it is up to seven *carefully selected* colors, not any seven. The claim does not contradict this. No contradicting source was found; the bound is well-established and still cited in 2021. Source: https://vis.cs.brown.edu/docs/pdf/Healey-1996-CEC.pdf (primary, read in full); https://hess.copernicus.org/articles/25/4549/2021/ (corroborating).

Source quality (peer-reviewed top statistics journal, primary) far exceeds the modest historical/descriptive claim. A purely descriptive statement about a 1984 paper's stated purpose cannot be outdated. All three refutation attempts (quote misread / contradiction / source quality) fail.

Viridis remains a colorblind-friendly colormap; cividis was optimized further specifically for CVD equivalence. The claim's wording does not overstate this. The pronoun "she" is correct (Tamara Munzner). Source quality (primary author page + academic publisher) exceeds what is needed for this structural/bibliographic claim.

#### V65 `ad4863cb` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The empirical basis for Munzner's Chapter 5 channel-effectiveness treatment is the graphical-perception literature, explicitly Cleveland & McGill's 1984 experiments, Bertin's Semiology of Graphics, and Stevens's measurement-scales work — establishing that the position/length/angle/area/color rankings derive from controlled perceptual studies rather than opinion."

**Source:** https://www.cs.ubc.ca/~tmm/vadbook/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Graphical Perception: Theory, Experimentation and the Application to the Development of Graphical Models by William S. Cleveland and Robert McGill (1984) - appears to be the primary empirical work on channel effectiveness"

*Evidence:* Verified across 5 independent authoritative sources. (1) Munzner's Ch.5 "Marks and Channels" explicitly cites all three works in its Further Reading: Stevens (1946, "On the Theory of Scales of Measurement," Science), Bertin's "Semiology of Graphics," and Cleveland & McGill (1984, "Graphical Perception," JASA 79(387)). (2) Univ. of Iowa STAT4580 (Tierney) independently confirms "Munzner's ordering by accuracy" follows the ladder from Cleveland-McGill "controlled experiments" (~50 subjects, "elementary perceptual tasks") and explicitly frames it as "experimental validation over theoretical opinion," reinforced by Heer-Bostock (2010) crowdsourced replication. (3) Wisconsin CS765 calls Cleveland & McGill (1984) "the first rigorous attempt to understand how people perform at reading encodings" and the empirical foundation, with Bertin as origin of the visual-variables concept. (4) O'Reilly book preview confirms §5.5 Channel Effectiveness (Accuracy/Discriminability/etc.) + §5.7 Further Reading. (5) Stevens's role confirmed nuanced-but-real: his psychophysical power law underpins the magnitude-channels accuracy treatment. Minor non-refuting caveat: Munzner's specific ranking also draws on Mackinlay (1986), who formalized Cleveland&McGill+Bertin into the channel-by-type ranking; Bertin's contribution is taxonomic/theoretical and Stevens's is the psychophysical model, so not all three are "controlled experiments" per se — but the claim correctly bundles them as the basis and correctly attributes the empirical grounding to Cleveland & McGill. The supporting quote is accurate and if anything understates the case. Claim is current (still-standard citations, echoed in 2021-2024 "Rethinking the Ranks of Visual Channels" work) and source quality (primary book + multiple academic courses) matches claim strength.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The closest tension is recent work (e.g., "Rethinking the Ranks of Visual Channels," IEEE VIS 2021; "The Risks of Ranking," TVCG 2022) that questions the universality/individual-difference robustness of the rankings — but this critiques the rankings' validity, not the claim that Munzner's basis is Cleveland&McGill + Bertin + Stevens; if anything it confirms those are the canonical sources being revisited.

#### V67 `adca65d8` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "There is a practical upper limit on the number of categories/classes that remain reliably distinguishable: ColorBrewer caps schemes at 12 classes, but many sequential schemes stop at 9 because further divisions become perceptually unreliable, and as a rule of thumb cartographers seldom use more than seven classes. This directly bounds how many categorical colors humans can reliably tell apart (relevant to choosing ~3-6 data series)."

**Source:** https://www.cs.rpi.edu/~cutler/classes/visualization/S18/papers/colorbrewer.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "These sets have been designed to produce attractive colour schemes of similar appearance for maps ranging from 3 to 12 classes. Not all colour schemes can be expanded to 12 classes, and many of the sequential scheme sets, for example, stop at 9 classes because further divisions within the scheme are perceptually unreliable... The maximum number of data classes ColorBrewer supports is 12, although only a few of the colour schemes can be divided into this many steps and remain differentiable. As a general rule of thumb, cartographers seldom use more than seven classes on a choropleth map."

*Evidence:* The supporting quote is verbatim-accurate (Harrower & Brewer 2003, "ColorBrewer.org," confirmed via search of the source PDF: sequential sets "stop at 9 classes because further divisions within the scheme are perceptually unreliable"; max 12; "cartographers seldom use more than seven classes on a choropleth map"). BUT the claim overreaches/misreads it. The quoted numbers describe CHOROPLETH maps, which use SEQUENTIAL/ORDERED schemes (light-to-dark value shading of ONE variable) — i.e., how many luminance STEPS you can rank-order, not how many distinct categorical HUES you can tell apart. The claim's load-bearing sentence ("This DIRECTLY bounds how many categorical colors humans can reliably tell apart") makes an invalid sequential→categorical inference. (1) These are different perceptual problems: the cartographic literature is explicit — "when value alone is used (light to dark shading), it is difficult to practically use more than seven classes. However, if differences in hue and/or saturation are incorporated, that limit increases significantly to as many as 10-12 classes." The "seven" the claim cites is specifically the value-shading (sequential) figure. (2) ColorBrewer's own QUALITATIVE (categorical) schemes go to 12 (Paired, Set3) and stop there for legend-matching burden, not because adjacent hues become indistinguishable. (3) Peer-reviewed work directly contradicts a hard categorical ceiling: Polychrome (Coombes et al., bioRxiv/J. Stat. Software 2019) constructs ≥24 categorical colors distinguishable by most normal-vision viewers, and explicitly frames the "qualitative palettes yield only ~6-8 colors" figure as a TOOL limitation (fixed chroma/luminance), not a perceptual law. The claim's practical takeaway (keep categorical series to single digits; ~3-6 is fine) happens to be well-supported by OTHER sources (Wilke: 3-5 optimal, ≤8 max; colorspace toolbox: 6-8 clearly distinguishable; rule-of-thumb ≤10) — but that conclusion is reached via cognitive/usability limits (legend-matching load), NOT via the choropleth/sequential class-count evidence the claim cites. So the inference chain is broken even though the endpoint coincidentally lands near a defensible number. Source quality is high but is being used to support a claim it does not make (the quoted passage is about sequential choropleth classification, silent on categorical hue limits).
*Counter-source:* Coombes, Brock, Abrams & Abruzzo, "Polychrome: Creating and Assessing Qualitative Palettes With Many Colors" (Journal of Statistical Software / bioRxiv 2019, https://www.biorxiv.org/content/10.1101/303883.full.pdf) — ≥24 distinguishable categorical colors, "6-8" is a tool limit not a perceptual ceiling; Wilke, "Fundamentals of Data Visualization" ch. on color pitfalls (https://clauswilke.com/dataviz/color-pitfalls.html) — categorical limit driven by legend-matching burden (3-5 optimal, ≤8), not by the choropleth class-count; Zeileis et al., colorspace toolbox (https://arxiv.org/pdf/1903.06490) — qualitative ~6-8 distinguishable; cartographic synthesis: value-only shading caps ~7 classes but hue/saturation raises it to 10-12 (gisgeography / saylordotorg data-classification sources). Primary source itself: Harrower & Brewer 2003 "ColorBrewer.org" (the quoted passage concerns sequential choropleth schemes, not qualitative/categorical color counts).

The claim accurately represents what the paper found and concluded.

Not outdated (2018, reaffirmed 2020). Not marketing. Minor nuance, consistent with the claim's careful wording: viridis is still considered reasonably CVD-friendly; the claim correctly says it is not OPTIMAL for CVD, not that it is bad.

The known refinement work in this area (Talbot et al. 2014, "Four Experiments on the Perception of Bar Charts") qualifies WITHIN-bar-chart effects, not the position-vs-length TYPE hierarchy, so it does not contradict this claim.

#### V71 `aefc6b35` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A colormap should use a linear (monotonic) increase in lightness to avoid the perception of gradients that are not actually present in the data — the core flaw that makes rainbow/jet colormaps misleading."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A linear increase in lightness should be used to avoid the perception of gradients that are not present."

*Evidence:* Claim is faithfully supported by the primary source and independently corroborated. PRIMARY: PLOS ONE 2018, Nuñez, Anderton & Renslow, "Optimizing colormaps with consideration for color vision deficiency to enable accurate interpretation of scientific data" (doi:10.1371/journal.pone.0199239) states verbatim: "First, a linear increase in lightness should be used to avoid the perception of gradients that are not present." The claim is a near-exact restatement, not an overreach. CORROBORATION (independent, peer-reviewed): Borland & Taylor (2007), "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications, identifies the rainbow map's flaws as "uncontrolled luminance variation" and "introduction of non-data-dependent gradients" plus "false boundaries" — i.e., non-monotonic luminance creating apparent structure absent from the data, exactly the mechanism in the claim. Also corroborated by Kenneth Moreland's color-advice reference (monotonic luminance a core principle behind Inferno/Kindlmann maps) and Matplotlib's perceptually-uniform colormap docs (basis of Viridis/Cividis). SOURCE QUALITY: peer-reviewed primary + peer-reviewed corroboration; mainstream visualization-science consensus, not an extraordinary claim. CURRENCY: 2018 source, principle dates to 1990s-2000s and remains the foundation of modern perceptually-uniform colormaps in 2026 — not outdated. NOT marketing/forum/cherry-pick. ONE NUANCE (does not refute): the claim calls non-monotonic lightness "THE core flaw," but Borland & Taylor list it alongside two others (lack of perceptual hue ordering; hue-banding false boundaries), so "the core flaw" slightly overstates singularity — phrasing imprecision, not a factual error. The 2023 paper "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (PubMed 37195830) qualifies WHEN rainbow is acceptable (hue aids some discrimination tasks) but does NOT dispute that non-monotonic luminance produces false gradients — so it does not contradict the mechanistic claim. Net: well-supported, current, source quality matches claim strength.
*Counter-source:* https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/37195830/ ("Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," 2023) — only qualifies use cases for rainbow maps; does NOT refute that linear/monotonic lightness avoids false gradients. No source found contradicting the core mechanism.

#### V72 `af17c46f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Cividis was created by mathematically optimizing colormaps using the CIECAM02-UCS (Uniform Color Space) model via a Python module (cmaputil), ensuring perceptual uniformity in the a'-b' plane and a linearized, maximized J' (lightness) range."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Equal Euclidean distances between values in this space leads to an equal color difference perception"

*Evidence:* All four technical components of the claim are confirmed by the primary peer-reviewed source (Nuñez, Anderton & Renslow, PLOS ONE 2018, "Optimizing colormaps with consideration for color vision deficiency," doi:10.1371/journal.pone.0199239; arXiv:1712.01662) and corroborating sources.

(1) cmaputil + CIECAM02-UCS: Abstract verbatim — "We developed a Python module, cmaputil, to create CVD-optimized colormaps, which imports colormaps and modifies them to be perceptually uniform in CVD-safe colorspace while linearizing and maximizing the brightness range." Paper body uses CIECAM02-UCS as the working colorspace (converting between sRGB and CIECAM02-UCS during optimization).

(2) a'-b' plane uniformity + maximized/linearized J': Paper describes the two-step optimization verbatim — "(i) interpolating a′ vs. b′ to generate points equidistance from each other, then (ii) linearizing and maximizing the J′ range." Cividis specifically is "generated by optimizing the viridis colormap and selecting the J' linearization that maximizes the range of J'." J', a', b' are the CIECAM02-UCS coordinates (J'=lightness, a'/b'=chrominance plane). The claim's "J' (lightness)" is correct CIECAM02 terminology.

(3) The supporting quote ("Equal Euclidean distances between values in this space leads to an equal color difference perception") accurately states the defining property of CIECAM02-UCS, independently corroborated: CIECAM02-UCS "was designed such that a Euclidean distance of one between two color coordinates corresponds to an equal perceptual color difference throughout the color space for individuals with normal color vision" (d3-cam02 / Luo et al. CIECAM02-UCS literature).

Checklist: Supported by quote — yes. Contradicting evidence — none found across PLOS, arXiv, OSTI, ResearchGate, d3-cam02. Source quality — peer-reviewed primary origin paper, matches claim strength. Not outdated (canonical 2018 source, not superseded). Not marketing/speculation. The only nuance is the abstract says "brightness range" colloquially while the body uses J'; this is a terminology-precision improvement in the claim, not an error. Sources: https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 ; https://arxiv.org/abs/1712.01662 ; https://gramaz.io/d3-cam02/

#### V73 `af49c2bc` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow colormap introduces visual artifacts not present in the data: it creates sharp/false boundaries where hue transitions are abrupt and flattens regions where hue changes slowly, biasing perceived structure and causing misinterpretation."

**Source:** https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "creates sharp boundaries that are not in the data"

*Evidence:* Source 4118486 is Borland & Taylor, "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," IEEE Computer Graphics and Applications, 2007 — the canonical primary peer-reviewed source for this exact claim. The paper identifies three problems with the rainbow colormap, two of which the claim restates faithfully: (a) it "creates sharp boundaries that are not in the data" (the supplied quote, verbatim — abrupt hue transitions read as false bands), and (b) it hides detail / flattens regions where hue changes slowly (perceptual non-uniformity). The claim's core perceptual mechanism is therefore accurately supported by the quote, not an overreach. The artifact itself is not disputed even by critics: the 2023 rebuttal "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (Ware, Stone, Szafir et al., IEEE CG&A, pubmed 37195830) lists the SAME three arguments and concedes rainbow "partition[s] the data into distinctly colored regions or bands, which can create false divisions." QUALIFICATION (why not high confidence): the claim's strong causal tail — that this "bias[es] perceived structure and caus[es] misinterpretation" — is genuinely contested. The 2023 paper found "no evidence ... that color categorization would lead observers to infer false data features," and notes banding can even be a cognitive advantage for reading values via a key. So the perceptual mechanism (sharp boundaries + flattening) is strongly supported and current; the downstream harm/misinterpretation outcome is mixed in the literature. Cross-checked corroboration: Survey of Colormaps in Visualization (PMC4959790) and HESS 2021 (hess-25-4549) independently affirm the false-boundary mechanism. Not refuted: well-supported by a primary source matching claim strength, mechanism not outdated, not marketing. Sources: https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/ (Borland & Taylor 2007); https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/37195830/ (rebuttal); https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC4959790/; https://hess.copernicus.org/articles/25/4549/2021/
*Counter-source:* https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/37195830/ — "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (Ware, Stone, Szafir et al., IEEE CG&A 2023): found no evidence that rainbow color categorization causes observers to infer false data features, and notes banding can aid value reading; qualifies (does not refute) the "causes misinterpretation" tail of the claim.

#### V74 `af4d7d16` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color vision deficiency affects up to 8% of men and 0.5% of women (over 600 million people globally), with deuteranomaly (red-green deficiency) being the most common form."

**Source:** https://journals.plos.org/plosone/article?id=10.1371%2Fjournal.pone.0199239 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "CVD reduces the ability to distinguish between certain colors and affects up to 8% of men and 0.5% of women."

*Evidence:* PARTIALLY REFUTED — core figures sound, but the "600 million" sub-claim is contradicted by the broader literature.

(1) The "8% of men / 0.5% of women" figure is VERBATIM in the primary PLOS ONE source (Nuñez, Anderton & Renslow 2018, journal.pone.0199239) and corroborated by Wikipedia's epidemiology table (deuteranomaly 5.0% / protanomaly 1.3% of males), Colour Blind Awareness, and standard texts. SUPPORTED.

(2) "Deuteranomaly is the most common form" is directly supported by the same source ("deuteranomaly... is by far the most common form of CVD") and Wikipedia (deuteranomaly ~5% vs protanomaly ~1.3% of males). SUPPORTED.

(3) "over 600 million people globally" is CONTRADICTED by the independent literature. Every epidemiological source checked (colorblind.io, gitnux, electroiq, market.us aggregators) cites ~300 MILLION, not 600 million. The arithmetic confirms ~300M: ~8% of men + ~0.5% of women across ~8B people ≈ 320M. The 600M figure appears in this one PLOS source but is roughly DOUBLE the consensus and not reproduced elsewhere — likely a miscalculation in the source. The claim inherits and asserts this erroneous number as fact ("over 600 million").

(4) Secondary overgeneralization: the 8%/0.5% rates apply specifically to Northern European / Caucasian-ancestry populations (Wikipedia attributes the figures to "Northern European ancestry"). Prevalence is lower in African and Asian male populations (~3-5%). The claim presents Caucasian-population rates as a flat global figure.

Net: the two load-bearing assertions are well-supported, but the bundled "600 million" is ~2x the established figure and contradicted, so the claim as written should not be cited verbatim. Use "~300 million" and note the Caucasian-population caveat.
*Counter-source:* https://colorblind.io/learn/statistics (and corroborating: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Color_blindness epidemiology table; https://www.colourblindawareness.org/colour-blindness/types-of-colour-blindness/) — all cite ~300 million globally, contradicting the "600 million" sub-claim

#### V75 `af922684` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For categorical/segmentation color coding where data marks must be found rapidly and accurately (preattentive visual search), only about 5 to 7 distinct colors can be reliably identified with a low error rate; Healey tested displays with 3, 5, 7, and 9 colors and found rapid low-error identification breaks down as the count rises toward 7-9. This directly bounds how many categorical colors to use for a small number of data series (~3-6 is safely within the reliable range)."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/2639665_Choosing_Effective_Colours_for_Data_Visualization (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Healey (1996) found that only 5 to 7 colours can be found rapidly and with a low error rate... studies using 3, 5, 7, and 9 colors found rapid identification for 3 and 5 colors but mixed results for 7 and 9 colors"

*Evidence:* VERIFIED across the peer-reviewed primary (Healey, IEEE Visualization '96) and an independent peer-reviewed survey. The survey "A Survey of Colormaps in Visualization" (PMC4959790) cites Healey 1996 and states verbatim: "users have no difficulty with three and five colors but have significant difficulty with seven and nine colors," and that Healey "proposes to optimize a seven-color scheme" as the practical maximum — directly confirming both the 3/5/7/9 test design and the "breaks down toward 7-9" characterization. Multiple independent sources restate "Healey (1996) found that only 5 to 7 colours can be found rapidly and with a low error rate." The experiment was Healey's own (38 observers, target identification among colored distractors). The claim's scope is correct: it is explicitly about RAPID PREATTENTIVE VISUAL SEARCH with low error — exactly Healey's task. SKEPTICAL CHECK on apparent counter-evidence: there IS literature (subitizing arXiv 2508.17460; Reda 2021 nameability) arguing 12-13+ colors are distinguishable/namable, but this does NOT refute the claim — it addresses a DIFFERENT task (static discrimination/naming and higher-level reasoning/subitizing across categories), not rapid search. The careful scoping of the claim to "found rapidly and accurately" keeps it inside Healey's validated finding and outside what the counter-literature disputes. The inference that ~3-6 series is "safely within the reliable range" is sound (3 and 5 are clean in Healey; degradation starts at 7). Minor nuance only: the "5-7" synthesis draws on prior cartography/perception work (color distance, linear separation, color category from predecessors), and Healey's stated goal was partly to push beyond naive limits via better selection — but the empirical 3/5/7/9 result attributed to him is genuine. Source quality (peer-reviewed, foundational, still cited approvingly through 2021+) matches claim strength.
*Counter-source:* Subitizing in data visualization (arXiv:2508.17460) and Reda et al. 2021 color-nameability work argue 12-13+ colors are distinguishable/namable — but these address static discrimination/naming and cross-category reasoning (a subitizing/cognitive limit), NOT the rapid preattentive visual-search task the claim is scoped to, so they qualify rather than refute it.

### Pass 2 — colour harmony / preference / emotion (2026-06-26)

#### V76 `a0a490e0` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Associations are driven by physical colour properties (lightness, saturation, colour temperature) and are many-to-many, not one-to-one: a single colour conveys multiple emotions. This contradicts pop 'one colour = one emotion' color-psychology claims; lighter/more-saturated colours skew positive/high-arousal/high-power."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13423-024-02615-z (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "While each color can convey multiple emotions – like red signalling both love and anger – these associations follow systematic patterns based on lightness, saturation, and 'color temperature.' ... Saturated colors were linked to positive, high arousal, and high power emotions, while desaturated colors were linked to negative, low arousal, and low power emotions."

*Evidence:* Source resolves to Jonauskaite & Mohr et al., "Do we feel colours? A systematic review of 128 years of psychological research linking colours and emotions," Psychonomic Bulletin & Review, 2024/2025 (PMID 39806242) — 132 peer-reviewed articles, 42,266 participants, 64 countries. A strong, current, primary/secondary source matching the claim's strength. Core claims are directly supported: "Most correspondences were many-to-many" with systematic effects "driven by lightness, saturation, and hue (colour temperature)"; saturated colors linked to positive/high-arousal/high-power and desaturated to negative/low-arousal/low-power; light→positive, dark→negative valence. The many-to-many/contradicts-pop-one-color-one-emotion thrust is well-founded. ONE overreach: the claim bundles "lighter" with high-arousal/high-power, but the review ties lightness only to VALENCE; high-arousal/high-power are driven by SATURATION. The review's own findings (BLACK→"negative, high arousal"; PURPLE/RED→"empowering") and Valdez & Mehrabian 1994 (brightness negatively related to arousal −0.31 and dominance −0.76) contradict a literal "lighter→high-power/high-arousal" reading. Under the natural distributive reading (lighter→positive; saturated→positive+high-arousal+high-power) the claim matches the source. The imprecision warrants a qualification, not a kill — central claims are accurate and well-sourced.
*Counter-source:* Valdez & Mehrabian (1994), J. Exp. Psychol. General: brightness negatively related to arousal (−0.31) and dominance (−0.76) — contradicts a literal "lighter→high-arousal/high-power" reading; the same review attributes high arousal/empowering qualities to BLACK, PURPLE, RED (dark/saturated), not to lightness.

*Claim under review:* "The paper EXPLICITLY concedes there is no formal or empirically validated definition of a harmonic color set; the harmony rules rest on artist consensus, not a proven preference formula. **Source:** https://igl.ethz.ch/projects/color-harmonization/harmonization.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Generating harmonic colors has been an open problem among artists and scientists [Holtzschue 2002]. ... Although currently there is no formulation that defines a harmonic set, there is a consensus among artists that defines when a set is harmonic, and there are some forms, schemes and relations in color space that describe a harmony of colors [Matsuda 1995; Tokumaru et al. 2002]. Professional artists usually rely on experience and intuition to choose their favorite harmonic colors."

*Evidence:* Verified verbatim against the primary PDF (Cohen-Or, Sorkine, Gal, Leyvand, "Color Harmonization," ACM TOG 25(3), SIGGRAPH 2006). Extracted text confirms the quote exactly: "Although currently there is no formulation that defines a harmonic set, there is a consensus among artists that defines when a set is harmonic, and there are some forms, schemes and relations in color space that describe a harmony of colors [Matsuda 1995; Tokumaru et al. 2002]. Professional artists usually rely on experience and intuition to choose their favorite harmonic colors." Preceding context frames Munsell/Goethe as attempts "to transfer the concept of color harmony from a subjective perspective to an objective one," and calls harmony generation "an open problem among artists and scientists [Holtzschue 2002]." This explicitly supports all three claim parts: (1) no formal/objective definition exists ("no formulation that defines a harmonic set"); (2) harmony rests on artist consensus and intuition, not a proven formula; (3) the optimized templates (Figure 2: i/V/L/I/T/Y/X/N hue-wheel types) derive from Matsuda 1995 / Tokumaru et al. 2002 conventions. A full-text keyword scan found ZERO instances of "user study," "experiment," "validated," "participants," or "preference" — the paper runs no controlled validation of its templates, confirming "not a controlled-study result." Web search surfaced no contradicting source; the only nuance (Matsuda's schemes have some psychophysical grounding) does not negate Cohen-Or's own explicit concession. "Empirically validated" is a faithful paraphrase of the paper's "no formulation." Source quality (the primary paper itself) matches the claim's strength; the claim is a historical in-paper admission so cannot be outdated.

#### V78 `a0b9ce04` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Perceived harmony and aesthetic preference for color pairs are strongly correlated but distinct constructs: harmony ratings correlate with preference at r = +.79 and alone explain 62.3% of preference variance, while a combined model (harmony + component color preferences + lightness contrast) explains 80.8%."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Correlation: r = +.79 between preference and harmony ratings ... Harmony alone explained 62.3% of variance in preference ratings ... Combined model (harmony + component preferences + lightness): 80.8% variance explained"

*Evidence:* The claim accurately reproduces the findings of Schloss & Palmer (2011), "Aesthetic response to color combinations: preference, harmony, and similarity," Attention, Perception, & Psychophysics 73(2), 551-571, DOI 10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0 — the primary source cited. All three numbers verified against independent full-text sources:

(1) r = +.79: The Palmer Lab's own page states verbatim "harmony ratings and preference ratings for color pairs showed a very strong correlation of r = +.79... 62% of the variance in preference ratings is explained by harmony ratings." This is internally consistent (r² = .79² = .624 ≈ 62.3%).

(2) 62.3% by harmony alone: A web source quoting the paper states "Harmony ratings alone explain 62.3% of the variance in color pair preference ratings... Preference for the ground color adds another 9.3%, and preference for the figure adds a further 4.7%" (62.3 + 9.3 + 4.7 = 76.3%, matching the lab's "~76%" figure for harmony + component preferences).

(3) 80.8% combined model: A second independent source quoting the paper states "A more comprehensive model explains 80.8% of the variance," incorporating harmony plus the additional component-preference and lightness-contrast factors — matching the claim's described model.

The "strongly correlated but distinct constructs" framing is the paper's central thesis: the best-fit line preference = −7.93 + 0.52·harmony shows the two are related but non-identical, and participants agree more on harmony than on personal preference. Source quality is a peer-reviewed Psychonomic Society journal — appropriate for a STRONG empirical claim. No contradicting evidence found; the paper is widely cited and uncontested on these descriptive statistics.

One precision note (not a refutation): the 80.8% combined model's exact predictor decomposition (harmony + figure pref + ground pref + lightness contrast) is consistent across sources, with harmony+component-prefs alone reaching ~76% and lightness contrast contributing the remainder to 80.8%.

#### V79 `a0d8b6f0` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Preference peaks at blue/cyan (associated with clear skies and clean water) and troughs at chartreuse and dark-yellow/dark-orange (brown) hues (associated with feces and rotting food) — i.e. preference tracks the valence of associated objects rather than a harmony rule."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/43345682_An_ecological_valence_theory_of_color_preferences (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A "narrow trough at chartreuse" and pronounced aversion to "dark yellow" and "dark orange (brown)," linked to objects like "feces and rotting food.""

*Evidence:* Claim is verbatim-supported by the primary source (Palmer & Schloss 2010, "An ecological valence theory of human color preference," PNAS 107:8877-8882, PMC2889342). Exact paper text: "a broad peak at blue and a narrow trough at chartreuse"; "dark orange (brown) and dark yellow (olive) were significantly less preferred"; associations given as "blues with clear skies and clean water" (liked) and "browns with feces and rotten food" (disliked); dark chartreuse linked to "bile, mucus, mold, sewage, snot" and dark yellow to "feces, pus, vomit." Effect size confirmed: the WAVE (object-valence) predictor correlated with preference at r = +0.893, "accounting for 80% of the variance," and "outperformed all three other models," which the paper interprets as "color preferences... determined by the average affective valence of people's responses to objects" — directly supporting the claim's "valence tracks object associations rather than a harmony rule" framing. Source quality (PNAS, highly cited primary peer-reviewed) matches the claim strength. The descriptive blue-peak/dark-yellow-trough pattern is independently replicated (Hurlbert & Ling 2007; Ou & Luo). Only qualifier: the EVT causal mechanism and cross-cultural generality are debated in follow-up work (Taylor, Clifford & Franklin 2013; Yokosawa et al.), but this does not contradict the claim, which is faithfully framed as this paper's finding. The supporting quote accurately reflects the source; no overreach.
*Counter-source:* No credible source refutes the descriptive peak/trough finding or the quoted object associations; follow-up critiques (Taylor, Clifford & Franklin 2013; Hurlbert & Ling 2007 cone-contrast account) debate the causal mechanism and cross-cultural scope, not the claim as stated.

#### V80 `a0f8dc73` — **upheld** · confidence: high

The article states for men rating women: "a small, statistically significant effect (d = 0.26 [0.12, 0.40], p = .0004, N = 2,961)" across 45 contrasts, with high heterogeneity (I² = 89%). Removing the Gilston & Privitera (2016) outlier (which reported d = 1.92 vs. median 0.18) gives "d = 0.19 [0.09, 0.29], p = .0004." Every numeric component of the claim (45 contrasts, N=2,961 male raters, both d values and CIs, the outlier-removal step) matches verbatim. Source is a primary peer-reviewed meta-analysis — strength matches the modest claim. The only non-source word is "incidental," which is the correct descriptor for this literature's manipulation (background/garment red) and not an overreach. No contradicting source found; figures reproduce across PMC, Sage, and the metadat dat.lehmann2018 dataset.

#### V81 `a119f797` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Three color-emotion dimensions can be predicted from color coordinates with strong cross-regional correlation: warm/cool r=0.78, heavy/light r=0.80, active/passive r=0.81 — these are the dimensions with robust support, not arbitrary emotion labels."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/326651521_Universal_models_of_colour_emotion_and_colour_harmony (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "For colour emotion, three new models were derived, showing satisfactory predictive performance in terms of an average correlation coefficient of 0.78 for "warm/cool", 0.80 for "heavy/light" and 0.81 for "active/passive"."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED. Source is a primary peer-reviewed paper: Ou, Luo, et al., "Universal models of colour emotion and colour harmony," Color Research & Application, 2018 (Wiley, DOI 10.1002/col.22243) — not a marketing/forum source. The quoted figures match the paper exactly: average correlation coefficients of 0.78 (warm/cool), 0.80 (heavy/light), 0.81 (active/passive) for three derived colour-emotion models. "Cross-regional" is accurate: models were built from psychophysical data from 12 regions (Argentina, China, France, Germany, Hungary, Iran, Japan, Spain, Sweden, Taiwan, Thailand, UK), hence "Universal models." The claim's framing — these three are the robustly supported dimensions, not arbitrary labels — is backed by the wider Ou & Luo literature (2004 onward), which found colour activity/weight/heat (= active-passive, heavy-light, warm-cool) culture-independent and replicating Kobayashi/Sato factor structures, while like-dislike and tense-relaxed differed across cultures. One minor caveat (not refuting): the r values are the model's predictive performance (predicted vs observed, averaged), which the authors label "satisfactory" rather than "strong," and "cross-regional correlation" slightly blurs model-fit-vs-between-region — but the magnitude and substance are faithfully represented. r≈0.8 is a strong correlation by standard interpretation. Source quality matches claim strength; claim is current and accurately quoted.

The supporting quote is confirmed verbatim on the authors' blog (thenewstatistics.com, 2017-08-21): "In both cases, we are sad to report that there was little-to-no effect of red on perceived attractiveness or desired sexual behavior." Both pre-registered, with original materials, positive controls, and planned informative sample sizes.
