The templates may be rotated by an arbitrary angle." The Appendix confirms they are angular hue sectors with sizes in degrees (V/Y/X = 26% of disk = 93.6 deg; T = 50% = 180 deg; small sectors = 5% = 18 deg). Two minor caveats that do NOT refute: (1) the paper operates on the HSV hue wheel, not a perceptual-uniform space (CIELAB/OKLab), so the claim's "perceptual/relational color spaces" gloss is loose on the "perceptual" half — only the "relational" half is what the paper supports; (2) the paper notes the N template is grayscale and "not dealt with in this work," leaving 7 actively-harmonized templates, though Figure 2 does define all 8 including N, so listing 8 is defensible. Core factual assertions (templates, labels, rotation property, relative-not-specific harmony) are all directly supported by the primary source.

Only nuance: the paper uses HSV hue, not a CIELAB/OKLab perceptual space, so the "perceptual" framing in the claim's interpretive bridge is slightly imprecise (the "relational/relative-hue" substance is fully correct).

#### V86 `a1dab7f0` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Preferred color pairings statistically match hue distributions in natural landscape images (peaks at blue and orange-yellow, minima at greens and purples), and dominant hues in natural scenes are most often separated by ~180 degrees -- evidence that preference tracks ecological exposure rather than an abstract harmony geometry. Replicated across two image datasets (12,000 and 4,319 images)."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/2508.15777 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the peaks and valleys of the distribution match remarkably well that of the Combinability index and absolute preferences (maximum for blue and orange-yellow tints, minimum for greens and purples)... dominant hues in natural scenes are most often separated by approximately 180°... we also measured the hue distribution using an independent dataset of 4,319 landscape images; the results were found to be virtually identical."

(1) Peaks/minima: the paper states the natural hue distribution matches "the Combinability index and absolute preferences (maximum for blue and orange-yellow tints, minimum for greens and purples)" — exact match to the claim. (2) "Dominant hues in natural scenes are most often separated by approximately 180°" — verbatim. (3) Replication: the 4,319-image second dataset gave results "virtually identical"; the claim correctly scopes this to IMAGE datasets (the n=346, 13-hue human survey was NOT replicated). (4) The ecological interpretation is the paper's own, phrased as "suggesting that our aesthetic preferences may be influenced by the colors we have most frequently been exposed to," and the paper explicitly challenges "universal harmony rules," so the claim's "tracks ecological exposure rather than abstract harmony geometry" framing is faithful (slightly de-hedged from "may be"). Refutation attempts failed: the established peer-reviewed literature is consistent, not contradictory — Palmer & Schloss Ecological Valence Theory (PNAS 2010) and Webster/Mizokami natural color-statistics independently support the blue preference peak and the blue-yellow natural daylight axis. Caveats that lower confidence but do not refute: single non-peer-reviewed preprint (physics authors), n=346, perceptually non-uniform HSL space (authors flag this), and a selection-bias confound — "landscape images" are curated aesthetic photographs, so the natural-stats↔preference correlation is partly circular for a CAUSAL ecological-exposure claim. Conclusion: report as convergent/emerging evidence, not settled STRONG fact, but the claim accurately represents the source and is not contradicted.

#### V87 `a1f3b51f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Preference peaks at blue/cyan (associated with clear skies and clean water) and troughs at chartreuse and dark-yellow/dark-orange (brown) hues (associated with feces and rotting food) — i.e. preference tracks the valence of associated objects rather than a harmony rule."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/43345682_An_ecological_valence_theory_of_color_preferences (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A "narrow trough at chartreuse" and pronounced aversion to "dark yellow" and "dark orange (brown)," linked to objects like "feces and rotting food.""

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully and accurately summarizes Palmer & Schloss (2010), "An ecological valence theory of human color preference," PNAS 107:8877-8882 — a high-quality primary peer-reviewed source that matches the claim's strength. Direct verification against the paper (PMC2889342):

(1) BLUE/CYAN PEAK — confirmed verbatim: preference functions show "a broad peak at blue," and the authors explicitly cite "blues and cyans with positively valued clear sky and clean water." Matches "peaks at blue/cyan (associated with clear skies and clean water)."

(2) TROUGH AT CHARTREUSE / DARK YELLOW / DARK-ORANGE(BROWN) — confirmed verbatim: "a narrow trough at chartreuse," with dark yellows significantly less preferred, and "browns with feces and rotten food." Matches the claim's "troughs at chartreuse and dark-yellow/dark-orange (brown) ... associated with feces and rotting food."

(3) VALENCE-OF-ASSOCIATED-OBJECTS THESIS — this IS the paper's central argument (EVT/WAVE), not an overreach. The WAVE (weighted affective valence estimate) correlated r = +0.893 with preference, "accounting for 80% of the variance with a single predictor." So "preference tracks the valence of associated objects" is the source's own demonstrated claim.

The supporting quote ("narrow trough at chartreuse," "dark yellow," "dark orange (brown)," "feces and rotting food") is genuine and the claim does not overreach it.

CAVEAT (qualification, not refutation): the CAUSAL interpretation is a theory with a competing physiological account — Hurlbert & Ling (2007) cone-contrast model. However, the cited paper itself addresses this and shows EVT outperforms cone-contrast on a wider color gamut (cone-contrast explained only ~37% of variance on the wider sample vs ~80% for WAVE). The DESCRIPTIVE pattern (blue peak, dark-yellow/chartreuse trough) is among the most replicated findings in color-preference research and is not contradicted by any credible source found. The "rather than a harmony rule" framing is the research-question's contrast, and it is fair: EVT explains single-color preference by ecological valence, not by intrinsic color or harmony properties. For a companion reference, the descriptive peak/trough should be tagged STRONG and the causal valence mechanism tagged as the EVT theory (well-supported, primary, but with a named competing account).

Source is current enough (2010, foundational, ~thousands of citations; color preference is not a fast-moving field), primary, peer-reviewed PNAS — not marketing, not cherry-picked, not forum speculation.
*Counter-source:* Hurlbert & Ling (2007, Current Biology) cone-contrast model offers a competing physiological account of the same blue-peak/yellow-trough pattern, but the cited paper rebuts it (WAVE ~80% vs cone-contrast ~37% variance on wider gamut); this qualifies the causal interpretation but does not contradict the descriptive claim.

#### V88 `a2091262` — **REFUTED** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A large, century-spanning evidence base supports systematic, cross-culturally consistent colour-emotion associations: 132 empirical studies (1895-2022), 42,266 participants, 64 countries. This is the strongest meta-level case that colour-emotion links are real rather than noise."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13423-024-02615-z (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Jonauskaite and Mohr collected 132 peer-reviewed reports of empirical studies on color and emotion. These studies spanned 128 years—from 1895 to 2022. Altogether, the studies had a total of 42,266 participants across 64 countries."

*Evidence:* The cited numbers are accurate (Jonauskaite & Mohr, "Do we feel colours? A systematic review of 128 years of psychological research linking colours and emotions," Psychonomic Bulletin & Review 2024/2025: 132 studies, 1895-2022, 42,266 participants, 64 countries — confirmed in the abstract and OSF dataset). But the interpretive claim overreaches beyond the supporting quote, which establishes only corpus SIZE, not consistency or "realness." Three problems: (1) It is a PRISMA systematic/narrative review, NOT a quantitative meta-analysis — the authors computed no pooled effect sizes, so it cannot quantify "signal vs noise"; corpus size is not evidence of effect reliability (silent on publication bias/heterogeneity). Calling it "the strongest meta-level case that links are real rather than noise" mischaracterizes what a vote-counting review establishes. (2) "Real rather than noise" directly contradicts the paper's own position: the abstract states most correspondences were tested "on an abstract level (i.e., associations)" and "it remains to be seen whether such correspondences translate to the impact of colour on experienced emotions," with that evidence called "inconclusive." The paper supports systematic color→affect ASSOCIATIONS (valence/arousal/power), not demonstrated emotional effects. (3) "Cross-culturally consistent" overreaches — the review pooled across countries and its stated conclusion calls for investigating cross-cultural DIFFERENCES; the same lead author's Jonauskaite et al. 2020 (Psychological Science) found universal patterns "further shaped by linguistic and geographic proximity," i.e., not uniform. Net: the factual core (large evidence base; systematic associations exist) is correct, but the strong framing that gives the claim its weight is unsupported by the quote and partly contradicted by the paper.
*Counter-source:* https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13423-024-02615-z (paper abstract: systematic review, no effect sizes, "remains to be seen"/"inconclusive" re experienced emotion); Jonauskaite et al. 2020 Psychological Science, "Universal Patterns in Color-Emotion Associations Are Further Shaped by Linguistic and Geographic Proximity"

Two Pre-Registered Replications of the Red-Romance Hypothesis," Social Psychology 48(3). This replicated Elliot et al. 2010 "Red, rank, and romance in women viewing men" — women are raters, men are targets, matching the claim's "women rating men" framing. Interpretation is accurate on all three points: (1) direction IS opposite — predicted positive red-enhances-attraction, observed d = −0.09 negative; (2) it IS null — 95% CI [−0.30, 0.12] crosses zero; (3) N = 360 correct. Numbers are also internally consistent: CI half-width ≈ 0.21 implies SE ≈ 0.107, matching a two-group between-subjects design with N=360 (two groups of ~180, SE = sqrt(2/180) ≈ 0.105). Checklist: (1) quote supports claim exactly; (2) WebSearch surfaced only corroborating, never contradicting, sources; (3) source quality strong — primary pre-registered peer-reviewed replication + OSF data repository + 2018 meta-analysis (Lehmann, Elliot & Calin-Jageman, Evolutionary Psychology); (4) not outdated — 2017/2018, still the current consensus that the red-attraction effect is small-to-nonexistent; (5) not marketing — a pre-registered replication, the opposite of cherry-picking.

Source quality (peer-reviewed pre-registered replication + meta-analysis) matches the claim's modest, well-hedged strength.

#### V92 `a419dc4f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Perceived harmony and aesthetic preference for color pairs are strongly correlated but distinct constructs: harmony ratings correlate with preference at r = +.79 and alone explain 62.3% of preference variance, while a combined model (harmony + component color preferences + lightness contrast) explains 80.8%."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Correlation: r = +.79 between preference and harmony ratings ... Harmony alone explained 62.3% of variance in preference ratings ... Combined model (harmony + component preferences + lightness): 80.8% variance explained"

*Evidence:* Verified directly from the full text of the primary source (Schloss & Palmer 2011, "Aesthetic response to color combinations: Preference, harmony, and similarity," Atten Percept Psychophys 73:551-571, DOI 10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0), extracted from the Palmer Lab PDF. Exact quotes: (1) "the correlation between average pair-wise preference ratings and average pair-wise harmony ratings was +0.79, accounting for 62% of the variance" and "r = +.79" — matches claim's r=+.79. (2) "Harmony ratings alone explain 62.3% of the variance (more than all 10 Munsell factors combined)" — matches 62.3%. (3) "the best linear model accounts for 80.8% of the variance in preference ratings (multiple r = +.90)... preference for the ground color adds another 9.3%, preference for the figure adds a further 4.7%, and the absolute value of the difference in Munsell values (lightnesses) adds a final 4.5%" — 62.3+9.3+4.7+4.5 = 80.8%; the combined model's factors (harmony + figure & ground color preferences + lightness contrast) exactly match the claim. The paper's central thesis is that preference and harmony are highly correlated but distinct constructs (residual systematic variance differs; harmony ratings are "essentially unaffected by their single color preferences" whereas preference is influenced by them). No contradicting source found; this is the correct, reputable, peer-reviewed primary source and the numbers match verbatim.

Moon & Spencer's classical color-harmony papers are indeed 1944 (JOSA), so the year is correct. Note: the WebFetch small-model summary initially claimed the similarity region WAS supported — this was a hallucination, self-contradictory, and is overridden by the verbatim HTML text and the WebSearch-returned abstract, both of which state it is "not consistently supported." Minor, non-fatal quibbles: claim says "NOT supported" vs paper's "not consistently supported" (slight strengthening); "robust" is justified only because the claim correctly confines the contrast preference to hue-averaged data, exactly where the paper says it holds ("contrast is generally favored"). The claim does not over-generalize beyond the paper. Caveat for downstream tagging: this is a single 2025 arXiv preprint (peer-review status unclear), so a [STRONG] label should cite it as one recent study, not settled consensus — but the claim itself, as a description of this paper's finding, is accurate, current, and primary-sourced.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source; the only apparent contradiction (WebFetch summary saying similarity IS supported) was a small-model hallucination, refuted by the paper's own verbatim text.

For women rating men it reports a statistically significant relationship between effect size and standard error: "r = .52 [.23, .72], p = .001, k = 36", strengthening to r = .63 [.38, .80], p < .001 with one outlier excluded. The CI and p-value match the claim exactly, and the directional gloss "smaller studies reported larger effects" is the correct reading of a positive effect-size/SE correlation (funnel-plot asymmetry test for small-study/publication bias). One minor framing nuance: the authors caution that such a relationship "can occur for reasons other than bias" and conclude effects are "at least somewhat optimistic," i.e., they hedge the word "bias" slightly — but the statistical result itself is genuinely significant and conventionally interpreted as bias evidence, so this does not refute the claim. #### V95 `a4894d2f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Both preference for a color pair and its perceived harmony increase as the hue similarity between the figure and ground colors increases (peaking when hues match and declining monotonically with hue difference); there is no support for art-theory 'harmony rules' that strong hue contrasts (e.g. complementary) are preferred or harmonious."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "pair preference and harmony both increase as hue similarity increases ... No evidence favoring art theoretic claims that color combinations with strong hue contrasts are either preferred or harmonious"

*Evidence:* Claim verified against the primary peer-reviewed source (Schloss & Palmer 2011, Attention, Perception, & Psychophysics 73(2):551-571, DOI 10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0) and four independent characterizations (Springer abstract, scite.ai, Palmer Lab Berkeley, eScholarship). All confirm both parts of the claim: (1) "both pair preference and harmony increase as hue similarity increases" with "similarity ratings decreased monotonically as the hue difference between the component colors increased" — so the monotonic-in-hue, peaks-at-match framing is accurate; (2) "pairs with highly contrastive hues are generally judged to be neither preferable nor harmonious" — directly supporting the "no support for art-theory complementary-harmony rules" claim. Source is the primary paper itself (not secondary/marketing); 2011 foundational study, still standard, not outdated; source quality matches claim strength. No contradicting evidence found. ONE legitimate caveat, but it is an omission not a contradiction: the paper's central three-way distinction also found that FIGURAL color preference (the third judgment type, not named in the claim) INCREASES with hue contrast against the background, partially vindicating art-theory's contrast intuition for figure-against-ground — and preference overall "relies more strongly on component color preference and lightness contrast" than hue, making "preference peaks at hue match" a main-effect simplification. These qualify but do not falsify the claim, which is correctly scoped to hue similarity and to pair preference / harmony specifically. Recommend the companion reference add the figural-preference nuance so the art-theory dismissal isn't overstated, but the claim as written is well-supported.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. The strongest qualifier comes from the SAME paper: figural color preference increases with hue contrast (third judgment type the claim omits), and preference is multi-determined by component-color preference and lightness contrast — qualifications, not refutations.

#### V98 `a5af93da` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Associations are driven by physical colour properties (lightness, saturation, colour temperature) and are many-to-many, not one-to-one: a single colour conveys multiple emotions. This contradicts pop 'one colour = one emotion' color-psychology claims; lighter/more-saturated colours skew positive/high-arousal/high-power."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13423-024-02615-z (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "While each color can convey multiple emotions – like red signalling both love and anger – these associations follow systematic patterns based on lightness, saturation, and 'color temperature.' ... Saturated colors were linked to positive, high arousal, and high power emotions, while desaturated colors were linked to negative, low arousal, and low power emotions."

*Evidence:* The source is Jonauskaite & Mohr (2024/2025), "Do we feel colours? A systematic review of 128 years of psychological research linking colours and emotions," Psychonomic Bulletin & Review 32(4):1457-1486 — a systematic review of 132 peer-reviewed articles (1895-2022), 42,266 participants, 64 countries. This is a top-tier primary source whose quality exceeds the claim's strength.

The review's own abstract verbatim supports the claim's two core assertions: (1) "Most correspondences were many-to-many, with systematic effects driven by lightness, saturation, and hue ('colour temperature')" — directly backs "many-to-many, not one-to-one" and "driven by physical colour properties." (2) "LIGHT and DARK colours were associated with positive and negative emotions, respectively" — backs "lighter ... skew positive." The example "red signalling both love and anger" (red is reported as "empowering, high arousal positive AND negative emotions") concretely demonstrates the many-to-many point against pop "one colour = one emotion." The saturation→positive/high-arousal/high-power mapping in the supporting quote is consistent with the review's dimensional account and is independently corroborated by Valdez & Mehrabian (1994), where brightness predicted pleasure and saturation predicted arousal/dominance. No credible source contradicts the dimensional, physical-property-driven account; it is mainstream consensus. The source is current (2024/2025), not marketing or a press release.

One minor imprecision (not refuting): the claim bundles "lighter" with "high-arousal/high-power," but in the review lightness primarily drives valence (positive/negative), while saturation drives arousal/power — and dark colours can be high-arousal (black = "negative, high arousal"). The hedge "skew" and the saturation-specific supporting quote keep this within bounds. Also worth noting the review's own caveat: most correspondences were tested at an abstract association level, not real-world emotional impact.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found; the dimensional/physical-property account is corroborated by Valdez & Mehrabian (1994, Journal of Experimental Psychology: General). Only minor qualifier: lightness drives valence while saturation drives arousal/power, so bundling "lighter" with "high-arousal/high-power" is slightly imprecise.

**Supporting quote:** "RED links to positive and negative, arousing, and high-power emotions (e.g., love, anger, passion)... YELLOW/ORANGE link to positive and high arousal emotions... GREEN/BLUE/BLUE-GREEN link to positive and low arousal emotions (e.g., comfort, relaxation, happiness)"

*Evidence:* The claim accurately reports findings from a high-quality primary source: Jonauskaite & Mohr (2024) "Do we feel colours? A systematic review of 128 years of psychological research" (Psychonomic Bulletin & Review, doi:10.3758/s13423-024-02615-z) — a systematic review of 132 peer-reviewed articles, 42,266 participants, 64 countries. The review's own summary, confirmed via independent search, states: "RED associated with empowering, high arousal positive and negative emotions, and BLUE, GREEN, GREEN–BLUE, and WHITE with positive, low arousal emotions" — matching the claim's three mappings (red→arousing/high-power; yellow/orange→positive high-arousal; green/blue/blue-green→positive low-arousal). (2020, i-Perception, PMC7027086, n=132): red/pink/yellow/orange yield more high-arousal concepts; yellow/orange/blue/turquoise bias positive valence; blue→low arousal; red/yellow/orange→high power; color terms vs patches correlate r=.82. CRITICAL SCOPING CHECK: the claim is explicitly about ASSOCIATIONS (what people report), which is exactly what these studies measure — it does NOT overreach into the overstated CAUSAL color-psychology territory (e.g., red impairs performance) that the research question flags as weak/unreplicated. Source is current (2024), peer-reviewed, primary, and the strongest available evidence base. Minor caveats (cultural intensity variation; green also→envy; weaker causal priming effects) do not undermine the association-level claim and are honestly reproduced from the source.
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the association-level finding. Behavioral-priming studies (e.g., blue→sad-face priming, found non-significant in one cited study) are weaker, but those concern causal effects, not the associations the claim makes — so they do not refute it.

#### V102 `a6aa448f` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "When preferences are averaged across all reference hues, there is a robust preference for the contrast region (hue separations of 160-220 degrees, i.e. near-complementary ~180 degrees), but the 'similarity' region near the reference hue that Moon & Spencer (1944) proposed is NOT supported -- so contrast is favored while the classical similarity rule fails."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/2508.15777 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Higher preferences are observed in the contrast region (between 160○and 220○), flanked by regions of lower preference at smaller angular distances. Such global preference for contrast aligns rather well with the universal harmony model proposed by Moon and Spencer[22]... However, the other preference regions they proposed—coined similarity and located near the reference color—are not consistently supported by our hue-averaged data."

*Evidence:* Verified against the primary source (arXiv 2508.15777, Forni, Darmon & Benzaquen 2025, "Harmonious Color Pairings: Insights from Human Preference and Natural Hue Statistics") via its ar5iv HTML rendering. Both halves of the claim are directly supported by verbatim text: "Higher preferences are observed in the contrast region (between 160 and 220)" (aligning with "the universal harmony model proposed by Moon and Spencer"), and "the other preference regions they proposed—coined similarity and located near the reference color—are not consistently supported by our hue-averaged data." The Moon & Spencer (1944) attribution is correct: their JOSA paper "Geometric Formulation of Classical Color Harmony" (34(1):46) proposed identity/similarity/contrast harmony regions, and the paper cites them ([22]) for exactly this. Methods confirmed: N=346 survey responses, 13 equally-spaced HSL hues, most/least-harmonious pairing selections. The claim is explicitly scoped to "averaged across all reference hues," which matches the paper's "hue-averaged data" framing exactly. Minor reservations, none fatal: (a) the claim hardens "not consistently supported" into "NOT supported"; (b) the word "robust" is the claim's, not the paper's; (c) the claim omits the paper's central caveat that preferences are highly hue-dependent ("the assumption of hue independence cannot be reasonably upheld"), though the explicit hue-averaged scoping prevents this from being misleading; (d) it is a single non-peer-reviewed preprint, online survey, stimuli in non-perceptual HSL — adequate for a descriptive "this study found X" but not for presenting the similarity-rule failure as settled consensus. As a faithful, correctly-scoped report of a real current primary source, the claim stands.
*Counter-source:* https://ar5iv.labs.arxiv.org/abs/2508.15777 (primary, confirms quote); Moon & Spencer 1944 JOSA 34(1):46 (confirms attribution)

#### V103 `a6ed5f7c` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Supporting quote:** "In both cases, we are sad to report that there was little-to-no effect of red on perceived attractiveness or desired sexual behavior."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by a peer-reviewed primary source. The OSF project j3fyq is "Romantic Red — Registered Replications of the Effect of Red on Attractiveness (Elliot & Niesta, 2008; Elliot et al. 2010)," published as Lehmann/Calin-Jageman et al., "Is Red Really Romantic? Two Pre-Registered Replications of the Red-Romance Hypothesis," Social Psychology 2017, 48(3). Reported results: men rating women (replicating Elliot & Niesta 2008) d = 0.09, 95% CI [−0.17, 0.34], N = 242 (weak, in predicted direction); women rating men (replicating Elliot et al. 2010) d = −0.09, 95% CI [−0.30, 0.12], N = 360 (weak, OPPOSITE direction). Both CIs span zero. The supporting quote matches verbatim: "in both cases, there was little-to-no effect of red on perceived attractiveness or desired sexual behavior." The claim's "in either direction" phrasing is precisely justified by one near-zero positive and one near-zero negative d. The Lehmann, Elliot & Calin-Jageman (2018) meta-analysis found a small overall effect but included these replications and flagged publication bias; it qualifies the broader literature, not this narrow factual claim about these two replications. Quote-support: exact; source quality: peer-reviewed + pre-registered; not outdated; not marketing.
*Counter-source:* Lehmann, Elliot & Calin-Jageman (2018), "Meta-Analysis of the Effect of Red on Perceived Attractiveness," Evolutionary Psychology — finds a small overall red effect, but this qualifies rather than refutes the specific replication-failure claim.

Source quality (top-tier peer-reviewed systematic review) exceeds the claim's strength; current (2024/2025); not marketing. Refutation passes 1 (unsupported-by-quote), 2 (contradicted), and 3 (low-quality-source) all fail.

#### V105 `a72d4c4e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The paper defines color harmony by RELATIVE hue relationships (rotatable templates on the hue wheel), not by specific colors — directly supporting the research question's premise that perceptual/relational color spaces, not absolute RGB values, govern harmony. Their 8 templates (i, V, L, I, T, Y, X, N) are angular hue sectors that 'may be rotated by an arbitrary angle.'"

**Source:** https://igl.ethz.ch/projects/color-harmonization/harmonization.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Harmony among colors is not determined by specific colors, but rather by their relative position in color space. ... We refer to these distributions as templates, since they define the radial relationships on the color wheel, rather than specific colors (meaning that any template may be rotated by an arbitrary angle)."

*Evidence:* The claim's core assertions are directly supported by the primary source (Cohen-Or, Sorkine, Gal, Leyvand, Xu, "Color Harmonization," SIGGRAPH 2006). The supplied verbatim quote literally states harmony "is not determined by specific colors, but rather by their relative position in color space" and that "any template may be rotated by an arbitrary angle" — exactly the claim. Independent corroboration (web search) confirms (a) the eight harmonic templates are named i, V, L, I, T, Y, X, N; (b) they are angular hue sectors on the hue wheel; (c) they may be rotated by an arbitrary offset angle (the alpha that minimizes hue-distance defines the best scheme). The source is the original peer-reviewed SIGGRAPH paper — appropriate quality for the claim strength; not outdated (foundational, still cited); not marketing. One caveat worth flagging for synthesis, but NOT sufficient to refute: the paper's templates operate in HSV color space (hue-saturation sectors), which is NOT a perceptually-uniform space (CIELAB/OKLab). So the claim's interpretive bridge "supporting... perceptual/relational color spaces" is technically loose — Cohen-Or supports the *relational* ("relative position, not absolute RGB") point, but does so in HSV, a member of the RGB/HSL family the research question contrasts *against* perceptual spaces. The claim hedges with "perceptual/relational," and every checkable factual assertion about the paper (relative hue relationships, rotatable templates, the 8 named templates, "arbitrary angle") is accurate.
*Counter-source:* Independent corroboration confirms templates i,V,L,I,T,Y,X,N operate as hue-saturation sectors in HSV color space (e.g. arxiv.org/pdf/1804.01225; mdpi.com/2076-3417/11/9/3915) — supports the relational claim but shows the space is HSV, not a perceptual space, qualifying (not refuting) the 'perceptual' framing.

Every element of the claim matches: r=+0.893, ~80% variance (r²=0.797), no fitted free parameters, WAVE built from color-object associations + object valence ratings + color-object matching strength, adult participants (n=48). Source quality (PNAS) matches the claim strength. Honest caveats that qualify but do NOT refute: (1) the r is across 32 chromatic color stimuli — it explains 80% of variance in the AVERAGE preference function across colors, not individual-level variance; (2) it is a within-sample model fit, and later cross-cultural work (Arabic EVT test; Taylor/Clifford/Franklin universality critiques) qualifies EVT's generalizability. But the claim is narrowly and precisely worded — it asserts only what the 2010 paper reported and makes no universality/causality/out-of-sample overreach — so these caveats do not contradict it.
*Counter-source:* No credible source disputes the original numeric finding. Critiques (Taylor, Clifford & Franklin 2013 "Color preferences are not universal"; Frontiers Arabic EVT test 2022) challenge cross-cultural generalizability of EVT, not the r=0.893 within-sample correlation reported in the 2010 paper.

Context reinforces it: the overall women-rating-men effect is weak (d = 0.13, p = .03), so strong small-study/publication-bias evidence is well-grounded. Primary peer-reviewed meta-analysis; source quality matches claim strength. One non-refuting nuance: ES–SE correlation technically indicates "small-study effects" (publication bias is one of several possible causes), but the paper presents it as a publication-bias analysis, so the claim's framing is faithful to the source.

#### V111 `a7e3449a` — **upheld** · confidence: high

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against the primary source. Palmer & Schloss (2010), "An ecological valence theory of human color preference," PNAS 107(19):8877-8882 (PMC2889342). Direct fetch of the paper confirms every component of the claim verbatim: (1) correlation r = +0.893; (2) "accounting for 80% of the variance"; (3) exact wording "no free parameters were estimated in calculating the WAVE; it is simply the outcome of a well-defined procedure... theoretically implied by the EVT." So the "80% / r=.893" figure is real and correctly attributed to the original US adult sample, but should NOT be presented as a universal or cross-cultural constant. The claim as stated ("predicts adult color preferences") is faithful to what the paper reports for its sample.

#### V113 `a872afa4` — **upheld** · confidence: high

The WAVE (weighted affective valence estimate) accounted for 80% of variance in preference, outperforming the color-emotion (55%), color-appearance (60%), and cone-contrast (37%) models — supporting the claim's "preference tracks object valence rather than a harmony rule" framing for single-color preference. Source is the primary peer-reviewed PNAS paper, highly cited; the blue-peak/dark-yellow-trough descriptive finding is among the most replicated in color-preference research (confirmed in extension studies, though WAVE effect size varies by sample, e.g. ~61% for British observers). Minor scope nuance: EVT concerns single colors while harmony rules typically concern color pairs, but this does not undermine the claim in its stated context. Not refuted.

#### V115 `a8924f93` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color-pairing preferences are highly hue-dependent, which empirically refutes the 'hue-independence' assumption underlying classical harmony rules (the idea that preference depends only on the angular distance between two hues, not their absolute positions)."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/2508.15777 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results reveal that preferences are highly hue dependent, challenging the assumption of universal harmony rules proposed in the literature... Our findings challenge this hue-independence assumption to a large extent... the assumption of hue independence cannot be reasonably upheld."

*Evidence:* The claim is faithful to the source and corroborated by independent peer-reviewed work. The primary source — Forni, Darmon & Benzaquen, "Harmonious Color Pairings: Insights from Human Preference and Natural Hue Statistics," arXiv 2508.15777 (Aug 2025), also SSRN 5377774 — states in its abstract: "Our results reveal that preferences are highly hue dependent, challenging the assumption of universal harmony rules proposed in the literature," and in the body: "the assumption of hue independence cannot be reasonably upheld." Method: 346 responses, 13 distinct hues in HSL (L=0.5, S=0.8 fixed), preference elicited by picking 3 most/3 least harmonious pairs from 8x8 checkerboards; a per-hue "combinability index" varies by absolute hue, which is the empirical content. The claim's parenthetical (preference = f(angular hue distance) only, independent of absolute position) accurately characterizes the assumption the paper attributes to classical theorists (Goethe, Chevreul, Ostwald, Itten, Moon-Spencer) and is exactly the rotation-invariance of computational harmony templates (Cohen-Or et al. 2006). No credible source disputes the claim; on the contrary, Schloss & Palmer (2011, Attention, Perception, & Psychophysics, doi 10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0) independently found pair preference depends on component color preference and lightness contrast — i.e., absolute colors matter, not just hue-angle — corroborating the claim. Caveats (do not refute, but qualify): (a) primary source is a preprint, N=346, single fixed lightness/saturation, limiting generalizability; (b) the same paper hedges that when averaged over hues, angular-distance regularities DO emerge ("certain hue separations perceived as more harmonious," a global contrast preference aligning with universal models), so "refutes" applies to strict hue-independence, not to all average structure. The claim asserts exactly the strict version, so it holds. Tag: [STRONG] for the empirical hue-dependence finding; treat the word "refutes" as the authors' own "cannot be reasonably upheld."
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Corroborating: Schloss & Palmer 2011 (peer-reviewed) — pair preference driven by component-color preference + lightness contrast, not pure hue-angle. Only qualifier is from the primary paper itself: hue-averaged angular-distance patterns still emerge, so harmony rules retain weak average validity.

#### V116 `a8d10505` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Perceived harmony and aesthetic preference for color pairs are strongly correlated but distinct constructs: harmony ratings correlate with preference at r = +.79 and alone explain 62.3% of preference variance, while a combined model (harmony + component color preferences + lightness contrast) explains 80.8%."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Correlation: r = +.79 between preference and harmony ratings ... Harmony alone explained 62.3% of variance in preference ratings ... Combined model (harmony + component preferences + lightness): 80.8% variance explained"

*Evidence:* Every number in the claim matches the primary source verbatim. I downloaded the actual paper (Schloss & Palmer 2011, "Aesthetic response to color combinations: preference, harmony, and similarity," Attention, Perception, & Psychophysics 73(2):551-571 — the exact DOI 10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0 cited) from palmerlab.berkeley.edu and extracted its text. Direct quotes:

(1) r = +.79: "the correlation between average pair-wise preference ratings and average pair-wise harmony ratings was +0.79, accounting for 62% of the variance." Restated: "there is a high correlation between pair preference and pair harmony in the data averaged over all participants (r = +.79)." EXACT MATCH.

(2) Harmony alone 62.3%: "Harmony ratings alone explain 62.3% of the variance (more than all 10 Munsell factors combined)." EXACT MATCH.

(3) Combined model 80.8%: "When the inherently relational factor of harmony ratings is also included as a predictor variable, the best linear model accounts for 80.8% of the variance in preference ratings (multiple r = +.90). Harmony ratings alone explain 62.3%... preference for the ground color adds another 9.3%, preference for the figure adds a further 4.7%, and the absolute value of the dif[ference in lightness]..." So the 80.8% model = harmony + figure/ground (component) color preferences + lightness contrast, exactly as the claim states. EXACT MATCH.

(4) "Strongly correlated but distinct constructs" is the paper's central thesis: the group-average r=.79 is high, yet the paper shows preference and harmony diverge (e.g. preference relies more on component-color preference and lightness contrast; correlation drops for individual participants, mean individual r=+.75). Internal consistency checks out: .789² = .623 (= 62.3%), .899² = .808 (= the multiple r=+.90 for the 80.8% model).

This is the primary peer-reviewed source matching the claim's strength; the figures are descriptive regression statistics, not an extraordinary or fast-moving claim, so the 2011 date is not a currency problem. A web-search summary mentioned a partial "76%" (harmony+figure+ground only), but the paper's full 80.8% model explicitly adds lightness contrast — so the claim is more accurate than that secondary summary, not contradicted by it. No overreach found.

#### V117 `a93e1726` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The Ecological Valence Theory holds that color preferences arise from people's average affective responses to the objects characteristically associated with each color (learned, ontogenetic mechanism), not from innate hue preference per se."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/43345682_An_ecological_valence_theory_of_color_preferences (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "color preferences arise from people's average affective responses to color-associated objects"

*Evidence:* Claim accurately states the Ecological Valence Theory (Palmer & Schloss 2010, PNAS, "An ecological valence theory of human color preference"). Core assertion is the theory's literal definition: color preferences arise from people's average affective responses to color-associated objects, operationalized as the WAVE (weighted affective valence estimate) model, which explained up to ~80% of variance in U.S. adult color preferences. The contrast "not from innate hue preference per se" is correct — EVT was explicitly positioned against direct innate/cone-contrast accounts (Hurlbert & Ling) and presented evidence against cone-contrast as a complete theory. The parenthetical "(learned, ontogenetic mechanism)" matches the paper's own framing: EVT "extends the range of potentially adaptive mechanisms to include individual organisms learning color preferences on an ontogenetic time scale." One nuance (not a refutation): EVT permits BOTH evolutionary AND ontogenetic/learned contributions ("parallels taste preferences, which have both an evolutionary and a learned component"), so labeling it strictly "learned" slightly understates the allowed evolutionary component — but the specific rejection of "innate hue preference per se" (direct innate liking for hues) holds. Source is the correct primary peer-reviewed source (PNAS), still cited as the dominant account of color preference in 2022 literature (Frontiers Psychol., Arabic color-preference test). Current, high-quality, claim strength matched.
*Counter-source:* No credible source refutes the core mechanism. Minor qualification: EVT allows an evolutionary component alongside the ontogenetic/learned one (per Palmer & Schloss's own taste-preference analogy and follow-up ecological-framework work, Schloss & Palmer 2017, Vision Research), so the "learned, ontogenetic" label is slightly narrower than the full theory but not wrong about the object-mediated, non-innate-hue claim.

#### V130 `abcc6d94` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The Ecological Valence Theory holds that color preferences arise from people's average affective responses to the objects characteristically associated with each color (learned, ontogenetic mechanism), not from innate hue preference per se."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/43345682_An_ecological_valence_theory_of_color_preferences (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "color preferences arise from people's average affective responses to color-associated objects"

*Evidence:* Core thesis is correct and well-sourced: EVT (Palmer & Schloss 2010, PNAS 107:8877-8882) holds that color preferences arise from average affective responses to color-associated objects (WAVE explained ~80% of preference variance), mediated by object associations rather than direct innate hue/wavelength preference. The supporting quote matches. BUT the claim's parenthetical "(learned, ontogenetic mechanism), not from innate hue preference" overreaches and is contradicted by the primary source itself: Palmer & Schloss explicitly treat the innate-vs-learned origin of color valences as an OPEN question ("not yet clear whether such universals are innate or learned"; "an open question whether hardwired versus learned"). EVT deliberately allows BOTH mechanisms — innate/evolutionary valences (aversion to feces-brown, attraction to blue sky/clean water) AND learned associations (red = your team). The quote supports "preferences = affective response to associated objects" but says nothing about learned/ontogenetic vs innate, so labeling the mechanism as "learned, ontogenetic" is an inference the theory's authors decline to make. The claim is partially unsupported by its quote and contradicted by the source's own framing on the specific innate/learned point.</parameter>
<parameter name="counterSource">Palmer & Schloss 2010, "An ecological valence theory of human color preference," PNAS 107(19):8877-8882 — its own discussion stating innate-vs-learned is an open question and the theory accommodates both innate and learned valences (https://www.pnas.org/doi/10.1073/pnas.0906172107)

#### V131 `abfcafd0` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Associations are driven by physical colour properties (lightness, saturation, colour temperature) and are many-to-many, not one-to-one: a single colour conveys multiple emotions. This contradicts pop 'one colour = one emotion' color-psychology claims; lighter/more-saturated colours skew positive/high-arousal/high-power."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13423-024-02615-z (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "While each color can convey multiple emotions – like red signalling both love and anger – these associations follow systematic patterns based on lightness, saturation, and 'color temperature.' ... Saturated colors were linked to positive, high arousal, and high power emotions, while desaturated colors were linked to negative, low arousal, and low power emotions."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully reflects its source — Jonauskaite & Mohr (2025), "Do we feel colours? A systematic review of 128 years of psychological research linking colours and emotions," Psychonomic Bulletin & Review (10.3758/s13423-024-02615-z; PubMed 39806242). This is a top-tier peer-reviewed systematic review: 132 articles, 42,266 participants, 64 countries, 1895–2022 — exactly the primary, large-N, cross-cultural evidence the strong claim requires.

Each claim component checks out against the paper's own abstract (confirmed via two independent retrievals plus the Psychonomic Society featured summary):
(1) "driven by physical colour properties (lightness, saturation, colour temperature)" — VERBATIM SUPPORTED: "Most correspondences were many-to-many, with systematic effects driven by lightness, saturation, and hue ('colour temperature')."
(2) "many-to-many, not one-to-one; single colour conveys multiple emotions" — SUPPORTED: "most correspondences being many-to-many"; e.g., "happiness was associated with 8 different colour categories"; "red signalling both love and anger."
(3) "contradicts pop 'one colour = one emotion'" — fair inference; many-to-many directly negates one-to-one mappings.
(4) "lighter/more-saturated skew positive/high-arousal/high-power" — SUPPORTED: "Lighter colours were tied to more positive emotions, while darker colours were more negative" and "Saturated colours were linked to positive, high arousal, and high power emotions, while desaturated colours were linked to negative, low arousal, and low power emotions."

Convergent validity: this matches the prior canonical finding (Valdez & Mehrabian 1994) that brightness and saturation are the strongest predictors of emotional response (brightness→pleasure, saturation→arousal), so it is not an isolated/unreplicated result.

Minor imprecision (not refuting): the claim bundles "lighter/more-saturated → positive/high-arousal/high-power," but the paper ties lightness primarily to valence (positivity) while saturation carries the valence+arousal+power loading; lightness is not itself the arousal/power driver. The phrasing slightly over-compresses but the gist is accurate.

No credible source contradicts the lightness/saturation→affect finding; it is among the most robust results in the field. Source is current (2024/2025), primary, peer-reviewed, and the supporting quote genuinely backs the claim.

The only minor caveat is emphasis: the paper's headline is BOTH strong universality AND cultural modulation, and the claim foregrounds the cultural-contingency side, but it does so without denying universality, so the representation is faithful.

#### V135 `ad1031c2` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The paper EXPLICITLY concedes there is no formal or empirically validated definition of a harmonic color set; the harmony rules rest on artist consensus, not a proven preference formula. This is the key 'math vs taste' admission: the templates the algorithm optimizes are aesthetic convention, not a controlled-study result."

**Source:** https://igl.ethz.ch/projects/color-harmonization/harmonization.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Generating harmonic colors has been an open problem among artists and scientists [Holtzschue 2002]. ... Although currently there is no formulation that defines a harmonic set, there is a consensus among artists that defines when a set is harmonic, and there are some forms, schemes and relations in color space that describe a harmony of colors [Matsuda 1995; Tokumaru et al. 2002]. Professional artists usually rely on experience and intuition to choose their favorite harmonic colors."

*Evidence:* Verbatim quote confirmed in the primary source (Cohen-Or, Sorkine, Gal, Leyvand, "Color Harmonization," ACM TOG / SIGGRAPH 2006, igl.ethz.ch). Two independent WebSearches returned the identical sentence: "Although currently there is no formulation that defines a harmonic set, there is a consensus among artists that defines when a set is harmonic, and there are some forms, schemes and relations in color space that describe a harmony of colors [Matsuda 1995; Tokumaru et al. 2002]." Combined with "Professional artists usually rely on experience and intuition," this directly supports every element of the claim: (1) no formal definition exists; (2) the basis is artist consensus, not a proven preference formula; (3) the harmonic templates the algorithm optimizes are imported from Matsuda 1995 (descended from Itten's Bauhaus color theory) — design convention, not a controlled preference experiment. The only refutation angle — that secondary sources call Matsuda's templates "psychophysical research" — is weak: it does not contradict the paper's own explicit statement, and that tradition is design-school color coordination, not the replicated controlled-study evidence the research question classifies as STRONG. Minor wording quibbles ("EXPLICITLY concedes," "empirically validated") are framing of a matter-of-fact motivation sentence, not substantive misreads. Source is primary and top-tier; quote matches claim strength.
*Counter-source:* Secondary sources (e.g. researchgate figure on Matsuda's templates; A Geometric Approach to Harmonic Color Palette Design) describe Matsuda 1995's 80 schemes as based on "extensive psychophysical research," which a strict reader could cite against "not a controlled-study result" — but this does not contradict the paper's own explicit concession and reflects design-tradition work, not replicated preference experiments.

#### V136 `ad10edc6` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Color-pairing preferences are highly hue-dependent, which empirically refutes the 'hue-independence' assumption underlying classical harmony rules (the idea that preference depends only on the angular distance between two hues, not their absolute positions)."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/2508.15777 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results reveal that preferences are highly hue dependent, challenging the assumption of universal harmony rules proposed in the literature... Our findings challenge this hue-independence assumption to a large extent... the assumption of hue independence cannot be reasonably upheld."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported and accurately scoped. The primary source (arXiv 2508.15777, Forni/Darmon/Benzaquen, "Harmonious Color Pairings," submitted Aug 2025, rev Nov 2025) states verbatim in its abstract: "Our results reveal that preferences are highly hue dependent, challenging the assumption of universal harmony rules proposed in the literature." The body (arxiv.org/html/2508.15777v2) confirms "the assumption of hue independence cannot be reasonably upheld." So "empirically refutes the hue-independence assumption" tracks the source's own wording. The claim's gloss of hue-independence (preference depends only on angular hue distance, not absolute hue position) accurately characterizes the Moon-Spencer/Itten angular formalization — corroborated independently by Sam Goree's color-harmony writeup ("Classical color harmony models assume color harmony is hue independent... the rules that apply to blue are the same as the rules that apply to red"). Crucially, NO contradicting evidence exists; prior PEER-REVIEWED work agrees: Schloss & Palmer (2011, Attention Perception & Psychophysics, doi 10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0) found pair preference "relies more strongly on component color preference and lightness contrast" — i.e., absolute/component colors matter, not just angular relationship — and Granger (1955) found hue-specific harmony effects. Caveats that do NOT refute: source is a preprint (not yet peer-reviewed), uses non-perceptual HSL with 13 fixed hues and unspecified N; and the paper still finds residual angular structure when averaged over hues ("certain hue separations perceived as more harmonious"). But the claim is narrowly about hue-independence specifically, and the paper's strong absolute-hue component on top of the angular component is exactly what refutes the "only angular distance matters" assumption. Claim is moderate strength, current, and corroborated, so preprint status is adequate.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found; the opposite — Schloss & Palmer (2011, Attention Perception & Psychophysics) and Granger (1955) — corroborate that absolute/component hue, not just angular relationship, drives pair preference. Caveat only: primary is a non-peer-reviewed arXiv preprint using non-perceptual HSL, 13 fixed hues, unspecified N; recommend tagging as preprint-grade evidence that aligns with peer-reviewed prior work rather than as a standalone definitive refutation.

The superlative "strongest meta-level case" is a defensible characterization given this is the largest and most comprehensive systematic review of the field. No credible contradicting source found.

*Evidence:* The claim is well-supported by a high-quality primary source, and every quantitative figure is triangulated across independent sources. The source is Jonauskaite & Mohr, "Do we feel colours? (2) Authors' explicit caveat: "most colour-emotion correspondences were tested on an abstract level (i.e., associations)... it remains to be seen whether such correspondences translate to the impact of colour on experienced emotions and specific contexts" — i.e., association ≠ experienced emotion in a real UI. (3) "Cross-culturally consistent" is broadly true but not uniform; the underlying primary data (Jonauskaite et al. 2020, JEP:General, 4,598 participants, 30 nations) document real cross-cultural variation predicted by geographic/linguistic distance, so consistency is a central tendency, not identity. None of these contradict the specific claim, which is scoped to color→emotion ASSOCIATIONS being systematic and broadly cross-cultural — exactly what the review concludes. The interpretive phrase "strongest meta-level case" is a defensible characterization since this is the largest, longest-spanning aggregation of the literature. The strongest available qualification comes from the authors themselves (associations tested at abstract level, may not translate to experienced emotion/context) and from the underlying Jonauskaite et al. 2020 data showing documented cross-cultural variation — both temper the framing but do not refute the scoped claim.

#### V140 `adb3e3fe` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Both preference for a color pair and its perceived harmony increase as the hue similarity between the figure and ground colors increases (peaking when hues match and declining monotonically with hue difference); there is no support for art-theory 'harmony rules' that strong hue contrasts (e.g. complementary) are preferred or harmonious."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "pair preference and harmony both increase as hue similarity increases ... No evidence favoring art theoretic claims that color combinations with strong hue contrasts are either preferred or harmonious"

*Evidence:* Claim is directly supported by the primary source. Schloss & Palmer (2011, Attention, Perception, & Psychophysics 73(2):551-571) abstract states pair preference AND harmony both increase as hue similarity increases, and that "pairs with highly contrastive hues are generally judged to be neither preferable nor harmonious" — confirming the monotonic similarity effect and the rejection of complementary/strong-contrast harmony rules. Independently corroborated, not contradicted: Ou & Luo (2006) two-color harmony model also finds harmony rises with hue similarity, and a 2025 arXiv study (2508.15777) confirms analogous pairs score highest while complementary pairs are not especially preferred, attributing it to natural hue statistics/ecological valence. Source quality (peer-reviewed primary, well-cited, cross-validated by independent work, mechanism since extended) matches the claim's strength; not outdated, not marketing. One scoping caveat (not a refutation): the paper actually makes a THREE-way distinction — a third judgment, preference for a FIGURAL color against a colored ground, INCREASES with hue contrast, and pair preference also depends on component-color preference and lightness contrast. The claim is correctly limited to "pair preference" and "harmony," both of which it characterizes accurately, but a UI/dataviz reference should add that a figure/text/mark on a background is preferred with MORE hue contrast — the figural exception the claim omits.
*Counter-source:* No credible source refutes the claim. Ou & Luo (2006) "A colour harmony model for two-colour combinations" and 2025 arXiv 2508.15777 both CORROBORATE it. The only qualification is internal to the primary source: figural-color preference increases with hue CONTRAST (Schloss & Palmer 2011 third judgment type), which is a different judgment than the pair-preference/harmony the claim addresses.

#### V141 `ae0f455c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A Weighted Affective Valence Estimate (WAVE), computed from color-object associations, object valence ratings, and color-object matching strength with no fitted free parameters, predicts adult color preferences at r = +0.893, accounting for ~80% of the variance."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/43345682_An_ecological_valence_theory_of_color_preferences (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The WAVE achieved a correlation of r = +0.893 with color preferences, "accounting for 80% of the variance with a single predictor." Notably, "no free parameters were estimated in calculating the WAVE.""

*Evidence:* All four sub-claims verified verbatim against the primary source (Palmer & Schloss 2010, "An ecological valence theory of human color preference," PNAS 107(19):8877-8882; PMC2889342). (1) r value: paper reports r = +0.893 between WAVE and color preference — confirmed exactly by the PMC full text and multiple independent sources. (2) Variance: paper states this "accounts for 80% of the variance with a single predictor" (0.893² = 0.797 ≈ 80%) — confirmed. (3) No free parameters: direct quote from paper — "despite its internal complexity, no free parameters were estimated in calculating the WAVE" — confirmed. (4) Three inputs: object-association task (color-object associations), object-valence rating task (object valence), and color-object matching task (match strength) — confirmed verbatim. Source quality matches claim strength (PNAS, peer-reviewed). The claim is correctly scoped to adult color preferences, which is the original study's sample (US college adults), so no overreach. Honest caveat that does NOT refute the claim as worded: r=+0.893 is an in-sample fit; replications are weaker/variable — Taylor & Franklin (2012) found WAVE explained ~61% of variance in British observers (74% male vs 45% female). The 80% figure is the original best-case fit, not a cross-cultural constant, but the claim attributes it specifically to this study and does not assert universality.
*Counter-source:* Taylor & Franklin (2012), Psychonomic Bulletin & Review — WAVE accounted for only ~61% of variance in British adults (74% male, 45% female), showing the +0.893 / 80% figure does not replicate at full strength across populations; this qualifies generalizability but does not contradict the original PNAS report.

#### V142 `ae268921` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Both preference for a color pair and its perceived harmony increase as the hue similarity between the figure and ground colors increases (peaking when hues match and declining monotonically with hue difference); there is no support for art-theory 'harmony rules' that strong hue contrasts (e.g. complementary) are preferred or harmonious."

**Source:** https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "pair preference and harmony both increase as hue similarity increases ... No evidence favoring art theoretic claims that color combinations with strong hue contrasts are either preferred or harmonious"

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the exact cited primary source, Schloss & Palmer (2011), Attention, Perception, & Psychophysics 73(2):551-571, DOI 10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0. The paper directly states "pair preference and harmony both increase as hue similarity increases" and "Similarity ratings decreased monotonically as the hue difference between component colors increased" — supporting Part 1 (peaks at hue match, monotonic decline). For Part 2, it states "pairs with highly contrastive hues are generally judged to be neither preferable nor harmonious," directly refuting art-theory (Itten/Goethe) complementary-harmony rules; the paper resolved prior conflicting literature in favor of similarity-increases-preference. Two real nuances qualify but do NOT contradict the claim: (a) pair preference "relies more strongly on component color preference and lightness contrast," so preference is not a pure hue-similarity function — but on the hue dimension it still increases with similarity, as claimed; (b) a SEPARATE third measure, figural color preference (figure color viewed against ground), INCREASES with hue contrast — but the claim explicitly scopes itself to "pair preference" and "perceived harmony," not figural preference, so it is not contradicted. Group-level preference/harmony correlation r=+.79 (individuals range -.03 to +.70); the claim reflects the group-level headline finding. Source quality (peer-reviewed primary study, the very one cited), currency (foundational, oft-cited, not a fast-moving field), and claim strength all match. Minor imprecision: "monotonically" is most strictly supported for similarity/harmony; pair preference has extra noise from component-color effects — but this does not rise to refutation.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found; art-theory complementary-harmony claims (Itten/Goethe, Ou & Luo harmony models) are exactly the conventions this empirical study refutes, not counter-evidence against it.

*Claim under review:* "The models were built on psychophysical data from 12 world regions (Argentina, China, France, Germany, Hungary, Iran, Japan, Spain, Sweden, Taiwan, Thailand, UK), making this a genuinely cross-cultural color-emotion/harmony dataset rather than a single-population study."

**Source:** https://www.researchgate.net/publication/326651521_Universal_models_of_colour_emotion_and_colour_harmony (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A new set of quantitative models of colour emotion and colour harmony were developed using psychophysical data collected from 12 regions in the world, including Argentina, China, France, Germany, Hungary, Iran, Japan, Spain, Sweden, Taiwan, Thailand, and the UK."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the primary source. The paper "Universal models of colour emotion and colour harmony" (Ou et al., 2018, Color Research & Application, Wiley, DOI 10.1002/col.22243) states in its abstract, verbatim and corroborated by both the Wiley page and ResearchGate: "psychophysical data collected from 12 regions in the world, including Argentina, China, France, Germany, Hungary, Iran, Japan, Spain, Sweden, Taiwan, Thailand, and the UK." The claim's 12-region list matches exactly, and the framing "cross-cultural rather than single-population" is accurate — the dataset pools color-emotion/harmony data contributed by multiple researchers across these regions (Ou: Argentina/France/Germany/Iran/Spain/Sweden/Taiwan/UK; Sato: Japan; Sueeprasan: Thailand harmony data). Source quality matches claim strength (peer-reviewed journal, not marketing). No contradicting source found. One minor, non-refuting nuance: the harmony sub-dataset rests on fewer regions than the emotion sub-dataset, and the PCA result noted harmony principles "were partly agreed by observers of the same region" — so "12-region" describes the pooled corpus more than every individual sub-model, but this does not undermine the cross-cultural-scope claim.

**Supporting quote:** "Higher preferences are observed in the contrast region (between 160○and 220○), flanked by regions of lower preference at smaller angular distances. The abstract independently confirms this and calls the averaged patterns "statistically meaningful." The claim maps faithfully: contrast region 160-220 deg (near-complementary ~180) preferred; similarity region near reference hue not supported; Moon & Spencer 1944 attribution correct (their classical model defines similarity and contrast regions). The claim correctly scopes to "averaged across all reference hues," matching the paper's "hue-averaged data" condition. Claim is accurate, current, and properly attributed to the named primary source.
*Counter-source:* Broader color-preference literature is mixed: Schloss & Palmer (2011, Atten Percept Psychophys) found preference increased with similarity in figure-ground pairs — opposite direction — but does not refute this paper's distinct hue-averaged finding. The paper itself stresses hue-dependence over any universal rule, qualifying (not contradicting) the contrast-wins framing.

#### V146 `ae716a2d` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Preferred color pairings statistically match hue distributions in natural landscape images (peaks at blue and orange-yellow, minima at greens and purples), and dominant hues in natural scenes are most often separated by ~180 degrees -- evidence that preference tracks ecological exposure rather than an abstract harmony geometry. Replicated across two image datasets (12,000 and 4,319 images)."

**Source:** https://arxiv.org/pdf/2508.15777 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the peaks and valleys of the distribution match remarkably well that of the Combinability index and absolute preferences (maximum for blue and orange-yellow tints, minimum for greens and purples)... dominant hues in natural scenes are most often separated by approximately 180°... we also measured the hue distribution using an independent dataset of 4,319 landscape images; the results were found to be virtually identical."

*Evidence:* The descriptive content is faithfully quoted from arXiv 2508.15777 ("Harmonious Color Pairings: Insights from Human Preference and Natural Hue Statistics," Benzaquen et al., Aug 2025): it does report preference peaks at blue/orange-yellow, minima at green/purple, ~180-deg dominant-hue separation, and "virtually identical" results on a 4,319-image second landscape set. So the claim is NOT a misread. BUT three things break it as a STRONG/established claim: (1) SOURCE QUALITY MISMATCH — it is a single non-peer-reviewed arXiv preprint by statistical physicists, and it is correlational, yet the claim is phrased as settled causal evidence ("evidence that preference tracks ecological exposure RATHER THAN an abstract harmony geometry"). (2) WEAK/CIRCULAR REPLICATION — both datasets (12,000 and 4,319) are Kaggle LANDSCAPE-PHOTO collections, i.e. same-genre, aesthetically composed/curated images; near-identical hue stats between two landscape-photo sets is unsurprising and does not test generalizability, and "natural hue statistics" measured on pleasing curated photos may already encode human aesthetic selection (circularity). (3) CONTESTED INTERPRETATION vs stronger peer-reviewed work — Palmer & Schloss Ecological Valence Theory (PNAS 2010, replicated) found raw color FREQUENCY/exposure is a WEAK predictor; object affective valence (WAVE) predicts ~80% of variance vs cone-contrast 37%, color-appearance 60%, color-emotion 55%; Taylor & Franklin found colors with FEWER associated objects are MORE preferred (opposite of "more exposure = more liked"). Classic single-color data also show a preference TROUGH at yellow/chartreuse, in partial tension with the claimed orange-yellow peak. Net: descriptive facts accurate, but the strong causal "exposure-not-harmony" framing and "replicated" robustness are overstated for one correlational preprint and conflict with the leading framework — should be tagged emerging/single-preprint, not [STRONG].
*Counter-source:* Palmer & Schloss, "An ecological valence theory of human color preference," PNAS 2010 (107:8877-8882; pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC2889342) — raw exposure/frequency is a weak predictor, object valence (WAVE) dominates (~80% variance); plus Taylor & Franklin (fewer associated objects -> more preferred). Source under review (arxiv.org/abs/2508.15777) is a non-peer-reviewed preprint.

#### V148 `af73c25b` — **upheld** · confidence: high

**Source:** https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC10480976/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the true effect of incidental red on attraction is very small, potentially nonexistent"

*Evidence:* Primary source (PMC10480976, "Meta-Analysis of the Effect of Red on Perceived Attractiveness," Lehmann, Elliot & Calin-Jageman, 2023) directly confirms the claim. The article is an explicit ADVERSARIAL COLLABORATION and states: "one of us (second author) is inclined to view the mixed literature as due to the inherent complexity of color research...The other two of us (first and last authors) have conducted a set of replication studies that did not support the red-romance hypothesis." First+third authors conclude "the true effect of incidental red on attraction is very small, potentially nonexistent" (verbatim supporting quote). Second author (Elliot, the red-romance hypothesis originator) interprets results as real-but-context-moderated, emphasizing moderator-variable testing. Effect sizes are small (d=0.26 men rating women; d=0.13 women rating men) with substantial heterogeneity, consistent with "not robustly established." Attribution (first/third = small-or-null; second = real-but-moderated) is accurate, and the quote is genuine. Source is a primary peer-reviewed meta-analysis matching the claim's strength; current; not marketing.

Quote supports claim; source is the primary peer-reviewed paper matching the claim's strength.

### Pass 3 — screens / contrast / gradients / design systems (2026-06-26)

#### V151 `a07b017d` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "A blue-text/red-background (blue–red) combination induced a heightened accommodative response — i.e. it forced greater focusing effort than other combinations — which is direct physiological evidence that saturated blue+red pairings are an on-screen anti-pattern."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0042698919302123 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the blue-red combination induced a heightened accommodative response"

*Evidence:* The verbatim quote is real and accurately sourced: Jiménez, Redondo, Molina, Martínez-Domingo, Hernández-Andrés & Vera, "Short-term effects of text-background color combinations on the dynamics of the accommodative response," Vision Research 166:33-42 (2020), n=20 young adults, 14 color combinations, 2-min LCD reading passages, continuous accommodation+pupil recording. The abstract does state "the blue-red combination induced a heightened accommodative response." HOWEVER, the CLAIM overreaches on three fronts and is an overreach/misread:

(1) "Anti-pattern / direct physiological evidence of harm" is NOT what the study found. The authors frame the result neutrally as the color combination "modulating" accommodative dynamics, and explicitly report that READING SPEED was UNAFFECTED by color — i.e., no demonstrated performance or legibility harm. Their conclusion is the cautious "may be of relevance for designing visuals... to minimize the risk of visual fatigue," not a recommendation to avoid blue-red. They never label it an anti-pattern.

(2) A "heightened accommodative response" is NOT unambiguously bad. It means the eye accommodated MORE (higher response magnitude), consistent with blue text focusing in front of the retina via longitudinal chromatic aberration (~1.25-1.50 D RGB interval). Higher accommodation can equally be read as more accurate/complete focus (less lag). The clinical literature (StatPearls) ties strain to extremes of either accommodative LAG or accommodative EXCESS/spasm — a transient elevated response within normal range over 2 minutes is not equivalent to pathological excess. So "forced greater focusing effort = anti-pattern" is an unproven interpretive leap.

(3) The study's CLEARLY-negative dynamics — MORE accommodative variability and smaller pupil sizes — were attributed to "positive polarities" (dark-on-light), NOT to blue-red. The claim cherry-picks the single neutral response-magnitude datum for blue-red and reframes it as harm, while the more strain-suggestive instability was tied to a different (and conventionally "good") combination.

Net: the literal sub-fact is sourced, but "direct physiological evidence that saturated blue+red is an on-screen anti-pattern" overstates a single n=20 proxy study that showed no performance harm and drew no such conclusion. (The broader claim that blue+red is a screen anti-pattern IS supported by OTHER, stronger mechanisms — chromatic aberration and chromostereopsis — but this paper is not the "direct physiological evidence" of an anti-pattern that the claim asserts.)
*Counter-source:* Jiménez et al. (2020) Vision Research 166:33-42 abstract (own neutral framing, reading speed unaffected, variability tied to positive polarities); StatPearls "Accommodative Excess"/"Accommodative Insufficiency" (NBK592379, NBK587363) on accommodation-strain relationship
