#### V153 `a095eb8a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Positive polarity displays (dark text on a light background) produce better proofreading performance than negative polarity displays (light text on a dark background) — direct, controlled disconfirming evidence against the claim that dark mode improves legibility for normal-vision young adults."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25135324/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Smaller pupil size and better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays ... Performance was better with positive than with negative polarity displays."

*Evidence:* The claim faithfully matches the primary source. PubMed 25135324 is Piepenbrock, Mayr & Buchner (2014), Ergonomics 57(11):1670-7, titled "Smaller pupil size and better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays." The abstract states pupil sizes were smaller and "proofreading performance was better with positive than with negative polarity displays," supporting the display-luminance mechanism (brighter positive-polarity screen → smaller pupil → sharper retinal image). This is a controlled experiment, so the claim's "direct, controlled disconfirming evidence" framing is accurate. The finding is replicated, not isolated: companion study Piepenbrock et al. (2013, PubMed 23654206) tested younger (18-33) and older (60-85) adults with normal/corrected-to-normal vision and no eye disease, finding positive polarity advantageous "irrespective of age"; Piepenbrock et al. (2014, Human Factors) found the advantage grows as font size shrinks; Buchner & Baumgartner (2007) found it holds across ambient illumination. NN/g's review concurs: "light mode won across all dimensions" on visual-acuity and proofreading tasks. The claim is correctly scoped to normal-vision young adults and to legibility/performance (not preference or eye strain), which sidesteps the legitimate qualifications I found (visual-impairment users can prefer dark; halation; dim-ambient conditions; subjective perception did not differ between modes). Those qualifications bound but do not refute the claim. Source quality (primary peer-reviewed + replication lineage) matches the claim strength; finding is current and remains the consensus. Could not refute on any of the five checks.
*Counter-source:* Qualifying (not refuting) evidence: NN/g notes participants reported no perceived readability difference despite the performance gap; arXiv 2203.16546 and accessibility sources note dark mode can help visually-impaired users and in low light; halation/astigmatism caveats apply — but none contradict the positive-polarity proofreading advantage for normal-vision young adults.

#### V154 `a0a753e8` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "M3 maps tonal DISTANCE directly to a contrast ratio: tones 50 and 98 give an accessible 3:1, and tones 30 and 98 give 7:1. This is a concrete, falsifiable token rule — contrast is driven by lightness/tone difference, and the system reserves wider tonal gaps for higher-contrast modes. [CONVENTION / best-practice]"

**Source:** https://m3.material.io/styles/color/system/how-the-system-works (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Using tones 50 and 98 for a button and its label creates an accessible 3:1 contrast"

*Evidence:* The claim's falsifiable content is confirmed against the primary source (m3.material.io). Two independent searches reproduced M3's documentation: "Using tones 50 and 98 for a button and its label creates an accessible 3:1 contrast" (matches the supplied supporting quote) and "Using colors of tones 30 and 98 for a button and its label create a 7:1 contrast"; plus "colors with a greater difference in tone create higher contrast" and "the algorithms assign tones even farther apart to achieve a 7:1 contrast." So both tone-pair→ratio mappings, the lightness-drives-contrast principle, and the wider-gap-for-higher-contrast directionality are all primary-sourced and current. The tag [CONVENTION / best-practice] is appropriate. Two caveats that bound but do not refute the claim: (1) the framing "tonal DISTANCE directly to a contrast ratio" is a mild overstatement — equal tonal distances do NOT uniquely determine a contrast ratio (contrast is a luminance ratio, not a function of distance; since HCT tone = CIELAB L*, Y=((L*+16)/116)^3, a distance of 48 yields ~4.27:1 at tones 50→98 but ~4.74:1 at 40→88); M3 states specific PAIRS, not a generic formula, which is exactly what the claim's concrete content lists. (2) M3's 3:1 and 7:1 are conservative floors — the neutral computed contrast for those pairs is actually ~4.27:1 and ~8.89:1. Neither caveat contradicts the documented token rule; the substantive claim is well-supported, from the primary source, current, and correctly labeled as convention not proven science.
*Counter-source:* No credible source contradicts the M3 figures. The only nuance is a mechanical one: contrast ratio is a function of luminances (WCAG relative luminance = CIE Y), and tonal distance does not uniquely map to a ratio — but M3 documents specific pairs, not a generic distance formula, so the claim's concrete content stands. M3 primary docs: https://m3.material.io/styles/color/system/how-the-system-works

#### V155 `a0bcfa1a` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "On LCD (emissive) displays, the most legible text/background pairings are high-contrast combinations with POSITIVE polarity (dark text on a light background), whereas on CRTs the most legible were NEGATIVE-polarity combinations (light text on dark) — so optimal polarity is display-technology-dependent, and this is direct empirical pushback on any universal 'dark mode reads better' claim for self-lit screens."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0003687014000696 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A new legibility table is proposed for LCDs where the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with positive polarity whereas for CRT displays, the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with negative polarity."

*Evidence:* The source (Humar et al. 2014, Applied Ergonomics, 308 participants; PubMed 24874503) is genuine and the quote is accurate. But the claim's distinctive thesis — that optimal text polarity is "display-technology-dependent" (positive on LCD, negative on CRT) — overreaches and is contradicted/heavily qualified by stronger, more numerous sources. (1) The CRT-half is contradicted by foundational VDU research: Bauer & Cavonius (1980), the study that drove the industry's move to dark-on-light, found readers ~26% MORE accurate with POSITIVE polarity (dark-on-light) on CRTs; Cushman (1986) and standard VDT ergonomics likewise recommended positive polarity on CRTs. Humar's "CRT legibility table" is a comparison reference (their own ~2008 CRT study), not a fresh CRT measurement. (2) The causal framing is directly undercut by Buchner, Mayr & Brandt (2009) "The advantage of positive text-background polarity is due to high display luminance" (Ergonomics; PubMed 19562598) and Buchner & Baumgartner (2007), which show the positive-polarity advantage holds irrespective of ambient light and color contrast and is driven by display LUMINANCE (brighter background → smaller pupil → better acuity/depth of field) — not by CRT-vs-LCD technology. CRTs ran dimmer than LCDs, so any apparent technology split is a luminance confound, not a technology law. (3) The "for self-lit screens" framing is incoherent: BOTH CRT and LCD are self-lit/emissive, so the comparison is within that category. The only well-supported part — positive polarity reads better on modern screens, rebutting "dark mode reads better" — is true UNIVERSALLY and luminance-driven, not because polarity flips with display technology. A strong, single-sourced generalization contradicted on its key component and reframed mechanistically by stronger work = refuted.
*Counter-source:* Buchner, Mayr & Brandt (2009), "The advantage of positive text-background polarity is due to high display luminance," Ergonomics (PubMed 19562598); and Bauer & Cavonius (1980), "Improving the legibility of visual display units through contrast reversal" (positive polarity ~26% more accurate on CRTs); corroborated by Buchner & Baumgartner (2007), Ergonomics 50(7).

#### V156 `a0c80776` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "On LCD (emissive) displays, the most legible text/background pairings are high-contrast combinations with POSITIVE polarity (dark text on a light background), whereas on CRTs the most legible were NEGATIVE-polarity combinations (light text on dark) — so optimal polarity is display-technology-dependent, and this is direct empirical pushback on any universal 'dark mode reads better' claim for self-lit screens."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0003687014000696 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A new legibility table is proposed for LCDs where the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with positive polarity whereas for CRT displays, the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with negative polarity."

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED. Source identified: "The impact of color combinations on the legibility of text presented on LCDs," Applied Ergonomics, May 2014 (308 participants, 56 color combinations, Snellen-chart character identification + 10-point Likert legibility ratings); abstract states verbatim that "neither the Le Courier legibility table nor the CRT legibility table is appropriate for LCDs, and a new legibility table is proposed for LCDs where the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with positive polarity whereas for CRT displays, the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with negative polarity." The supporting quote is exact; the claim's factual core (LCD->positive best, CRT->negative best, polarity optimum differs by display) is a faithful restatement, not an overreach. Source quality is sufficient (peer-reviewed primary, large n). The load-bearing conclusion -- positive polarity/dark-on-light most legible on emissive LCDs, undercutting any universal "dark mode reads better" -- is independently corroborated by a large body of work (Buchner & Baumgartner; Piepenbrock et al. 2013 Ergonomics 56:7 "Positive display polarity is advantageous for both younger and older adults"; Piepenbrock et al. 2014 "Positive Display Polarity Is Particularly Advantageous for Small Character Sizes"; 2016 Applied Ergonomics glance-reading study; 2025 mixed-reality polarity paper). So the claim is on the side of the dominant evidence, not a cherry-picked outlier. ONE BOUNDING CAVEAT (not a refutation): the broader literature attributes the positive-polarity advantage to display LUMINANCE, not display TECHNOLOGY per se (Buchner & Baumgartner, "The advantage of positive text-background polarity is due to high display luminance," Ergonomics) -- CRTs were dimmer, so "display-technology-dependent" is more precisely "luminance-dependent," and the CRT/negative-polarity half is itself contested (Bauer & Cavonius 1980 found positive polarity better on CRT acuity). This re-attributes mechanism and bounds the "technology-dependent" framing but does not contradict the paper's reported result or the claim's central pushback against universal dark-mode-superiority. The reference should present it as "positive (dark-on-light) is most legible on emissive screens; the LCD-vs-CRT difference is largely a luminance confound" rather than a hard technology law.
*Counter-source:* Buchner & Baumgartner / Piepenbrock et al., "The advantage of positive text-background polarity is due to high display luminance" (Ergonomics) and Piepenbrock et al. 2013 (Ergonomics 56:7) -- attribute the polarity advantage to luminance not display technology, bounding the "display-technology-dependent" framing; Bauer & Cavonius (1980) found positive polarity better even on CRT, contesting the CRT/negative-polarity half.

#### V157 `a0f3c839` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The mechanism is that discrete color bands / discontinuities in rainbow and cool-warm ramps -- which perceptual-uniformity advocates treat as undesirable artifacts -- actually AID gradient perception, because viewers use the apparent size and frequency of colored patches as proxies for gradient magnitude. So 'hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing.'"

**Source:** https://scholarworks.indianapolis.iu.edu/server/api/core/bitstreams/3a72f1a3-24d9-4635-a16f-de6b08accd9d/content (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "although discrete color bands are essentially artifacts [5], viewers may rely on such features as proxies for gradient. For instance, a viewer could estimate the apparent size of the blue and red patches in Figure 1, or how frequently they seem to occur in the image... the experiment suggests that hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing. Color bands may aid some tasks where the goal is to compare structural differences, including net gradients."

*Evidence:* Source verified as a peer-reviewed primary: Khairi Reda & Michael Papka, "Evaluating Gradient Perception in Color-Coded Scalar Fields," IEEE VIS'19 (short paper). The bitstream URL resolves to this paper; the author's copy is khreda.com/papers/VIS19-gradient-percept.pdf and it is indexed at ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/8933760. Two independent web searches returned near-verbatim corroboration of the supporting quote ("although discrete color bands are essentially artifacts, viewers may rely on such features as proxies for gradient... estimate the apparent size of colored patches or how frequently they seem to occur... hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing"). The claim is a faithful paraphrase that PRESERVES the paper's hedges ("may not always," "aid some tasks"), not an overreach. Empirical backing is a controlled just-noticeable-difference experiment: rainbow gave higher gradient sensitivity (JND 3.05%, CI 2.82-3.28%) than viridis monotonic-luminance (JND 3.61%, CI 3.31-3.91%), with the authors concluding "apparent discontinuities in the color ramp may help viewers discern subtle structural differences in gradient." Contrary literature (Borland & Taylor "Rainbow Color Map Still Considered Harmful"; Quinan et al. 2019 "Examining Implicit Discretization in Spectral Schemes"; Crameri/HESS hydrology guidance) attacks rainbow for OTHER tasks (smooth-data banding misleads, obscures structure, not CVD-safe) and does NOT contradict this bounded claim; Quinan even frames the real danger as the UNPREDICTABILITY of discretization, not discretization per se. Caveat: the stated mechanism (size/frequency of patches as gradient proxies) is the authors' proposed hypothesis/interpretation rather than a directly measured mechanism, but the claim mirrors the paper's own hypothesis wording with a "may" hedge, so it is not a misread. Source quality (peer-reviewed primary, 2019, not outdated, not marketing) matches the modest hedged claim.
*Counter-source:* Borland & Taylor 2007 "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful"; Quinan et al. 2019 "Examining Implicit Discretization in Spectral Schemes" (CGF/EuroVis); Crameri et al. / HESS 2021 hydrology rainbow guidance — these qualify rainbow use for other tasks but do not refute Reda's bounded gradient-discrimination finding.

#### V160 `a1dcde27` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "For judging which of two scalar fields has steeper average gradient, the perceptually-uniform monotonic-luminance colormap viridis performed WORST: its just-noticeable-difference was 3.61% gradient (CI 3.31-3.91%), significantly larger (worse sensitivity) than diverging cool-warm at 2.86% (CI 2.62-3.09%) and rainbow at 3.05% (CI 2.82-3.28%); main effect of colormap F(2,520)=14.771, p<0.001, both cool-warm-vs-viridis and rainbow-vs-viridis p<0.001. **Source:** https://scholarworks.indianapolis.iu.edu/server/api/core/bitstreams/3a72f1a3-24d9-4635-a16f-de6b08accd9d/content (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "It appears that cool-warm (JND gradient: 2.86%, CI: 2.62-3.09%) and rainbow (JND: 3.05%, CI: 2.82-3.28%) afford higher sensitivity to gradients as compared to viridis (JND: 3.61%, CI: 3.31-3.91%)."

Source is Reda & Papka, "Evaluating Gradient Perception in Color-Coded Scalar Fields," IEEE VIS 2019 (peer-reviewed; IEEE Xplore doc 8933760; author copy khreda.com/papers/VIS19-gradient-percept.pdf). All numbers confirmed: (a) JND values cool-warm 2.86% [2.62-3.09], rainbow 3.05% [2.82-3.28], viridis 3.61% [3.31-3.91] are verbatim in the supporting quote and corroborated by independent search reads of the source; (b) the F-statistic NOT in the quote — F(2,520)=14.771, p<0.001 — was independently corroborated by a separate search read of the source content, along with Tukey-adjusted pairwise tests cool-warm-vs-viridis p<0.001 and rainbow-vs-viridis p<0.001. So among the three tested colormaps, viridis had the largest (worst) gradient JND. The interpretive conclusion is NOT an overreach: the claim explicitly scopes itself to gradient-steepness discrimination ("for this task class"), matching the authors' own framing that to the extent viridis is perceived uniformly, viewers struggle to apply gradient-perception heuristics, and discontinuities in cool-warm/rainbow ramps aid spatial gradient tasks. No genuine contradiction exists: the competing "viridis wins" literature (Liu & Heer 2018, Somewhere Over the Rainbow) concerns magnitude/quantitative reading, a different task, so it does not refute a gradient-discrimination result. Finding reinforced by Reda's later Rainbow Colormaps work (TVCG 2023). Checklist: supported by quote (yes), no applicable contradicting evidence, source quality matches claim strength (top-tier primary), not outdated (2019 psychophysics, stable), not marketing/cherry-pick (scope explicitly bounded). Could not render the PDF locally (no poppler/pdf libs), but two independent search-engine reads of the primary-source content returned the identical numbers including the F-statistic.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. The apparent counter — Liu & Heer 2018 "Somewhere Over the Rainbow" (idl.cs.washington.edu/files/2018-QuantitativeColor-CHI.pdf), which favors perceptually-uniform/viridis — tests magnitude estimation, a different task, and therefore does not contradict this gradient-discrimination finding. The claim's own scope qualifier ("for this task class") forecloses the over-generalization that would be needed to create a conflict.

#### V161 `a21026eb` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "Material Design 3's color system is built on the HCT color space (hue, chroma, tone), whose defining property is that tone (= luminance) can be held fixed while hue and chroma change — something RGB and HSL cannot do. This is the mechanism that lets a token system guarantee accessible contrast independent of hue. [CONVENTION / best-practice, with a perceptual-science basis]"

**Source:** https://m3.material.io/styles/color/system/how-the-system-works (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Unlike other color spaces (like HSL or RGB), HCT allows the manipulation of a color's hue and chroma without affecting its tone."

*Evidence:* Core claim is well-supported by the primary source and corroborating technical sources. The supporting quote ("Unlike other color spaces like HSL or RGB, HCT allows manipulation of hue and chroma without affecting tone") directly backs parts 1-2. The contrast mechanism (part 3) is confirmed with documented numbers: an HCT tone difference of 40 guarantees contrast >=3.0 and 50 guarantees >=4.5 (multiple sources, e.g. material3-themes-manual.amoebelabs.com, facelessuser.github.io/coloraide). HSL's flaw (its "L" axis is NOT perceptually constant across hues) is well established, so the HSL/RGB contrast is fair. Source quality (primary M3 docs + technical secondaries) matches the modest [CONVENTION] tag; the claim is current (HCT/M3 2021-present), not marketing fluff.

ONE genuine imprecision, not a refutation: the parenthetical "tone (= luminance)" is technically wrong. Sources confirm HCT tone is EXACTLY CIELAB L* (perceptual LIGHTNESS), not luminance (Y): "HCT tone is exactly the same as CIELab's lightness (L*)... tone uses perceptually accurate lightness, not physical luminance" (amoebelabs M3 analysis; ColorAide HCT docs). But this does not break the claim because L* is a bijective monotonic function of Y, so same tone => same luminance => same WCAG contrast: "Because CIE L* maps directly to WCAG relative luminance, same tone = same contrast ratio, regardless of hue or chroma." So the mechanism the claim asserts is real; only the gloss should read "perceptual lightness L*" rather than "luminance."

Caveat surfaced: sRGB gamut clipping reduces CHROMA (not tone) to stay in-gamut, so the tone/contrast link is preserved; and the tone-delta->contrast mapping is M3's documented heuristic, not an exact identity. Neither undermines the claim's substance.
*Counter-source:* https://material3-themes-manual.amoebelabs.com/basics/m3-analysis-introduction/ and https://facelessuser.github.io/coloraide/colors/hct/ — both confirm HCT tone = CIELAB L* (perceptual lightness), not luminance Y; the claim's "= luminance" is imprecise but the contrast mechanism still holds because L* maps monotonically to relative luminance.

#### V162 `a2118b67` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "High colour contrast does NOT guarantee fast/legible perception when luminance contrast is small (the equiluminant case), because human visual acuity is far lower for pure-chromatic information than for luminance information. This [STRONG] finding refutes the common [MYTH] that strong hue contrast alone is enough for readable UI text."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0141938204000046 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Moderate or even high colour contrast does not guarantee quick visual perception, if the luminance contrast between characters and background is small. This is probably due to the fact that visual acuity (the ability to see small details) is considerably lower for pure colour information than for luminance information."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported, current, and the source quality matches the [STRONG] tag. (1) Quote support: the claim faithfully restates the cited quote (only "considerably"→"far," trivial). No overreach. (2) Source: the primary citation (Displays journal 2004, "Effects of luminance and colour contrast on the search of information on display devices," S0141938204000046) is peer-reviewed applied ergonomics. (3) Independent corroboration of the mechanism: Mullen 1985 (J. Physiol. 359:381-400) — chromatic grating acuity ~11-12 cpd vs achromatic ~33 cpd; web sources confirm "visual acuity for pure color contrast is clearly lower than for luminance" and "luminance contrast is the basis of text readability." WCAG operationalizes this by using relative luminance (not chroma) for text contrast. (4) The only bounding nuance — at LOW spatial frequencies (<0.5 cpd) chromatic sensitivity exceeds luminance (Mullen 1985) — does NOT apply to text, which is small/high-spatial-frequency where luminance dominates; the claim is correctly scoped to "readable UI text"/"small details." (5) Equiluminant text is readable-but-slower, matching the claim's hedge ("does not guarantee fast/legible"), not contradicting it. The [MYTH] framing (hue contrast alone suffices) is defensible since accessibility standards require luminance contrast.
*Counter-source:* No credible refuting source found. Closest qualifier: Mullen 1985 (J. Physiol. 359:381-400) shows chromatic contrast sensitivity EXCEEDS luminance below ~0.5 cpd — but this applies to coarse/large features, not text, so it bounds rather than refutes the text-scoped claim.

All four claim components verified independently: (1) "new method for evaluating the feature detection threshold function across a colormap" — verbatim from abstract; (2) "nine colormaps for three feature sizes" — verbatim; (3) crowdsourced via Amazon Mechanical Turk — abstract says "crowdsourced studies" and the same group's companion methodology paper documents "a study using Amazon Mechanical Turk," with AMT validated as their platform for color-based feature-detection studies; (4) empirical-over-CIELAB thesis — the paper EXPLICITLY tested the hypothesis that uniform CIELAB space accurately models feature-detection thresholds and REJECTED it, finding that reduced weights on the green-red and blue-yellow (chromaticity) terms produce a more accurate model for smaller features; companion text states "uniformity in terms of feature detection may not be achieved by current methods which are based on the use of uniform color spaces." The paraphrase "discriminative power should be measured behaviorally, not assumed from CIELAB spacing" is faithful to the paper's actual finding, not an overreach. #### V164 `a26421f5` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Naive piecewise-linear interpolation in CIELAB space produces a visible Mach-band artifact at the white midpoint of a diverging colormap (where luminance abruptly switches from rising to falling); interpolating instead in Moreland's polar Msh color space smooths this by letting luminance level off while chromaticity changes faster. This is the core mechanism behind 'CIELAB-linear is not enough' for diverging maps."

**Source:** https://www.kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Using piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space causes Mach bands in the white part of diverging color maps (left image). The transition can be softened by interpolating in Msh space (right image)."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the primary source (Moreland, "Diverging Color Maps for Scientific Visualization," ISVC 2009, and the expanded PDF). The provided quote is verbatim from the paper's figure caption: "Using piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space causes Mach bands in the white part of diverging color maps (left image). The transition can be softened by interpolating in Msh space (right image)." Two independent web searches also surfaced the paper's deeper mechanistic statements that confirm the claim's added detail: (a) "Msh space (a polar version of CIELAB)" — matches "polar Msh color space"; (b) "a 'leveling off' of the luminance as the color map approaches white is required. To compensate, the chromaticity must change more dramatically in this part of the color map" — matches the claim's "letting luminance level off while chromaticity changes faster." The Mach-band cause is physically consistent: piecewise-linear CIELAB interpolation through a white midpoint creates a luminance peak (slope sign-flip from rising to falling), exactly the gradient discontinuity that triggers perceptual Mach bands. Adversarial checks found no contradicting source despite a targeted search for criticism/limitations; the method is widely adopted (matplotlib coolwarm, gonum/palette/moreland, coloripy). Source is primary and peer-reviewed; not outdated, not marketing. The only soft spot — framing the Mach band as "the core mechanism behind 'CIELAB-linear is not enough'" — is defensible because within diverging maps with a white midpoint, the Mach band is precisely the reason Moreland gives for preferring Msh over CIELAB-linear interpolation; the distinct grey-dead-zone problem concerns endpoint-to-endpoint interpolation, which the claim does not conflate. Not a refuting-level overreach.

#### V165 `a29f4430` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "A blue-text/red-background (blue–red) combination induced a heightened accommodative response — i.e. it forced greater focusing effort than other combinations — which is direct physiological evidence that saturated blue+red pairings are an on-screen anti-pattern."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0042698919302123 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the blue-red combination induced a heightened accommodative response"

*Evidence:* The cited paper is real and the quote is accurate: Aldaba/Vera et al., "Short-term effects of text-background color combinations on the dynamics of the accommodative response," Vision Research 2019/2020 (DOI 10.1016/j.visres.2019.11.006, PII S0042698919302123), did report "the blue-red combination induced a heightened accommodative response" (20 adults, 14x 2-min reading passages, open-field autorefractometer). BUT the CLAIM is an overreach/misread on two counts. (1) "Heightened accommodative response" is not "greater focusing effort" or harm — it is a wavelength-driven focus-plane shift from longitudinal chromatic aberration (blue focuses in front of the retina, eye over-accommodates), a predictable optical bias; the paper measured no fatigue/effort/asthenopia variable. (2) The label "direct physiological evidence ... anti-pattern" is the source's inference, NOT the paper's conclusion. The same abstract states "the manipulation of text-background color did not have a significant effect on reading speed" — the functional outcome was null. The study also found POSITIVE polarity (dark-on-light, the conventionally recommended condition) "was associated with more variability of the accommodative response and smaller pupil sizes," undercutting any "blue-red is uniquely bad" framing; and the only legibility complaint was tied to LOW luminance contrast (white-yellow), not blue-red. The authors framed everything as a SHORT-TERM modulation of accommodative dynamics, not as eye strain, harm, or a design prohibition, and did not isolate "saturation." A single 20-subject, 2-minute, reading-speed-null study cannot carry the strong normative claim "direct physiological evidence that saturated blue+red pairings are an on-screen anti-pattern." Note: the blue-red anti-pattern is genuinely supported by OTHER mechanisms (chromostereopsis / chromatic-aberration "vibration"), but this specific paper as cited does not establish it.
*Counter-source:* https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0042698919302123 (abstract: "the manipulation of text-background color did not have a significant effect on reading speed"; positive polarity associated with MORE accommodative variability) — the paper's own null and counter-findings refute the "anti-pattern" / "greater focusing effort" gloss

#### V166 `a323f74e` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In relative-distance color discrimination tasks, viridis maintained consistently low error across all comparison spans, while the single-hue Blues colormap matched viridis accuracy at larger spans but degraded notably at the smallest span — showing perceptual-uniformity benefits are span/task-dependent, not uniform."

**Source:** https://idl.uw.edu/papers/quantitative-color (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Viridis exhibits consistently low error across the board ... [Blues] drops notably for the smallest span."

*Evidence:* The empirical core is DIRECTLY and exactly supported by the primary source. The paper is Liu & Heer, "Somewhere Over the Rainbow: An Empirical Assessment of Quantitative Colormaps," CHI 2018 (idl.uw.edu/papers/quantitative-color) — a peer-reviewed, highly cited study by a top visualization researcher (Heer). The supporting quote is verbatim from the paper's own figure caption: "Viridis exhibits consistently low error across the board. The accuracy of blues matches that of viridis at larger spans, but drops notably for the smallest span." Method: subjects made relative-distance judgments among color triplets from 4 single-hue and 5 multi-hue colormaps; error/accuracy was the metric. Independent corroboration: WebSearch surfaced the paper's broader statement "All single-hue colormaps similarly suffer from resolution issues when the span is small," and the conclusion that "scales varying in hue while also varying monotonically in lightness were better for judging relative distances ... compared with single-hue scales." Direction, metric (error), and span effect all match the claim exactly.

Source quality, currency, and provenance all check out: primary peer-reviewed source (not marketing/forum), 2018 and still standard (viridis is now the matplotlib/MATLAB default), not contradicted by any source found.

Minor caveat (does not rise to refutation): the interpretive clause "perceptual-uniformity benefits are span/task-dependent" slightly relabels the mechanism. The paper attributes viridis's edge to MULTI-HUE variation (hue + monotonic lightness) giving better fine-grained resolution, not to perceptual uniformity per se — viridis happens to be both multi-hue and perceptually uniform. The more precise framing would be "multi-hue/resolution benefits." Also, "task-dependent" extends slightly beyond this single span-focused figure, though the paper as a whole does report task-dependence. These are framing nuances, not factual errors, and they do not reverse or contradict the finding. The practical conclusion — that the advantage of the better colormap over a decent single-hue one is span-dependent (concentrated at the smallest span), not uniform — is exactly what the data show.

#### V168 `a33967bf` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A lightness difference between two hues used to convey content counts as an additional (non-color) visual distinction only when the relative-luminance contrast ratio between the colors is 3:1 or greater."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "If content is conveyed through the use of colors that differ not only in their hue, but that also have a significant difference in lightness, then this counts as an additional visual distinction, as long as the difference in relative luminance between the colors leads to a contrast ratio of 3:1 or greater."

*Evidence:* Verbatim quote confirmed on the primary W3C source (https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html, Understanding SC 1.4.1): "If content is conveyed through the use of colors that differ not only in their hue, but that also have a significant difference in lightness, then this counts as an additional visual distinction, as long as the difference in relative luminance between the colors leads to a contrast ratio of 3:1 or greater." The claim restates this faithfully: (a) "only when ≥3:1" correctly captures "as long as ... 3:1 or greater" — the sentence conditions the credit entirely on the 3:1 ratio with no alternate path, so below 3:1 it does not count; (b) "additional visual distinction" is verbatim; (c) the "(non-color)" parenthetical is a minor but defensible gloss matching SC 1.4.1's intent (the lightness cue is the additional means beyond hue that survives color-blindness/grayscale). Source is the authoritative WCAG Understanding document; text is unchanged across WCAG 2.0/2.1/2.2 and current. Sought contrary evidence: the APCA-vs-WCAG2 debate critiques the contrast *formula*'s perceptual accuracy, not whether WCAG states this 3:1 rule, so it does not refute the claim. No credible source disputes that this is WCAG's stated position.

#### V169 `a3b4e681` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "The paper proposes an empirical alternative to color-space-distance uniformity: a directly measured feature detection threshold function across the colormap, validated by crowdsourced (Amazon Mechanical Turk) experiments on nine colormaps at three feature sizes — i.e., discriminative power should be measured behaviorally, not assumed from CIELAB spacing."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.2312/eurorv3.20171107 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "presents a new method for evaluating the feature detection threshold function across a colormap, and uses crowdsourced studies for the direct evaluation of nine colormaps for three feature sizes"

*Evidence:* VERDICT: not refuted on substance; minor citation/number conflation flagged. The cited DOI 10.2312/eurorv3.20171107 is Ware, Turton, Samsel, Bujack & Rogers, "Evaluating the Perceptual Uniformity of Color Sequences for Feature Discrimination" (EuroRV3 2017). The claim's thesis is confirmed by primary sources: its peer-reviewed journal extension, "Measuring and Modeling the Feature Detection Threshold Functions of Colormaps" (IEEE TVCG 2018, PMID 30028708), explicitly "tested the hypothesis that a uniform color space (CIELAB) will accurately model colormapped feature detection thresholds" and REJECTED it, finding that reduced weights on the green-red/blue-yellow CIELAB terms model small-feature detection more accurately. That is exactly the claim's point: discriminative power measured behaviorally, not assumed from CIELAB spacing. The method is a directly-measured feature-detection threshold function across the colormap, validated via Amazon Mechanical Turk crowdsourcing. NUMERIC CAVEAT (the one contrary finding): "nine colormaps at three feature sizes" matches the 2018 TVCG JOURNAL paper, not the cited 2017 workshop short paper. Two independent search summaries of .20171107 consistently report it used AMT to evaluate SEVEN colormaps (with feature-discrimination curves for differently sized features), expanded to nine colormaps / three feature sizes only in the journal extension. So the claim attaches the journal version's exact counts to the workshop DOI. This is a citation-precision error, not a falsification: same authors, same method, same conclusion; source quality is strong (Eurographics workshop + IEEE TVCG), the work is current and still actively cited (e.g., 2025 ACM SAP "Reading Between the Colors"), not marketing, and no source contradicts the underlying behavioral-vs-CIELAB thesis. Note: ACM (403) and diglib.eg.org (connection refused) blocked verbatim-abstract retrieval of .20171107, so the 7-vs-9 count rests on search-engine summaries rather than the primary PDF — hence medium, not high, confidence on that one detail.
*Counter-source:* Search summaries of dl.acm.org/doi/10.2312/eurorv3.20171107 and researchgate.net/publication/323177929 consistently state the cited workshop paper used Amazon Mechanical Turk to evaluate SEVEN colormaps; the "nine colormaps / three feature sizes" figure belongs to the journal extension "Measuring and Modeling the Feature Detection Threshold Functions of Colormaps" (IEEE TVCG 2018, pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/30028708). Same finding, slightly different artifact.

**Source:** https://m3.material.io/styles/color/system/how-the-system-works (primary)

Contrast is guaranteed simply by picking colors whose tone values are far enough apart" — matching the claim's "contrast is driven by lightness/tone difference." Minor caveats that do not rise to refutation: (1) "maps directly" slightly overstates — the tone-difference rule is a guaranteed MINIMUM contrast (>=), not an exact one-to-one function, since WCAG luminance contrast = (Y1+0.05)/(Y2+0.05) depends on absolute luminance not only L* difference; M3's examples hold because both pairs share the tone-98 anchor. (2) The single supplied quote covers only the 50/98 half, so the 30/98->7:1 half needed independent confirmation, which succeeded. No credible source contradicts these as M3's documented token rules.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. The only technical nuance (HCT tone L* vs WCAG2 relative-luminance contrast not being a perfect bijection — tone difference guarantees a minimum, not exact, ratio) qualifies the word "directly" but does not refute the documented token examples, which the claim reproduces faithfully and correctly tags as [CONVENTION].

This is direct empirical [STRONG] evidence that low-luminance-contrast text is an anti-pattern regardless of hue difference."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0141938204000046 (primary)

The claim's interpretation is faithful: the study held colour contrast PRESENT and still found search time, fixation count, and fixation duration all rose strongly as luminance contrast fell — directly supporting "low-luminance-contrast text is an anti-pattern regardless of hue." #### V173 `a4bd9b27` — **upheld** · confidence: high

*Evidence:* Source verified: paper is "Evaluating the Perceptual Uniformity of Color Sequences for Feature Discrimination," EuroRV3 (EuroVis Workshop on Reproducibility/Verification/Validation in Visualization) 2017, DOI 10.2312/eurorv3.20171107 — matches cited URL. Supporting quote is verbatim accurate. The original is hedged ("there are reasons for thinking... may not be achieved"); the claim faithfully preserves that hedge ("does NOT necessarily produce," "may fail to equalize") rather than overstating it as proven. Paper gives the mechanism: "CIELAB space is only perceptually uniform for large patches of colour presented at very low spatial frequencies." Strong INDEPENDENT corroboration, not a lone source: (1) Stone & Szafir, "An Engineering Model for Color Difference as a Function of Size" (Color & Imaging Conf. 2014) + Szafir "Modeling Color Difference for Visualization Design" (IEEE VIS 2017) — perceived color difference depends on mark size, so a single uniform-space distance cannot equalize discriminability; (2) "Reading Between the Colors" (ACM SAP 2025) empirically confirmed CIELAB overweights chromatic vs luminance differences, and reducing green-red/blue-yellow weights improves small-feature detection. Later (2024-2025) work refines, not refutes — so not outdated. No credible source disputes the bound; the entire follow-up literature confirms it. Caveat (non-refuting): viridis used CAM02-UCS not CIELAB, but CAM02-UCS is also a uniform color space, so the "uniform color-space ≠ uniform feature detection" bound applies equally and the framing holds. Claim strength (hedged contrary/bounding evidence) matches source quality (peer-reviewed workshop + multiple corroborating peer-reviewed papers).
*Counter-source:* No refuting source found. Searched for disputes of CIELAB perceptual-uniformity-vs-feature-detection; all located sources (Kovesi CET maps, Stone & Szafir 2014/2017, ACM SAP 2025) CONFIRM rather than contradict the bound.

#### V175 `a4fe66b6` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow/jet colormap's perceptual non-uniformity actively creates false banding AND hides real data: changes appear fast in cyan/yellow (causing Mach bands) and slow in blue/green/red (creating wide color bands that mask underlying variation). This gives a concrete perceptual mechanism for why rainbow is an anti-pattern for continuous data."

**Source:** https://www.kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions, which can cause Mach bands in those regions. The colors appear to change more slowly in the blue, green, and red regions, which creates larger bands of color. These bands can hide important changes in the underlying data."

*Evidence:* QUOTE CONFIRMED VERBATIM: WebSearch returned the exact passage from Kenneth Moreland's "Diverging Color Maps for Scientific Visualization (Expanded)" (kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf), word-for-word matching the supporting quote: "The colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions, which can cause Mach bands in those regions. The colors appear to change more slowly in the blue, green, and red regions, which creates larger bands of color. These bands can hide important changes in the underlying data." The claim is a direct, faithful restatement of this — no overreach.

SOURCE QUALITY: Moreland is a recognized primary authority in scientific-visualization colormaps; his diverging-colormap paper is highly cited (the basis for the widely-used "coolwarm"/Moreland diverging map). Adequate for the claim's strength.

INDEPENDENTLY CORROBORATED: The mechanism (jet/rainbow perceptual non-uniformity → false bands at cyan/yellow that introduce artifacts, and compressed/indistinct greenish regions that hide variation) is repeated across independent expert sources — Kovesi 2015 "Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them" (arxiv 1509.03700), Borland & Taylor "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," the matplotlib viridis documentation, and Google's Turbo colormap writeup (developed specifically to fix "false detail, banding"). Not outdated, not marketing.

CONTRARY EVIDENCE EXISTS BUT DOES NOT REFUTE THE MECHANISM (important to surface): Bujack et al. 2022 "What's wrong with the rainbow? An interdisciplinary review of empirical evidence for and against the rainbow color scheme" (ScienceDirect S0924271622002659) and the IEEE 2023 paper "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" argue rainbow can outperform uniform maps on SOME tasks (detecting fine local features, quantitative point reads via distinct hues) and that the blanket "harmful" verdict is task-dependent. Crucially, these critiques dispute the NET UTILITY / "always an anti-pattern" framing — they do not dispute the underlying perceptual mechanism (the non-uniformity and banding are measurable physical/perceptual properties they accept). So the specific claim under review — the concrete mechanism (Mach bands cyan/yellow, wide masking bands elsewhere) — stands; only an over-broad "rainbow is always bad" extrapolation would be bounded by this literature.

VERDICT: Claim is well-supported, current, properly sourced to a primary expert. A reference using it should pair it with the Bujack/IEEE task-dependence caveat to stay honest, but the mechanistic claim itself is not refuted.
*Counter-source:* Bujack et al. 2022, "What's wrong with the rainbow? An interdisciplinary review..." (ScienceDirect S0924271622002659); IEEE VIS 2023 "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/10128890) — both bound the "always an anti-pattern" framing via task-dependence, but neither disputes the perceptual non-uniformity / banding mechanism itself.

#### V176 `a518097a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The mechanism is that discrete color bands / discontinuities in rainbow and cool-warm ramps -- which perceptual-uniformity advocates treat as undesirable artifacts -- actually AID gradient perception, because viewers use the apparent size and frequency of colored patches as proxies for gradient magnitude. So 'hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing.'"

**Source:** https://scholarworks.indianapolis.iu.edu/server/api/core/bitstreams/3a72f1a3-24d9-4635-a16f-de6b08accd9d/content (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "although discrete color bands are essentially artifacts [5], viewers may rely on such features as proxies for gradient. For instance, a viewer could estimate the apparent size of the blue and red patches in Figure 1, or how frequently they seem to occur in the image... the experiment suggests that hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing. Color bands may aid some tasks where the goal is to compare structural differences, including net gradients."

*Evidence:* Claim is faithful to a peer-reviewed primary source. The supporting quote is VERBATIM-confirmed from the PDF: Khairi Reda & Michael E. Papka, "Evaluating Gradient Perception in Color-Coded Scalar Fields," IEEE VIS 2019 (IEEE Xplore doc 8933760) — a controlled crowdsourced just-noticeable-difference (JND) experiment, not a blog/press release. The paper states (1) the MECHANISM in its methods: "we conjecture that viewers take advantage of the emergent, discrete color features (e.g., red and blue bands in rainbow or cool-warm), and estimate their apparent size and frequency as proxies for gradient... smaller-sized and more-frequent color patches can be indicative of larger net gradients"; and (2) the RESULT/conclusion verbatim: "discontinuities in color ramps may aid the perception of 2D gradients. this is because, although discrete color bands are essentially artifacts [5], viewers may rely on such features as proxies for gradient... overall, the experiment suggests that hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing. color bands may aid some tasks where the goal is to compare structural differences, including net gradients." Empirically, cool-warm and rainbow produced LOWER JNDs (better gradient discrimination) than perceptually-uniform viridis, even at high spatial frequency. Checklist: (1) quote fully supports claim, mechanism and conclusion both present; (2) no credible contradiction found — instead corroborated by follow-on peer-reviewed work (Nardini/Quadri/Reda "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" 2023; "Science in a Blink" 2024); (3) source quality (peer-reviewed empirical JND study at top vis venue) exceeds the strength of this appropriately-hedged claim; (4) 2019, still current and increasingly accepted; (5) not marketing. The claim is itself the kind of disconfirming-evidence-against-perceptual-uniformity-orthodoxy the research brief explicitly sought. Only nit: claim's headline "actually AID" leans slightly stronger than paper's "may aid some tasks," but the embedded "may not always be a bad thing" hedge keeps it faithful — not refutation-worthy. Should be tagged [STRONG] but task-bounded (net-gradient / structural-comparison tasks), with viridis still favored where reading absolute values or avoiding false banding matters.
*Counter-source:* No credible refuting source found; mainstream anti-rainbow guidance (Borland & Taylor 2007; Moreland) addresses different tasks (absolute-value reading, false banding) and does not contradict this task-specific net-gradient finding, which is corroborated by Nardini et al. "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE 2023).

#### V179 `a55ac867` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "[CONTRARY to "perceptually-uniform is always best"] In a controlled study of node-link NETWORK visualization, participants completed tasks SIGNIFICANTLY FASTER with a simple single-hue sequential blue colormap than with viridis (the perceptually-uniform multi-hue colormap), RdYlBu divergent, or jet rainbow — i.e., the perceptually-uniform colormap was NOT the fastest."

**Source:** https://www.mdpi.com/2076-3417/9/20/4228 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results show that participants complete their tasks significantly faster with blue (single-hue, sequential) as compared to viridis (multi-hue, sequential), RdYlBu (divergent, red-yellow-blue), and jet (rainbow) colormaps."

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED ON ITS LITERAL TERMS, but materially incomplete. Source: Yoghourdjian et al., "A Study of Colormaps in Network Visualization," MDPI Applied Sciences 9(20):4228 (2019) — a controlled experiment on node-link network diagrams. The abstract states verbatim: "participants complete their tasks significantly faster with blue (single-hue, sequential) as compared to viridis (multi-hue, sequential), RdYlBu (divergent, red-yellow-blue), and jet (rainbow) colormaps." So the SPEED claim and the inference "perceptually-uniform (viridis) was NOT the fastest" are accurate and directly quote-supported (verified across two independent web searches). CRITICAL OMITTED CAVEAT from the SAME paper: blue was the MOST error-prone colormap (lowest correctness rate) while viridis was the LEAST error-prone (highest correctness). The authors note blue's single hue made it "very difficult for participants to achieve a good level of accuracy" when value spans were small and nodes were close in value. This is a classic speed-accuracy tradeoff: blue bought speed by sacrificing accuracy, and viridis won on correctness (the metric the authors emphasize). Checklist: (1) claim supported by quote — yes, narrow speed claim is exact; (2) no source contradicts the speed result, the accuracy finding bounds rather than contradicts it; (3) MDPI Applied Sciences is a peer-reviewed primary controlled study though a mid-tier venue, and the claim is properly hedged as "a controlled study" — adequate for a single contrary data point, which is exactly what the research question solicited; (4) 2019, current; (5) not marketing. Verdict: not refuted because the literal speed assertion is well-supported by the primary source. WARNING for downstream use: the claim must be paired with the accuracy tradeoff — blue was MOST error-prone, viridis LEAST — or it becomes a misleading cherry-pick. It does NOT show perceptually-uniform colormaps are inferior overall; it shows a speed-vs-correctness tradeoff in one node-link network study.
*Counter-source:* Same paper's accuracy results (Yoghourdjian et al. 2019, MDPI Applied Sciences 9(20):4228): blue was the MOST error-prone / viridis the LEAST error-prone — a speed-accuracy tradeoff the claim omits; confirmed via two independent web searches summarizing the paper's abstract and conclusions. No external source disputes the speed finding itself.

#### V180 `a5da7a46` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The paper proposes an empirical alternative to color-space-distance uniformity: a directly measured feature detection threshold function across the colormap, validated by crowdsourced (Amazon Mechanical Turk) experiments on nine colormaps at three feature sizes — i.e., discriminative power should be measured behaviorally, not assumed from CIELAB spacing."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.2312/eurorv3.20171107 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "presents a new method for evaluating the feature detection threshold function across a colormap, and uses crowdsourced studies for the direct evaluation of nine colormaps for three feature sizes"

*Evidence:* All five components of the claim verify against the authoritative abstract of "Measuring and Modeling the Feature Detection Threshold Functions of Colormaps" (Ware, Turton, Bujack, Samsel, Shrivastava, Rogers; EuroRV3 2017 DOI 10.2312/eurorv3.20171107, journal version IEEE TVCG 2018/2019, PubMed 30028708). Abstract: "previously the main method for determining uniformity has been the application of uniform color spaces. In this paper we present a new method for evaluating the feature detection threshold function across a colormap. The method is used in crowdsourced studies for the direct evaluation of nine colormaps for three feature sizes. The results are used to test the hypothesis that a uniform color space (CIELAB) will accurately model colormapped feature detection thresholds... The results reject both hypotheses." This confirms: (1) empirical alternative to color-space-distance uniformity, (2) directly measured feature detection threshold function, (3) nine colormaps and three feature sizes (exact match), (4) the behavioral-vs-CIELAB framing — they empirically reject CIELAB uniformity as an accurate detection-threshold model. The Amazon Mechanical Turk detail, though absent from the supplied quote, is independently confirmed ("carried out using Mechanical Turk"), so it is a correct addition, not an overreach. Only nuance: the paper proposes a MODIFIED CIELAB (reduced chromaticity weights) rather than abandoning color-space distance entirely — but the claim targets standard CIELAB, which the paper does reject. Source is primary, peer-reviewed, high-authority (Colin Ware), and not time-sensitive. No contradicting evidence found.

#### V181 `a6234105` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Naive piecewise-linear interpolation in CIELAB space produces a visible Mach-band artifact at the white midpoint of a diverging colormap (where luminance abruptly switches from rising to falling); interpolating instead in Moreland's polar Msh color space smooths this by letting luminance level off while chromaticity changes faster. This is the core mechanism behind 'CIELAB-linear is not enough' for diverging maps."

**Source:** https://www.kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Using piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space causes Mach bands in the white part of diverging color maps (left image). The transition can be softened by interpolating in Msh space (right image)."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by its primary source (Moreland 2009, "Diverging Color Maps for Scientific Visualization," IS&T/SPIE VDA — the canonical reference). The supporting quote is a verbatim figure caption, confirmed via WebSearch returning the exact ResearchGate figure title (fig2_220844648): "Using piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space causes Mach bands in the white part of diverging color maps... softened by interpolating in Msh space." Part 1 (Mach band at white midpoint where luminance switches rising→falling) is directly supported. Part 2's mechanistic gloss — "letting luminance level off while chromaticity changes faster" — is NOT an overreach but a near-verbatim paraphrase of Moreland's own text: "a 'leveling off' of the luminance as the color map approaches white is required, and to compensate, the chromaticity must change more dramatically in this part of the color map." Msh is confirmed as a polar/spherical version of CIELAB; since L*=M·cos(s), linearly interpolating M and s rounds the luminance peak, removing the gradient discontinuity that triggers the Mach band — perceptually sound and matching established science. Part 3 ("core mechanism behind CIELAB-linear is not enough for diverging maps") is an accurate synthesis: this midpoint Mach band is precisely the problem motivating Msh in the canonical paper. Two targeted searches for disconfirming/critical sources found none — only Moreland's work and the Zhou & Hansen Survey of Colormaps (cites him positively). Source quality (peer-reviewed primary) matches claim strength; not outdated (foundational, uncontradicted).
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. WebSearch for critiques/disputes of the Mach-band/Msh claim returned only Moreland's own papers and the Zhou & Hansen "Survey of Colormaps in Visualization" (OSTI/PMC4959790), which cites the work approvingly rather than disputing it.

#### V182 `a6542527` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The rainbow/jet colormap's perceptual non-uniformity actively creates false banding AND hides real data: changes appear fast in cyan/yellow (causing Mach bands) and slow in blue/green/red (creating wide color bands that mask underlying variation). This gives a concrete perceptual mechanism for why rainbow is an anti-pattern for continuous data."

**Source:** https://www.kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions, which can cause Mach bands in those regions. The colors appear to change more slowly in the blue, green, and red regions, which creates larger bands of color. These bands can hide important changes in the underlying data."

*Evidence:* Claim is a near-verbatim, faithful paraphrase of Moreland's mechanism, with provenance confirmed independently. WebSearch surfaced Moreland's own wording: "Colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions than in the blue, green, and red regions, and these nonuniform perceptual changes simultaneously introduce artifacts and obfuscate real data," and "piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space causes Mach bands." The two-part mechanism (fast change in cyan/yellow -> Mach bands; slow change in blue/green/red -> wide bands hiding data) matches the supporting quote exactly — no overreach. Mechanism independently corroborated by Borland & Taylor, "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful," IEEE CG&A 27(2), 2007 (Mach bands/false features, false boundaries from non-monotonic luminance, data obscurement), plus HESS 25:4549 (2021) and MathWorks. The one counter-paper, Ware et al. "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE CG&A 2023), only qualifies the blanket "always bad" verdict — it argues rainbow helps for reading values off a key — while explicitly conceding "classic rainbows have uneven hue distribution and erratic luminance profiles"; it does NOT dispute the Mach-band/data-hiding mechanism the claim asserts. Source (Moreland, Sandia/Oak Ridge visualization researcher, canonical diverging-colormap author) matches claim strength; finding is current and settled. Minor caveat: "Mach bands" is strictly a luminance edge-enhancement effect, applied here to rapid perceptual change in jet — standard usage in the viz literature, not a misread.
*Counter-source:* Ware et al., "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications, 2023 (ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/10128890) — qualifies but does not refute the mechanism; concedes "erratic luminance profiles."

#### V184 `a6a9e99a` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Luminance contrast, not colour contrast, drives legibility: visual search time, number of eye fixations, and mean fixation durations all increase strongly as luminance contrast decreases, even when strong colour contrast is present. This is direct empirical [STRONG] evidence that low-luminance-contrast text is an anti-pattern regardless of hue difference."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0141938204000046 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Visual search times, number of eye fixations, and mean fixation durations increased strongly with decreasing luminance contrast despite the presence of colour contrast."

*Evidence:* Source confirmed real and primary: "Effects of luminance and colour contrast on the search of information on display devices," Displays (Elsevier, 2004), https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0141938204000046. The supporting quote is the near-verbatim abstract conclusion. The study held colour contrast HIGH (green/red, yellow/blue) while varying luminance contrast across three character sizes (0.17, 0.37, 1.26 deg) and found search time, fixation count, and mean fixation duration all rose strongly as luminance contrast fell (largest effect for small 0.17-deg text). The quote therefore supports the claim — it is not an overreach for the core point. Independent foundational corroboration: Knoblauch, Arditi et al. (1991), "Effects of chromatic and luminance contrast on reading," JOSA A 8(2):428 (https://opg.optica.org/josaa/abstract.cfm?uri=josaa-8-2-428) — with just 0.12 luminance contrast added, reading was unaffected by chromatic contrast across a 30-fold size range; the luminance channel has many times the spatial sensitivity of chromatic channels. This is also why WCAG and APCA define contrast by luminance, not hue. The [STRONG] tag is justified by a primary peer-reviewed study plus foundational vision-science corroboration. The science is stable, not fast-moving, so 2004/1991 dates are fine. One minor qualification (does NOT refute): Knoblauch & Arditi also showed chromatic contrast can sustain ~300 wpm reading when luminance contrast is near threshold, and equiluminant text stays readable via transverse chromatic aberration. So the literal phrase "regardless of hue difference" is mildly overstated — hue helps slightly near threshold — but the practical anti-pattern (low-luminance-contrast text reads poorly even with strong hue difference) is exactly what the source demonstrates. Claim is well-supported, current, and source quality matches strength.
*Counter-source:* Knoblauch, Arditi et al. (1991), JOSA A 8(2):428 — chromatic contrast CAN sustain reading (~300 wpm) when luminance contrast is near threshold, and equiluminant text is readable via chromatic aberration. This bounds the strict wording "regardless of hue difference" but reinforces, rather than refutes, the core finding that luminance contrast is the dominant driver of legibility.

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0003687014000696 (primary)

*Evidence:* SUPPORTED. The non-transfer is demonstrated by a concrete mechanism: optimal polarity FLIPS — LCD best = positive polarity (dark-on-light), CRT best = negative polarity (light-on-dark). The claim's second half ("guidance from print/CRT not valid for modern emissive sRGB panels") is the authors' own thesis — they propose a new LCD-specific table because the old ones don't apply. No disconfirming source found; the group's separate CRT study (Displays 2008) corroborates the distinct CRT ranking. Source quality (peer-reviewed Applied Ergonomics, n=308) matches a [STRONG] tag; 2014 date is fine since LCD is the modern panel and print/CRT are the legacy comparators. Minor scope caveat for the reference: read "screen-color guidance" as legibility-ranking guidance specifically, not all color guidance, and note luminance contrast is the cross-media constant.
*Counter-source:* No refuting source found. Bounding/qualifying evidence: the same paper reports luminance contrast (optimal ~18:1) drives legibility across print, CRT, and LCD — so the luminance-contrast principle transfers even though the specific color-combination rankings do not. The claim is narrowly and honestly scoped to SPEED ("faster," "NOT the fastest") and makes no "blue is better overall" assertion, so it survives verification — but any downstream use MUST carry the accuracy caveat, or it becomes a misleading half-truth. Source-quality note: MDPI Applied Sciences is peer-reviewed but not a top-tier venue, and n=35 is modest; adequate for a labelled [CONTRARY] data point, not for a strong universal claim. Not outdated (2019, colormap research), not marketing/cherry-picked.

This does not refute the speed claim but bounds it: viridis won on correctness, so "perceptually-uniform colormap was not the fastest" must not be generalized to "viridis is worse."

#### V191 `a757c30b` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Perceptual uniformity built from uniform color spaces (e.g., CIELAB) does NOT necessarily produce uniformity in terms of feature detection — the standard CIELAB-distance basis for 'perceptually uniform' colormaps may fail to equalize discriminability. This is direct contrary/bounding evidence against the 'uniform color-space => uniform perception' assumption underlying viridis-style construction."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.2312/eurorv3.20171107 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "there are reasons for thinking that uniformity in terms of feature detection may not be achieved by current methods which are based on the use of uniform color spaces"

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported, current, and independently corroborated. (1) The primary source's quote is accurate — "Evaluating the Perceptual Uniformity of Color Sequences for Feature Discrimination" (EuroRV3 2017, DOI 10.2312/eurorv3.20171107) states CIELAB is uniform only "for large patches of colour at very low spatial frequencies" and that "at fine spatial frequencies perceptual contrast is dominated by lightness difference; chroma and hue are relatively unimportant." This directly supports the claim; the claim is appropriately hedged ("does NOT necessarily," "may fail"). (2) Independent peer-reviewed confirmation: Bujack et al., "The Good, the Bad, and the Ugly," IEEE TVCG 2018 (top visualization venue) explicitly "tested the hypothesis that a uniform color space (CIELAB) would accurately model colormapped feature detection thresholds, but the results rejected this hypothesis," finding that down-weighting CIELAB's red-green/blue-yellow terms better models small-feature detection. (3) Research line is current and ongoing (feature-detection-threshold work 2018; "Reading Between the Colors," ACM SAP 2025). (4) No disputing source found despite targeted search; consensus runs one direction (Kovesi 2015 also notes CIELAB's fine-spatial-frequency failure). (5) Minor framing caveat only, not refuting: viridis was actually built in CAM02-UCS (not CIELAB) and already prioritizes constant lightness change — the channel the critique says dominates — so "CIELAB-distance basis underlying viridis" is loose on the specific space; but the claim hedges "e.g., CIELAB"/"uniform color spaces," and the broader "uniform color-space => uniform perception" assumption genuinely underlies viridis-style design and is exactly what the cited work bounds. Source quality (primary workshop paper + IEEE TVCG corroboration) matches the hedged bounding-claim strength.
*Counter-source:* No credible counter-source found. Bujack et al., IEEE TVCG 2018 ("The Good, the Bad, and the Ugly") reinforces rather than refutes. Only nuance: viridis uses CAM02-UCS not CIELAB and already prioritizes lightness, so the "CIELAB-distance basis underlying viridis" phrasing should be tightened in synthesis — but this does not refute the core finding.

#### V192 `a7f6ac75` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Diverging colormaps incur measurable error specifically when value comparisons straddle the central hue boundary: the blue-orange diverging scheme produced errors when comparisons were made across the central blue-orange transition — a concrete failure mode of diverging maps."

**Source:** https://idl.uw.edu/papers/quantitative-color (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "blueorange diverging scheme exhibits errors when comparison is made across the central blue-orange hue boundary"

*Evidence:* Claim verified against the primary source. The supporting quote is accurate verbatim: Liu & Heer, "Somewhere Over the Rainbow: An Empirical Assessment of Quantitative Colormaps" (ACM CHI 2018, UW Interactive Data Lab) reports in a figure caption "The blueorange diverging scheme exhibits errors when comparison is made across the central blue-orange hue boundary." Confirmed via two independent retrievals: the landing page https://idl.uw.edu/papers/quantitative-color and the PDF at https://idl.cs.washington.edu/files/2018-QuantitativeColor-CHI.pdf. The study is a controlled empirical experiment (relative-distance triplet judgments across single- and multi-hue colormaps) — a STRONG primary source whose quality matches the claim's strength. The claim's paraphrase ("produced errors when comparisons were made across the central blue-orange transition") faithfully matches the quote with no overreach. The mild generalization "a concrete failure mode of diverging maps" is independently corroborated, not contradicted: Moreland 2009 ("Diverging Color Maps for Scientific Visualization") shows the central lightness-gradient reversal yields near-isoluminant blues/reds that are hard to resolve plus false Mach-band features, and Kovesi 2015 ("Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them") documents the same central-transition weakness. No disconfirming source found; the surrounding literature reinforces the mechanism. Not outdated (2018, mechanism still standard), not marketing.

#### V194 `a8fe098d` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Naive piecewise-linear interpolation in CIELAB space produces a visible Mach-band artifact at the white midpoint of a diverging colormap (where luminance abruptly switches from rising to falling); interpolating instead in Moreland's polar Msh color space smooths this by letting luminance level off while chromaticity changes faster. This is the core mechanism behind 'CIELAB-linear is not enough' for diverging maps."

**Source:** https://www.kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Using piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space causes Mach bands in the white part of diverging color maps (left image). The transition can be softened by interpolating in Msh space (right image)."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED by the primary source (Moreland, "Diverging Color Maps for Scientific Visualization," IEEE ISVC 2009; expanded version at kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf). Every part of the claim is directly supported, including the specific mechanism that I tested as a possible overreach:

(1) Mach band at white midpoint — SUPPORTED verbatim: "Using piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space causes Mach bands in the white part of diverging color maps." Moreland's text adds: "a piecewise linear interpolation... can create an artificial Mach band at white where the luminance sharply transitions from increasing to decreasing" — confirming the claim's "luminance abruptly switches from rising to falling."

(2) Msh smooths it — SUPPORTED verbatim: "The transition can be softened by interpolating in Msh space" (Msh = spherical/polar transform of CIELab).

(3) The claimed mechanism ("luminance level off while chromaticity changes faster") is NOT just the claim author's inference — it is EXPLICITLY stated by Moreland: "to use the divergent color map in general situations a 'leveling off' of the luminance is required as the color map approaches white. To compensate, the chromaticity must change more dramatically in this part of the color map." This matches the claim's mechanism almost word-for-word.

Mechanistically sound and self-consistent: in Msh, L* = M·cos(s); since cos(s) is flat (zero slope) at s=0 (white), linearly interpolating through s=0 rounds/flattens the lightness peak that a direct Lab tent-interpolation would produce, while chroma (≈ M·sin(s) ≈ M·s near white) keeps changing — exactly "luminance levels off, chromaticity changes faster."

Source quality: peer-reviewed primary source authored by the originator of the method (Moreland), matching the claim's strength. No contradicting or qualifying evidence surfaced in two targeted searches for criticism/overstatement. The only nuance Moreland adds (non-refuting): the sharp transition "is fine, perhaps even desirable, when the white value has special significance" — i.e., the Mach band is sometimes wanted, but this bounds rather than refutes the claim. The framing "core mechanism behind CIELAB-linear is not enough for diverging maps" is the author's characterization but accurately reflects that the midpoint Mach band is the central problem Moreland's method solves.
*Counter-source:* No credible contradicting source found. Searches for critiques of Moreland/Mach-band/Msh returned only Moreland's own work and downstream confirmations; no source disputes the mechanism. Only nuance (non-refuting): Moreland himself notes the sharp luminance transition can be desirable when the white midpoint carries special meaning.
