#### V196 `a9780fa1` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The mechanism is that discrete color bands / discontinuities in rainbow and cool-warm ramps -- which perceptual-uniformity advocates treat as undesirable artifacts -- actually AID gradient perception, because viewers use the apparent size and frequency of colored patches as proxies for gradient magnitude. So 'hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing.'"

**Source:** https://scholarworks.indianapolis.iu.edu/server/api/core/bitstreams/3a72f1a3-24d9-4635-a16f-de6b08accd9d/content (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "although discrete color bands are essentially artifacts [5], viewers may rely on such features as proxies for gradient. For instance, a viewer could estimate the apparent size of the blue and red patches in Figure 1, or how frequently they seem to occur in the image... the experiment suggests that hue-based discretization may not always be a bad thing. Color bands may aid some tasks where the goal is to compare structural differences, including net gradients."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported and faithfully paraphrases its source. Source verified as Reda & Papka, "Evaluating Gradient Perception in Color-Coded Scalar Fields," peer-reviewed IEEE VIS'19 short paper (also at khreda.com/papers/VIS19-gradient-percept.pdf and IEEE Xplore doc 8933760). The supporting quote is genuine. CRUCIALLY, the paper's experiment provides empirical backing, not just speculation: in a crowdsourced JND study where participants judged which of two scalar fields had "steeper terrain on average," participants showed LOWER JNDs (better gradient discrimination) with diverging/cool-warm and spectral/rainbow schemes than with viridis (monotonic luminance). The authors conclude "apparent discontinuities in the color ramp may help viewers discern subtle structural differences in gradient." This directly supports "bands aid gradient perception." (1) Quote-support: faithful, including the hedge "may not always be a bad thing." (2) Contradicting evidence sought: the large anti-rainbow literature (Borland & Taylor 2007; HESS 2021; medvis.org) targets OTHER tasks (false contours, reading absolute values) and does not contradict this narrow finding; the claim is INDEPENDENTLY CORROBORATED by Ware, Stone & Szafir, "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE CG&A 43(3):88-93, 2023, which found real costs-and-benefits and that rainbows "should not be dismissed." (3) Source quality sufficient: peer-reviewed IEEE primary source. (4) Current: 2019 finding with 2023 confirmation. (5) Not marketing. Minor quibble: the size/frequency-as-proxy MECHANISM is the authors' hypothesis ("viewers MAY rely"), stated slightly more firmly in the claim ("viewers use"), but the claim retains the proper overall hedge. Verdict: not refuted.
*Counter-source:* Anti-rainbow literature (Borland & Taylor 2007 "Rainbow Color Map Still Considered Harmful"; Stoelzle & Stein HESS 2021) exists but targets different tasks and does NOT refute this scoped finding; the "not always bad" position is independently corroborated by Ware, Stone & Szafir, IEEE CG&A 2023.

#### V197 `a97a1159` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "High colour contrast does NOT guarantee fast/legible perception when luminance contrast is small (the equiluminant case), because human visual acuity is far lower for pure-chromatic information than for luminance information. This [STRONG] finding refutes the common [MYTH] that strong hue contrast alone is enough for readable UI text."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0141938204000046 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Moderate or even high colour contrast does not guarantee quick visual perception, if the luminance contrast between characters and background is small. This is probably due to the fact that visual acuity (the ability to see small details) is considerably lower for pure colour information than for luminance information."

*Evidence:* The claim is well-supported and matches the quote without overreach. The mechanism (chromatic acuity << luminance acuity) is established primary vision science, independent of the single applied source cited:

[STRONG primary] Mullen 1985, Journal of Physiology 359:381-400 ("Contrast sensitivity of human colour vision to red-green and blue-yellow chromatic gratings", PMC1193381): chromatic (R-G and B-Y) contrast-sensitivity functions are low-pass with limiting acuity of ~11-12 cycles/deg, versus the luminance channel which extends to ~50-60 cpd. Above 0.5 cpd, luminance contrast sensitivity exceeds chromatic. This is the exact physiological basis for "acuity is far lower for pure chromatic than luminance information," and ~11-12 vs ~50-60 cpd justifies "far/considerably lower."

[CONVENTION/authoritative applied] NASA Color Usage Guidelines (colorusage.arc.nasa.gov): "Luminance contrast is the most important determinant of legibility of symbols and text... chromaticity contrast cannot be substituted for luminance contrast... red-green and yellow-blue spatial sensitivities fall off much faster than luminance... a minimum luminance contrast of 3.0 should always be present." Directly affirms the MYTH framing — hue contrast alone is insufficient for text.

[Supporting] Pinkelink et al. 1996, J. SID ("Chromaticity contrast, luminance contrast, and legibility of text"); Legge et al. "Effects of chromatic and luminance contrast on reading" (PubMed 2007918). Both converge on luminance contrast as the primary legibility driver.

Quote-vs-claim: the supporting quote ("Moderate or even high colour contrast does not guarantee quick visual perception if the luminance contrast... is small... visual acuity is considerably lower for pure colour information than for luminance information") maps one-to-one onto the claim; the MYTH characterization is a fair restatement, not an embellishment.

Source-strength check: the cited ScienceDirect article (S0141938204000046, ~2004 applied displays study) is secondary/applied, but the [STRONG] tag is warranted because the finding is anchored in primary physiology (Mullen 1985) and an authoritative applied standard (NASA). I could not fetch the ScienceDirect page (HTTP 403), but its conclusion is redundant with the primary literature, so access is not load-bearing.

Bounding nuance (not a refutation, and consistent with the claim's scope): the deficit applies to FINE detail / small symbols / text. At low spatial frequencies (large color fields) chromatic contrast is detectable and useful for area coding — but the claim is explicitly scoped to readable UI text, where the finding holds firmly. No credible source disputes the core result; WCAG's luminance-only contrast model exists precisely because of it.
*Counter-source:* No credible disconfirming source found. The only qualifier is scope (chromatic contrast is adequate for large-area coding, not fine text) — which agrees with, rather than refutes, the text-scoped claim. Mullen 1985 and NASA Color Usage Guidelines both corroborate.

#### V200 `abad3aa9` — **upheld** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "On LCD (emissive) displays, the most legible text/background pairings are high-contrast combinations with POSITIVE polarity (dark text on a light background), whereas on CRTs the most legible were NEGATIVE-polarity combinations (light text on dark) — so optimal polarity is display-technology-dependent, and this is direct empirical pushback on any universal 'dark mode reads better' claim for self-lit screens."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0003687014000696 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "A new legibility table is proposed for LCDs where the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with positive polarity whereas for CRT displays, the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with negative polarity."

*Evidence:* Source VERIFIED as a peer-reviewed primary study: Humar, Gradisar, Turk & Erjavec (2014), "The impact of color combinations on the legibility of text presented on LCDs," Applied Ergonomics 45(6):1510-1517 (PubMed 24874503; matches PII S0003687014000696). 308 participants, 56 color combinations. The supporting quote is reproduced VERBATIM by independent indexing ("...highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with positive polarity [for LCDs] whereas for CRT displays, the highest ranked are contrastive color combinations with negative polarity"). The claim's polarity definitions are correct (positive = dark text on light background), confirmed by the source's own gloss, and the "display-dependent" inference is the study's own stated conclusion — so the claim is a faithful report, not an overreach.

Core message is CORROBORATED by independent literature, not contradicted: Piepenbrock, Mayr & Buchner (2014, PubMed 25135324) — faster reading + more errors caught under positive polarity; Buchner & Baumgartner (2007, Ergonomics) — positive-polarity advantage holds irrespective of ambient illumination and color contrast. This squarely supports the "empirical pushback on universal 'dark mode reads better'" framing.

ONE contested element (bounds, does not refute): Buchner, Mayr & Brandt (2009) "The advantage of positive text-background polarity is due to high display luminance" found the advantage disappears when overall luminance is equated — i.e., the effect is a luminance/pupil-constriction mechanism that holds ACROSS display technologies, which is in tension with the strong reading of "optimal polarity is display-technology-DEPENDENT" and with the CRT-negative sub-finding (classic CRT polarity studies often found positive polarity superior too). But this contrary literature concerns monochrome luminance polarity, whereas Humar tested COLOR combinations on CRT phosphors specifically, and it disputes only the mechanism/CRT half — not the LCD-positive core or the dark-mode pushback. Source quality (Elsevier peer-reviewed, n=308, primary) matches the claim strength; not marketing, not outdated for a historical/comparative claim.
*Counter-source:* Buchner, Mayr & Brandt (2009), "The advantage of positive text-background polarity is due to high display luminance" (https://www.researchgate.net/publication/26326659); Buchner & Baumgartner (2007), "Text-background polarity affects performance irrespective of ambient illumination and colour contrast," Ergonomics (https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/00140130701306413) — both attribute the positive-polarity advantage to luminance and find it technology-independent, qualifying the "display-technology-dependent" framing.

#### V202 `abd7965c` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "A lightness difference between two hues used to convey content counts as an additional (non-color) visual distinction only when the relative-luminance contrast ratio between the colors is 3:1 or greater."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "If content is conveyed through the use of colors that differ not only in their hue, but that also have a significant difference in lightness, then this counts as an additional visual distinction, as long as the difference in relative luminance between the colors leads to a contrast ratio of 3:1 or greater."

*Evidence:* Verbatim-confirmed from the primary source. WebFetch of https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html returned the exact quoted sentence in the Intent section: "If content is conveyed through the use of colors that differ not only in their hue, but that also have a significant difference in lightness, then this counts as an additional visual distinction, as long as the difference in relative luminance between the colors leads to a contrast ratio of 3:1 or greater." The claim is a faithful paraphrase: "only when ... 3:1 or greater" maps to the quote's "as long as ... 3:1 or greater" (WCAG uses 3:1 as the threshold in both directions — below it the lightness cue does not count, at/above it it does). The parenthetical "(non-color)" is an accurate gloss of the SC's stated intent: luminance contrast is a distinction perceivable independent of hue, which is precisely why it aids color-vision-deficient users. WCAG-2 contrast ratio is defined via relative luminance (L1+0.05)/(L2+0.05), so "relative-luminance contrast ratio" is correct. No contradicting evidence found; WebAIM corroborates. Source is primary, normative W3C, sufficient for this definitional claim; text is current (unchanged in WCAG 2.2). The APCA-vs-WCAG2 dispute targets the contrast formula's perceptual accuracy, not this specific 3:1 additional-distinction rule, so it does not refute the claim. Only nitpick — "as long as" vs "only when" — does not alter meaning.

#### V203 `abd82b83` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Positive polarity displays (dark text on a light background) produce better proofreading performance than negative polarity displays (light text on a dark background) — direct, controlled disconfirming evidence against the claim that dark mode improves legibility for normal-vision young adults."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25135324/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Smaller pupil size and better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays ... Performance was better with positive than with negative polarity displays."

*Evidence:* Claim verified, not refuted. (1) The supporting quote directly states the claim: Piepenbrock, Mayr & Buchner (2014), Ergonomics 57(11):1670-1677, "Smaller pupil size and better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays" (PMID 25135324) — a primary, peer-reviewed, controlled study. (2) Terminology mapping is correct: positive polarity = dark-on-light = light mode; negative = light-on-dark = dark mode, so framing it as disconfirming evidence against "dark mode improves legibility" is accurate, not an overreach. The display-luminance mechanism (positive polarity → smaller pupil → sharper retinal image) is identified and pupil size predicted performance. (3) Replicated, not a one-off: same group's Piepenbrock et al. 2013 Ergonomics 56(7) "Positive display polarity is advantageous for both younger and older adults" (PMID 23654206) and 2014 Human Factors "Positive Display Polarity Is Particularly Advantageous for Small Character Sizes" both find the positive-polarity advantage for normal-vision participants. (4) Current consensus: Nielsen Norman Group's dark-mode article states "light mode was better for both visual-acuity tasks and for proofreading tasks across all dimensions, regardless of age" — not outdated. (5) Population fits: studies used participants without visual disorders; restricting to normal-vision young adults is conservative and is where the effect is strongest. Known dark-mode benefits (low-light, photophobia, cataract) are real boundary conditions but do not contradict the normal-vision-young-adult proofreading finding. Source quality matches the strength of this [STRONG] disconfirming claim.
*Counter-source:* Nielsen Norman Group (nngroup.com/articles/dark-mode/) and companion studies (PMID 23654206; Human Factors 2014, doi 10.1177/0018720813515509) corroborate rather than refute; no credible source found showing dark/negative polarity superior for legibility in normal-vision young adults.

#### V205 `ac051210` — **REFUTED** · confidence: medium
*Claim under review:* "A blue-text/red-background (blue–red) combination induced a heightened accommodative response — i.e. it forced greater focusing effort than other combinations — which is direct physiological evidence that saturated blue+red pairings are an on-screen anti-pattern."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0042698919302123 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "the blue-red combination induced a heightened accommodative response"

*Evidence:* OVERREACH/MISREAD. The source (Vision Research 2019, "Short-term effects of text-background color combinations on the dynamics of the accommodative response," n=20, LCD reading) is legitimate, primary, on-screen, and the narrow quote "blue-red combination induced a heightened accommodative response" is accurate. But the claim inflates it three ways: (1) The paper reports a NEUTRAL physiological measurement (accommodation magnitude) and never uses "effort," "strain," "fatigue," or "anti-pattern"; recasting it as "forced greater focusing effort" conflates accommodation magnitude with effort/fatigue. (4) "Heightened response = bad" is not the paper's stance — positive polarities (dark-on-light) showed MORE accommodative variability. The real anti-pattern case for saturated red/blue rests on chromostereopsis and simultaneous-contrast vibration (different mechanisms), not this accommodation finding. The literal fact is true; the strong "direct physiological evidence of an on-screen anti-pattern" characterization is unsupported by the quote and partly contradicted by the paper itself.
*Counter-source:* The paper's own abstract (sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0042698919302123): "The manipulation of text-background color did not have a significant effect on reading speed" and "These results may have important applications" — a neutral physiological measurement with no reading-speed cost, no eye-strain/fatigue measurement, and 2-minute short-term scope; never characterized as an anti-pattern.

#### V207 `ac638003` — **upheld** · confidence: high

The document's separate caveat (additional indicator needed "regardless of contrast ratio" when accurate color perception is required) is a distinct condition that does not refute the claim.

#### V208 `acabfae3` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Material Design 3's color system is built on the HCT color space (hue, chroma, tone), whose defining property is that tone (= luminance) can be held fixed while hue and chroma change — something RGB and HSL cannot do. This is the mechanism that lets a token system guarantee accessible contrast independent of hue. [CONVENTION / best-practice, with a perceptual-science basis]"

**Source:** https://m3.material.io/styles/color/system/how-the-system-works (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Unlike other color spaces (like HSL or RGB), HCT allows the manipulation of a color's hue and chroma without affecting its tone."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported, current, and primary-sourced; tag [CONVENTION/best-practice w/ science basis] matches its strength. (1) M3 is built on HCT (H,C from CAM16; T from CIELAB) — confirmed by Google's material-color-utilities and independent sources (ColorAide, amoebelabs). (2) The hold-tone-fixed-vary-hue/chroma property is stated verbatim in the cited primary M3 page. (3) 'RGB/HSL cannot do this' is faithful to M3's documented rationale ('no color system could adjust lightness while maintaining colorfulness and hue'); HSL's L channel is non-perceptual, so holding it fixed does NOT hold perceived lightness fixed. (4) The contrast mechanism is genuinely hue-independent and is M3's own stated guarantee: material-color-utilities states tone diff 40 guarantees WCAG contrast >=3.0 and diff 50 guarantees >=4.5. This holds for ALL hues because tone is a strict 1:1 monotonic function of relative luminance Y, and WCAG contrast is luminance-only — equal tone => equal luminance => equal contrast regardless of hue (robust even to the APCA/WCAG2 debate since APCA is also luminance-driven). ONE imprecision, not fatal: the parenthetical 'tone (= luminance)' is technically wrong — ColorAide/material-color-utilities confirm tone = CIELAB L* (perceptual lightness), a NONLINEAR function of luminance, not luminance itself. But since L* maps one-to-one to luminance, fixing tone fixes luminance, so the described mechanism survives. Recommend the reference say 'tone (= CIELAB L*, a perceptual function of luminance)' instead of '= luminance'. This is a wording correction, not a refutation.
*Counter-source:* https://facelessuser.github.io/coloraide/colors/hct/ and https://github.com/material-foundation/material-color-utilities confirm tone = CIELAB L* (perceptual lightness), not relative luminance — the only inaccuracy, a minor parenthetical gloss that does not break the claim. material-color-utilities also corroborates the contrast-guarantee thresholds (tone diff 40 -> >=3.0, 50 -> >=4.5).

#### V210 `ada07b4d` — **upheld** · confidence: high

This gives a concrete perceptual mechanism for why rainbow is an anti-pattern for continuous data."

**Source:** https://www.kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions, which can cause Mach bands in those regions. The colors appear to change more slowly in the blue, green, and red regions, which creates larger bands of color. These bands can hide important changes in the underlying data."

*Evidence:* The supporting quote is verbatim in the primary source. I downloaded ColorMapsExpanded.pdf and extracted: "The colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions, which can cause Mach bands in those regions. The colors appear to change more slowly in the blue, green, and red regions, which creates larger bands of color. These bands can hide important changes in the underlying data. Thus, nonuniform perceptual changes simultaneously introduce artifacts and obfuscate real data [1]." The claim restates this with no overreach: fast-change cyan/yellow -> Mach bands (false banding), slow-change blue/green/red -> wide bands that mask variation. Moreland (recognized colormap authority; 2009 diverging-colormaps paper) cites Borland & Taylor 2007 "Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful" for the point, and the mechanism is independently corroborated (Kovesi 2015; viridis becoming matplotlib's 2015 default because of these issues). The main disconfirming source, Bujack et al. 2023 "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE CG&A), does NOT refute the mechanism — it acknowledges the non-uniformity and only bounds the practical verdict as task-dependent (rainbows can help compare distributions / discriminate fine detail). Caveat for synthesis: Moreland attributes the bands to perceived color-change rate, while some sources pin the false bands specifically on jet's non-monotonic luminance profile (peaks at cyan/yellow) — compatible framings, not a contradiction; and "anti-pattern for continuous data" is the strong default convention, not an absolute (per Bujack 2023). Source: https://www.kennethmoreland.com/color-maps/ColorMapsExpanded.pdf
*Counter-source:* Bujack et al. 2023, "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE Computer Graphics and Applications (https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/10128890) — qualifies the practical recommendation as task-dependent but does not refute the perceptual mechanism (non-uniformity -> Mach bands + masking) described in the claim.

The study measured relative-distance judgments among color triplets; the error arises because the diverging map's achromatic mid-point is perceived as closer than a saturated same-hue color on the opposite side. The supporting quote matches the paper word-for-word, the source is a peer-reviewed top-tier (CHI 2018) primary study, the finding is current/canonical, and it is not marketing. #### V213 `ae18ea69` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Positive polarity displays (dark text on a light background) produce better proofreading performance than negative polarity displays (light text on a dark background) — direct, controlled disconfirming evidence against the claim that dark mode improves legibility for normal-vision young adults."

**Source:** https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25135324/ (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Smaller pupil size and better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays ... Performance was better with positive than with negative polarity displays."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED — claim stands. The supporting quote is the LITERAL TITLE of the primary source: Piepenbrock, Mayr & Buchner, "Smaller pupil size and better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays," Ergonomics 2014;57(11):1670-7 (PMID 25135324). Conclusion: "pupil sizes were smaller and proofreading performance was better with positive than with negative polarity displays... compatible with the hypothesis that the positive polarity advantage is an effect of display luminance." Positive polarity = dark text on light background = light mode, so this IS direct, controlled disconfirming evidence against "dark mode improves legibility." No quote overreach.

CORROBORATION (not contradiction) from independent sources: (a) companion study Piepenbrock, Mayr & Buchner 2013 Ergonomics (PMID 23654206), powered design with 84 younger + 85 older participants, found positive polarity advantageous across visual-acuity and proofreading for normal-vision adults; (b) Dobres et al. 2017 (MIT) found light mode better at night, no daytime disadvantage; (c) lineage Buchner & Baumgartner 2007, Bauer & Cavonius 1980; (d) Nielsen Norman Group (nngroup.com/articles/dark-mode) — an authoritative source named in the research question — explicitly concludes "in users with normal vision, light mode leads to better performance most of the time." The finding is replicated, not a one-off.

GENUINE NUANCES THAT BOUND BUT DO NOT REFUTE: advantage shrinks under bright ambient light and grows with smaller fonts (Piepenbrock 2014 Human Factors); some reframe it as a "negative-polarity disadvantage"; a 2024 arXiv study (2409.10841) found individual variation; cataract/low-vision users benefit from dark mode (opposite result); users often subjectively prefer dark mode. None contradict the claim, which is correctly scoped to proofreading performance for NORMAL-VISION adults — the exact condition where the effect holds. The claim does not overclaim universality.

SOURCE QUALITY: peer-reviewed controlled experiment with pupillometry in a respected human-factors journal; physiological mechanism (brighter display -> smaller pupil -> sharper retina) holds on emissive sRGB displays; re-confirmed post-2014. Not marketing/cherry-picked. Only unverified detail: exact participant sample of PMID 25135324 (ResearchGate 403), but the corpus + NN/g synthesis fully support the scope. Tag: [STRONG] for the disconfirming finding; the broader "light mode always better" would be [CONVENTION] given context-dependence.
*Counter-source:* Contrary/bounding evidence exists but does not refute: arXiv 2409.10841 (2024) found individual variation across age groups (both polarities benefited comparable proportions); NN/g notes cataract/low-vision users perform better in dark mode and that users subjectively prefer dark mode despite worse objective performance; positive-polarity advantage is ambient-light and font-size dependent (near zero under bright light). These qualify universality, not the scoped proofreading/normal-vision claim.

#### V215 `ae61a780` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Perceptual uniformity built from uniform color spaces (e.g., CIELAB) does NOT necessarily produce uniformity in terms of feature detection — the standard CIELAB-distance basis for 'perceptually uniform' colormaps may fail to equalize discriminability. This is direct contrary/bounding evidence against the 'uniform color-space => uniform perception' assumption underlying viridis-style construction."

**Source:** https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.2312/eurorv3.20171107 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "there are reasons for thinking that uniformity in terms of feature detection may not be achieved by current methods which are based on the use of uniform color spaces"

*Evidence:* VERIFIED. Source correctly identified as "Evaluating the Perceptual Uniformity of Color Sequences for Feature Discrimination" by Colin Ware & Terece Turton, EuroRV3 (Eurographics Workshop on Reproducibility/Verification/Validation in Visualization) 2017, DOI 10.2312/eurorv3.20171107. The supporting quote was returned verbatim by web search and is genuine: "the practical value of uniformity is for features in the data to be equally distinctive no matter where they lie in the colormap, but there are reasons for thinking that uniformity in terms of feature detection may not be achieved by current methods which are based on the use of uniform color spaces." The claim faithfully restates this without overreach. The claim is STRENGTHENED, not refuted, by the authors' follow-on peer-reviewed journal paper "Measuring and Modeling the Feature Detection Threshold Functions of Colormaps" (Ware, Turton et al., IEEE TVCG / OSTI 1463498, 2018), which empirically TESTED and REJECTED the hypothesis that CIELAB (a uniform color space) accurately models colormapped feature-detection thresholds, finding that REDUCED weights on the green-red and blue-yellow CIELAB chromatic terms produce a more accurate model for detecting small features. They evaluated 9 colormaps including viridis across 3 feature sizes via crowdsourced studies. Mechanism is sound: CIELAB is calibrated on large color patches, but chromatic channels have lower spatial acuity than luminance, so CIELAB over-weights chroma when features are small — exactly why feature-detection uniformity diverges from color-space uniformity. No credible contradicting source found; line of work continues (e.g., SAP 2025 "Reading Between the Colors"). Lead author Colin Ware is a recognized authority (Information Visualization: Perception for Design). Source strength (peer-reviewed workshop + top-venue TVCG journal) matches the claim's bounding/contrary-evidence strength. Current (2017/2018, perception findings stable). Not marketing.
*Counter-source:* No disconfirming source found. On the contrary, the authors' own follow-on IEEE TVCG paper "Measuring and Modeling the Feature Detection Threshold Functions of Colormaps" (Ware, Turton et al., 2018; OSTI 1463498) empirically corroborates the claim by rejecting the CIELAB-uniformity-equals-feature-detection-uniformity hypothesis.

#### V216 `ae874e53` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Material Design 3's color system is built on the HCT color space (hue, chroma, tone), whose defining property is that tone (= luminance) can be held fixed while hue and chroma change — something RGB and HSL cannot do. This is the mechanism that lets a token system guarantee accessible contrast independent of hue. [CONVENTION / best-practice, with a perceptual-science basis]"

**Source:** https://m3.material.io/styles/color/system/how-the-system-works (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Unlike other color spaces (like HSL or RGB), HCT allows the manipulation of a color's hue and chroma without affecting its tone."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by primary source and corroborating sources. (1) The supporting quote directly states HCT manipulates hue+chroma without affecting tone "unlike HSL or RGB" — the claim mirrors it faithfully, not an overreach. (2) HCT tone = CIELAB L* confirmed: "HCT's lightness measure, tone, is the same as L*a*b*'s lightness; built using CAM16 for Hue and Chroma, and Lightness (L*) from CIE-Lab" (material3-themes-manual.amoebelabs.com; m3.material.io/blog/science-of-color-design). (3) The contrast-guarantee mechanism is documented MD3 behavior, not inference: "A difference of 40 in tone guarantees a WCAG contrast ratio ≥ 3.0; a difference of 50 guarantees ≥ 4.5" — MD3 deliberately converts WCAG ratios into hue-independent tone differences. (4) The only imprecision: "tone (= luminance)" — strictly tone is L* (perceptual lightness), and one source notes contrast in L* is linear "unlike the non-linear contrast ratio method based on luminance." BUT L* is a strictly monotonic bijection of relative luminance Y (L* depends only on Y), so fixing tone fixes Y, which fixes WCAG contrast — the claim's conclusion survives the loose terminology, and Google's own materials use the same shorthand ("Tone is sometimes also referred to as luminance"). (5) Searched specifically for critiques that HCT does NOT guarantee hue-independent contrast — found none refuting the core mechanism; only bounding nuances (the 40-tone→3:1 mapping is approximate/depends on absolute tone values; achievable chroma at fixed tone varies by hue due to sRGB gamut). Source quality (primary m3.material.io) matches the [CONVENTION/best-practice] strength tag. Recommend the reference fix the parenthetical to "tone (= perceptual lightness L*, a monotonic function of luminance)."
*Counter-source:* https://facelessuser.github.io/coloraide/contrast/ (notes L* contrast is linear, distinct from non-linear luminance-based WCAG ratio — supports that tone is L*, not luminance proper, but does not refute the hue-independent-contrast conclusion)

#### V217 `aeefed27` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The conclusions rest on a large empirical survey base: over 37,000 figures across 11,808 papers were systematically analyzed in six disciplines (information visualization, neuroscience, hydrology, geography, remote sensing, planetary science), making this a quantitative evidence corpus on rainbow prevalence rather than opinion."

*Evidence:* Claim is accurate and well-supported. The source is Geng et al. (2022), "What's wrong with the rainbow? Its abstract states the rainbow-prevalence analysis was "based on over 37,000 figures in 11,808 papers in information visualization, neuroscience, hydrology, geography, remote sensing and planetary science." WebSearch independently reproduced the identical numbers and the exact six disciplines, confirming the supporting quote. The numbers, discipline list, and prevalence scope all match. Source quality is high (peer-reviewed Elsevier/ISPRS journal; numbers from the abstract, not marketing). Not outdated (2022, appropriate for a prevalence corpus). One nuance, non-refuting: the paper is a review whose good/bad effectiveness conclusions rest on synthesizing OTHER studies, while the 37,000-figure corpus underpins specifically the prevalence/temporal-trend findings — but the claim correctly scopes itself to "rainbow prevalence," so it does not overreach. #### V218 `aef28423` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "WCAG 1.4.1 (Level A) normatively requires that color not be the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element — i.e. color-coding alone is non-conformant and must be paired with text, shape, icons, or patterns."

**Source:** https://www.w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Color is not used as the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED against primary source (w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html). The normative SC 1.4.1 text matches the supporting quote verbatim: "Color is not used as the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element." Conformance level confirmed = Level A (unchanged across WCAG 2.0/2.1/2.2, so current, not outdated). The success criteria are normative, so "normatively requires" is accurate (the Understanding doc cited is informative but faithfully reproduces the normative SC). The interpretation "color-coding alone is non-conformant" is correct: if color is the ONLY visual means it fails. ONE MINOR REFINEMENT (not a refutation): the claim's enumeration "must be paired with text, shape, icons, or patterns" is illustrative (uses "or") but under-specifies the rule — per the normative understanding, an additional LIGHTNESS/luminance difference giving a contrast ratio >=3:1 ALSO satisfies the criterion (colors differing in both hue and lightness count as an additional visual distinction), and there is a visited-link carve-out. These refine, not contradict, the claim. No credible source disputes the Level A status or substance; foundational, uncontested accessibility doctrine; primary W3C source matches the claim's strength.
*Counter-source:* No credible refuting source found. Minor qualifying nuance (3:1 luminance difference also satisfies SC 1.4.1; visited-link exception) sourced from the same W3C Understanding page, which refines rather than refutes the claim.

#### V219 `af1a9254` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "[CONTRARY to "perceptually-uniform is always best"] In a controlled study of node-link NETWORK visualization, participants completed tasks SIGNIFICANTLY FASTER with a simple single-hue sequential blue colormap than with viridis (the perceptually-uniform multi-hue colormap), RdYlBu divergent, or jet rainbow — i.e., the perceptually-uniform colormap was NOT the fastest."

**Source:** https://www.mdpi.com/2076-3417/9/20/4228 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Our results show that participants complete their tasks significantly faster with blue (single-hue, sequential) as compared to viridis (multi-hue, sequential), RdYlBu (divergent, red-yellow-blue), and jet (rainbow) colormaps."

*Evidence:* Claim is well-supported by the primary source and not an overreach. The paper "A Study of Colormaps in Network Visualization" (Applied Sciences 9(20):4228, 2019, Karim et al.; MDPI, peer-reviewed) is a controlled user study on node-link network diagrams comparing blue (single-hue sequential), viridis (multi-hue sequential), RdYlBu (divergent), and jet (rainbow). The abstract states verbatim: participants "complete their tasks significantly faster with blue (single-hue, sequential) as compared to viridis (multi-hue, sequential), RdYlBu (divergent, red-yellow-blue), and jet (rainbow) colormaps" — matching the supporting quote exactly. Statistical significance was reported. The claim is honestly scoped: it confines itself to node-link NETWORK visualization and to task SPEED, and does not overgeneralize; "perceptually-uniform colormap was NOT the fastest" is literally true here. Source quality (primary peer-reviewed controlled study with significance testing) matches the claim's strength; not marketing/press release/forum. Current enough (2019, slow-moving field). IMPORTANT BOUNDING CAVEAT (does not refute): the same study reports a speed-accuracy tradeoff — viridis was the LEAST error-prone while blue was the MOST error-prone (single-hue blue had poor discriminability at small value spans and high luminance hurt accuracy on a white background). The claim only asserts speed, so it stands, but it should be paired with the accuracy finding, not read as "blue beats viridis overall." One secondary AI summary garbled this as "viridis takes shortest time," contradicting the authoritative abstract; discounted as a summarization error since the primary abstract is unambiguous (blue fastest, viridis most accurate).
*Counter-source:* No credible source disputes the SPEED finding. The only near-contradiction was an AI-generated secondary summary on the same study claiming "viridis takes the shortest time," which conflicts with the paper's own abstract and is a summarization error, not independent evidence. The real qualifier is internal: the study's own accuracy results (viridis least error-prone, blue most error-prone) show a speed-accuracy tradeoff that bounds — but does not refute — the speed-only claim.

#### V220 `af55f6f6` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "The paper's central thesis is contrarian to blanket anti-rainbow dogma: rather than declaring the rainbow colormap universally harmful, it frames the evidence as both 'for and against' and seeks to establish precisely WHEN (under which tasks/conditions) rainbow impairs performance versus when it does not. This bounds the 'perceptually-uniform is always better' claim as task-dependent."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0924271622002659 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "consolidates findings from empirical studies to better understand why and precisely when the rainbow color scheme might impair human performance with visualizations"

*Evidence:* The claim accurately describes the source paper (Gao/Kovesi et al., ISPRS Journal of Photogrammetry & Remote Sensing, Vol. 194, pp. 195-208, 2022; ADS 2022JPRS..194..195G). Three points of verification:

1. QUOTE SUPPORT: The supporting quote is genuine and matches the paper's own framing. The full title is literally "What's wrong with the rainbow? An interdisciplinary review of empirical evidence FOR AND AGAINST the rainbow color scheme in visualizations" — the "for and against" framing is in the title itself, not an interpretive overreach.

2. "PRECISELY WHEN / TASK-DEPENDENT" THESIS CONFIRMED: Independent retrievals of the abstract/conclusions state: "Conflicting findings about human performance with the rainbow color scheme are most likely explained by what users are asked to do with it (i.e., task type)" and "the rainbow colormap is not universally bad — its effectiveness is task-dependent and context-dependent." The abstract documents BOTH sides: sequential colors facilitate most quantitative map-reading tasks better, BUT rainbow may "competitively facilitate extracting specific values and support hue recall." This is exactly the "establishes precisely WHEN it impairs vs. when it does not" reading the claim asserts.

3. SOURCE QUALITY & CORROBORATION: Primary, peer-reviewed interdisciplinary review (analyzing 37,000+ figures in 11,808 papers), 2022 — current for an active subfield. No contradicting source found; instead independent corroboration exists in Ware et al., "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad" (IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications, 2023), which makes the same task-dependent argument.

Minor caveat (does not rise to refutation): the title "What's wrong with the rainbow?" leads with critique, so calling the central thesis flatly "contrarian to anti-rainbow dogma" slightly overstates the paper's rhetorical center of gravity — it is a both-sides review that still leans critical. But the claim's substantive content (for-and-against framing, establishing when rainbow impairs vs. helps, hence bounding "perceptually-uniform is always better" as task-dependent) is faithfully supported by the title, abstract, and stated conclusions, plus an independent corroborating source. WebFetch on ScienceDirect returned 403, but ADS, ResearchGate, and Semantic Scholar metadata triangulate the same wording.
*Counter-source:* No disconfirming source found. Searched for critiques; the only adjacent work (Ware et al., "Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad," IEEE CG&A 2023, https://dl.acm.org/doi/abs/10.1109/MCG.2023.3246111) corroborates rather than contradicts the task-dependent thesis.

#### V221 `af860ea4` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "In relative-distance color discrimination tasks, viridis maintained consistently low error across all comparison spans, while the single-hue Blues colormap matched viridis accuracy at larger spans but degraded notably at the smallest span — showing perceptual-uniformity benefits are span/task-dependent, not uniform."

**Source:** https://idl.uw.edu/papers/quantitative-color (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Viridis exhibits consistently low error across the board ... [Blues] drops notably for the smallest span."

*Evidence:* Verified against the primary PDF (Liu & Heer, "Somewhere Over the Rainbow: An Empirical Assessment of Quantitative Colormaps," CHI 2018, UW IDL — peer-reviewed controlled study). The body contains the exact figure caption: "Viridis exhibits consistently low error across the board. The accuracy of blues matches that of viridis at larger spans, but drops notably for the smallest span." A section heading independently states "Error: Viridis Excels; Blues Degrades for Low Spans," and the abstract states "single-hue colormaps exhibit higher error over small data value ranges, supporting the argument that multi-hue colormaps can provide improved resolution." The task is confirmed as "relative similarity judgments across varying scale locations and value spans" (spans 15/30/60, comparing which point is farther from a reference) = a relative-distance discrimination task. Viridis is confirmed as the multi-hue perceptual-uniformity colormap (CAM02-UCS, ramping hue+luminance); Blues is single-hue. The interpretive gloss ("perceptual-uniformity benefits are span/task-dependent, not uniform") is supported, even understated, by the paper's own framing. Quote matches claim exactly; source is primary and current; no contradicting evidence found.

*Claim under review:* "WCAG 1.4.1 (Level A) normatively requires that color not be the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element — i.e. color-coding alone is non-conformant and must be paired with text, shape, icons, or patterns."

**Supporting quote:** "Color is not used as the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element."

*Evidence:* Verified against W3C primary source (w3.org/WAI/WCAG21/Understanding/use-of-color.html). The supporting quote is the VERBATIM normative text of Success Criterion 1.4.1 Use of Color: "Color is not used as the only visual means of conveying information, indicating an action, prompting a response, or distinguishing a visual element." Conformance Level A: confirmed. The claim's interpretation that color-coding alone is non-conformant is correct and matches W3C's documented common failures (status indicators by color only, links without underlines, red-asterisk-only required fields). The "must be paired with text, shape, icons, or patterns" wording is framed as examples ("i.e.") and matches W3C's documented sufficient techniques; the SC does not prescribe those specific alternatives but requires *some* additional visual means, which the claim states correctly. Current standard (WCAG 2.1; SC 1.4.1 retained unchanged in 2.2). No credible source disputes this foundational criterion. Claim is correctly scoped to color used to convey meaning (decorative color is exempt), which the claim's "color-coding" phrasing respects. Source quality (W3C primary normative spec) matches the claim's strength.

#### V223 `af944dad` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Radix specifies text-step contrast using APCA (Lc) rather than WCAG 2 ratios, guaranteeing the two text steps reach Lc 60 and Lc 90 against the step-2 background of the same scale. This is a modern design system adopting APCA as its contrast metric, bearing directly on the APCA-vs-WCAG2 debate."

**Source:** https://www.radix-ui.com/colors/docs/palette-composition/understanding-the-scale (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Steps `11` and `12`—which are designed for text—are guaranteed to Lc 60 and Lc 90 APCA contrast ratio on top of a step `2` background from the same scale."

*Evidence:* Verified verbatim on the official Radix Colors primary source (https://www.radix-ui.com/colors/docs/palette-composition/understanding-the-scale): "Steps 11 and 12—which are designed for text—are guaranteed to Lc 60 and Lc 90 APCA contrast ratio on top of a step 2 background from the same scale." Step 11 = low-contrast text (Lc 60), Step 12 = high-contrast text (Lc 90). This is the design system's own normative documentation, so it is the authoritative source for what Radix specifies — no higher-quality source exists. The claim's factual core (Radix uses APCA/Lc, guarantees Lc 60 and Lc 90 for the two text steps against a step-2 background) matches the quote exactly. Radix is a current, widely-used modern design system, and APCA remains the candidate contrast metric in the WCAG 3 draft, so the claim is not outdated. The one minor caveat: the phrase "rather than WCAG 2 ratios" is slightly stronger than the page text (the page documents APCA but does not explicitly reject WCAG 2) — but it is a fair characterization since the documented metric for the text steps is APCA, not WCAG 2. This caveat does not rise to refutation. The framing that this "bears directly on the APCA-vs-WCAG2 debate" is reasonable interpretation, as Radix adopting APCA as its documented text-contrast metric is a genuine real-world data point for that debate.

#### V224 `af9957ea` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "Legibility rankings do not transfer across display technologies: neither the printed (Le Courier) legibility table nor the CRT legibility table correctly predicts text legibility on LCDs, so screen-color guidance derived from print or older CRT studies is not valid for modern emissive sRGB panels."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0003687014000696 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "The results show that neither the Le Courier legibility table nor the CRT legibility table is appropriate for LCDs."

*Evidence:* VERIFIED, NOT REFUTED. Source: Humar, Gradišar, Turk & Erjavec (2014), Applied Ergonomics 45(6):1510-1517, "The impact of color combinations on the legibility of text presented on LCDs" (PubMed 24874503; ScienceDirect S0003687014000696). Peer-reviewed, 308 participants, 56 color combinations. The paper's stated conclusion is verbatim the supporting quote: "neither the Le Courier legibility table nor the CRT legibility table is appropriate for LCDs." The Le Courier table (1912) is a print/reflective ranking; the CRT table is a prior emissive-display study — so the claim's first clause is a faithful paraphrase, not an overreach. Mechanism is concrete: the study found a polarity REVERSAL — positive-polarity (dark-on-light) combos rank highest on LCD vs negative-polarity (light-on-dark) highest on CRT — demonstrating rankings actually invert across technologies. Independently corroborated by Hollands, Parker, McFadden & Boothby (2002, Human Factors): LCD vs CRT differ for colored symbols (reduced red/blue sensitivity off-axis on LCD) because CRT uses phosphor emission while LCD uses backlight+color-filter, giving different per-channel spectra. No contradicting study found; corroboration converges. Two minor caveats (noted, below refutation bar): (1) the study tested 2014 LCDs, not OLED/wide-gamut DCI-P3, yet the claim generalizes to "modern emissive sRGB panels" — but newer panels diverge from print/CRT MORE, reinforcing the claim; (2) LCD is technically transmissive (backlit), so "emissive" is loose terminology. Neither caveat contradicts the substantive, well-evidenced claim. Source quality (reputable ergonomics journal, large N) matches claim strength; claim is current in principle.
*Counter-source:* No contradicting source found. Closest scope-limiter: study tested only 2014-era LCDs, not OLED/DCI-P3 panels — but Hollands et al. 2002 (Human Factors) corroborates cross-technology divergence rather than refuting it.

#### V225 `afd4dfcc` — **upheld** · confidence: high
*Claim under review:* "High colour contrast does NOT guarantee fast/legible perception when luminance contrast is small (the equiluminant case), because human visual acuity is far lower for pure-chromatic information than for luminance information. This [STRONG] finding refutes the common [MYTH] that strong hue contrast alone is enough for readable UI text."

**Source:** https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0141938204000046 (primary)
**Supporting quote:** "Moderate or even high colour contrast does not guarantee quick visual perception, if the luminance contrast between characters and background is small. This is probably due to the fact that visual acuity (the ability to see small details) is considerably lower for pure colour information than for luminance information."

*Evidence:* Claim survives adversarial review. (1) FAITHFUL TO QUOTE: the claim restates the source quote almost verbatim — high/moderate colour contrast does not guarantee quick perception when luminance contrast is small, because acuity is lower for pure-chromatic than luminance info. The [MYTH] framing is a fair inversion, no overreach. (2) STRONG INDEPENDENT SUPPORT: the underlying physiology is textbook, anchored in Mullen 1985 (J. Physiol., PMC1193381): chromatic contrast-sensitivity is low-pass and falls off faster than luminance above ~0.5 c/deg; red-green acuity cutoff ~11 c/deg, blue-yellow (S-cone) worse, vs luminance ~50 c/deg — so 'far lower' chromatic acuity is defensible. (3) APPLIED CORROBORATION: LCD legibility literature (Applied Ergonomics, S0003687014000696) states luminance contrast — not the specific colour — is the key factor for text legibility and that a minimum luminance contrast must always be present; WCAG 1.4.3 and APCA both score text contrast on luminance, not hue, which is the same principle in practice. (4) ADVERSARIAL CHECK: the only contrary literature I found is the Livingstone-Hubel isoluminance debate — but it disputes a DIFFERENT, stronger claim (that colour plays no role in form/motion/illusions; later studies showed tilt illusions and contour integration survive isoluminance). Even those critics concede chromatic spatial acuity is poor/low-pass, so this does not refute the specific acuity-for-fine-detail claim under review. (5) NOT OUTDATED (vision-science fundamental stable since 1980s) and NOT marketing (peer-reviewed). Minor caveat: the [STRONG] tag is best cited to Mullen 1985 rather than the 2004 Displays paper alone, but that strengthens rather than weakens the claim. Note: could not fetch the source URL directly (HTTP 403), but the quoted content is fully consistent with the convergent peer-reviewed and standards literature.
*Counter-source:* Livingstone-Hubel isoluminance debate (Frontiers Psychology 2014, fpsyg.2014.00932) — but it disputes colour's role in FORM/MOTION, not the chromatic-acuity claim; even critics agree chromatic spatial acuity is poor/low-pass, so it does not refute this claim.

      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The single most-cited controlled experiment challenging Tufte's chartjunk/data-ink doctrine. Bateman, Mandryk, Gutwin, Genest, McDine & Brooks compared Holmes-style embellished charts against plain minimalist versions: interactive comprehension/accuracy was no worse for embellished charts, and long-term recall (after a delay) was significantly BETTER for the embellished ones. This is the empirical anchor for 'data-ink ratio is opinion, not a tested law' — directly serving the evidence-vs-myth goal. Gives the 'it depends on task complexity' nuance that an evidence-based skill needs when deciding whether decoration is acceptable."

{
  "results": [
    {
      "url": "https://arxiv.org/abs/1509.03700",
      "title": "Kovesi, P. (2015). Good Colour Maps: How to Design Them (arXiv:1509.03700)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The canonical citeable methodology for perceptually-uniform colormap design. Establishes the core empirical principle: uniform incremental change in perceptual LIGHTNESS is the single most important factor, and that CIELAB is only perceptually uniform at very LOW spatial frequencies (a subtlety earlier work missed, requiring CAM02-UCS-based correction). Quantifies the failure mode of vendor maps: 'perceptual flat spots' that hide features up to 1/10 of the data range, and 'perceptual discontinuities' that induce false features. Underpins the ColorCET/CET map family. Directly answers the magnitude-encoding (continuous) question and explains WHY rainbow/jet fails mechanistically."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://ieeexplore.ieee.org/document/4118486/",
      "title": "Borland, D. & Taylor, R.M. (2007). Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful. IEEE Computer Graphics & Applications, 27(2):14-17",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The foundational peer-reviewed statement of why rainbow/jet is harmful, with three named, evidence-illustrated failure modes: (1) lack of perceptual ordering, (2) uncontrolled/non-monotonic luminance that obscures data, (3) introduction of non-data-dependent gradients ('kinks') that actively mislead. Cites the clinical finding that physicians using jet for cardiac diagnosis are slower and make more errors. This is the paper to cite for 'rainbow is harmful' as established science rather than opinion."

Quantifies perceptual uniformity in CAM02-UCS: cividis keeps local perceptual deltas <0.2 vs >0.8 for jet — a hard number for the 'how non-uniform is jet' claim. Directly serves both the continuous-magnitude encoding question and the CVD-safe (~8% of men) requirement in one citeable source."

      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The primary peer-reviewed reference behind ColorBrewer. Establishes the three-way scheme taxonomy keyed to data type — SEQUENTIAL (ordered magnitude), DIVERGING (deviation from a midpoint, relevant to threshold/reference framing), and QUALITATIVE (categorical series) — plus colorblind-safe and display-environment (LCD/laptop/print) filters.       "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Empirical source for the categorical-color CEILING. Healey (with Enns) showed that color distance, linear separation, AND color category must all be controlled to get a set of equally distinguishable colors, and found only ~5-7 colors can be identified rapidly and at low error rate (preattentively). This is the citeable basis for capping a categorical palette around 6-7 series — directly answers the '3-6 data series' palette question and the preattentive distinguishability limit."

{
  "results": [
    {
      "url": "https://www.nngroup.com/articles/dark-mode/",
      "title": "Dark Mode vs. Light Mode: Which Is Better? — Nielsen Norman Group",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The single best entry point for the contested cluster: a literature synthesis by the leading UX-research authority that separates evidence from folklore and cites the foundational primary studies. Reports the 'positive polarity advantage' — light mode wins for both visual-acuity AND proofreading tasks across young (18-33) and old (60-85) normal-vision adults (Piepenbrock et al. 2013). Gives the mechanism: higher light-mode luminance constricts the pupil, reducing spherical aberration and increasing depth of field. Crucially documents the EXCEPTIONS that make 'light is always better' a myth: people with cloudy ocular media / cataracts read FASTER in dark mode (Legge et al. 1985), and the daytime polarity effect can vanish (Dobres et al. 2017). Also surfaces the one long-term counterweight — sustained light-mode reading thinned the choroid, a myopia-associated change, while dark mode thickened it (Aleman et al. 2018). Directly answers the 'is light/dark easier on the eyes' and background-choice questions for an analytical dashboard."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25135324/",
      "title": "Smaller pupil size and better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays (Piepenbrock, Mayr & Buchner, Ergonomics 2014) — PubMed",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The keystone peer-reviewed primary source establishing the causal mechanism behind the polarity effect, and a directly citeable knowledge-base anchor. Measured pupil size simultaneously with task performance and found pupils were smaller AND proofreading better under positive polarity (dark-on-light). This is the empirical support for the 'display luminance hypothesis' — brighter displays shrink the pupil, sharpening the retinal image — which reframes the debate: the benefit is about luminance/contrast, not the colors per se. (Note: the page is CAPTCHA-gated for automated fetch; finding confirmed via the indexed abstract.)"

That last point is the closest peer-reviewed evidence against the popular 'green-or-amber on black is easier on the eyes' intuition: the polarity (luminance) effect dominates the specific hue pairing. {
  "results": [
    {
      "url": "https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/01621459.1984.10478080",
      "title": "Cleveland & McGill (1984), \"Graphical Perception: Theory, Experimentation, and Application to the Development of Graphical Methods,\" J. American Statistical Association 79(387):531-554",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "THE foundational primary source for the assigned angle. Proposes the theory of graphical perception (visual decoding of encoded quantitative information) and, via controlled experiments, establishes the canonical ranking of elementary perceptual tasks by judgment accuracy: (1) position along a common scale, (2) position along non-aligned/identical scales, (3) length/direction/angle, (4) area, (5) volume/curvature, (6) shading/color saturation. Directly grounds the design rule 'encode with the highest-accuracy channel possible' and the core caution against using AREA (rank 4) to encode magnitude in a bubble/scatter timeline. This JASA link is the canonical publisher-of-record citation; full text is widely mirrored (Semantic Scholar paper id fa459de6, ResearchGate pub 6062457)."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://idl.uw.edu/papers/crowdsourcing-graphical-perception",
      "title": "Heer & Bostock (2010), \"Crowdsourcing Graphical Perception: Using Mechanical Turk to Assess Visualization Design,\" Proc. ACM CHI 2010, pp. 203-212 (UW IDL author page)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The key modern replication, confirmed via this canonical author-hosted page (authors, venue CHI 2010, pp. 203-212, PDF). Re-ran Cleveland & McGill's proportional-judgment experiments on Mechanical Turk and found crowdsourced results reproduce the lab ranking and adhere to theoretical predictions (validating the position>length>angle>area ordering on a modern population/medium). Adds NEW experiments most relevant to the bubble-encoding question: rectangular-area perception (as in treemaps/cartograms) and chart size / gridline spacing. Their area analysis builds on Stevens' power law (psi = k*I^alpha) and Spence's work, the quantitative basis for why area judgments are systematically biased (people under-estimate large areas). Strongest-evidence layer for area/size encoding accuracy."

#### `ac67b913` (Pass 1 — encoding / CVD / colormap science (2026-06-21))
```json
{
  "summary": "The peer-reviewed literature gives a strong, convergent answer for the parts of this question it covers. (1) Graphical perception is empirically settled: Cleveland & McGill (1984) established and ranked elementary perceptual tasks by decoding accuracy — position along a common scale is most accurate, then length, then angle, then AREA, with color/shading least accurate — and Heer & Bostock (2010) reproduced this ranking via crowdsourcing (position significantly beat length; area performed worse than angle and far worse than position). This directly warns against encoding magnitude by dot/bubble AREA. (2) Color-encoding science is also well-supported: perceptually-uniform colormaps (Viridis, Cividis) are built on uniform lightness progression in perceptual color spaces (CIELAB/CIECAM02-UCS), rainbow/jet maps are demonstrably harmful (no perceptual order, uncontrolled non-monotonic luminance, false boundaries), ColorBrewer's sequential/diverging/qualitative typology is the foundational palette-matching framework, and for rapid (preattentive) visual search only ~5-7 categorical colors are reliably identifiable — so a 3-6 series chart is safely within range. (3) By contrast, Tufte's chartjunk/data-ink prescription is convention, not a uniformly supported empirical finding: controlled experiments found no comprehension penalty from embellishment, with mixed results overall. #### `a2dd4a17` (Pass 2 — colour harmony / preference / emotion (2026-06-26))
```json
{
  "summary": "For the two sub-topics the surviving evidence actually covers — color harmony and color emotion/psychology — the verdict is consistent and skeptical. (1) Classical \"harmony rules\" (complementary, analogous, triadic) are aesthetic CONVENTION, not validated predictors of human preference: the foundational algorithm (Cohen-Or 2006) concedes no empirical definition of harmony exists, and controlled studies disagree on which rule even holds (Schloss & Palmer 2011 find similarity preferred; Forni 2025 find hue-averaged contrast preferred and refute the hue-independence the rules assume). What does predict color-pair preference is component-color preference plus lightness contrast, with \"harmony\" a correlated-but-distinct construct; single-color preference is best explained by Ecological Valence Theory (preference tracks the valence of objects associated with a color — WAVE r=0.893, ~80% variance, peaks at blue/cyan, troughs at chartreuse/brown). (2) Color→emotion ASSOCIATIONS are empirically real, systematic, and broadly cross-cultural (132 studies, 42,266 participants, 64 countries; cross-nation pattern r=.88), organized on robust dimensions (warm/cool, heavy/light, active/passive; valence/arousal/power) and driven by lightness/saturation/temperature — but they are MANY-TO-MANY and partly culture-modulated, which falsifies the pop \"one color = one emotion\" trope. The specific \"red boosts attractiveness\" claim is the cautionary MYTH-adjacent case: meta-analytic d≈0.13–0.26 with strong publication bias, and two pre-registered replications returned null (d=0.09 and d=−0.09). Critically, the surviving claims cover ONLY sub-topics 1 and 2 — screens, gradients, functional palette structure, anti-patterns, constructive perceptual effects, and modern design systems have no verified claims here and need separate sourcing.",
  "findings": [
    {
      "claim": "[CONVENTION/MYTH] Classical 'harmony rules' (complementary, analogous, triadic, split-complementary) are aesthetic convention with no validated power to predict human preference. The foundational color-harmonization algorithm itself concedes there is no formal or empirically validated definition of a harmonic color set — its rotatable hue-wheel templates rest on artist consensus, not controlled-study results — and the templates are defined by RELATIVE hue relationships, not absolute RGB values. Controlled studies do not even agree on which rule holds: Schloss & Palmer (2011) found pair preference and harmony both INCREASE with hue similarity (strong-contrast/complementary pairs were judged neither preferred nor harmonious), while Forni et al. (2025) found that hue-averaged preference favors the CONTRAST region (~160-220°) and that the classical 'similarity' rule fails. Forni et al. also show preference is highly hue-dependent, empirically refuting the hue-independence assumption underlying the rules (that only the angular distance between hues matters). The shared takeaway across the disagreement: no single geometric harmony rule reliably predicts preference.",
      "confidence": "high",
      "vote": "3-0 across all merged claims",
      "sources": [
        "https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0",
        "https://igl.ethz.ch/projects/color-harmonization/harmonization.pdf",
        "https://arxiv.org/pdf/2508.15777"
      ],
      "evidence": "Cohen-Or et al. 2006 (SIGGRAPH, primary PDF verified verbatim): 'currently there is no formulation that defines a harmonic set, there is a consensus among artists... Professional artists usually rely on experience and intuition' and 'Harmony among colors is not determined by specific colors, but rather by their relative position in color space' (8 rotatable templates i/V/L/I/T/Y/X/N). Schloss & Palmer 2011 (Atten Percept Psychophys 73:551-571, primary): 'pair preference and harmony both increase as hue similarity increases... No evidence favoring art theoretic claims that color combinations with strong hue contrasts are either preferred or harmonious.' Forni/Darmon/Benzaquen 2025 (arXiv:2508.15777, N=346): 'the assumption of hue independence cannot be reasonably upheld' and 'Higher preferences are observed in the contrast region (between 160 and 220)... the other preference regions [Moon & Spencer] proposed—coined similarity—are not consistently supported.' NOTE the Schloss-Palmer (similarity preferred, lab, Munsell) vs Forni (contrast preferred, online survey, HSL) tension is itself the strongest evidence that no single rule generalizes; both are correctly scoped. CAVEATS: Cohen-Or operates in non-perceptual HSV; Forni is a non-peer-reviewed preprint using non-perceptual HSL with an online sample."
    },
    {
      "claim": "[STRONG] What actually predicts preference for a color PAIR is component-color preference plus lightness contrast, with perceived 'harmony' a strongly correlated but statistically distinct construct. Harmony and preference correlate at r=+.79 (harmony alone explains 62.3% of preference variance); a combined model of harmony + figure preference + ground preference + lightness-difference explains 80.8%. Practical implication for UI: pick individually-liked colors and ensure adequate lightness contrast rather than chasing a hue-wheel 'scheme.'",
      "confidence": "high",
      "vote": "Claim 1 (3-0 unanimous)",

Harmony and preference are distinct: harmony ratings are 'essentially unaffected by their single color preferences' whereas preference is driven by them.     },
    {
      "claim": "[STRONG] Single-color preference is best explained by Ecological Valence Theory: preference tracks the average affective valence of objects characteristically associated with each color (a learned/ontogenetic mechanism), not an innate hue preference or a geometric harmony rule. A Weighted Affective Valence Estimate (WAVE) — built from color-object associations, object-valence ratings, and match strength with NO fitted free parameters — predicts adult color preference at r=+0.893 (~80% of variance). Preference peaks at blue/cyan (clear skies, clean water) and troughs at chartreuse and dark-yellow/brown (feces, rotting food). Convergent evidence: preferred color PAIRINGS statistically match hue distributions in natural landscape images (peaks blue and orange-yellow, minima greens/purples), suggesting preference is shaped by ecological exposure rather than abstract harmony geometry.",

      "evidence": "Palmer & Schloss 2010 (PNAS 107:8877-8882, primary, verified via PMC2889342): WAVE r=+0.893, 'accounting for 80% of the variance with a single predictor', 'no free parameters were estimated in calculating the WAVE'; 'broad peak at blue' linked to 'clear sky and clean water', 'narrow trough at chartreuse' and browns linked to 'feces and rotten food'. #### `a8613a8a` (Pass 2 — colour harmony / preference / emotion (2026-06-26))
```json
{
  "results": [
    {
      "url": "https://link.springer.com/article/10.3758/s13414-010-0027-0",
      "title": "Schloss & Palmer (2011), \"Aesthetic response to color combinations: preference, harmony, and similarity,\" Attention, Perception, & Psychophysics",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The single most load-bearing empirical paper for sub-topic 1. It operationally SEPARATES pair preference (do you like the pair) from pair harmony (do the colors 'go together') and from similarity, then shows both preference and harmony rise as hue SIMILARITY increases. Crucially: empirical support exists for analogous-hue harmonies, but virtually NONE for complementary/contrastive hues once statistical corrections are applied — i.e., the textbook complementary 'rule' does not predict preference. Preference also depends more on component-color preference and lightness contrast than on the hue relationship. This is the citation that lets you tag complementary/triadic 'rules' as [CONVENTION]/[MYTH] and analogous as [STRONG-ish]."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.researchgate.net/publication/43345682_An_ecological_valence_theory_of_color_preferences",
      "title": "Palmer & Schloss (2010), \"An ecological valence theory of color preferences,\" PNAS",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The canonical Ecological Valence Theory (EVT) paper named in the question. Argues color preference is the average affective valence of the objects/entities a person associates with that color (e.g., people who like clear-sky blue, dislike rotten-brown). Predicts ~80% of preference variance from object valences — a primary, large-effect alternative to innate 'color psychology.' Anchors the evidence-based framing that preference is learned/ecological, not a fixed property of the wavelength."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://igl.ethz.ch/projects/color-harmonization/harmonization.pdf",
      "title": "Cohen-Or, Sorkine, Gal, Leyvand & Xu (2006), \"Color Harmonization,\" ACM SIGGRAPH / ACM TOG 25(3):624-630 (full PDF)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The exact harmonization paper cited in the prompt (full author PDF). Defines harmony COMPUTATIONALLY via fixed harmonic 'templates' — gray sectors on the HSV hue wheel (types i,V,L,I,T,Y,X) that can be rotated to any angle; colors are harmonic by their relative POSITIONS, not by being specific colors. Method finds the best-fit template for an image and warps hues into it. Important caveat for the math-vs-taste split: it operates in HSV (not a perceptual space) and the templates are Matsuda's design convention, not validated against measured human preference — so it's an algorithmic encoding of [CONVENTION], useful but not proof a rule predicts liking."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.researchgate.net/publication/326651521_Universal_models_of_colour_emotion_and_colour_harmony",
      "title": "Ou, Luo, et al., \"Universal models of colour emotion and colour harmony,\" Color Research & Application",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Seeds the Ou & Luo strand named in the prompt. Builds quantitative regression models of two-color harmony and of color emotion (activity/weight/heat factors) from cross-cultural observer ratings, expressed in CIELAB/CIELCh terms — a primary source for parametric harmony scoring and for the color-emotion dimensions. Useful for showing where harmony can be modeled numerically (lightness/chroma/hue-difference terms) and where it remains observer-dependent."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://arxiv.org/pdf/2508.15777",

      "relevance": "medium",
      "snippet": "Recent empirical re-test directly on the math-vs-taste question. Collects human harmony/preference ratings for color pairs and compares perceived-harmonious hue separations against hue distributions measured in natural scenes — finding the preferred separations align with natural-image statistics rather than with the clean geometric harmony angles (complementary 180 deg, triadic 120 deg). Supports the EVT/ecological reading and is good for a current, citable counterweight to classical wheel-geometry harmony rules."

Introduces the Msh color space (a polar CIELAB) and an interpolation method that inserts an unsaturated neutral midpoint when the two endpoint hues are sufficiently distinct — the standard technique for avoiding muddy/gray dead-zones and false bands when blending two saturated endpoint colors. Adopted into VTK/ParaView (cool-warm map). Directly answers sub-topic 4's 'science of a good gradient' and why naive RGB interpolation fails."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://bids.github.io/colormap/",
      "title": "Smith & van der Walt — \"A Better Default Colormap for Matplotlib\" (viridis design + talk)",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Source for viridis (the de-facto perceptually-uniform default). Documents the design goals empirically: monotonically increasing perceptual lightness (uniform under CAM02-UCS), maximal perceptual range, robustness when converted to grayscale, and safety for the most common color-vision deficiency. Concrete, reproducible methodology — the 'how viridis was built' reference for building perceptually smooth, CVD-safe sequential gradients on sRGB displays."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.mathworks.com/content/dam/mathworks/tag-team/Objects/r/81137_92238v00_RainbowColorMap_57312.pdf",
      "title": "Borland & Taylor (2007), \"Rainbow Color Map (Still) Considered Harmful\" (IEEE CG&A) — overview + annotated bibliography",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "The canonical [STRONG] anti-pattern source for gradients/colormaps. Documents three mechanisms: (1) no perceptual ordering (hue/wavelength order is not magnitude order), (2) uncontrolled luminance variation hides low-contrast detail that a luminance ramp would reveal, and (3) non-data-dependent gradients create FALSE BOUNDARIES — sharp 'edges' that are colormap artifacts, not data. Note the counterpoint 'Rainbow Colormaps Are Not All Bad' (IEEE VIS) for the nuanced replication view."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oklab_color_space",
      "title": "Oklab / OKLCh color space (Ottosson 2020) — reference + CSS Color 4 gradient interpolation",
      "relevance": "high",
      "snippet": "Covers interpolation in a perceptual space for UI gradients on emissive sRGB. OKLCh is the CSS Color 4-recommended space for gradients/color-mix(); interpolating red->blue in OKLCh yields a vivid purple midpoint vs the muddy result in sRGB. Oklab specifically fixes the CIELAB blue-hue/lightness shift (blues drifting toward purple). Useful caveat for the reference: polar OKLCh can introduce an unintended-hue 'detour' (blue->yellow passing through green) and an off-line bow near the achromatic axis — so Oklab (rectangular) vs OKLCh (polar) matters for which gradient artifact you avoid."
    },
    {
      "url": "https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC10910436/",
      "title": "Longitudinal chromatic aberration & accommodation / S-cone acuity (vision-science primary sources)",
      "relevance": "medium",
      "snippet": "Display-ergonomics [STRONG] basis for why saturated pure-blue (and pure-red) is a poor choice for text/fine detail on screens. Mechanisms: longitudinal chromatic aberration changes fastest at short wavelengths so narrowband blue is the worst-focused; the S-cone (blue) channel's MTF cutoff is 3-5x lower than L/M channels; and the foveal S-cone-free zone means blue detail at fixation is intrinsically blurred. Grounds the rule 'don't render small blue type or thin blue lines at high saturation on sRGB.' (See also MIT 6.813 Reading 16: Color for a teaching-level synthesis.)"
    }
  ]
