#### `aa267c32` (Pass 3 — screens / contrast / gradients / design systems (2026-06-26))
```json
{
  "summary": "Across the six previously-unverified practical colour topics, the verified evidence converges on one through-line: luminance, not hue, governs legibility and contrast on self-lit screens, and several popular \"best practices\" are weaker or more bounded than commonly stated. Controlled studies show positive polarity (dark-on-light) out-reads dark mode for normal-vision adults on emissive LCDs, and low-luminance-contrast or equiluminant text is an anti-pattern even with a strong hue difference (chromatic acuity ~11-12 cpd vs luminance ~50-60 cpd). The strongest contrary finding targets colormaps: \"perceptually-uniform is always better\" is a MYTH — viridis was the WORST of three maps for gradient-steepness discrimination and the slowest (though most accurate) for network tasks, and CIELAB-distance uniformity imperfectly predicts feature detection. Modern design systems give concrete, falsifiable token rules tying contrast to lightness/tone difference (Radix 12-step semantic scales; Material 3 HCT tonal palettes where tones 50/98≈3:1 and 30/98≈7:1), and the APCA-vs-WCAG2 contrast debate is live in production (Radix specifies APCA Lc 60/90; M3 maps tonal distance to WCAG2 ratios). Major gaps survive: the 60-30-10 dominance convention, the classic perceptual-attention effects (pop-out, simultaneous contrast, chromostereopsis, Gestalt), and the pure-blue/red on-screen anti-patterns produced no surviving verified claims (the one physiological blue-red claim was refuted).",
  "findings": [
    {
      "claim": "[STRONG / MYTH-buster] Positive polarity (dark text on light background, i.e. light mode) reads better than negative polarity (dark mode) for normal-vision adults on emissive screens; the advantage is luminance-driven, and legibility rankings from print or CRT studies do NOT transfer to modern LCD/sRGB panels. Direct disconfirming evidence against any universal 'dark mode reads better' claim.",
      "confidence": "high",
      "vote": "two 3-0 claims + one 2-1 claim merged",
      "sources": [
        "https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25135324/",
        "https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0003687014000696"
      ],
      "evidence": "Piepenbrock, Mayr & Buchner 2014 (Ergonomics 57(11), PMID 25135324, controlled, normal-vision adults): 'better proofreading performance with positive than with negative polarity displays'; mechanism = positive polarity -> smaller pupil -> sharper retinal image; replicated 2013/2014 across younger+older adults and small character sizes; NN/g concurs (light mode better for acuity and proofreading 'across all dimensions, regardless of age'). Huang et al. 2014 Applied Ergonomics (308 participants, 56 colour combinations, Snellen task): 'neither the Le Courier [print] legibility table nor the CRT legibility table is appropriate for LCDs; for LCDs the highest ranked are contrastive combinations with positive polarity whereas for CRT displays the highest ranked are negative polarity.' BOUNDING CAVEAT (the 2-1 sub-claim): the LCD-vs-CRT difference is more precisely a LUMINANCE confound than a technology law (Buchner & Baumgartner: 'the advantage of positive text-background polarity is due to high display luminance'); CRTs were dimmer. Real boundary conditions where dark mode helps (low light, photophobia, cataract) do not contradict the normal-vision proofreading finding."
    },
    {
      "claim": "[STRONG / MYTH-buster] Luminance contrast — not hue/chroma contrast — drives text legibility. Search time, fixation count and fixation duration all rise strongly as luminance contrast falls even when colour contrast is high; moderate-to-high colour contrast does NOT guarantee fast perception at low luminance contrast (the equiluminant case). Refutes the myth that strong hue contrast alone makes UI text readable, and is the reason WCAG/APCA define contrast by luminance.",
      "confidence": "high",

      "evidence": "Displays (Elsevier, 2004, controlled, 3 character sizes): with colour contrast held HIGH, 'visual search times, number of eye fixations, and mean fixation durations increased strongly with decreasing luminance contrast despite the presence of colour contrast'; effect largest for small (0.17 deg) text. Mechanism is primary physiology — Mullen 1985 (J. Physiol. 359:381): chromatic (R-G, B-Y) contrast-sensitivity is low-pass, limiting acuity ~11-12 cycles/deg, vs luminance channel ~50-60 cpd; above ~0.5 cpd luminance sensitivity exceeds chromatic. NASA Color Usage Guidelines: 'chromaticity contrast cannot be substituted for luminance contrast... a minimum luminance contrast of 3.0 should always be present.' Minor scope note: near luminance threshold, hue can sustain ~300 wpm reading (Knoblauch/Arditi JOSA A 1991), so 'regardless of hue' is mildly overstated — but the anti-pattern (low-luminance-contrast text reads poorly even with strong hue difference) holds firmly for readable UI text. Also covers Topic 6's equiluminant-boundary anti-pattern."

      "evidence": "Moreland 2009 (ISVC, basis of the widely-used coolwarm map): 'piecewise linear interpolations in CIELAB color space cause Mach bands in the white part of diverging color maps; the transition can be softened by interpolating in Msh space' (Msh = polar CIELAB); fix requires 'leveling off of the luminance as the color map approaches white' while 'chromaticity must change more dramatically.' For rainbow/jet: 'colors appear to change faster in the cyan and yellow regions, which can cause Mach bands... change more slowly in the blue, green, and red regions, which creates larger bands of color [that] can hide important changes in the underlying data.'
```
